AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Java: A Secure Platform for M2M
DATE: 01/04/2013 17:34:39
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
If not already, certainly very soon, the number of devices connected to the Internet will exceed the human population on planet Earth.&nbsp; With this phenomenon, referred to as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_of_Things"><i>Internet of Things</i></a>, one famous prediction states that the number of addressable devices will reach 50 billion in the not too distant future.&nbsp; <br /></p> 
  <p>As the potential for Java is fully realized in the burgeoning M2M (Machine to Machine) market, a new group of decision makers, developers and administrators, perhaps not entirely familiar with this technology, will likely be brought into the fold.&nbsp; On more than one occasion recently, we've heard suitors ask a question 
similar to the following:&nbsp; &quot;We understand that Java has some inherent 
security advantages.&nbsp; Can you explain what you mean by that?&quot;</p> 
  <p>Much has been written about Java security, some in excruciating detail.&nbsp; The standard online Java 7 documentation set contains a <a href="http://docs.oracle.com/javase/7/docs/technotes/guides/security/">Java security section</a>.&nbsp; And <a href="http://www.amazon.com/Java-Security-Edition-Scott-Oaks/dp/0596001576/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1355375150&amp;sr=8-1&amp;keywords=java+security">Scott Oaks' <i>Java Security</i> book</a>, even dated, is still an excellent and relevant reference for understanding this subject.&nbsp; What follows is a high-level overview of Java security designed to be read and digested in the few moments it takes to read this article.</p> 
  <p>Security considerations have been built in to the Java platform on many levels, from the syntax of the programming language itself to the behavior of the runtime environment to the add-on capabilities that can be plugged in to further enhance security.&nbsp; So let's look at it from those perspectives.</p> 
  <p> <b>Java Language Security Features</b>: From the outset, developer productivity and security were given top priority by the Java designers when considering Java programming language syntax.&nbsp; In many cases, the goals of these two priorities are quite complimentary.&nbsp; That is to say, the consequences of introducing a construct to enhance productivity often brought with it the benefit of added security.&nbsp; In general, it is much more difficult to exploit traditional security weaknesses found in older languages like C and C++.&nbsp; For example, Java:<br /></p> 
  <ul> 
    <li><b>Automatically performs range checking on array references</b> - by default, every array reference is automatically checked by the Java runtime to make sure that it falls within the range of the size of the array.&nbsp; This feature not only enables the developer to more easily diagnose errors, but virtually eliminates the chances of the dreaded <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buffer_overflow">buffer overflow</a>, the culprit behind many a security exploit.&nbsp; In the name of performance, some languages (C,C++) do no range checking at all, and fall prey to all sorts of problems.</li> 
    <li><b>Eliminates pointers</b> - pointers avail developers with the sharpest of double-edged swords.&nbsp; They indeed provide great power to the developer, but also the potential to wreak tremendous havoc by allowing arbitrary access to a program's memory.&nbsp; There are no pointers in Java; thus the class of errors associated with that feature are greatly diminished.</li> 
    <li><b>Performs extensive compile-time checking</b> - Java, as a statically typed programming language is strict in the way it allows the mixing of different data types.&nbsp; The end result is a lot of potential problems will be flagged as errors by the Java compiler at compile time, forcing the developer to fix the conflict then and there.&nbsp; Other languages deemed more dynamically typed, are more permissive of certain constructs.&nbsp; Previously uncaught errors and security vulnerabilities are exposed here only during program execution.&nbsp; These types of errors are much harder to find and much more expensive to fix.</li> 
  </ul> 
  <p><b>Bytecode Verification</b>: Java source code is compiled down to binary <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Java_class_file">class files</a> consisting of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Java_bytecode">Java bytecodes</a>.&nbsp; When external class files are loaded into the Java Runtime Environment for execution, they are first examined by the bytecode verifier to see that the sequence of bytecodes are well behaved.&nbsp; The Java compiler does a great job of making sure data access is done in an appropriate manner and hence prevents many malevolent bytecode sequences from ever occurring.&nbsp; But what if someone were to construct a Java class file by hand bypassing the Java compiler and its strict rules?&nbsp; That's where the bytecode verifier comes into play.&nbsp; Among many other capabilities, the verifier will check to make sure branch instructions point to an appropriate contextual address and furthermore that data access is always type-safe.&nbsp; For a more detailed description of verification specifics, check out the <a href="http://docs.oracle.com/javase/specs/jvms/se7/html/jvms-4.html#jvms-4.10.2.2">Bytecode Verifier section in the Java Virtual Machine Specification documentation</a>.</p> 
  <p><b>The Sandbox</b>: Untrusted Java applications, applets and class files that are retrieved from external sources (e.g. the Internet), are run in such a way that they have no access to critical system resources.&nbsp; The computer science and security community often times refers to this construct as a <a href="http://docstore.mik.ua/orelly/java-ent/security/ch01_02.htm">sandbox</a>.&nbsp; As one of many possible examples, access to a computer or device's local file system would be forbidden in this mode of operation.&nbsp; Programs written with malicious intent or those that are just plain poorly written, would have minimal impact within the confines of the sandbox since they cannot get at any of the components deemed vital.&nbsp; A highly restrictive sandbox lends itself to a secure environment, but also one of limited functionality too.&nbsp; So the question arises: What if you need to provide additional permissions outside the default sandbox for an application?&nbsp; The answer lies in extending Java's SecurityManager which we'll touch on next.</p> 
  <p><b>The SecurityManager</b>: With the addition of a <a href="http://docs.oracle.com/javase/7/docs/api/java/lang/SecurityManager.html">SecurityManager</a> object, you can define a custom security policy for your application.&nbsp; The SecurityManager serves as a sentry, inspecting any operation that could be deemed dangerous prior to allowing that operation to take place.&nbsp; If the operation in question is not permitted, the SecurityManager will throw a <a href="http://docs.oracle.com/javase/7/docs/api/java/lang/SecurityException.html">SecurityException</a>, indicating a security violation.&nbsp; If the operation is allowed, the SecurityManager will silently return without throwing an exception.&nbsp; With this mechanism in place, one or more policy configuration files can be created to specify what resources an application has legitimate access to.&nbsp; Within these policy configuration files, <a href="http://docs.oracle.com/javase/7/docs/api/java/security/Permission.html">Permission</a> objects can be defined.&nbsp; By no means exhaustive, custom permissions can be associated with resources, such as:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>File system access</li> 
    <li>Network socket access</li> 
    <li>Ability to use Java features like Serialization and Reflection</li> 
    <li>Access to system resources like windows and print queues</li> 
    <li>Ability to manage the Java Virtual Machine instance<br /></li> 
    <li>Access to other Java classes and packages</li> 
    <li>Access to other thread groups</li> 
    <li>And much, much more!</li> 
  </ul> 
  <p><b>Cryptographic Framework</b>:&nbsp; This topic is worthy of it own voluminous publication, the Java platform provides cryptographic engines for the components needed to support full cryptography.&nbsp; These include authentication, encryption, message digests, digital signatures plus key and digital certificate management.&nbsp; Although default implementations of the various ingredients are provided by the Java Development Kit (JDK), the platform is architected in such a way that you have the option of providing alternative implementations.</p> 
  <p><b>Java Security is Continually Evolving</b>: No platform has been proven perfectly secure, and due to the complexities involved, likely never will.&nbsp; The cat-and-mouse game behind finding and fixing security exploits will be with us long into the future.&nbsp; Understanding this fact, Java security must (and will) continue to evolve.&nbsp; Major Java updates often bring with them significant enhancements to security.&nbsp; Here's a list of planned <a href="http://openjdk.java.net/projects/jdk8/features">features for Java 8</a>, a good number of which have security ramifications.&nbsp; Furthermore, periodic updates also address security concerns too.&nbsp; Java 7 update 10, as discussed in <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/henrik/entry/oracle_jdk_7u10_released_with">Henrik Stahl's blog</a>, contains a few interesting security enhancements including:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>Being able to track if the installed version of the Java runtime is updated to the latest security baseline.&nbsp; If the runtime is not at the latest level, the user will be warned if an attempt to run an unsigned applet takes place.<br /></li> 
    <li>Providing a &quot;best used before date&quot;.&nbsp; Assuming periodic security updates will take place, if the runtime hasn't been checked for an update prior to this expiration date, the runtime will assume it's in an insecure state and warn the user.</li> 
    <li>The ability from the Java Control Panel UI to quickly set a low-medium-high-very high security level<br /></li> 
  </ul> 
  <p>In summary, the <i>write once run anywhere</i> capabilities of Java make it an ideal common platform for M2M devices as they increase in functionality and connectivity.&nbsp; Security, both in it's current from, and as it continues to evolve, is yet another of the additional benefits derived by the Java platform.<br /> </p>
-----

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Subversion in a Solaris Zone
DATE: 09/17/2007 03:20:33
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
1. Create a Solaris zone called 'svn'<br /><p><i>Note: This step
is system specific.&nbsp; For example, the hostname and IP address for
'svn' was predefined.&nbsp; In addition, the network interface (i.e.
'set physical=iprb0') is likely to be different also.</i><br />
<br />
Here's the command-line session for creating the zone:<br />
</p><pre>phoenix://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">mkdir /zone</span><br />phoenix://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">zonecfg -z svn</span><br />svn: No such zone configured<br />Use 'create' to begin configuring a new zone.<br />zonecfg:svn&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">create</span><br />zonecfg:svn&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set zonepath=/zone/svn</span><br /><span style="font-weight: bold;"></span><span style="font-weight: bold;"></span>zonecfg:svn&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set autoboot=true</span><br />zonecfg:svn&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">add net</span><br />zonecfg:svn:net&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set address=svn</span><br />zonecfg:svn:net&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set physical=iprb0</span><br />zonecfg:svn:net&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">end</span><br />zonecfg:svn&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">info</span><br />zonepath: /zone/svn<br />autoboot: true<br />pool:<br />inherit-pkg-dir:<br />        dir: /lib<br />inherit-pkg-dir:<br />        dir: /platform<br />inherit-pkg-dir:<br />        dir: /sbin<br />inherit-pkg-dir:<br />        dir: /usr<br />net:<br />        address: svn<br />        physical: iprb0<br />zonecfg:toolzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">verify</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">commit</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">\^D<br /><br /></span>phoenix://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">zoneadm list -vc</span><br />  ID NAME             STATUS         PATH<br />   0 global           running        /<br />   - svn              configured     /zone/svn<br /><br />phoenix://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">zoneadm -z svn</span><span style="font-weight: bold;"> install</span><br />Preparing to install zone &lt;svn&gt;.<br />Creating list of files to copy from the global zone.<br />Copying &lt;6666&gt; files to the zone.<br />Initializing zone product registry.<br />Determining zone package initialization order.<br />Preparing to initialize &lt;945&gt; packages on the zone.<br />Initialized &lt;945&gt; packages on zone.<br />Zone &lt;svn&gt; is initialized.<br />Installation of &lt;2&gt; packages was skipped.<br />Installation of these packages generated warnings: &lt;SUNWcsu SUNWsogm&gt;<br />The file &lt;/zone/svn/root/var/sadm/system/logs/install_log&gt; contains a log of the zone installation.<br /></pre>2.&nbsp; Configure the zone.<br />
<br />
2a. Create a zone-specific /usr/local directory and copy the contents of
the
global zone's /usr/local to this new directory.&nbsp; This is required because by default, /usr/local is read-only in a local zone.&nbsp; So let's create our own local version so that we can add to it.<br />
<pre style="font-weight: bold;"><span style="font-weight: normal;">phoenix://#</span> mkdir -p /zone/svn/usr/local<br /><span style="font-weight: normal;">phoenix://#</span> cd /usr/local<br /><span style="font-weight: normal;">phoenix://#</span> tar cf - . | (cd /zone/svn/usr/local; tar xfp -)<br /></pre>
2b. Create a loopback mount for toolzone to this new version of
/usr/local:<br />
<pre><span style="font-weight: normal;">phoenix://</span># <span style="font-weight: bold;">zonecfg -z svn</span><br />zonecfg:svn&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">addfs</span><br />zonecfg:svn:fs&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set dir=/usr/local</span><br />zonecfg:svn:fs&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set special=/zone/svn</span><span style="font-weight: bold;">/usr/local</span><br />zonecfg:svn:fs&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set type=lofs</span><br />zonecfg:svn:fs&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">end</span><br />zonecfg:svn&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">\^D</span><br /></pre>
2c. Boot and configure the newly created zone:<br />
<pre>phoenix://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">zoneadm -z svn</span><span style="font-weight: bold;"> boot</span><br />phoenix://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">zlogin -C svn</span><!--<--><br /><br /></pre><p>2d. Further configuration/customization of the zone is left as an exercise to the user.</p><p>3. The <a title="SunFreeware" href="http://sunfreeware.com">SunFreeware</a> website contains many versions of popular open source applications that are available for the Solaris platform.&nbsp; For this exercise, we'll base our installation on subversion-1.4.4 which can be found <a href="http://sunfreeware.com/indexintel10.html">here</a>. Versions change quickly, and it is fully expected that in the future the dependent versions will become out of date.&nbsp; </p><p>There are a good number of software packages that are needed by subversion.&nbsp; The <a href="http://sunfreeware.com/subversion.html">additional installation packages</a> can be better understood by taking a look at the aforementioned link.</p><p>3a. Install within the svn zone all of the packages listed at the sunfreeware link above.&nbsp; They should be:</p><pre>   neon-0.25.5<br />   apache-2.0.59<br />   swig-1.3.29<br />   expat-1.95.5<br />   gdbm-1.8.3<br />   libxml2-2.6.26<br />   db-4.2.52.NC<br />   openssl-0.9.8e<br />   libiconv-1.11<br />   zlib-1.2.3<br />   libgcc-3.4.6<br />   subversion-1.4.4<br /><br /></pre><p>The downloadable packages are gzip'ed and in Solaris pkg format.&nbsp; For example, to install the first package, neon-0.25.5, download the appropriate Solaris 10 package (i.e. neon-0.25.5-so10-x86.local.gz), gunzip(1) it and pkgadd(1M) it.</p><pre>svn://# <b>gunzip neon-0.25.5-so10-x86.local.gz</b><br />svn://# <b>pkgadd -d &nbsp;neon-0.25.5-so10-x86.local</b><br /></pre><p>repeat for all of the required packages.</p><p>3b.&nbsp; You can confirm that things are at least marginally ok by issuing the following command:</p><pre>svn://# <b>ldd /usr/local/bin/svn</b><br />        libsvn_client-1.so.0 =&gt;  /usr/local/lib/libsvn_client-1.so.0<br />        libsvn_wc-1.so.0 =&gt;      /usr/local/lib/libsvn_wc-1.so.0<br />        libsvn_ra-1.so.0 =&gt;      /usr/local/lib/libsvn_ra-1.so.0<br />        libsvn_diff-1.so.0 =&gt;    /usr/local/lib/libsvn_diff-1.so.0<br />        libsvn_ra_local-1.so.0 =&gt;        /usr/local/lib/libsvn_ra_local-1.so.0<br />        libsvn_repos-1.so.0 =&gt;   /usr/local/lib/libsvn_repos-1.so.0<br />        libsvn_fs-1.so.0 =&gt;      /usr/local/lib/libsvn_fs-1.so.0<br />        libsvn_fs_fs-1.so.0 =&gt;   /usr/local/lib/libsvn_fs_fs-1.so.0<br />        libsvn_fs_base-1.so.0 =&gt;         /usr/local/lib/libsvn_fs_base-1.so.0<br />        libsvn_ra_svn-1.so.0 =&gt;  /usr/local/lib/libsvn_ra_svn-1.so.0<br />        libsvn_ra_dav-1.so.0 =&gt;  /usr/local/lib/libsvn_ra_dav-1.so.0<br />        libsvn_delta-1.so.0 =&gt;   /usr/local/lib/libsvn_delta-1.so.0<br />        libsvn_subr-1.so.0 =&gt;    /usr/local/lib/libsvn_subr-1.so.0<br />        libaprutil-0.so.0 =&gt;     /usr/local/lib/libaprutil-0.so.0<br />        libdb-4.2.so =&gt;  /usr/local/BerkeleyDB.4.2/lib/libdb-4.2.so<br />        libiconv.so.2 =&gt;         /usr/local/lib/libiconv.so.2<br />        libapr-0.so.0 =&gt;         /usr/local/lib/libapr-0.so.0<br />        libsendfile.so.1 =&gt;      /usr/lib/libsendfile.so.1<br />        librt.so.1 =&gt;    /usr/lib/librt.so.1<br />        libm.so.2 =&gt;     /usr/lib/libm.so.2<br />        libresolv.so.2 =&gt;        /usr/lib/libresolv.so.2<br />        libpthread.so.1 =&gt;       /usr/lib/libpthread.so.1<br />        libneon.so.25 =&gt;         /usr/local/lib/libneon.so.25<br />        libssl.so.0.9.8 =&gt;       /usr/local/ssl/lib/libssl.so.0.9.8<br />        libcrypto.so.0.9.8 =&gt;    /usr/local/ssl/lib/libcrypto.so.0.9.8<br />        libnsl.so.1 =&gt;   /usr/lib/libnsl.so.1<br />        libexpat.so.0 =&gt;         /usr/local/lib/libexpat.so.0<br />        libsocket.so.1 =&gt;        /usr/lib/libsocket.so.1<br />        libz.so =&gt;       /usr/local/lib/libz.so<br />        libc.so.1 =&gt;     /usr/lib/libc.so.1<br />        libgcc_s.so.1 =&gt;         /usr/local/lib/libgcc_s.so.1<br />        libaio.so.1 =&gt;   /lib/libaio.so.1<br />        libmd5.so.1 =&gt;   /lib/libmd5.so.1<br />        libdl.so.1 =&gt;    /lib/libdl.so.1<br />        libmp.so.2 =&gt; Assuming all goes swimmingly to this point, you'll need to modify the following configuration file, /usr/local/apache2/conf/httpd.conf, in order to use subversion with apache.&nbsp; You should append these to the LoadModule section of the file:</p><pre>LoadModule dav_svn_module     modules/mod_dav_svn.so<br />LoadModule authz_svn_module   modules/mod_authz_svn.so&nbsp;</pre><p>5.<br />&nbsp;</p><pre>&nbsp;</pre><p><br /></p><pre><br /></pre>

TITLE: Creating Custom JDK9 Runtime Images
DATE: 02/16/2017 14:42:14
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1

BODY: 
<p>For a brief overview of how to take advantage of a new feature in JDK9, namely the ability to create custom runtime images, please view the YouTube video that follows.&nbsp; As an addendum to this video, a recently published blog explains how the
 creation of runtime images can be automated inside a NetBeans project.&nbsp; 
Please Check out <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/automating_the_creation_of_jdk9">Automating the Creation of JDK9 Reduced Runtime Images</a> for further edification.<br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Automating The Creation of JDK9 Reduced Runtime Images in NetBeans
DATE: 02/13/2017 19:25:38
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>With the upcoming release of&nbsp;<a href="https://jdk9.java.net/">JDK9</a>,
 the Java SE platform will cease to exist in monolith fashion and will 
instead be built from the ground up as a series of modules. &nbsp;This sea 
change will allow developers to modularize their own applications and 
furthermore enable them to create runtime images with only those modules
 that are required to run their application.&nbsp; Much has been, and 
certainly will be, written about this massive evolution.&nbsp; Today we'll 
focus on the ability to create custom runtime images and how their 
creation might be automated by an Integrated Development Environment 
like <a href="https://netbeans.org/">NetBeans</a>.</p> 
  <p>First off you can <a href="https://jdk9.java.net/download/">download an&nbsp;early access build of JDK9</a> and a <a href="http://bits.netbeans.org/download/trunk/nightly/latest/">corresponding early access JDK9 NetBeans build</a> to get started.</p> 
  <p>For this recipe, we'll create a very simple JDK9 application called <em>SimpleApp</em> which just a logs a message using the <a href="https://docs.oracle.com/javase/8/docs/api/java/util/logging/package-summary.html">Java Logging API</a>. &nbsp;All Java 9 programs require the use of a module called <tt>java.base;</tt>&nbsp;the
 rationale behind choosing to invoke a logging method in this program is
 that it will require the module system to pull in an additional module 
called <tt>java.logging</tt>. &nbsp;Here's what the NetBeans project and source code look like:</p> 
  <p><img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/netbeans-jlink/SimpleAppJava.jpg" style="width: 640px; height: 345px;" /><br /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><br />
When we run this program in NetBeans the output window shows this:<br /> <br /> </p> 
  <p><img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/netbeans-jlink/SimpleAppOutput.jpg" height="162" width="445" /> </p> 
  <p><br />

    To modularize this program, the first thing we'll need to identify
    is its module dependencies.&nbsp; We can accomplish this task by
    taking advantage of a JDK9 utility called <tt>jdeps</tt> as
    follows:<br /> <br /> <img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/netbeans-jlink/SimpleAppJdeps.jpg" height="342" width="677" /><br /> </p> 
  <p>First we we first just invoke <tt>jdeps


      -version</tt> to confirm that indeed we're using a JDK9 version of the tool<font face="monospace">. </font><tt><font face="Times New Roman"> </font></tt>Next we invoke <tt>jdeps -s</tt> on the
    NetBeans generated <tt>SimpleApp.jar</tt> file to get its module
    dependencies.&nbsp; In this instance, our program requires two
    modules.&nbsp; As briefly mentioned earlier, all Java 9 applications
    by default require the <tt>java.base</tt> module.&nbsp;
    Additionally our simple program calls a method in the <tt>java.logging</tt>
    module and hence has a dependency on it too.</p> 
  <p>

    With this information, we can introduce a <tt>module-info.java</tt> file into
    our NetBeans project by right clicking on the <em>SimpleApp</em> project and
    selecting <em>New-&gt;Java Module Info...</em><br /> <br /> <img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/netbeans-jlink/SimpleAppNewModuleInfo.jpg" height="298" width="506" /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>Once created, the <tt>module-info.java</tt> file will appear in the
    <em>SimpleApp</em>'s default package.&nbsp; The <tt>module</tt>
    specification will initially be empty, add the two <tt>requires</tt>
    clauses and one <tt>exports</tt> clause as depicted below.</p> 
  <p> <img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/netbeans-jlink/SimpleAppModuleInfo1.jpg" /></p> 
  <p>With the <tt>module-info.java</tt> file properly situated and populated, the next time <em>SimpleApp</em> is built, NetBeans will add the compiled <tt>module-info.class</tt> file to contents of the <tt>SimpleApp.jar</tt> file, making it a modular jar.</p> 
  <p>It may still be a little early in the game, what appears to be missing in NetBeans at this point is the ability to construct runtime images using the JDK9 <tt>jlink</tt> utility. &nbsp;So let's do some customization to NetBeans to provide this capability. &nbsp;There are no doubt more elegant solutions, this one will enable you to output the the actual java commands that run, as they run, to aid in debugging.</p> 
  <p>The first step is to locate and edit <em>SimpleApp's</em> NetBeans <tt>project.properties</tt> file. &nbsp;You can find this file in NetBeans by clicking on the <em>Files</em> tab which gives you a filesystem view of your project. &nbsp;Underneath the <tt>nbproject/</tt> directory you'll find the <tt>project.properties</tt> file. &nbsp;Double clicking on&nbsp;<tt>project.properties</tt> will open that file for editing:</p> 
  <p><img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/netbeans-jlink/SimpleAppProjectProperties.jpg" /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>Add the following text to the end of the&nbsp;<tt>project.properties</tt> file:&nbsp;</p> 
  <blockquote style="margin: 0px 0px 0px 40px; border: none; padding: 0px;"><tt>#</tt><br /><tt># Added to support creation of modular jar and jlink image.</tt><br /><tt># Change this property to match your&nbsp;</tt><tt>JDK9 location</tt><br /><tt>#</tt><br /><tt>jdk9.basedir=C:\\Users\\jtconnor\\jdk-9</tt><br /><tt>#</tt><br /><tt>modular.jar.command=${jdk9.basedir}\\bin\\jar.exe</tt><br /><tt>modular.jar.file=${dist.dir}\\${application.title}.jar</tt><br /><tt>#</tt><br /><tt>modular.jar.arg1=--verbose</tt><br /><tt>modular.jar.arg2=--update</tt><br /><tt>modular.jar.arg3=--file</tt><br /><tt>modular.jar.arg4=${modular.jar.file}</tt><br /><tt>modular.jar.arg5=--main-class</tt><br /><tt>modular.jar.arg6=${main.class}</tt><br /><tt>modular.jar.arg7=--module-version</tt><br /><tt>modular.jar.arg8=1.0</tt><br /><tt>modular.jar.arg9=-C</tt><br /><tt>modular.jar.arg10=${build.dir}\\classes</tt><br /><tt>modular.jar.arg11=module-info.class</tt><br /><tt>modular.jar.args.concatenated=${modular.jar.arg1}
 ${modular.jar.arg2} ${modular.jar.arg3} ${modular.jar.arg4} 
${modular.jar.arg5} ${modular.jar.arg6} ${modular.jar.arg7} 
${modular.jar.arg8} ${modular.jar.arg9} ${modular.jar.arg10} 
${modular.jar.arg11}</tt><br /><tt>#</tt><br /><tt>jlink.command=${jdk9.basedir}\\bin\\jlink.exe</tt><br /><tt>jlink.module.dependency1=${modular.jar.file}</tt><br /><tt>jlink.module.dependency2=${jdk9.basedir}\\jmods</tt><br /><tt>jlink.module.path=${jlink.module.dependency1};${jlink.module.dependency2}</tt><br /><tt>jlink.image.dir=${dist.dir}\\jimage</tt><br /><tt>#</tt><br /><tt>jlink.arg1=--module-path</tt><br /><tt>jlink.arg2=${jlink.module.path}</tt><br /><tt>jlink.arg3=--add-modules</tt><br /><tt>jlink.arg4=${application.title}</tt><br /><tt>jlink.arg5=--output</tt><br /><tt>jlink.arg6=${jlink.image.dir}</tt><br /><tt>jlink.arg7=--compress=2</tt><br /><tt>jlink.args.concatenated=${jlink.arg1} ${jlink.arg2} ${jlink.arg3} ${jlink.arg4} ${jlink.arg5} ${jlink.arg6} ${jlink.arg7}</tt></blockquote> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><span style="font-family: &quot;&lt;span id=&quot;;"></span>It's not as bad as it looks. &nbsp;Basically most of the work here involves setting up the command line arguments for running the JDK9 <tt>jar</tt> and <tt>jlink</tt> utilities. &nbsp; And with this setup, you should be able to more easily debug the modular jar and runtime image creation should it go awry. &nbsp;The one important change that you will need to make is the setting of the <tt>jdk9.basedir</tt> property:</p> 
  <blockquote style="margin: 0px 0px 0px 40px;"> 
    <p><tt>#</tt><br /><tt># Added to support creation of modular jar and jlink image.</tt><br /><tt># Change this property to match your&nbsp;</tt><tt>JDK9 location</tt><br /><tt>#</tt><br /><tt>jdk9.basedir=C:\\Users\\jtconnor\\jdk-9 &nbsp;<span style="color: #ff0000;">&lt;-- Change This to point to your JDK9 location!</span></tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>With that step completed, the final modification goes to the <em>SimpleApp's</em> <tt>build.xml</tt> file. &nbsp;Just as before, this file can be found in the <em>Files</em> tab, under the <tt>SimpleApp/</tt> directory. &nbsp;Double click on <tt>build.xml</tt> to edit the file:</p> 
  <p><img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/netbeans-jlink/SimpleAppBuildXml.jpg" /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>Once the file is open, move to the end of the file. &nbsp;We are going to insert additional <em>ant</em> tasks right before <tt>&lt;/project&gt;</tt> delimiter as follows:</p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> <img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/netbeans-jlink/SimpleAppBuildXmlEdit.jpg" /></p> 
  <p>And here's the text that should be placed there:</p> 
  <pre>    &lt;target name="-post-jar" depends="-do-modular-jar,-do-jlink"&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;/target&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;target name="-do-modular-jar"&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;echo message="Updating ${dist.jar} to be a modular jar."/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;echo message="Executing: ${modular.jar.command} ${modular.jar.args.concatenated}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;exec executable="${modular.jar.command}"&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${modular.jar.arg1}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${modular.jar.arg2}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${modular.jar.arg3}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${modular.jar.arg4}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${modular.jar.arg5}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${modular.jar.arg6}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${modular.jar.arg7}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${modular.jar.arg8}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${modular.jar.arg9}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${modular.jar.arg10}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${modular.jar.arg11}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;/exec&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;/target&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;target name="-do-jlink"&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;echo message="Creating jlink image in ${jlink.image.dir}/."/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;echo message="Executing: ${jlink.command} ${jlink.args.concatenated}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;exec executable="${jlink.command}"&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${jlink.arg1}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${jlink.arg2}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${jlink.arg3}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${jlink.arg4}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${jlink.arg5}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${jlink.arg6}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;arg value="${jlink.arg7}"/&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;/exec&gt;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;/target&gt;
</pre> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>The <em>SimpleApp</em> project is now modified. &nbsp;Issuing a NetBeans &quot;Clean and Build&quot; on the <em>SimpleApp</em> project will now run the additional ant tasks that were incorporated into the project's <tt>build.xml</tt> file. &nbsp;Here's what the NetBeans output window should show:</p> 
  <p><img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/netbeans-jlink/SimpleAppCleanAndBuildOutput.jpg" /><br /></p> 
  <p>Finally, let's take a look at what was built, and how the custom runtime image can be run. &nbsp;The screenshot that follows examines the <em>SimpleApp's</em> <tt>dist/</tt> directory built by NetBeans. &nbsp;It shows that there are two entries: <tt>SimpleApp.jar</tt> (the modular jar file) and a <tt>jimage/</tt> directory (the custom runtime image). &nbsp;It goes on to run <tt>java -version</tt> from the <tt>jimage/</tt> directory to show that it's a JDK9 runtime, and then issues a <tt>java --list-modules</tt> to show what modules are part of this runtime. &nbsp;Notice only three appear versus what would normally be somewhere in the neighborhood of 90 for a full-fledged JDK9 runtime. &nbsp;And to top it off, the last command shows how the <em>SimpleApp</em> application is run from the jimage/ runtime.</p> 
  <p><img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/netbeans-jlink/SimpleAppJavaDist.jpg" /> </p> 
  <p>The example shown here is specific to the Windows platform. &nbsp;If you wish to duplicate this effort on a Linux or MAC desktop, you'll need to modify the <tt>project.properties</tt> file accordingly.</p> 
  <p>Here's hoping this sheds some light on some of the new Java 9 features.</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Adding a Timestamp to a Signed Java RIA
DATE: 03/08/2016 21:15:40
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>As the title suggests, the focus for this article revolves around adding timestamps to signed Java Rich Internet Applications.&nbsp; The related subtopics are worth mentioning up front in case the reader is interested in jumping right to one of those areas:</p> 
  <ol> 
    <li><a href="#signedRIAs">Example Signed (and Timestamped) RIAs</a><br /></li> 
    <li><a href="#WhatIsTimestamping">What is Timestamping and Why Should I Care?</a></li> 
    <li><a href="#HowToTimestamp">How Can Code Be Signed and Timestamped?</a></li> 
    <li><a href="#verify">How Can you Verify That a Jar File Has Been Signed?</a></li> 
    <li><a href="#netbeans">How Can you Integrate Signing and Timestamping into a NetBeans Project?<br /></a></li><a href="#netbeans"> 
      <p> </p> </a> 
  </ol><a href="#netbeans"> </a><a name="signedRIAs"></a> 
  <p><font size="2"><b>Example Signed (and Timestamped) RIAs</b></font></p> 
  <p>If your interest lies solely in getting access to a signed and timestamped Java web application, here are two that can be run by clicking on the images below.&nbsp; The <a href="http://jtconnors.com:8081/SocketServerFX/SocketServerFX.jnlp">SocketServerFX</a> and <a href="http://jtconnors.com:8081/SocketClientFX/SocketClientFX.jnlp">SocketClientFX</a> applications, when run simultaneously and connected, demonstrate how simple text can be sent and received over sockets. For those experimenting with <a href="https://docs.oracle.com/javase/8/docs/technotes/guides/deploy/deployment_rules.html">Deployment Rule Sets</a>, these two web applications could serve as test examples for use in managing RIA access.<br /> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <table width="626" cellspacing="0" cellpadding="12" border="0" height="211"> 
    <tbody> 
      <tr> 
        <td style="width: 50%;"> 
          <p><a href="http://jtconnors.com:8081/SocketServerFX/SocketServerFX.jnlp"><img width="286" height="203" src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/sign-timestamp/SocketServerFX.jpg" /><br /></a></p><a href="http://jtconnors.com:8081/SocketServerFX/SocketServerFX.jnlp"> </a> 
        </td> 
        <td style="width: 50%;"> 
          <p><a href="http://jtconnors.com:8081/SocketClientFX/SocketClientFX.jnlp"><img width="286" height="203" src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/sign-timestamp/SocketClientFX.jpg" /></a></p><a href="http://jtconnors.com:8081/SocketClientFX/SocketClientFX.jnlp"> </a> 
        </td> 
      </tr> 
    </tbody> 
  </table> <a name="WhatIsTimestamping"></a> <b><font size="2">What is Timestamping and Why Should I Care?</font><br /> </b> 
  <p> </p><b> </b> 
  <p> </p><b> </b> 
  <p>Applications signed with a trusted certificate come with an expiration date.&nbsp; At expiration, the code signer has to re-issue the software package with with an updated certificate in order to maintain a valid trusted signature.&nbsp; There are a whole host of reasons why re-signing may be impractical; the question becomes, is it possible to validate trusted signatures even after they have expired, thus prolonging their lifetime?&nbsp; The answer is yes by including a timestamp verified by a Timestamp Authority.&nbsp; With the timestamp, you're essentially proving that your code signing certificate was still valid at the time of signing.<br /></p> 
  <p><b> </b></p> <a name="HowToTimestamp"></a> 
  <p><font size="2"><b>How Can Code be Signed and Timestamped?</b></font></p> 
  <p>The <a href="http://docs.oracle.com/javase/8/docs/technotes/tools/windows/jarsigner.html">jarsigner</a> utility, found in the <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/javase/downloads/index.html">Java Development Kit</a>, is the mechanism used to for signing Java applications.&nbsp; A <tt>-tsa</tt> argument can be included on the command-line to specify a Timestamp Authority.&nbsp; A sample invocation from a Windows system might look something like this:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p>&gt; <tt><b>jarsigner -keystore code-sign.jks <span style="color: #0003ff;">-tsa http://timestamp.comodoca.com</span> \<br />
    SocketServerFX.jar &quot;jim connors's comodo ca limited id&quot;</b><br />Enter Passphrase for keystore:<br />jar signed.</tt></p><tt> </tt> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>As the code signing certificate referenced above comes from Comodo, one of many trusted certificate authorities, we use their Timestamp Authority to authorize the signature.</p> 
  <p><a name="verify"></a><font size="2"><b>How Can You Verify That a Jar File Has Been Signed?</b></font></p> 
  <p>Perhaps not the most elegant solution, you can utilize additional command-line arguments provided for by the <tt>jarsigner</tt> utility (<tt>-verify -verbose -certs</tt>) and search for a timestamp that is formatted in a specific way, as demonstrated by the following sample invocation:</p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>&gt; <b>jarsigner -verify -verbose -certs SocketServerFX.jar | findstr signed</b><br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; [entry was signed on 3/1/16 8:48 AM]<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; [entry was signed on 3/1/16 8:48 AM]<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; . . .<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; [entry was signed on 3/1/16 8:48 AM]</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>If you see text of the form <tt>&quot;[entry was signed on ...]&quot;</tt>, then you know the jar file has been signed and timestamped.&nbsp; If the jar is not timestamped, no such output will appear.<br /></p> 
  <p><a name="netbeans"></a><font size="2"><b>How Can you Integrate Signing and Timestamping into a NetBeans Project?</b></font></p> 
  <p>Within the NetBeans IDE, if you'd like to sign and timestamp your application automatically as part of your build process, you can do so by making a few modifications to your NetBeans project.</p> 
  <blockquote>1. Add the following properties onto your project's <tt>project.properties</tt> file:<br /><br /><tt># Properties for custom signjar<br /><b>jnlp.signjar.alias</b>=<i>&lt;your certificate alias&gt;</i><br /><b>jnlp.signjar.keystore</b>=<i>&lt;keystore file containing certificate private key&gt;</i></tt><tt></tt><br /><tt><b>jnlp.signjar.storepass</b>=&lt;keystore passphrase&gt;<br /><b>jnlp.signjar.keypass</b>=<i>&lt;private key passphrase&gt;</i><br /><b>jnlp.signing.tsaurl</b>=<i>&lt;URL for TimeStamp Authority&gt;</i></tt><br /><br />2. Add the following target to the project's <tt>build.xml</tt> file. <br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This should be placed at the bottom of the file but before the <tt>&lt;/projects&gt;</tt> directive.<br /><br /><tt>&lt;!-- Custom Code Timestamping using Ant's signjar instead of NetBeans --&gt;<br />&lt;target name=&quot;sign-jars&quot; depends=&quot;-check-signing-possible&quot;&gt;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;echo message=&quot;Using custom code for signing and timestamping via build.xml...&quot;/&gt;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;signjar<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; alias=&quot;${jnlp.signjar.alias}&quot;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; storepass=&quot;${jnlp.signjar.storepass}&quot;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; keystore=&quot;${jnlp.signjar.keystore}&quot;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; keypass=&quot;${jnlp.signjar.keypass}&quot;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; tsaurl=&quot;${jnlp.signing.tsaurl}&quot;&gt;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;path&gt;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;fileset dir=&quot;dist&quot; includes=&quot;*.jar&quot; /&gt;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;/path&gt;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;/signjar&gt;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;/target&gt;</tt><br /></blockquote>By running the <i>sign-jar</i>s ant target, your project's jar file will be signed and timestampped.<br /> 
  <blockquote><br /> </blockquote> 
  <blockquote> </blockquote> 
  <p> </p> 
  <ol> </ol>
-----

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: The $5 Raspberry Pi
DATE: 01/25/2016 13:28:21
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
Less than a year after the introduction of the <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/welcome_raspberry_pi_2">Raspberry Pi 2</a>, the Raspberry Pi foundation has once again outdone itself with yet another landmark product launch: the <i>Raspberry Pi Zero</i>.</p> 
  <p><img src="http://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/pizero/rpizero.jpg" /></p> 
  <p align="left"><font size="1">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Image taken from https://www.raspberrypi.org/blog/raspberry-pi-zero/</font></p> 
  <p align="left">The Raspberry Pi Zero is truly revolutionary.&nbsp; At 40% the size of the original Pi, it consumes less than half of the power, and can be configured <a href="http://www.midwesternmac.com/blogs/jeff-geerling/raspberry-pi-zero-conserve-energy">to draw as little as 80mA</a>. It has a faster processor than the original (Java performance graph below), and maintains binary compatibility with previous generations.&nbsp; And with a list price of $5US, the dream of offering serious computing power to nearly anyone on the planet comes ever closer to reality.&nbsp; Here are a some of the high level features:<br /></p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>1 GHz ARM11 Broadcom BCM2835 processor
 </li> 
    <li>512MB RAM</li> 
    <li>Micro-SD card slot</li> 
    <li>Multiple Linux distributions available plus support for Windows 10<br /></li> 
    <li>Mini-HDMI socket for 1080p video<br /></li> 
    <li>Micro-USB sockets for data and power</li> 
    <li>GPIO header (unpopulated).&nbsp; Pinout the same as Model A+/B+/2B </li> 
    <li>Composite video header (unpopulated)<br /></li> 
  </ul> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p align="left">At the time of this article's creation (Jan 2016), availability is limited, as the first shipment sold out quite rapidly (no surprise there).&nbsp; I was able to procure my Pi Zero on the black market.&nbsp; That is to say I bought it on eBay at a substantial uplift.</p> 
  <p align="left">In terms of Java performance, the chart that follows compares SPECjvm2008 benchmark results for a Raspberry Pi and a Raspberry Pi Zero utilizing the latest available Oracle JDK (1.8.0_71).&nbsp; The larger the number, the better, the Pi Zero outperforms the original by 45%.</p> 
  <p align="left"><img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/pizero/pivszero.jpg" /></p> 
  <p align="left">Another feel-good aspect to this story is that the Pi Zero will be manufactured in Wales.&nbsp; Here's hoping that in the near future supplies will meet the pent up demand.&nbsp; <br /> </p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Installing Trusted Certificates into a Java Keystore
DATE: 11/16/2015 20:49:28
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>As software environments continue to ratchet up security measures, the odds of having to deal with digital certificates in more than a superficial manner only increases over time.&nbsp; Furthermore, platforms are not only mandating the use of certificates, they are to a greater extent shunning the self-signed variety and instead insisting upon certs that originate from a trusted authority.&nbsp; When managing certificates in the Java world, the utility you're most likely to encounter is <a href="https://docs.oracle.com/javase/8/docs/technotes/tools/unix/keytool.html"><tt>keytool</tt>,</a> an integral part of the Java Development Kit.&nbsp; Although <tt>keytool</tt> can be effectively used to generate self-signed private/public key pairs, it's a little lacking when it comes to incorporating in certs generated by trusted authorities.&nbsp; <i>[Based upon feedback and recommendations received after the original posting of this article, a </i><b>Postscript</b><i> section has been appended which discusses alternative solutions to the original discussed below]</i><br /></p> 
  <p>In particular, we'll focus on overcoming the following two shortcomings:<br /></p> 
  <ol> 
    <li>An existing private key and certificate generated by a trusted Certificate Authority (CA) cannot be imported by <tt>keytool</tt>, at least not in the format traditionally provided by CAs.<br /></li> 
    <li>Not only must the unique private key be imported into the keystore, in some instances the root CA certificate and any intermediate certificates (referred to as a certificate chain) must be included, and more importantly <b><i>in the correct order</i></b>.&nbsp; The <tt>keytool</tt> utility doesn't help much in the way of ensuring a valid order.</li> 
  </ol> 
  <p>The following example uses a real SSL certificate chain from a real certificate authority and was done in the context of the <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/javaseproducts/advanced-mgmt/advancedmanagementconsole-2254207.html">Java Advanced Management Console</a> 2.1 application.&nbsp; Java AMC is a Java EE application and requires <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/middleware/weblogic/overview/index-085209.html">Oracle's WebLogic</a> application server to function.&nbsp; In this instance we'll be updating a keystore associated with WebLogic, but in reality this Java keystore should be no different from any other Java keystore, so these steps should apply elsewhere just fine.</p> 
  <p>We'll leave the minutiae of applying for a trusted certificate as an exercise for the reader; one of the first steps towards getting a certificate involves creating and submitting a CSR (Certificate Signing Request) to a Certificate Authority.&nbsp; A byproduct of this process is that a private key file is generated based on the information in the CSR.&nbsp; This file, when opened, looks something like this:</p> 
  <blockquote><tt>-----BEGIN PRIVATE KEY-----<br />encryptedgobbledygook line 1<br />...<br /> encryptedgobbledygook line n<br />-----END PRIVATE KEY-----</tt><br /></blockquote> 
  <p>This private key file is in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X.509#Certificate_filename_extensions">PEM</a> (Privacy Enhanced Mail) format, and will take a <tt>.pem</tt> suffix.&nbsp; For our example, the file is stored as:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li><b><tt>private-key.pem</tt></b><br /> </li> 
  </ul> 
  <p>For this article, we used <a href="https://www.comodo.com/">Comodo</a> (one of many alternatives) as our Certificate Authority.&nbsp; When the entire process with Comodo was complete, the following documents were ultimately received from them:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li><b><tt>amc-server_jtconnors_com.crt</tt></b>  - This is the Comodo-generated host-specific SSL certificate for a machine with a Fully Qualified Domain Name (FQDN) of <tt>amc-server.jtconnors.com</tt><br /></li> 
    <li><b><tt>AddTrustExternalCARoot.crt</tt></b> - Comodo Root Certificate<br /></li> 
    <li><b><tt>COMODORSAAddTrustCA.crt</tt></b> - Comodo Intermediate Certificate 1<br /></li> 
    <li><b><tt>COMODORSADomainValidationSecureServerCA.crt</tt></b> - Comodo Intermediate Certificate 2</li> 
  </ul> 
  <p> 
  With these files in place we can now begin the importing process.&nbsp; We will take advantage of enhancements added to <tt>keytool</tt> with the Java 6 release: namely that <tt>keytool</tt> can merge and import keystores that are in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PKCS_12">PKCS12</a> format.<br /></p> 
  <p>With this new information what remains is to figure out how to convert our private key and certificate chain into a PKCS12 file.&nbsp; For this functionality we resort to the capabilities found in the ubiquitous <a href="http://www.openssl.org/">OpenSSL</a> toolkit, available on virtually all popular compute platforms.&nbsp; In the example that follows, we'll be running on a Windows system and will utilize the <a href="https://www.cygwin.com/">Cygwin</a> environment to run the required OpenSSL commands.<br /><tt><tt> </tt></tt></p><tt><tt> </tt></tt> 
  <blockquote> </blockquote> 
  <p>Step 1. (From Cygwin) Concatenate the certificates comprising the CA-supplied root certificate chain to one file.&nbsp; Include only the root certificate and intermediate certificate(s) and exclude the host-specific SSL certificate.</p> 
  <blockquote> </blockquote> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><b><tt>$ cat AddTrustExternalCARoot.crt COMODORSAAddTrustCA.crt COMODORSADomainValidationSecureServerCA.crt &gt; BUNDLE.crt</tt></b></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <blockquote> </blockquote> 
  <p>Step 2. (From Cygwin) Create a PKCS12 keystore.&nbsp; This command incorporates both the certificate chain along with the SSL private key and certificates.&nbsp; Your passwords may vary.<br /></p> 
  <blockquote> </blockquote> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><b><tt>$ openssl pkcs12 -export -chain -in amc-server_jtconnors_com.crt -inkey private-key.pem -out keystore.p12 -name amc-server -CAfile BUNDLE.crt<br />Enter Export Password: </tt></b><tt>changeit</tt><b><tt><br />Verifying - Enter Export Password: </tt></b><tt>changeit</tt><br /></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Step 3. (From Windows CMD) Using <tt>keytool</tt>, import the PKCS12 keystore into the resulting JKS keystore called <tt>keystore.jks</tt>. Again, you may select different passwords.<br /></p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><b><tt>&gt; &quot;c:\Program Files\Java\jdk1.8.0_66\bin\keytool.exe&quot; -importkeystore -destkeystore keystore.jks -srckeystore keystore.p12 -alias amc-server<br />Enter destination keystore password: </tt></b><tt>changeit</tt><b><tt><br />Re-enter new password: </tt></b><tt>changeit</tt><b><tt><br />Enter source keystore password: </tt></b><tt>changeit</tt><br /></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Step 4. With the keystore successfully created we can now take the optional step of further verifying it.&nbsp; Certain Java-based application frameworks, like Oracle's WebLogic for example, can be finicky about the completeness and order of the certificate chain.&nbsp; To help validate the keystore, we can use the <tt>ValidateCertChain</tt> program which comes bundled with WebLogic 12c and can be found in the distribution's <tt>weblogic.jar</tt> file.&nbsp; Here's a sample invocation of the program on our recently created <tt>keystore.jks</tt> file:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><b><tt>&gt; java -cp %MW_HOME%\wlserver\server\lib\weblogic.jar utils.ValidateCertChain -jks amc-server keystore.jks<br />Cert[0]: CN=amc-server.jtconnors.com,OU=PositiveSSL,OU=Domain Control Validated<br />Cert[1]: CN=COMODO RSA Domain Validation Secure Server CA,O=COMODO CA Limited,L=Salford,ST=Greater Manchester,C=GB<br />Cert[2]: CN=COMODO RSA Certification Authority,O=COMODO CA Limited,L=Salford,ST=Greater Manchester,C=GB<br />Cert[3]: CN=AddTrust External CA Root,OU=AddTrust External TTP Network,O=AddTrust AB,C=SE<br />Certificate chain appears valid</tt></b></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>Here's hoping these examples save you some time, and more importantly, a little grief.<br /></p> 
  <p><b>Postscript</b></p> 
  <p>After having received recommendations from the engineers far better versed in AMC and WebLogic, the following section was added to briefly discuss alternatives to the above mentioned OpenSSL solution.</p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><b>Alternative A</b>:&nbsp; For people that have complete control of the certificate generating process, they can, as previously hinted, use <tt>keytool</tt> exclusively for this process. For the first phase of the process they can use <tt>keytool</tt> in the following general manner (arguments to <tt>keytool</tt> will vary) to generate a keypair and certificate request:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><b><tt>keytool -keystore keystore.jks -genkeypair -keyalg rsa
<br />keytool -keystore keystore.jks -certreq</tt></b> </p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>In this scenario, OpenSSL would not be required since the keypair is already stored in the keystore.&nbsp; From here you can import the certificates following a form similar to this:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><b><tt>keytool -import -keystore keystore.jks -alias root -file AddTrustExternalCARoot.crt
<br />
keytool -import -keystore keystore.jks -alias intermediate1 -file COMODORSAAddTrustCA.crt
<br />
keytool -import -keystore keystore.jks -alias intermediate2 -file COMODORSADomainValidationSecureServerCA.crt
<br />
keytool -import -keystore keystore.jks -alias mykey -file amc-server_jtconnors_com.crt</tt></b></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>In the last command, &quot;<tt>-alias mykey</tt>&quot; is essential and must match the key pair in the keystone.&nbsp; As a shortcut, you could also concatenate all PEM-encoded certificates into a big file and then call:
</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><b><tt>keytool -import -keystore keystore.jks -alias mykey -file thebigfile</tt></b> </p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p><b>Alternative B</b>: Along with the <tt>ValidateChain</tt> program WebLogic 12c's <tt>weblogic.jar</tt> file also includes a utility called <tt>ImportPrivateKey</tt> which can also used to import a certificate chain into a Java keystore.&nbsp; This utility
      command is
      described in <a href="http://docs.oracle.com/middleware/1213/wls/SECMG/identity_trust.htm#SECMG791">Creating
        a Keystore Using ImportPrivateKey</a> subsection of WebLogic
      documention on <a href="http://docs.oracle.com/middleware/1213/wls/SECMG/identity_trust.htm">Configuring
        Keystores</a>.&nbsp; The command follows a form like this:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <pre style="font-family: Consolas,monospace; font-size: inherit; white-space: pre-wrap;"><b><tt>java -cp %WL_HOME%\server\lib\weblogic.jar utils.ImportPrivateKey -keystore <i>newkeystore</i> -storepass <i>**keystorepassword** </i> -alias <i>amctrust</i> -certfile <i>certificate.pem </i> -keyfile <i>privatekey.pem</i> [-keyfilepass <i>**privatekeypassword**</i>]</tt></b></pre> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>For further edification please consult the WebLogic docs.<br /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><br /></p>

<p>You may need to add &quot; -srcstoretype PKCS12&quot; to the keytool command for importing the keystore : </p>


<p>&gt; &quot;c:\Program Files\Java\jdk1.8.0_66\bin\keytool.exe&quot; -importkeystore -destkeystore keystore.jks -srckeystore keystore.p12 -srcstoretype PKCS12 -alias amc-server</p>


<p>I needed it to resolve an &quot;Invalid keystore format&quot; issue.<br/>
Other than that, the procedure (the original one, I didn't try the alternatives) worked brilliantly for me.</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Using Java Flight Recorder with Compact Profiles
DATE: 09/08/2015 17:07:19
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
Like it's big brother <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/javase/downloads/index.html">Java SE</a>, the <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/embedded-se/downloads/index.html">Java SE-Embedded 8</a> platform provides support for <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/javaseproducts/mission-control/java-mission-control-1998576.html">Java Flight Recorder</a>, an invaluable profiling and event collection framework built into the Java Runtime Environment.&nbsp; However, Flight Recorder is only available with the Java SE Embedded 8 <i>Full JRE</i>, not with the smaller <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/resources/tech/compact-profiles-overview-2157132.html">Compact Profiles</a>.&nbsp; So the question becomes, is there anything that can be done to use Java Flight Recorder with Compact Profiles?</p> 
  <p>At the current time, the smaller Compact1 and Compact2 profiles cannot realistically support Java Flight Recorder without substantial changes.&nbsp; Hence we'll avoid discussing their prospects for inclusion here.&nbsp; What we will focus on is Compact3 as <a href="http://openjdk.java.net/jeps/161">its specification</a> includes among others, the <tt>javax.management</tt> APIs, making for a more reasonable match.&nbsp; As it turns out, all that is required to enable Flight Recorder use with the Compact3 profile is to copy over a few files over from the Full JRE.&nbsp; The instructions that follow should aid in creating a Flight Recorder enabled instance:<br /> </p> 
  <p>1. Download a Java SE-Embedded EJRE for your platform.&nbsp; You can do so <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/embedded-se/downloads/index.html">here</a>.&nbsp; For this example, we'll do this on a Linux host and choose the ARMv6/v7 Hard Float option (suitable for the venerable Raspberry Pi).</p> 
  <p>2. Extract the EJRE.</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>$ tar xvf ejdk-8u51-linux-armv6-vfp-hflt.tar.gz</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>3. Use the EJRE's <tt>jrecreate.sh</tt> script to create a full JRE:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>$ ./ejdk1.8.0_51/bin/jrecreate.sh --dest full_jre -g -k<br />Building JRE using Options {<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ejdk-home: /home/pi/ejdk1.8.0_51<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; dest: /home/pi/full_jre<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; target: linux_armv6_vfp_hflt<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; vm: all<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; runtime: jre<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; debug: true<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; keep-debug-info: true<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; no-compression: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; dry-run: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; verbose: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; extension: []<br />}<br /><br /><br />Target JRE Size is 59,389 KB (on disk usage may be greater).<br />Embedded JRE created successfully</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>4. Create a Compact3 JRE:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>$ ./ejdk1.8.0_51/bin/jrecreate.sh --profile compact3 --dest compact3 -g -k<br />Building JRE using Options {<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ejdk-home: /home/pi/ejdk1.8.0_51<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; dest: /home/pi/compact3<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; target: linux_armv6_vfp_hflt<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; vm: client<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; runtime: compact3 profile<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; debug: true<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; keep-debug-info: true<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; no-compression: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; dry-run: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; verbose: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; extension: []<br />}<br /><br /><br />Target JRE Size is 24,336 KB (on disk usage may be greater).<br />Embedded JRE created successfully</tt> <br /></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>5.&nbsp; Check the size of the compact3 JRE.&nbsp; We'll use this to see how much space was needed to add support for Flight Recorder.<br /></p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>$ du -sk compact3<br />24480&nbsp;&nbsp; compact3</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>6.&nbsp; Create the <tt>compact3/lib/jfr/</tt> directory</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>$ mkdir compact3/lib/jfr</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>7.&nbsp; Copy the following files over from the full JRE to the <tt>compact3</tt> instance with these commands:</p> 
  <blockquote> <tt>$ cd full_jre/<br />
    $ cp ./lib/jfr.jar ../compact3/lib<br />
    $ cp ./lib/arm/libjfr.so ../compact3/lib/arm<br />
    $ cp ./lib/arm/libbci.so ../compact3/lib/arm<br /> $ cp ./lib/jfr/default.jfc ../compact3/lib/jfr<br />
    $ cp ./lib/jfr/profile.jfc ../compact3/lib/jfr</tt> </blockquote> 
  <p>8. That's all that is required.&nbsp; You can compare the disk usage of the compact3/ directory before and after these modifications to get an idea of the additional space required to utilize Java Flight Recorder. </p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>$ du -sk ../compact3<br />24824&nbsp;&nbsp; ../compact3</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>So comparing the disk usage of step (5), we see that less than 400KB is added in order to enable usage of Java Flight Recorder. To better understand how you might remotely connect to a Flight Recorder enabled instance in a secure fashion, check out <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/managing_java_flight_recorder_enabled">this article</a>.<br /></p>
-----

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: New for Java 9: jshell
DATE: 07/24/2015 21:12:16
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>A sampling of indices gauging computer programming language popularity (e.g. <a href="http://pypl.github.io/PYPL.html">PYPL</a> and <a href="http://www.tiobe.com/index.php/content/paperinfo/tpci/index.html">TIBOE</a>) shows that Java, after 20 years, still enjoys a huge following.&nbsp; In general, depending upon whom and/or when you ask, Java usually comes in first or second place in these surveys.&nbsp; Although it might be hard to imagine recent computer science graduates without Java exposure, one trend is evident: top universities are gravitating towards teaching &quot;simpler&quot; languages in lieu of Java for their introductory programming classes.</p> 
  <p><img width="472" height="302" src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/jshell/pypl.jpg" /> </p> 
  <p>The standard Java platform, compared to its counterparts like Python, currently lacks a <i>Read-Eval-Print-Loop</i> otherwise known as a <i>REPL</i>.&nbsp; Instead of having to construct and compile complete syntactically correct programs before feedback can be achieved, REPLs allow much more interactivity, enabling the student/programmer to enter small snippets of code and receive immediate feedback.&nbsp; According to the <a href="http://openjdk.java.net/jeps/222">Java Enhancement Proposal</a> which outlines the new jshell functionality, &quot;The number one reason schools cite for moving away from Java as a
teaching language is that other languages have a &quot;REPL&quot; and have far lower
bars to an initial <code class="prettyprint">&quot;Hello, world!&quot;</code> program.&quot;&nbsp; With the introduction of jshell in the upcoming Java 9 release, this shortcoming will be eliminated.</p> 
  <p>Falling under the auspices of an OpenJDK project called <a href="http://openjdk.java.net/projects/kulla/">Kulla</a>, the code representing the REPL capability has yet to be incorporated into the core <a href="https://jdk9.java.net/download/">JDK 9 early access release</a>.&nbsp; As of the writing of this article (late July 2015), a separate build is required to get the jshell functionality.&nbsp; In the (hopefully not-too-distant) future an early access release will bundle all these features together obviating the need for extra work.&nbsp; In the interim, here's a brief video demonstrating some of it's features.<br /> </p> 
  <iframe width="420" height="315" frameborder="0" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/caBTae7UyVw"></iframe> 
  <p>As we near the &quot;feature complete&quot; phase for JDK 9, we look forward to better integration and incorporation of yet more new important features into this upcoming release.</p>
-----

<p>Here is a web frontend in case someone likes to quickly get a feel of what JShell could do <a href="http://137.74.64.141/jshell-frontend/" rel="nofollow">http://137.74.64.141/jshell-frontend/</a></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Update to JavaFX, Sockets and Threading: Lessons Learned
DATE: 04/09/2015 20:13:50
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
Recently, a reader commented on a dated article of mine, circa 2010, entitled <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/javafx_sockets_and_threading_lessons">JavaFX, Sockets and Threading: Lessons Learned</a>.&nbsp; In it he correctly stated that, as the original content is based on the deprecated JavaFX 1.3 framework (aka JavaFX Script), he cannot test or utilize that code with the modern JavaFX API.&nbsp; What follows is an update to that blog entry with code and references appropriate for the <a href="http://docs.oracle.com/javase/8/javafx/api/toc.htm">JavaFX 8 (Java SE 8 with JavaFX) platform</a>.</p> 
  <p><b>Overview</b></p> 
  <p>For a more thorough understanding of the method behind this madness, please consult the <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/javafx_sockets_and_threading_lessons">original article</a>.&nbsp; Briefly stated, socket programming, especially in Java, often times lends itself to utilizing threads.&nbsp; To facilitate using sockets, both from the &quot;client&quot; and &quot;server&quot; side, an abstract class called <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/socket_FX8/GenericSocket.java">GenericSocket.java</a> was and is provided and, baring a few minor changes, remains quite similar to the original version. Just as in the Original JavaFX Script framework, <i><b>The JavaFX UI is still not thread-safe and its scene graph must only be accessed through the JavaFX application thread</b></i>.&nbsp;&nbsp; What has changed between old and new is the class and method name required to perform work on the JavaFX application thread.</p> 
  <p>Just like before, we've identified two methods associated with socket manipulations that need to perform work on the main thread.&nbsp; These abstract method calls are incorporated into the GenericSocket.java source and are specified in the <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/socket_FX8/SocketListener.java">SocketListener.java</a> interface file:</p> 
  <pre>    public interface SocketListener {
        public void onMessage(String line);
        public void onClosedStatus(boolean isClosed);
    }</pre> 
  <p>Within <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/socket_FX8/GenericSocket.java">GenericSocket.java</a>, you'll see references to these method calls as follows:</p> 
  <pre>    /*
     * The onClosedStatus() method has to be implemented by
     * a sublclass.  If used in conjunction with JavaFX,
     * use Platform.runLater() to force this method to run
     * on the main thread.
     */
     onClosedStatus(true);</pre> 
  <p>&nbsp;and</p> 
  <pre>    /*
     * The onMessage() method has to be implemented by
     * a sublclass.  If used in conjunction with JavaFX,
     * use Platform.runLater() to force this method to run
     * on the main thread.
     */
     onMessage(line);</pre> 
  <p>As implied by these comments, JavaFX-specifc classes that extend the <tt>GenericSocket</tt> class and implement the <tt>SocketListener</tt> interface are required.&nbsp; Correspondingly two helper classes have been created: <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/socket_FX8/FxSocketClient.java">FxSocketClient.java</a> and <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/socket_FX8/FxSocketServer.java">FxSocketServer.java</a>.&nbsp; These extend the <tt>GenericSocket</tt> class and implement the <tt>SocketListener</tt> interface from the perspective of a JavaFX environment.&nbsp; Both classes override the <tt>onMessage()</tt> method like this:</p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> <img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/socket_FX8/onMessage.jpg" /><br /></p> 
  <p>Likewise, the <tt>onClosedStatus()</tt> method is implemented as follows:</p> 
  <p><img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/socket_FX8/onClosedStatus.png" /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>As these two code snippets exhibit, we enclose our execution requirements within a call to the <a href="https://docs.oracle.com/javafx/2/api/javafx/application/Platform.html#runLater%28java.lang.Runnable%29"><tt>Platform.runLater()</tt></a> method, ensuring that&nbsp; it will be executed on the JavaFX application thread.&nbsp; (For the Java 8 aficionados, you may notice that these two methods could be converted to lambda expressions. That exercise is left to the reader.)&nbsp; What remains now is for any referencing JavaFX class to implement the <tt>SocketListener</tt> interface.<br /></p> 
  <p>To demonstrate the usage of <tt>FxSocketClient</tt> and <tt>FxSocketServer</tt> classes within JavaFX 8, this article provides two NetBeans projects represented by the screenshot that follows.</p> 
  <p><img width="623" height="218" src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/socket_FX8/SocketClient-ServerFX.png" /></p> 
  <p>The user interface for these programs was created with the assistance of a terrific tool called the <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/javase/downloads/javafxscenebuilder-info-2157684.html">JavaFX Scene Builder</a>.&nbsp; You can download the source code, including the UI (represented in FXML), here:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/socket_FX8/SocketClientFX.zip">SocketClientFX.zip</a></li> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/socket_FX8/SocketServerFX.zip">SocketServerFX.zip</a></li> 
  </ul> 
  <p><b>The <tt>javafx.concurrent</tt> Package</b></p> 
  <p>The intent of this article was to focus solely on the <tt>Platform.runLater()</tt> method, however, it is important to note that JavaFX 2.x also provides an additional means to create background tasks that safely interact with the JavaFX application thread.&nbsp; Similar in capability to the venerable <a href="https://docs.oracle.com/javase/8/docs/api/java/util/concurrent/package-summary.html"><tt>java.util.concurrent</tt></a>&nbsp; package (and in fact extending some of those classes), the <a href="https://docs.oracle.com/javafx/2/api/javafx/concurrent/package-summary.html"><tt>javafx.concurrent</tt></a> package also furnishes the ability to safely control the execution
and track the progress of the application code running in background tasks. For an overview of their use, check out the following article published as part of the Oracle documentation: <a href="http://docs.oracle.com/javafx/2/threads/jfxpub-threads.htm">Concurrency in Java FX</a>.<br /></p> 
  <ul> </ul> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p>

<p>Jim,<br/>
This is a great tutorial.  However, I am having problems with re-opening the socket server when it is shut down in auto connect mode.  I think that the thread is not getting stopped properly.</p>


<p>Here is what I do to reproduce the problem.<br/>
Bring up the SocketClientFX application - Okay<br/>
Bring up the SocketServerFX appliation - Okay<br/>
On the SocketServer application, click on the button to Auto Connect - Okay<br/>
On the SocketClient Application, click on the Connect button; Connection made - Okay<br/>
On the SocketClient Application, write a message, and send it; Message sent - Okay<br/>
On the SocketClient Application, click on Disconnect - Okay<br/>
On the SocketServer application, exit the application - Okay<br/>
Bring the SocketServer Application back up - Okay<br/>
Click on the Connect or Auto-Connect button - Not Okay</p>


<p>I think that the socket thread is still running from the previous running of the SocketServer application, and it won't connect.</p>


<p>Do you know how to properly shut down the thread so that if it is in auto-connect, it won't leave the thread going?</p>


<p>Thanks,<br/>
Wayne</p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jim Connors
EMAIL: james.connors@oracle.com
URL: null
DATE: 08/11/2015 21:28:33

<p>Hi Wayne,</p>


<p>Thanks so much for your comment, you are correct.  There is a shutdown() method that can be called to close the SocketServer's socket but it looks like the application never gets signaled to do so because the SocketServer is in a separate thread.</p>


<p>1. A quick and dirty solution would be to detect when the JavaFX application is terminated (when the user clicks on the red &quot;x&quot;) and simply exit.  To add slightly more elegance, a shutdownHook can be added whose method gets called at program exit.  From within that method, we could call the SocketServer's shutdown() method if the socket is open.</p>


<p>2. Another solution would be to include an &quot;exit&quot; button that would shut the program down more gracefully.</p>


<p>I'll work on the quick and dirty fix</p>


<p>-- Jim C</p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jim Connors
EMAIL: james.connors@oracle.com
URL: null
DATE: 04/14/2017 03:40:13

<p>You could use the binary-to-text encoding technique.  When sending binary data, you encode (convert) it into printable characters, and on the remote side you decode it to its original form.  One of the popular encoding standards in is Base64.  The Java 8 encode/decode API is here:</p>


<p><a href="https://docs.oracle.com/javase/8/docs/api/java/util/Base64.Encoder.html" rel="nofollow">https://docs.oracle.com/javase/8/docs/api/java/util/Base64.Encoder.html</a><br/>
<a href="https://docs.oracle.com/javase/8/docs/api/java/util/Base64.Decoder.html" rel="nofollow">https://docs.oracle.com/javase/8/docs/api/java/util/Base64.Decoder.html</a></p>
