AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Welcome Raspberry Pi 2!
DATE: 02/17/2015 16:38:20
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
Having <a href="http://www.zdnet.com/article/raspberry-pi-sales-edge-towards-four-million/">surpassed 4 million units shipped</a> and clearly establishing itself as a de facto reference platform, the folks at the Raspberry Pi Foundation are not resting on their laurels.&nbsp; Earlier this month (February 2015), they introduced the <em><strong><a href="http://www.raspberrypi.org/products/raspberry-pi-2-model-b/">Raspberry Pi 2 Model B</a></strong></em>:<br /></p><img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/pi2/pi2.jpg" /> 
  <p>Compared to its predecessor, the <a href="http://www.raspberrypi.org/products/model-b-plus/">Raspberry Pi B+</a>, this newer model is packaged with a Broadcom BCM2836 SoC at 900MHz.&nbsp; It is not only superior in performance to the original single core BCM2835 processor on core-by-core basis, it now brings the added benefit of including 4 cores.&nbsp; Add to that a doubling of RAM from 512MB to 1GB and it should be no mystery that this new platform can handle more serious workloads.&nbsp; <a href="#perf">Later on down, a chart compares the SPECjvm2008 benchmark performance</a> of the original Raspberry Pi Model B with the new Raspberry Pi 2 Model B.</p> 
  <p>In addition to the substantial performance bump, major kudos are to be given to the Raspberry Pi Foundation engineers for their focus on hardware and software compatibility:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>The Pi2 maintains the same physical form factor as the Raspberry Pi B+, meaning that all of the previous accessories (USB power cable, serial console cable, microSD card ...) all work perfectly well with the new version.&nbsp; That also includes any Raspberry Pi B+ enclosures you may have too.</li> 
    <li>The ARMv7-based BCM2836 processor is backwards-compatible with the original ARMv6 version.&nbsp; In order to maintain binary compatibility, the latest version of the <a href="http://www.raspbian.org/">Raspbian distribution</a> will boot either the new or old versions of the Raspberry Pi.&nbsp; Note that in this mode, applications will not be able to take full advantage of the ARMv7 enhancements.&nbsp; Alternate OS distributions may provide the extra performance boost at the expense of breaking backwards compatibility.</li> 
    <li>Perhaps best of all, the Raspberry Pi maintains its appeal to the masses.&nbsp; At approximately £23/$35, no price increase comes along with this upgrade.</li> 
  </ul> 
  <p><a name="perf"></a><strong>A Brief Look at  Performance</strong></p> 
  <p>The chart that follows examines Raspberry Pi Java performance via the <a href="https://www.spec.org/jvm2008/">SPECjvm2008</a> benchmark.&nbsp; Both original and Raspberry Pi 2 systems ran the latest available Raspbian Image (2015-02-02) and the latest available Java 7 JDK (Java 7 update 72)<sup>1</sup>.&nbsp; The four vertical bars (blue, red, green, purple) represent four separate runs of the benchmark:</p> 
  <ol> 
    <li><strong><span style="color: #4f81bd;">Blue</span></strong>: <em>Raspberry Pi Model B</em>.&nbsp; Invoked with JVM argument <tt>-Xmx400m</tt> specifying a maximum heap of 400MB.</li> 
    <li><strong><span style="color: #c0504d;">Red</span></strong>: <em>Raspberry Pi 2, single threaded</em>.&nbsp; Invoked with JVM argument <tt>-Xmx400m </tt>specifying a maximum heap of 400MB, and application argument <tt>-bt 1</tt> instructing the SPECjvm2008 test harness to run the benchmark tests with a single thread.&nbsp; This is meant to approximate an apples-to-apples comparison of single core performance between the two Raspberry Pi systems.<br /></li> 
    <li><strong><span style="color: #9bbb59;">Green</span></strong>: <em>Raspberry Pi 2, multithreaded (default)</em>.&nbsp; Invoked with JVM argument <tt>-Xmx600m</tt> specifying a maximum heap of 600MB<sup>2</sup>.</li> 
    <li><strong><span style="color: #8064a2;">Purple</span></strong>: <em>Raspberry Pi 2, multithreaded (default), larger heap</em>.&nbsp;  Invoked with JVM argument <tt>-Xmx900m</tt> specifying a maximum heap of 900MB<sup>3</sup>.<br /></li> 
  </ol> 
  <ul> </ul> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/pi2/SPECjvm2008-composite.jpg" /><br /></p> 
  <p>The larger the measurement (ops/min), the faster the result, the single core performance of the Raspberry Pi 2 is a big improvement over its predecessor.&nbsp; As the SPECjvm2008 suite does include a healthy dose of mutithreaded code, enabling the test harness to take advantage of the 4 hardware threads available to the Pi 2 added another huge jump in performance.&nbsp; And finally utilizing the extra RAM (in the form of a larger heap) provided for by the Raspberry Pi 2 yields only a very modest gain in throughput.&nbsp; In particular, 2 of the 11 component tests (compress, xml) show an improvement with the extra heap.&nbsp; For more detailed results, you can view this <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/pi2/SPECjvm2008-results.xlsx">spreadsheet</a>.</p> 
  <p>The added horsepower that the Raspberry Pi 2 brings solidifies the Raspberry Pi family as an important reference platform in the present as well as the future for companies like Oracle.&nbsp; As the Internet of Things phenomena plays out and more processing is brought to the edge, the new Raspberry Pi is better suited to handle the increased workload that applications like <a href="http://www.oracle.com/us/technologies/java/embedded/event-processing/overview/index.html">Oracle Event Processing for Oracle Java Embedded</a> and others require. <br /></p> 
  <p><strong>Notes</strong></p> 
  <p><sup>1</sup> The latest Raspbian distro includes a recent version of Oracle's Java 8 JDK.&nbsp; Unfortunately 
the somewhat dated SPECjvm2008 benchmark has a dependency that requires 
Java 1.7 or earlier, so for these benchmarks, we needed to use a Java 7 
JDK.&nbsp; The latest available, 7u72, was used for these tests.<br /></p> 
  <p><sup>2</sup> With 4 cores, the SPECjvm2008 test suite would produce an <tt>OutOfMemoryError</tt> when run with the initial max heap size argument of <tt>-Xmx400m</tt>.&nbsp; Increasing the max heap size to 600MB (<tt>-Xmx600m</tt>) enabled the tests to complete.&nbsp; Evidently SPECjvm2008 requires more heap space as more hardware threads are configured in.<br /></p> 
  <p><sup>3</sup> In order to utilize the full amount of RAM (1GB) available with the Raspberry Pi 2, a firmware upgrade may be required.&nbsp; If necessary, you can invoke '<tt>sudo rpi-update</tt>'&nbsp; from a linux shell on the Raspberry Pi 2 to accomplish this task.<br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Managing Java Flight Recorder Enabled JVMs with SSL
DATE: 01/14/2015 18:22:58
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p> One advantage <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/javaseproducts/mission-control/java-mission-control-1998576.html">Java Flight Recorder</a> enjoys is that its event collection and profiling framework is built right into the JDK, allowing instrumented JVM instances to operate with near-zero overhead.&nbsp; At arguably negligible expense, would it then make sense to consider enabling Java Flight Recorder on production applications?&nbsp; We say absolutely!</p> 
  <p>You can google around to search for how this framework can be configured to monitor remote JVMs, and nearly all of the reference material will help you get up to speed quickly.&nbsp; However, you'll find that these examples favor simplicity over security.&nbsp; They generally disable authentication and encryption leaving the remote application vulnerable to anyone who knows or can guess its host name and port number.&nbsp; In the real world, if you wanted to monitor a production application remotely, you'd need to carefully consider the security ramifications.</p> 
  <p>Not finding much in the way of instruction here, we thought it might be helpful to document, by example, how a sample remote Java application can be more securely instrumented and managed with Java Flight Recorder and Java Mission Control.&nbsp; What follows are the steps necessary to enable monitoring over a connection that is secured using Secure Sockets Layer (SSL).</p> 
  <p>For the remainder of this article, we'll refer to the instrumented JVM as the <b><i>remote</i></b> side of the solution<i><b>.</b></i>&nbsp; To demonstrate the ubiquity of the Java SE platform, our remote JVM will actually reside on a <a href="http://www.raspberrypi.org/">Raspberry Pi</a>.&nbsp; As Java SE and Java SE Embedded runtime environments both now bundle the Java Flight Recorder feature set, it doesn't really matter what type of remote JVM instance we have.&nbsp; The location where the Java Mission Control application runs will be referred to as the <i><b>client</b></i> side.&nbsp; For this example we'll use an Ubuntu-based system.&nbsp; In reality, the client could have run on any of the alternative supported Java clients like Windows or MacOS.<br /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><b>On the <i>remote</i> side (hostname pi1): </b><br /></p> 
  <p>Remote side, step 0: Create a directory to house the signed digital certificates required for SSL communication.&nbsp; Moreover, it should have minimal access.<br /></p> 
  <blockquote><tt>pi1$ <b>mkdir $HOME/.certs</b><br />pi1$ <b>chmod 700 $HOME/.certs</b></tt><br /></blockquote> 
  <p> </p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p> </p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Remote side, step 1: Create a self-signed cryptographic key pair with the JDK <tt>keytool(1)</tt> utility.&nbsp; The are many alternatives to creating (and requesting) cryptographic keys.&nbsp; The method we'll use for this example is the most straightforward, as <tt>keytool</tt> is part of the JDK, not to mention the least expensive too!</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>pi1$ <b>keytool -genkey -alias jfrremote -keyalg RSA -validity 730 \</b></tt><br /> <tt><b> -keystore $HOME/.certs/jfrremoteKeyStore</b><br />Enter keystore password: <b>changeit</b><br />Re-enter new password: <b>changeit</b><br />What is your first and last name?<br />  [Unknown]:&nbsp; <b>Joe Schmo</b><br />What is the name of your organizational unit?<br />&nbsp; [Unknown]:&nbsp; <b>Acme Corp</b><br />What is the name of your organization?<br />&nbsp; [Unknown]:&nbsp; <b>Skunkworks</b><br />What is the name of your City or Locality?<br />&nbsp; [Unknown]:&nbsp; <b>New York</b><br />What is the name of your State or Province?<br />&nbsp; [Unknown]:&nbsp; <b>NY</b><br />What is the two-letter country code for this unit?<br />&nbsp; [Unknown]:&nbsp; <b>US</b><br />Is CN=Joe Schmo, OU=Acme Corp, O=Skunkworks, L=New York, ST=NY, C=US correct?<br />&nbsp; [no]:&nbsp; <b>yes</b><br /><br />Enter key password for &lt;jfrremote&gt;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; (RETURN if same as keystore password):</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Remote side, step 2: Restrict the access of the newly created <tt>jfrremoteKeyStore</tt> file.</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>pi1$ <b>chmod 600 $HOME/.certs/jfrremoteKeyStore</b></tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Remote side, step 3: Verify that the keystore contains our newly created key:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>pi1$ <b>keytool -keystore $HOME/.certs/jfrremoteKeyStore -list -v</b><br />Enter keystore password: <b>changeit</b><br /><br />Keystore type: JKS<br />Keystore provider: SUN<br /><br />Your keystore contains 1 entry<br /><br />Alias name: jfrremote<br />Creation date: 13-Jan-2015<br />Entry type: PrivateKeyEntry<br />Certificate chain length: 1<br />Certificate[1]:<br />Owner: CN=Joe Schmo, OU=Acme Corp, O=Skunkworks, L=New York, ST=NY, C=US<br />Issuer: CN=Joe Schmo, OU=Acme Corp, O=Skunkworks, L=New York, ST=NY, C=US<br />Serial number: 258d7624<br />Valid from: Tue Jan 13 17:32:51 UTC 2015 until: Thu Jan 12 17:32:51 UTC 2017<br />Certificate fingerprints:<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MD5:&nbsp; 2E:AD:5F:85:61:21:8D:1A:4B:ED:02:7C:67:26:8B:95<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; SHA1: 82:12:D6:A0:4C:20:E4:7F:C5:C1:C7:BC:AD:C7:D1:E8:47:76:F2:A6<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; SHA256: AF:E1:D8:7F:67:F3:DA:F1:22:58:42:B9:A5:50:37:6A:BA:49:76:BC:15:5F:11:9D:F0:1E:13:15:39:BB:9F:C4<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Signature algorithm name: SHA256withRSA<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Version: 3<br /><br />Extensions:<br /><br />#1: ObjectId: 2.5.29.14 Criticality=false<br />SubjectKeyIdentifier [<br />KeyIdentifier [<br />0000: 5E EC 1E F8 D5 33 F4 E6&nbsp;&nbsp; 29 06 B6 65 39 85 68 05&nbsp; ^....3..)..e9.h.<br />0010: F7 19 B3 AF&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ....<br />]<br />]<br /><br /><br />*******************************************<br />*******************************************</tt> </p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Remote side, step 4: Export the certificate associated with the recently generated key pair. This will be used by the remote JVM instance and also has to be imported into the trust store of the client application (Java Mission Control or <tt>jmc</tt>).</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>pi1$ <b>keytool -export -alias jfrremote -keystore \</b></tt><br /> <tt><b>$HOME/.certs/jfrremoteKeyStore -rfc -file jfrremote.cer</b><br />Enter keystore password: <b>changeit</b><br />Certificate stored in file &lt;jfrremote.cer&gt;</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p> 
  Remote side, step 5: Securely transfer the certificate stored in the <tt>jfrremote.cer</tt> file over to the system where the Java Mission Control client (<tt>jmc</tt>) will be run</p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><b>Switching over to the <i>client</i> side (hostname R840):</b></p> 
  <p>Client side, step 0: Create a directory to house the signed digital certificates required for
 SSL communication, with minimal access.</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>R840$ <b>mkdir $HOME/.certs</b><br />R840$ <b>chmod 700 $HOME/.certs</b></tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Client&nbsp; side, step 1: Import the certificate, represented by the <tt>jfrremote.cer</tt> file, into the client's trust store.</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>R840$ <b>keytool -importcert -keystore $HOME/.certs/jfrremoteTrustStore \</b></tt><br /> <tt><b>-alias jfrremote -file jfrremote.cer</b><br />Enter keystore password: <b>changeit</b><br />Re-enter new password: <b>changeit</b><br />Owner: CN=Joe Schmo, OU=Acme Corp, O=Skunkworks, L=New York, ST=NY, C=US<br />Issuer: CN=Joe Schmo, OU=Acme Corp, O=Skunkworks, L=New York, ST=NY, C=US<br />Serial number: 4fec928c<br />Valid from: Tue Jan 13 08:41:48 EST 2015 until: Thu Jan 12 08:41:48 EST 2017<br />Certificate fingerprints:<br /> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MD5:&nbsp; 3D:81:45:16:49:13:85:38:E8:E9:90:50:4A:59:F5:5E<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; SHA1: 6E:FA:63:D7:9A:58:26:A4:22:94:33:9F:AA:1A:6C:B6:E4:16:2C:DE<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; SHA256: 58:EB:F0:C9:DD:F9:D4:F7:FD:95:4B:2B:61:4C:88:6D:57:E3:87:9F:71:F5:BD:25:67:FB:3C:C0:05:0B:C6:0F<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Signature algorithm name: SHA256withRSA<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Version: 3<br /><br />Extensions:<br /><br />#1: ObjectId: 2.5.29.14 Criticality=false<br />SubjectKeyIdentifier [<br />KeyIdentifier [<br />0000: D9 A6 A2 0E CE D2 F7 9D&nbsp;&nbsp; FA 96 9C B9 9A 32 E2 3A&nbsp; .............2.:<br />0010: 98 ED A7 5F&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ..._<br />]<br />]<br /><br />Trust this certificate? [no]:&nbsp; <b>yes</b><br />Certificate was added to keystore</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Client side, step 2: Restrict the access of the newly created <tt>jfrremoteTrustStore</tt> file.</p> 
  <blockquote> </blockquote> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>R840$ <b>chmod 600 $HOME/.certs/jfrremoteTrustStore</b></tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Client side, step 3:  Create a self-signed cryptographic key pair with the JDK <tt>keytool(1)</tt> utility.&nbsp; This represents the certificate for the client-side Java Mission Control (<tt>jmc</tt>) application.</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>R840$ <b>keytool -genkey -alias jmc -keyalg RSA -validity 730 \</b><br /> <b>-keystore $HOME/.certs/jmcKeyStore</b><br />Enter keystore password: <b>changeit</b><br />Re-enter new password: <b>changeit</b><br />What is your first and last name?<br />&nbsp; [Unknown]:&nbsp; <b>Joe Schmo</b><br />What is the name of your organizational unit?<br />&nbsp; [Unknown]:&nbsp; <b>Acme Corp</b><br />What is the name of your organization?<br />&nbsp; [Unknown]:&nbsp; <b>Skunkworks</b><br />What is the name of your City or Locality?<br />&nbsp; [Unknown]:&nbsp; <b>New York</b><br />What is the name of your State or Province?<br />&nbsp; [Unknown]:&nbsp; <b>NY</b><br />What is the two-letter country code for this unit?<br />&nbsp; [Unknown]:&nbsp; <b>US</b><br />Is CN=Joe Schmo, OU=Acme Corp, O=Skunkworks, L=New York, ST=NY, C=US correct?<br />&nbsp; [no]:&nbsp; <b>yes</b><br /><br />Enter key password for &lt;jmc&gt;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; (RETURN if same as keystore password):</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Client side, step 4: Restrict the access of the newly created <tt>jmcKeyStore</tt> file.</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p> <tt>R840$ <b>chmod 600 $HOME/.certs/jmcKeyStore</b></tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Client side, step 5: Export the certificate associated with the recently generated key pair. 
This will be used by the Java Mission Control application and also has to be imported
 into the trust store of the remote JVM instance.</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>R840$ <b>keytool -export -alias jmc -keystore $HOME/.certs/jmcKeyStore \</b></tt><br /> <tt><b>-rfc -file jmc.cer</b><br />Enter keystore password: <b>changeit</b><br />Certificate stored in file &lt;jmc.cer&gt;</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Client side, step 6: Securely transfer the certificate stored in the <tt>jmc.cer</tt> file over to the system where the remote JVM instance will be run</p> 
  <p><b>Returning to the <i>remote</i> side (hostname pi1): </b></p> 
  <p>Remote side, step 6: Import the certificate, represented by the <tt>jmc.cer</tt> file, into the remote JVM instance's trust store.</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>pi1$ <b>keytool -import -alias jmc -file jmc.cer \</b></tt><br /> <tt><b>-keystore $HOME/.certs/jmcTrustStore</b><br />Enter keystore password: <b>changeit</b><br />Re-enter new password: <b>changeit</b><br />Owner: CN=Joe Schmo, OU=Acme Corp, O=Skunkworks, L=New York, ST=NY, C=US<br />Issuer: CN=Joe Schmo, OU=Acme Corp, O=Skunkworks, L=New York, ST=NY, C=US<br />Serial number: 860e0e4<br />Valid from: Tue Jan 13 20:15:33 UTC 2015 until: Thu Jan 12 20:15:33 UTC 2017<br />Certificate fingerprints:<br /> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; MD5:&nbsp; 7B:D7:F3:9D:71:52:F9:35:03:3A:68:BF:02:C2:52:51<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; SHA1: 38:95:6D:2F:DE:FC:99:D6:63:55:00:A8:57:E2:31:FF:53:35:18:F7<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; SHA256: 7D:87:87:01:E5:21:58:02:67:0E:7E:2F:14:77:86:12:9D:52:CD:11:A4:B1:C5:D3:32:D8:05:30:61:7B:F5:3E<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Signature algorithm name: SHA256withRSA<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Version: 3<br /><br />Extensions:<br /><br />#1: ObjectId: 2.5.29.14 Criticality=false<br />SubjectKeyIdentifier [<br />KeyIdentifier [<br />0000: 0A E8 E3 5E 0A 3C 48 FF&nbsp;&nbsp; D4 DB 10 A8 62 31 1E F9&nbsp; ...^.&lt;H.....b1..<br />0010: 55 D8 4C 7A&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; U.Lz<br />]<br />]<br /><br />Trust this certificate? [no]:&nbsp; <b>yes</b><br />Certificate was added to keystore</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Remote side, step 7: Restrict the access of the newly created <tt>jmcTrustStore</tt> file.</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p> <tt>pi1$ <b>chmod 600 $HOME/.certs/jmcTrustStore</b></tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p><b>Putting it All Together</b></p> 
  <p> With key stores and trust stores set up on both sides, we can now start up both components.&nbsp; Our sample application is a very simple one called <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/jmc-jfr-ssl/Allocator.java">Allocator.java</a>. Download it onto your remote system and compile it with the <tt>javac</tt> program.&nbsp; When ready, you can start up an SSL-enabled remote JVM instance of the <tt>Allocator</tt> program in continuous flight recorder mode with the following invocation:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>pi1$ <b>java \<br />
    -Dcom.sun.management.jmxremote.port=7091\<br />
    -Dcom.sun.management.jmxremote.ssl=true \<br />
    -Dcom.sun.management.jmxremote.authenticate=false \<br />
    -Djavax.net.ssl.keyStore=$HOME/.certs/jfrremoteKeyStore \<br />
    -Djavax.net.ssl.keyStorePassword=changeit \<br />
    -Djava.rmi.server.hostname=pi1 \<br />
    -XX:+UnlockCommercialFeatures \<br />
    -XX:+FlightRecorder \<br />
    -XX:FlightRecorderOptions=defaultrecording=true \<br />
    Allocator</b></tt> </p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <blockquote> </blockquote> 
  <p>On the client side, Java Mission Control is started in the following way:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>R840$ <b>jmc -vmargs \</b></tt><b><br /> <tt>-Djavax.net.ssl.trustStore=$HOME/.certs/jfrremoteTrustStore \</tt><br /> <tt>-Djavax.net.ssl.trustStorePassword=changeit \</tt><br /> <tt>-Djavax.net.ssl.keyStore=$HOME/.certs/jmcKeyStore\</tt><br /> <tt> -Djavax.net.ssl.keyStorePassword=changeit</tt></b></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Once Java Mission Control has started, we connect to the VM instance on host pi1 as shown in the screenshots that follow.&nbsp; First off, we select &quot;Connect...&quot; from the File menu.<br /></p> 
  <p><img src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/jmc-jfr-ssl/connect.jpg" /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>A &quot;New Connection&quot; window appears.&nbsp; Select &quot;Create a new connection&quot; and click the &quot;Next&gt;&quot; button.</p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><img width="613" height="481" src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/jmc-jfr-ssl/new_connection.jpg" /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>In the next window that appears, &quot;pi1&quot; is selected as the host and &quot;7091&quot; as the port.&nbsp; Clicking the &quot;Finish&quot; button continues the process. </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><img width="634" height="498" src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/jmc-jfr-ssl/connect_to_pi1.jpg" /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> <br /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>&nbsp;And here's a screenshot of the MBean Server window for the JVM running on host pi1.<br /></p> 
  <p><img width="810" height="462" src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/jmc-jfr-ssl/jmc_pi1.jpg" /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><b>Conclusion</b></p> 
  <p>To assist in this repetitive and potentially error-prone task, a series of shell scripts have been created which just might be of use.&nbsp; Download <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/jmc-jfr-ssl/SSL_jfr_jmc.tar.gz">this tarball</a>, and check out the README files found in the two directories, one for the remote side of the equation, the other for the client.</p> 
  <p>For further edification, please check out these links:</p> 
  <p>Marcus Hirt's blog for all things related to Java Flight Recorder and Java Mission Control<br /> <a href="http://hirt.se/blog/">http://hirt.se/blog/</a></p> 
  <p>If you run into SSL handshake problems, this link may prove helpful. <br />
  How to Anazyze Java SSL Errors:<br /> <a href="http://java.dzone.com/articles/how-analyze-java-ssl-errors">http://java.dzone.com/articles/how-analyze-java-ssl-errors</a></p> 
  <p>Erik Costlow's article on Self-signed certificates for a known community:<br /> <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/java-platform-group/entry/self_signed_certificates_for_a">https://blogs.oracle.com/java-platform-group/entry/self_signed_certificates_for_a</a></p><br /> 
  <p><br /></p> 
  <blockquote> </blockquote> 
  <blockquote> </blockquote> 
  <blockquote> </blockquote> 
  <blockquote> </blockquote> 
  <p> </p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jim Connors
EMAIL: james.connors@oracle.com
URL: null
DATE: 12/15/2015 16:28:19

<p>A couple of things here Marty:</p>


<p>1. The example that is used in this article would not be possible if both ends were Windows because Java SE Embedded is not available for the Raspberry Pi on Windows -- only for Linux.</p>


<p>2. If the remote platform were a Windows desktop or server, then a very similar proccess to what is described above can be used.  The most important utility required to accomplish Flight Recorder with SSL is keytool.  It works exactly the same on both Linux and Windows.  What would have to change in the examples would be the location of the files in question and the directory separators (&quot;/&quot; versus &quot;\&quot;).  Addtionally there are some Linux specific commands in the article (e.g. mkdir $HOME/.certs, chmod 700 $HOME/.certs ...) that would need a suitable Windows replacement if relevant.</p>

<p>Looking back, creating the the jmcTrustStore on the remote side looks unnecessary, however you still need to create the client side jmcKeyStore. My guess is there's some discrepancy in your runtime options for either/or starting the remote jfr application or the jmc client.</p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jay Sheth
EMAIL: shethju@gmail.com
URL: null
DATE: 03/02/2017 19:31:24

<p>For standalone application, we need add jmcTrustStore as truststore while starting application else ssl handshake is not successful.</p>


<p>(See <a href="https://www.lullabot.com/articles/monitor-java-with-jmx" rel="nofollow">https://www.lullabot.com/articles/monitor-java-with-jmx</a> for keystore i used. Instead of jconsole i have used jmc). In my use case both application and jmc are on same box, but the logic is still valid.</p>


<p>For my windows program I used the following on application side:<br/>
-Dcom.sun.management.jmxremote=true <br/>
-Dcom.sun.management.jmxremote.port=3614 <br/>
-Dcom.sun.management.jmxremote.authenticate=true<br/>
-Dcom.sun.management.jmxremote.password.file=&quot;c:\jmx\jmxremote.password&quot; <br/>
-Dcom.sun.management.jmxremote.access.file=&quot;c:\jmx\jmxremote.access&quot;<br/>
-Dcom.sun.management.jmxremote.ssl=true<br/>
-Djavax.net.ssl.keyStore=C:\keytool\tomcat.jks<br/>
-Djavax.net.ssl.keyStorePassword=&lt;password&gt;<br/>
-Djavax.net.ssl.trustStore=C:\keytool\tomcattruststore.jks<br/>
-Djavax.net.ssl.trustStorePassword=&lt;password&gt;<br/>
-Dcom.sun.management.jmxremote.ssl.need.client.auth=true<br/>
-Djavax.net.debug=ssl</p>


<p>For client side i used the following:<br/>
&quot;C:\Program Files\Java\jdk1.8.0_111\bin\jmc.exe&quot; -vmargs -Djavax.net.ssl.trustStore=C:\keytool\jmctruststore.jks -Djavax.net.ssl.trustStorePassword=&lt;password&gt;<br/>
-Djavax.net.ssl.keyStore=c:\keytool\jmc.jks -Djavax.net.ssl.keyStorePassword=&lt;password&gt;<br/>
-Djavax.net.debug=ssl </p>

--------
AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: USB Device Access for Java SE and OSGi
DATE: 10/31/2014 13:27:48
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p> One of the challenges in creating the content for the <a href="https://oracleus.activeevents.com/2014/connect/sessionDetail.ww?SESSION_ID=2097">Java One 2014 Java SE Embedded Internet of Things Hands-on-Lab</a> concerned interacting, via Java and OSGi, with a USB temperature sensor. &nbsp; Unfortunately a USB communications API is not part of the Java SE standard (as of this post: Halloween 2014).&nbsp; So the question is, how can Java/USB communication be established, and furthermore and how does this work within the OSGi framework?<br /><br /></p> 
  <p><img width="247" height="156" src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/Java_USB/setup.jpg" /> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>In looking around at some of the available options, we chose the <a href="http://code.google.com/p/javahidapi/">javahidapi</a> as the basis for this connectivity.&nbsp; As a Java/JNI wrapper around the <a href="http://www.signal11.us/oss/hidapi/">C/C++ HID API for Linux, MacOS X and Windows</a>, the appeal behind this API is that using it does not require the
use of a custom driver for each device on each platform.</p> 
  <p>In order to operate within an OSGi framework (in this case Apache Felix 4.4), javahidapi's open source code has been slightly modified/enhanced. The end result is that an OSGi bundle is available that can be dropped into standard OSGi frameworks to support USB communication for HID devices.&nbsp; It does contain a native component, and for the sake of simplicity, we've decided to include a separate jar file for each supported architecture.&nbsp; For the OSGi enthusiast, here's what the generated <tt>MANIFEST.MF</tt> file looks like for the Linux/armhf (suitable for the Raspberry Pi) architecture: </p> 
  <p><tt>Manifest-Version: 1.0<br />Bnd-LastModified: 1415889978962<br />Build-Jdk: 1.7.0_51<br />Built-By: jtconnor<br />Bundle-Activator: com.codeminders.hidapi.Activator<br />Bundle-ManifestVersion: 2<br />Bundle-Name: hidapi OSGi Bundle for Linux/armhf<br />Bundle-NativeCode: native/linux/armv6l/libhidapi-jni-32.so; osname=Linux; processor=armv6l<br />Bundle-SymbolicName: com.codeminders.hidapi-armhf<br />Bundle-Version: 1.0.0<br />Created-By: Apache Maven Bundle Plugin<br />Export-Package: com.codeminders.hidapi;uses:=&quot;org.osgi.framework&quot;;version=&quot;1.0.0&quot;<br />Import-Package: org.osgi.framework;version=&quot;[1.6,2)&quot;<br />Tool: Bnd-1.50.0</tt></p> 
  <p>Here are some pre-built hidapi OSGi bundles for popular Linux platforms:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/Java_USB/com.codeminders.hidapi-armhf-1.0.jar.zip">com.codeminders.hidapi-armhf-1.0.jar</a> (ARMv6 hard float - Suitable for Raspberry Pi)</li> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/Java_USB/com.codeminders.hidapi-armv5tel-1.0.jar.zip">com.codeminders.hidapi-armv5tel-1.0.jar</a> (ARMv5- Suitable for Plug Computers)</li> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/Java_USB/com.codeminders.hidapi-x86-1.0.jar.zip">com.codeminders.hidapi-x86-1.0.jar</a> (Linux/x86 32-bit)</li> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/Java_USB/com.codeminders.hidapi-x86_64-1.0.jar.zip">com.codeminders.hidapi-x86_64-1.0.jar</a> (Linux/x86 64-bit)</li> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/Java_USB/com.codeminders.hidapi-quark-1.0.jar.zip">com.codeminders.hidapi-quark-1.0.jar</a> (Linux/x86 Intel Quark processor)<br /></li> 
  </ul> 
  <p>To get a feel for the changes made to the original source, here's what was done:</p> 
  <ol> 
    <li>A NetBeans project was created under the Maven-&gt;OSGi Bundle category</li> 
    <li>The Java source code for <a href="http://code.google.com/p/javahidapi/">javahidapi</a> was placed in the project's <tt>src/main/java/com/codeminders/hidapi</tt> directory<br /></li> 
    <li>The architecture specific native library was placed in the project's <tt>src/main/resources/native/linux/<i><b>architecture</b></i></tt> directory.&nbsp; For example, the Linux/x86 version of the project places the <tt>libhidapi-jni-32.so</tt> file in the <tt>src/main/resources/native/linux/x86</tt> directory.</li> 
    <li>An <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/Java_USB/Activator.java"><tt>Activator.java</tt></a> class was added in the project's <tt>src/main/java/com/codeminders/hidapi</tt> directory.&nbsp; In OSGi, the <tt>start()</tt> method in this class gets called when this bundle is activated.&nbsp; It is specified in the bundle's <tt>MANIFEST.MF</tt> file.</li> 
    <li>The original <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/Java_USB/ClassPathLibraryLoader.java">ClassPathLibraryLoader.java</a> file was simplified and is currently only appropriate for Linux deployments.<br /></li> 
    <li>As this is a Maven-based project, the project's <tt>pom.xml</tt> file was edited (<a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/Java_USB/pom.xml">here's what the x86 version looks like</a>), such that at build time it will generate a <tt>MANIFEST.MF</tt> file similar to the one referenced above.<br /></li> 
  </ol> 
  <p>And here are the associated NetBeans Projects which can be used to build the 4 bundles referenced above.</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/Java_USB/hidapi_armhf.zip">hidapi_armhf</a></li> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/Java_USB/hidapi_armv5tel.zip">hidapi_armv5tel</a></li> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/Java_USB/hidapi_x86.zip">hidapi_x86</a></li> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/Java_USB/hidapi_x86_64.zip">hidapi_x86_64</a></li> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/Java_USB/hidapi_quark.zip">hidapi_quark</a> <br /></li> 
  </ul>If you'd like to extend this template to include OSGi bundles for additional architectures, you can start with one of the projects above, clone it, and make the appropriate changes for your new environment.&nbsp; If the native javahidapi component for your new platform is not available, you'll have to <a href="https://github.com/signal11/hidapi/downloads">pull down the source for hidapi</a> and build it for inclusion into your project.&nbsp; If anyone is interested in going through this exercise, I'd be glad to post the fruits of their labor here.<br /> 
  <ul> </ul>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors
EMAIL: james.connors@oracle.com

DATE: 11/13/2014 05:19:28

<p>Got my hands on an Intel Galileo board, based on the Intel Quark processor.  This uses the x86 instruction set, but may not support all of the recent x86 instructions.  The 32-bit x86 version of the hidapi bundle on the Galileo board was seg faulting, so I compiled the hidapi native component on the Galileo board and provided another bundle specific to the Linux/quark platform.  The quark specific packaged solved the problems.</p>


<p>This blog has been updated to include the quark bundle and NetBeans project.</p>

TITLE: Java SE Embedded IoT Hands On Lab Returns for Java One 2014
DATE: 08/26/2014 15:48:21
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>After a one year hiatus, a Hands On Lab featuring Java SE for Embedded devices will return for <a href="https://www.oracle.com/javaone/index.html">The Java One 2014 Conference</a>.&nbsp; Entitled <a href="https://oracleus.activeevents.com/2014/connect/search.ww?eventRef=javaone#loadSearch-event=null&amp;searchPhrase=HOL2097&amp;searchType=session&amp;tc=0&amp;sortBy=&amp;p=&amp;i%2810009%29=10111"><i><b>HOL2097 Java SE Embedded IoT Hands On Lab</b></i></a>, students who attend this lab will:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>Have their own individual embedded device to gain valuable
          hands on experience</li> 
    <li>Learn how to create Java 8 <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/compact_profiles_demonstrated">Compact Profiles</a> using the eJDK
          <tt>jrecreate</tt> tool.&nbsp; Students will use and deploy those Compact
          Profiles on their individual device.</li> 
    <li>Learn about profile-aware tools available both at the JDK
          and IDE (NetBeans) level that aid developers in creating Java
          applications suitable for specific Compact Profiles.</li> 
    <li>Learn about and utilize OSGi, an important Java-based
          modular service platform ideal for dynamically managing the lifecycle of software
          components, especially suited for gateway devices.<br /></li> 
    <li>Attach a sensor to the gateway device and deploy a Java/OSGi bundle on the device to capture data from the sensor.</li> 
    <li>Build and deploy a Java/OSGi web service bundle on the gateway device that
          publishes data captured by the sensor.&nbsp; Students will be able
          to remotely access this web service to retrieve the sensor
          data in standard JSON format.</li> 
  </ul> 
  <p>For those attending the <a href="https://www.oracle.com/javaone/index.html">Java One 2014 Conference</a>, seating for this lab is limited.&nbsp; So reserve your spot early.&nbsp; We could also conceivably host a smaller workshop at a customer location effectively duplicating this lab.&nbsp; If you're interested, you can drop me a line at <a href="mailto:james.connors@oracle.com">james.connors@oracle.com</a>.<br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Java Serial Communications Revisited
DATE: 06/20/2014 19:10:52
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
Last touched upon in <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/serial_port_communication_for_java">this article</a> dated August 2011, it's high time to revisit the state of Java serial port communications options for the following reasons:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>As the hype around Internet of Things continues to ratchet up, serial communications are a requirement for a certain class of IoT sensors and devices.</li> 
    <li>RxTx, the platform with arguably the most history, cannot be built with the recently released Java 8 JDK without some modifications.</li> 
    <li>For some time now the website hosting the RxTx project: <a href="http://rxtx.qbang.org/wiki/index.php/Main_Page">http://rxtx.qbang.org/wiki/index.php/Main_Page</a> has not been available.<br /></li> 
    <li>Building support for additional specifications like RS-485 and parallel is available in RxTx but was never addressed in the previous serial communications article.<br /></li> 
    <li>An alternative framework called <a href="http://code.google.com/p/java-simple-serial-connector/">jSSC</a> is gaining in popularity and is worth further discussion.</li> 
    <li>Work in the <a href="http://openjdk.java.net/projects/dio/">OpenJDK Device I/O Project</a> is progressing. Among the goals of this project is support for serial communications.</li> 
  </ul> 
  <p><b>RxTx</b></p> 
  <p>In my customer experiences, RxTx, despite its age, still gets mentioned most when considering serial communications with Java.&nbsp; For whatever reasons, the RxTx project has gone off line, and access to the project source code is not readily available.&nbsp; Fortunately, we have a copy, and have made a few enhancements such that:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>The source can now be compiled with a JDK versions 6, 7 and 8.</li> 
    <li>The <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/serial_port_communication_for_java">original article</a> discussed only enough modifications to build the&nbsp; <tt>librxtxSerial.so</tt> shared object required for traditional serial communications.&nbsp; The <tt>librxtxParallel.so</tt>, <tt>librxtxRaw.so</tt>, <tt>librxtxI2C.so</tt> and <tt>librxtxRS485.so</tt> shared objects could not be built.&nbsp; With some very slight modifications, these can successfully be built too.&nbsp; I make absolutely no promises as to their usefulness, but they do compile. :)</li> 
  </ul> 
  <p>The source code is based upon the original 2.1-7r2 version and in this instance is now called <b><tt>2.1.7r2-Java8</tt></b>.&nbsp; You can download the source <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/rxtx-2.1-7r2-Java8/rxtx-2.1-7r2-Java8.tar.gz">here</a>.&nbsp; If you want to get a feel for the changes made, take a look at this file:&nbsp; <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/rxtx-2.1-7r2-Java8/JAVA8_MODS.txt"><b><tt>JAVA8_MODS.txt</tt></b></a> which can be found in the main directory of the source code.&nbsp; To build RxTx on your native platform:</p> 
  <pre>   $ tar xvf 2.1.7r2-Java8.tar.gz
   $ cd 2.1.7r2-Java8/
   $ ./configure
   $ make
</pre> 
  <p><b>jSSC </b></p> 
  <p>The Java Simple Serial Connector, or jSSC for short, is another open source project that can be found here: <a href="http://code.google.com/p/java-simple-serial-connector/">http://code.google.com/p/java-simple-serial-connector/</a>.&nbsp; It is available for a host of processor/OS combinations and, in addition to source availability, comes bundled in binary form too.&nbsp; Here's a list of supported platforms for its current 2.6.0 release:<br /></p> 
  <blockquote><a href="http://code.google.com/p/java-simple-serial-connector/downloads/list?q=label:Win32" class="label">Win32</a> <a href="http://code.google.com/p/java-simple-serial-connector/downloads/list?q=label:Win64" class="label">Win64</a> <a href="http://code.google.com/p/java-simple-serial-connector/downloads/list?q=label:Linux_x86" class="label">Linux_x86</a> <a href="http://code.google.com/p/java-simple-serial-connector/downloads/list?q=label:Linux_x86_64" class="label">Linux_x86_64</a> <a href="http://code.google.com/p/java-simple-serial-connector/downloads/list?q=label:Linux_ARM" class="label">Linux_ARM</a> <a href="http://code.google.com/p/java-simple-serial-connector/downloads/list?q=label:Solaris_x86" class="label">Solaris_x86</a> <a href="http://code.google.com/p/java-simple-serial-connector/downloads/list?q=label:Solaris_x86_64" class="label">Solaris_x86_64</a> <a href="http://code.google.com/p/java-simple-serial-connector/downloads/list?q=label:MacOSX_x86" class="label">MacOSX_x86</a> <a href="http://code.google.com/p/java-simple-serial-connector/downloads/list?q=label:MacOSX_x86_64" class="label">MacOSX_x86_64</a> <a href="http://code.google.com/p/java-simple-serial-connector/downloads/list?q=label:MacOSX_PPC" class="label">MacOSX_PPC</a> <a href="http://code.google.com/p/java-simple-serial-connector/downloads/list?q=label:MacOSX_PPC64" class="label">MacOSX_PPC64</a></blockquote> 
  <p>Like RxTx, it contains a native component but is packaged in a nice transparent fashion.&nbsp; The one potential challenge here may be in trying to figure out how to support a new platform that isn't on this list.&nbsp; I didn't have a whole lot of success finding out how to build the binary, and admittedly didn't spend an inordinate amount of time trying to figure it out either.&nbsp; Nonetheless, this project is gaining in popularity and has a dedicated individual supporting the software.</p> 
  <p><b>OpenJDK Device I/O</b></p> 
  <p>Finally, a project is underway to treat serial communication and device I/O in general as a first class citizen for the Java SE standard. The wiki can be found here: <a href="https://wiki.openjdk.java.net/display/dio/Main">https://wiki.openjdk.java.net/display/dio/Main</a>. It is based on the work done to provide device I/O to the Java ME 8 platform.&nbsp; Further solidifying the universality theme of Java 8, the ultimate goal would be to have a consistent device I/O API across both Java SE and Java ME.&nbsp; If you want to further understand what those APIs look like you can view them here: <a href="http://docs.oracle.com/javame/8.0/api/dio/api/index.html">http://docs.oracle.com/javame/8.0/api/dio/api/index.html</a>.</p> 
  <p>In conclusion, support for serial communications in Java SE is -- albeit slowly -- progressing.&nbsp; There are multiple open source projects and commercial alternatives too.&nbsp; Ideally, it will be great to see a formal API supported by the Java SE Standard.<br /></p>

AUTHOR: Myk

URL: null
DATE: 11/07/2014 03:50:54

<p>Another cross-platform library we use is SerialIO's SerialPort library. It is effective and robust but it has several problems.  (1) it is only supported for 30-90 days after purchase, (2) most support enquiries receive the grumpy response &quot;modify the source and recompile it yourself&quot;, (3) it must be re-purchased every time there is a new platform (e.g. Windows 8, Windows 8.1), (4) it is expensive to re-buy each time especially with source included and (4) it doesn't include any Linux ARM binaries at all (eg. for Raspberry PI) - fiddling about with the source to achieve this is not something we're willing or able to do.</p>

<p>The 'serial communication manager (scm)' is a java library designed and developed to exchange data on serial port. It supports RS-232 control signals handshaking, monitoring and has been ported to Linux, MAC, Solaris and Windows operating system. It is consistent, scalable, testable, efficient, reliable and portable.</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: An Embedded Java 8 Lambda Expression Microbenchmark
DATE: 03/17/2014 18:06:36
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Personal
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p> It's been a long road, but Java 8 has finally arrived.&nbsp; Much has been written and said about all the new features contained in this release, perhaps the most important of these is the introduction of <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/thejavatutorials/entry/javaone_2013_lambda_expressions">Lambda Expressions</a>.&nbsp; Lambdas are now intimately integrated into the Java platform and they have the potential to aid developers in the traditionally tricky realm of parallel programming.</p> 
  <p>Following closely behind, <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/a_first_look_at_compact">Compact Profiles</a> promise to open up the tremendous benefits of Java Standard Edition compatibility to embedded platforms previously thought to be too small.&nbsp; Can you see where this is heading?&nbsp; It might be interesting to use these two technologies simultaneously and see how well they work together.&nbsp; What follows is the description of a small program and its performance measurements -- a microbenchmark if you will -- that aims to highlight how programming with the new Lambda Expression paradigm can be beneficial not only for typical desktops and servers, but also for a growing number of embedded platforms too.</p> 
  <p><b>The Hardware/OS Platform(s)</b></p> 
  <p>Of primary interest for this article is the <a href="http://boundarydevices.com/products/sabre-lite-imx6-sbc/">Boundary Devices BD-SL-i.MX6</a> single board computer.&nbsp; It is a quad-core ARM® Cortex™-A9 based system with 1GB RAM running an <i>armhf</i>&nbsp; Debian Linux distribution.&nbsp; At the time of this article's publication, its list price is US $199.<br /></p> 
  <p><img height="225" width="300" src="http://boundarydevices.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/06/imx6q_sabrelite_top1-300x225.jpg" class="attachment-medium wp-post-image" alt="imx6q_sabrelite_top1" /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>What makes it more interesting is that we'll not only run Java 8 Lambda Expressions on device, we'll do it within the confines of the new Java 8 Compact1 profile.&nbsp; <i><b>The static footprint of this Java runtime environment is 10½ MB.</b></i></p> 
  <p>A second system, altogether different in capability and capacity from our embedded device will be used as a means to compare and contrast execution behavior across disparate hardware and OS environments.&nbsp; The system in question is a <a href="http://www.toshiba.com/us/computers/laptops/tecra/R840/">Toshiba Tecra R840</a> laptop running Windows 7/64-bit.&nbsp; It has a dual-core Intel® Core™ i5-2520M processor with 8GB RAM and will use the standard Java 8 Runtime Environment (JRE) for Windows 64-bit.<br /></p> 
  <p><b>The Application</b></p> 
  <p>Looking for a sample dataset as the basis for our rudimentary application, <a href="https://launchpad.net/test-db">this link</a> provides an ideal (and fictional) database of employee records.&nbsp; Among the available formats, a comma-delimited CSV file is supplied with approximately 300,000 entries.&nbsp; Our sample application will read this file and store the employee records into a <tt>LinkedList&lt;EmployeeRec&gt;.&nbsp;</tt> The <tt>EmployeeRec</tt> has the following&nbsp; fields:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>public class EmployeeRec {<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; private String id;<br /></tt><tt>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; private String birthDate;</tt><br /><tt>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; private String lastName;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; private String firstName;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; private String gender;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; private String hireDate;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ...<br />}</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>With this data structure initialized, our application is asked to perform one simple task:&nbsp; <b><i>calculate the average age of all male employees</i></b>.</p> 
  <p><b>Old School</b></p> 
  <p>First off let's perform this calculation in a way that predates the availability of Lambda Expressions.&nbsp; We'll call this version <b>OldSchool</b>.&nbsp; The code performing the &quot;average age of all male employees&quot; calculation looks like this:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>double sumAge = 0;<br />long numMales = 0;<br />for (EmployeeRec emp : employeeList) {<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; if (emp.getGender().equals(&quot;M&quot;)) {<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; sumAge += emp.getAge();<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; numMales += 1;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }<br />}<br />double avgAge = sumAge / numMales;</tt> </p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p><b>Lamba Expression Version 1<br /></b></p> 
  <p>Our second variation will use a Lambda expression to perform the identical calculation.&nbsp; We'll call this version <b>Lamba stream()</b>.&nbsp; The key statement in Java 8 looks like this:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p> <tt>double avgAge = employeeList.stream()<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; .filter(s -&gt; s.getGender().equals(&quot;M&quot;))<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; .mapToDouble(s -&gt; s.getAge())<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; .average()<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; .getAsDouble();</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p><b>Lambda Expression Version 2</b><br /></p> 
  <p>Our final variation uses the preceding Lambda Expression with one slight modification: it replaces the <tt>stream()</tt> method call with the <tt>parallelStream()</tt> method, offering the potential to split the task into smaller units running on separate threads.&nbsp; We'll call this version <b>Lambda parallelStream()</b>. The Java 8 statement looks as follows:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>double avgAge = employeeList.<b><span style="color: #ff0000;">parallelStream()</span></b><br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; .filter(s -&gt; s.getGender().equals(&quot;M&quot;))<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; .mapToDouble(s -&gt; s.getAge())<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; .average()<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; .getAsDouble();</tt> <br /></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p><b>Initial Test Results</b></p> 
  <p>The charts that follow display execution times of the sample problem solved via our three aforementioned variations.&nbsp; The left chart represents times recorded on the ARM Cortex-A9 processor while the right chart shows recorded times for the Intel Core-i5.&nbsp; The smaller the result, the faster, both examples indicate that there is some overhead to utilizing a serial Lambda <tt>stream()</tt> over and above the old school pre-Lambda solution.&nbsp; As far as <tt>parallelStream()</tt> goes, it's a mixed bag.&nbsp; For the Cortex-A9, the <tt>parallelStream()</tt> operation is negligibly faster than the old school solution, whereas for the Core-i5, the overhead incurred by <tt>parallelStream()</tt> actually makes the solution slower.</p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><img src="http://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/lambda-microbench/ComputeAvgChart.jpg" /> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>Without any further investigation, one might conclude that parallel streams may not be worth the effort. But what if performing a trivial calculation on a list of 300,000 employees simply isn't enough work to show the benefits of parallelization?&nbsp; For this next series of tests, we'll increase the computational load to see how performance might be effected.<br /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><b>Adding More Work to the Test<br /></b></p> 
  <p>For this version of the test, we'll solve the same problem, that is to say, calculate the average age of all males, but add a varying amount of intermediate computation.&nbsp; We can variably increase the number of required compute cycles by introducing the following identity method to our programs:<br /></p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>/* <br />&nbsp;* Rube Goldberg way of calculating identity of 'val',<br />&nbsp;* assuming number is positive<br />&nbsp;*/<br />private static double identity(double val) {<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; double result = 0;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; for (int i=0; i &lt; <span style="color: #ff0000;"><b>loopCount</b></span>; i++) {<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; result += Math.sqrt(Math.abs(Math.pow(val, 2)));&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; return result / loopCount;</tt><br />}</p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>As this method takes the square root of the square of a number, it is in essence an expensive identity function. By changing the value of <tt>loopCount</tt> (this is done via command-line option), we can change the number of times this loop executes per <tt>identity()</tt> invocation.&nbsp; This method is inserted into our code, for example with the Lambda ParallelStream() version, as follows:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>double avgAge = employeeList.parallelStream()<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; .filter(s -&gt; s.getGender().equals(&quot;M&quot;))<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; .mapToDouble(<span style="color: #ff0000;"><b>s -&gt; identity(s.getAge())</b></span>)<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; .average()<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; .getAsDouble();</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>A modification identical to what is highlighted in red above is also applied to both Old School and Lambda Stream() variations.&nbsp; The charts that follow display execution times for three separate runs of our microbenchmark, each with a different value assigned to the internal <tt>loopCount</tt> variable in our Rube Goldberg <tt>identity()</tt> function.<br /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><img src="http://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/lambda-microbench/ComputeAvgExtraChart.jpg" /> </p> 
  <p>For the Cortex-A9, you can clearly see the performance advantage of <tt>parallelStream()</tt> when the loop count is set to 100, and it becomes even more striking when the loop count is increased to 500.&nbsp; For the Core-i5, it takes a lot more work to realize the benefits of <tt>parallelStream()</tt>.&nbsp; Not until the loop count is set to 50,000 do the performance advantages become apparent.&nbsp; The Core-i5 is so much faster and only has two cores; consequently the amount of effort needed to overcome the initial overhead of <tt>parallelStream()</tt> is much more significant.</p> 
  <p><b>Downloads<br /></b></p> 
  <p> The sample code used in this article is available as a NetBeans project.&nbsp; As the project includes a CSV file with over 300,000 entries, it is larger than one might expect.&nbsp; The <a href="http://blogs.oracle.com">blogs.oracle.com</a>&nbsp; site prohibits storing files larger than 2MB in size so this project source has been compressed and split into three parts.&nbsp; Here are the links:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li><a href="http://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/lambda-microbench/LambdaMicrobench.zip.part1">LambdaMicrobench.zip.part1</a></li> 
    <li><a href="http://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/lambda-microbench/LambdaMicrobench.zip.part2">LambdaMicrobench.zip.part2</a></li> 
    <li><a href="http://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/lambda-microbench/LambdaMicrobench.zip.part3">LambdaMicrobench.zip.part3</a></li> 
  </ul> 
  <p>Just concatenate the three downloaded files together to recreate the original LambdaMicrobench.zip file.&nbsp; In Linux, the command would look something like this:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p> <tt>$ cat LambdaMicrobench.zip.part? &gt; LambdaMicrobench.zip</tt><br /></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <ul> </ul><a href="http://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/lambda-microbench/LambdaMicrobench.zip.part1"></a><b>Conclusion</b><br /> 
  <p>A great deal of effort has been put into making Java 8 a much more universal platform.&nbsp; Our simple example here demonstrates that even an embedded Java runtime environment as small as 10½ MB can take advantage of the latest advances to the platform.&nbsp; This is just the beginning.&nbsp; There is lots more work to be done to further enhance the performance characteristics of parallel stream Lambda Expressions.&nbsp; We look forward to future enhancements.<br /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: guest
EMAIL: james.connors@oracle.com

<p>Normally these projects would run within the context of NetBeans, but as they are built using ant, I see how you'd like to try running these outside the IDE.</p>


<p>In order to get this to run outside NeBeans, on an Ubuntu 14.05 system, I did the following:</p>


<p>1. Install the latest Java 8 JDK (JDK 1.8.0 Update 45) and put it in my<br/>
   $PATH (/home/jimc/jdk1.8.0_45/bin)<br/>
2. Install ant ($sudo apt-get install ant)<br/>
3. Download and extract the LambdaMicroBench project.  This creates a<br/>
   LambdaMicrobench/ directory<br/>
4. To build, inside the LambdaMicrobench/ directory, type:</p>


<p>    $ ant -Dplatforms.jdk_1.8.home=$HOME/jdk1.8.0_45</p>


<p>You may have to change the location of the JDK directory</p>


<p>5. Once built, sample runs include:</p>


<p>$ java -cp dist/LambdaMicrbench.jar lambdademo.LambdaAvgExtraParallel 50000</p>


<p>$ java -cp dist/LambdaMicrbench.jar lambdademo.OldSchoolAvgExtra 50000</p>


<p>Hope this helps,<br/>
-- Jim C</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Introducing the EJDK
DATE: 03/10/2014 13:45:19
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>In lock step with the introduction of <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/a_first_look_at_compact">Compact Profiles</a>, Java 8 includes a new distribution mechanism for Java SE Embedded called the <i>EJDK</i>.&nbsp; As the potential exists to confuse the EJDK with the standard JDK (Java Development Kit), it makes sense to dedicate a few words towards highlighting how these two packages differ in form and function. <br /></p> 
  <p><b>The JDK</b></p> 
  <p>The venerable Java Development Kit is the mainstay of Java developers.&nbsp; It incorporates not only a standard Java Runtime Environment (JRE), but also includes critical tools required by those same developers.&nbsp; For example, among many others, the JDK comes with a Java compiler (<tt>javac</tt>), a Java console application (<tt>jconsole</tt>), the Java debugger (<tt>jdb</tt>) and the Java archive utility (<tt>jar</tt>).&nbsp; It also serves as the underpinnings for very popular Java Integrated Development Environments (IDEs) such as <a href="https://netbeans.org/">NetBeans</a>, <a href="https://www.eclipse.org/">Eclipse</a>, <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/developer-tools/jdev/overview/index.html">JDeveloper</a> and <a href="http://www.jetbrains.com/idea/">IntelliJ</a> to name a few.</p> 
  <p>Like Java, the Java Development Kit is constantly evolving, and Java 8 brings about its fair share of enhancements to the JDK.&nbsp; For Java 8, <tt>javac</tt> can now be instructed (via the <tt>-profile</tt> command-line option) to insure that your source code is compatible with a specific compact profile.&nbsp; Furthermore, the Java 8 JDK comes with a new useful tool called <tt>jdeps</tt>, providing a means to analyze your compiled class and jar files for dependencies.<br /></p> 
  <p><b>The EJDK</b></p> 
  <p>The EJDK is new to Java 8, and although similar in namesake to the JDK, it serves quite a different purpose.&nbsp; Prior to Java 8, supported Java SE-Embedded runtime platforms were provided as binaries by Oracle.&nbsp; With the advent of Compact Profiles, the number of possible binary options per supported platform would simply be too unweildy.&nbsp; Rather than furnishing binaries for each of the possible combinations, an EJDK will be supplied for each supported Java SE-Embedded platform.&nbsp; It contains the tools needed to create the profile you wish to use.</p> 
  <p>The EJDK is designed to be run with either Windows or Linux/Unix platforms alongside a Java runtime environment.&nbsp; It contains a wrapper called <i>jrecreate</i> (<tt>jrecreate.sh</tt> for Unix/Linux and <tt>jrecreate.bat</tt> for Windows) whose function it is to create deployable compact profile instances. In the examples that follow, we'll show two sample invocations.</p> 
  <p>First off, let's briefly take a look at the contents of a typical EJDK. &nbsp; For our first example, we've installed the EJDK on a linux/x86 system. &nbsp; Listing the contents of the <tt>ejdk1.8.0/</tt> directory, we see a subdirectory named <tt>linux_arm_vfp_hflt/</tt>.&nbsp; This tells us what platform this instance of the EJDK supports.&nbsp; For all our examples we'll use an EJDK that creates compact profiles suitable for Linux/Arm Hard Float platform, often times referred to as <i>armhf.</i><br /></p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>$ ls ejdk1.8.0<br />bin&nbsp; doc&nbsp; lib&nbsp; <span style="color: #ff0000;"><b>linux_arm_vfp_hflt</b></span></tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Looking one level deeper into the <tt>bin/</tt> directory, we see the <tt>jrecreate.bat</tt> and <tt>jrecreate.sh</tt> files:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>$ ls ejdk1.8.0/bin<br /><span style="color: #ff0000;"><b>jrecreate.bat</b></span>&nbsp; jrecreate.config.properties&nbsp; <span style="color: #ff0000;"><b>jrecreate.sh</b></span></tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>As we're on a Linux system, let's use the <tt>jrecreate.sh</tt> script to create a compact profile:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>$ ./ejdk1.8.0/bin/jrecreate.sh --profile compact1 --dest compact1-minimal --vm minimal</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Briefly reviewing this invocation, the <tt>--profile compact1</tt> option instructs <tt>jrecreate</tt> to use the Compact1 profile.&nbsp; The <tt>--profile</tt> option accepts [<tt>compact1 | compact2 | compact3</tt>]&nbsp; as an argument. The <tt>--dest compact1-minimal</tt> option specifies the name of the destination directory containing the newly generated profile.&nbsp; Note that the directory argument to <tt>--dest</tt> must not exist prior to invocation.&nbsp; Finally, the <tt>--vm minimal</tt> option tells <tt>jrecreate</tt> to use the minimal (i.e. the smallest) virtual machine for this instance.&nbsp; The <tt>--vm</tt> option accepts&nbsp; <tt>[minimal | client | server | all]</tt> as an argument.&nbsp; Running the complete <tt>jrecreate.sh</tt> command, we get the following output:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>$ ./ejdk1.8.0/bin/jrecreate.sh --profile compact1 --dest compact1-minimal --vm minimal<br />Building JRE using Options {<br /> &nbsp;&nbsp; ejdk-home: /home/java8/ejdk1.8.0<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; dest: /home/java8/compact1-minimal<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; target: linux_arm_vfp_hflt<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; vm: minimal<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; runtime: compact1 profile<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; debug: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; keep-debug-info: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; no-compression: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; dry-run: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; verbose: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; extension: []<br />}<br /><br />Target JRE Size is 10,595 KB (on disk usage may be greater).<br />Embedded JRE created successfully</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>This creates a Compac1 profile distribution of about 10 ½ MB in the <tt>compact-1-minimal/</tt> directory.&nbsp; For our second example, we'll create a profile based on Compact2 and the client VM, this time from a Windows 7/64-bit system:<br /></p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>c:\demo&gt;ejdk1.8.0\bin\jrecreate.bat --profile compact2 --dest compact2-client --vm client<br />Building JRE using Options {<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ejdk-home: c:\demo\ejdk1.8.0\bin\..<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; dest: c:\demo\compact2-client<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; target: linux_arm_vfp_hflt<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; vm: client<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; runtime: compact2 profile<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; debug: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; keep-debug-info: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; no-compression: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; dry-run: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; verbose: false<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; extension: []<br />}<br /><br />Target JRE Size is 17,552 KB (on disk usage may be greater).<br />Embedded JRE created successfully</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>This Compact2 instance is created in the <tt>compact2-client/</tt> directory and has an approximate footprint of 17 ½ MB.&nbsp; Additional options to <tt>jrecreate</tt> are available for further customization. <br /></p> 
  <p>Finally, lets migrate the generated profiles over to a real device.&nbsp; As a host platform we'll use none other than the ubiquitous <a href="http://www.raspberrypi.org/">Raspberry Pi</a>.&nbsp; Here's a listing of the two profiles and their size (in 1K blocks) on the filesystem:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>pi@pi0 ~/java8 $ ls<br />compact1-minimal&nbsp; compact2-client<br /><br />pi@pi0 ~/java8 $ du -sk compact*<br />10616&nbsp;&nbsp; compact1-minimal<br />17660&nbsp;&nbsp; compact2-client</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>And here's what each version outputs when <tt>java -version</tt> is run: <br /></p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>pi@pi0 ~/java8 $ ./compact1-minimal/bin/java -version<br />java version &quot;1.8.0&quot;<br />Java(TM) SE Embedded Runtime Environment (build 1.8.0-b127, <span style="color: #ff0000;"><b>profile compact1</b></span>, headless)<br />Java HotSpot(TM) Embedded <span style="color: #ff0000;"><b>Minimal VM</b></span> (build 25.0-b69, mixed mode)<br /><br />pi@pi0 ~/java8 $ ./compact2-client/bin/java -version<br />java version &quot;1.8.0&quot;<br />Java(TM) SE Embedded Runtime Environment (build 1.8.0-b127, <span style="color: #ff0000;"><b>profile compact2</b></span>, headless)<br />Java HotSpot(TM) Embedded <span style="color: #ff0000;"><b>Client VM</b></span> (build 25.0-b69, mixed mode)</tt><br /></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p> In conclusion, you are encouraged to experiment with the EJDK.&nbsp; It will very quickly give you a feel for the compact profile configuration options available for your device.<br /></p>

<p>Correct, Compact Profiles are a fundamantal part of Java SE-Embedded, however if you're ambitious you can build an OpenJDK version from source.  An additional blog entry: <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/compact_profiles_demonstrated," rel="nofollow">https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/compact_profiles_demonstrated,</a> says the following:</p>


<p> &quot;It's not as intimidating as it sounds, you can pull down the source code for OpenJDK 8, and build it yourself. By default, compact profiles are not built, but this forum post (<a href="http://mail.openjdk.java.net/pipermail/jdk8-dev/2013-February/002062.html)shows" rel="nofollow">http://mail.openjdk.java.net/pipermail/jdk8-dev/2013-February/002062.html)shows</a> you how.  The reference platform for this software is linux/x86.  Functionally, the generated compact profiles will contain the pared down modules for each compact profile, but you'll find the footprint for each to be much larger than the ones demonstrated in this video, as none of the Java SE-Embedded space optimizations are performed by default.&quot;</p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jim Connors
EMAIL: james.connors@oracle.com

DATE: 10/28/2014 20:56:15

<p>Looking back on this entry, it may not be entirely clear that in order to use the eJDK, you must also have a Java Runtime Environment installed on the same system as the eJDK (not the target system).  The jrecreate.sh script actually starts up a Java program to generate Compact Profiles.</p>


<p>First suggestion is to (1) check to see that you have a JRE installed, (2) that the 'java' executable is in your PATH and (3) the JAVA_HOME variable is set accordingly.  For example:</p>


<p>$ echo $PATH<br/>
/home/jimc/jdk1.7.0_55/bin:/usr/local/sbin:/usr/local/bin:/usr/sbin:/usr/bin:/sbin:/bin:/usr/games:/usr/local/games:/home/jimc/ct-ng/bin</p>


<p>$ java -version<br/>
java version &quot;1.7.0_55&quot;<br/>
Java(TM) SE Runtime Environment (build 1.7.0_55-b13)<br/>
Java HotSpot(TM) 64-Bit Server VM (build 24.55-b03, mixed mode)</p>


<p>$ echo $JAVA_HOME<br/>
/home/jimc/jdk1.7.0_55</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Java SE Embedded Pricing Explained
DATE: 12/06/2013 13:58:11
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>You're probably asking yourself, &quot;Pricing?&nbsp; Really?&nbsp; In a techie blog?&quot;, and I would normally agree wholeheartedly with your assessment.&nbsp; But in this one instance the topic might be worthy of a few words.&nbsp; There is, as the expression goes, no such thing as a free lunch.&nbsp; Whether you pay for software outright, or roll your own with open source projects, a cost must be paid.</p> 
  <p>Like clockwork, we regularly receive inquiries for Java embedded information that go something like this:<br /></p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><i>Dear Oracle,&nbsp; We've downloaded and evaluated Java SE-Embedded and have found it to be a very appealing platform to run our embedded application.&nbsp; We understand this is commercial software; before we decide to deploy our solution with your runtime, can you give us a feel for the royalties associated with shipping x number of units?</i></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Seems pretty straightforward, right?&nbsp; Well, yes, except that in the past Oracle required the potential customer to sign a non-disclosure agreement prior to receiving any embedded pricing information.&nbsp; It didn't matter if the customer was interested in deploying ten units or ten thousand, they all had to go through this process.&nbsp; Now certain aspects of pricing may still require confidential agreements, but why not make quantity 1 list prices available?&nbsp;&nbsp; With the release of <a href="http://www.oracle.com/us/corporate/pricing/price-lists/java-embedded-price-list-1977272.pdf">this document</a>, that pricing information is now public.</p> 
  <p>The evidence is out there, both anecdotal and real, demonstrating that 
Oracle's Java SE-Embedded platform is unquestionably superior in quality
 and performance to the OpenJDK variants.&nbsp; For the latest example, <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/comparing_arm_linux_jvms_revisited#javase-e-vs-openjdk">take a look at this blog entry</a>.&nbsp;
 So the question becomes, is it actually more affordable to pay for a 
commercial platform that is fully supported, faster and more reliable or
 to opt for a &quot;free&quot; platform and support it yourself. <br /></p> 
  <p><b>So What Does Java SE-Embedded Cost?</b></p> 
  <p> The universal answer to such a question is: <i>it depends</i>.&nbsp; That is to say it depends upon the capability of the embedded processor.&nbsp; Before we lose you, let's show the list price for Java embedded licensing associated with three platforms and then explain how we arrived at the numbers.&nbsp; As of the posting of this entry, 06 December, 2013, here they are:</p> 
  <ol> 
    <li>Per-unit cost for a <a href="http://www.raspberrypi.org/faqs">Raspberry Pi</a>: US $0.71</li> 
    <li>Per-unit cost for system based on <a href="http://ark.intel.com/products/41175/Intel-Atom-Processor-Z510P-512K-Cache-1_10-GHz-400-MHz-FSB">Intel Atom Z510P</a>: US $2.68<br /></li> 
    <li>Per-unit cost for a Compulab <a href="http://utilite-computer.com/web/trim-slice">Trim-Slice</a>: US $5.36<br /></li> 
  </ol> 
  <p><b>How Does It Work?</b></p> 
  <p>These bullet points help describe the process, then we'll show how we arrived at our three sample platform prices. 
  </p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>Pricing is done on a per-core basis.</li> 
    <li>Processors are classified based on their capability and assigned a core factor.&nbsp; The more capable the processor, the higher the core factor.</li> 
    <li>Per-core pricing is determined by multiplying the standard per-core Java embedded price by the core factor.</li> 
    <li>A 19% Software Update License &amp; Support Fee is automatically added onto each system.</li> 
  </ul> 
  <p>The core factor table that follows, found in the <a href="http://www.oracle.com/us/corporate/pricing/price-lists/java-embedded-price-list-1977272.pdf">Oracle Java Embedded Global Price List, dated September 20, 2013</a>, groups processors of similar capabilities into buckets called chip classes.&nbsp; Each chip class is assigned a core factor.</p>

/></p> 
  <ul> </ul> 
  <p><b>Example 1</b></p> 
  <p>To compute the per-unit cost, use this formula:<br /></p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p>Oracle Java Embedded per-core license fee&nbsp; *&nbsp; core factor&nbsp; *&nbsp; number of cores&nbsp; *&nbsp; support uplift</p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>The standard per-core license fee is always $300.&nbsp; The Raspberry Pi is a Class I device and therefore has a core factor of .002.&nbsp; There is only one core in the Raspberry Pi, and the Software Update License &amp; Support fee is always 19%.&nbsp; So plugging in the numbers, we get:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p>$300&nbsp; *&nbsp; .002&nbsp; *&nbsp; 1&nbsp; *&nbsp; 1.19&nbsp; =&nbsp; $0.714<br /></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p><b>Example 2</b></p> 
  <p>The processor in this example, the Intel Atom Z510P, is a Class II device and has a core factor of .0075.&nbsp; Using the same formula from Example 1, here's what we get:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p>$300&nbsp; *&nbsp; .0075&nbsp; *&nbsp; 1&nbsp; *&nbsp; 1.19&nbsp; =&nbsp; $2.6775 <br /></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p><b>Example 3</b></p> 
  <p>The processor for the Trim-Slice is based on the ARM Cortex-A9, a Class II device.&nbsp; Furthermore it is a dual-core system.&nbsp; Using the same formula as the previous examples, we arrive at the following per-unit pricing:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p>$300&nbsp; *&nbsp; .0075&nbsp; *&nbsp; 2&nbsp; *&nbsp; 1.19&nbsp; = $5.355</p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p><b>Conclusion</b></p> 
  <p>With your hardware specs handy, you should now have enough information to make a reasonable estimate of Oracle Java embedded licensing costs.&nbsp; At minimum, it could be a help in your &quot;buy vs. roll your own&quot; decision making process.&nbsp; And of course, if you have any questions, don't be afraid to ask.<br /></p> 
  <p><br /></p> 
  <blockquote> </blockquote>

<p>Hi Wolfgang,</p>


<p>The support fee is annual.  Provided your're paying the annual support fee, you can update and upgrade your deployed Java Runtime Environments. <br/>
Support is not mandatory but strongly, repeat strongly recommended.</p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jim Connors
EMAIL: james.connors@oracle.com
URL: null
DATE: 05/20/2014 19:53:36

<p>In terms of royalty payments, at this juncture, it would be you, the software vendor.</p>


<p>As far as suitable hardware, for Java SE-Embedded, we just offer guidelines.  If your harware matches the requirements, it should work.  Check out: <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/resources/se-embeddocs/index.html#sysreqs" rel="nofollow">http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/resources/se-embeddocs/index.html#sysreqs</a></p>


<p>There are a much smaller number of supported Java ME platforms.  My recommendation would be to get a Raspberry Pi.  It can run both Java SE-Embedded and Java ME Embedded.  It makes for a real inexpensive development and reference platform. </p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jim Connors

URL: null
DATE: 06/11/2014 20:28:21

<p>Hi Caroline,</p>


<p>There is no mimimum quantity.  That being said, asking for say 10 licenses might not get you a whole lot of attention :)</p>


<p>Java ME and Java SE Embedded both need to be licensed when solutions are deployed with this technology.  Almost certainly, Java ME devices will not be any larger than Chip Class I category.  For Java ME, especially if volumes are high, you should get contact Oracle sales to get a better understanding of how it may be priced.</p>

<p>With respect to development, you are free to use Java SE-Embedded for development purposes.  When you deploy commercially, a redistribution license applies.</p>


<p>There is no minimum quantity.  I believe we are trying to direct these smaller type opportunities to our Partner Business Consultants (PBCs).  If you're looking to speak to someone, let me know.</p>


<p>-- Jim C</p>

<p>Hello Jim, thanks for posting this.</p>


<p>Are your numbers here still relevant today? For instance, Raspberry Pi is now a 1.2GHz 64-bit quad-core ARMv8 CPU. If I understand your numbers correctly, the price of putting Oracle Java on an rPi 3 is $10.71 ($300 * .0075 * 4 * 1.19), an increase of 15x. </p>


<p>When I've seen this kind of thing in the past, it's because the licensing hasn't kept pace with Moore's Law and eventually gets adjusted toward a more competitive unit cost. Forward development of the JVM requires community support and I think myself and others want to be a part of that, but as you know there are also efforts around LLVM such as with Scala Native that will someday provide benefits that cannot be matched in a virtualized environment at any price. So there's a lot to think about.</p>


<p>Any thoughts here? Thanks!</p>
