AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Oracle Java Now Part of Raspberry Pi Raspian Distribution
DATE: 10/14/2013 14:19:13
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>Having sold more than <a href="http://www.raspberrypi.org/archives/5016">1.75 million units in its brief lifespan</a>, there is no denying the profound impact the Raspberry Pi has had on the embedded development community.&nbsp; Amid all the hoopla surrounding the recently completed <a href="http://www.oracle.com/openworld/index.html">Oracle OpenWorld</a> and <a href="http://www.oracle.com/javaone/index.html">Java One</a> events, one announcement that flew under the radar dealt with the fact that the <i>Oracle JDK is now part of the Raspberry Pi Raspian distribution</i>.
</p>
<p>This means that by default, when you download and install the latest Raspian distribution on your Raspberry Pi,&nbsp;the Oracle Java runtime environment and development tools&nbsp;are&nbsp;automatically part of the list of packages installed.&nbsp; Here's a screenshot showing a login session to a Raspberry Pi running the 2013-09-25-wheezy-raspian distribution.&nbsp; The JDK is installed as the <i>oracle-java7-jdk</i> package and is utilizing the Java 7u40 release.
  <br />
</p>
<p><img src="http://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/raspberry-pi/pi-oracle-jdk.jpg" />
</p>
<p>For those who either cannot or do not wish to perform an entire Raspian upgrade, the Oracle JDK is available via the following command:
</p>
<blockquote>
  <pre>$ sudo apt-get update &amp;&amp; sudo apt-get install oracle-java7-jdk</pre>
</blockquote>
<p>Great news.
  <br />
</p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jim Connors
EMAIL: null
URL: null
DATE: 11/08/2013 13:26:38

<p>To avoid any confusion, the wording of this blog has been modified to reflect the fact that the Oracle Java added to Raspian was the Oracle JDK, not Java SE-Embedded as originally stated.  The primary difference here is that the JDK footprint, including development tools and all, is significantly larger than the Java SE-E footprint.  If you would prefer to use Java SE-Embedded, you can download it here: <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/downloads/javase/index.html." rel="nofollow">http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/downloads/javase/index.html.</a></p>

<p>I have some experience with Java, but am new to Raspberry.</p>


<p>Do the Java Security Libraries work fine on Raspberry? In particular, is it possible to generate RSA Keys on Raspberry using Java? If yes, are these Keys known to have any Cryptographic Weaknesses?</p>


-----
COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: guest
EMAIL: james.connors@oracle.com
URL: null
DATE: 11/14/2016 13:14:50

<p>&gt;&gt; Do the Java Security Libraries work fine on Raspberry?</p>


<p>Yes.  This is a Java SE Complaint JDK that contains all of the features and functionality found with Java SE on platforms like Windows, Solaris and Linux</p>


<p>&gt;&gt; In particular, is it possible to generate RSA Keys on Raspberry using Java?<br/>
Yes.  Take a look at the keytool(1) utility that comes with the JDK.</p>


<p>&gt;&gt; If yes, are these Keys known to have any Cryptographic Weaknesses?<br/>
Take a look at <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/java-platform-group/entry/strengthening_signatures_part_2" rel="nofollow">https://blogs.oracle.com/java-platform-group/entry/strengthening_signatures_part_2</a><br/>
Oracle along with other major vendors (like Microsoft) are insisting that signatures generated with MD5 be phased out.</p>

--------
AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Comparing Linux/Arm JVMs Revisited
DATE: 09/17/2013 04:04:28
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>It's been about 18 months since we last compared Linux/Arm JVMs, and with the formal&nbsp;release of the much anticipated <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/downloads/javase/index.html">Java SE Embedded for Arm hard float</a> binary, it marks a good time to revisit JVM performance.&nbsp; The&nbsp;information and results&nbsp;that follow will highlight the following comparisons:
</p> 
  <ol> 
    <li><a href="#armel-vs-armhf">Java SE-E Arm VFP (armel) vs. Arm Hard Float (armhf) </a></li> 
    <li><a href="#c1-vs-c2">Java SE-E armhf Client Compiler (c1) vs. armhf Server Compiler (c2) </a> </li> 
    <li>And last but certainly not least ... <a href="#javase-e-vs-openjdk">Java SE-E 7u40 armhf vs. Open JDK armhf</a> </li> 
  </ol> 
  <p><b>The Benchmark</b></p> 
  <p>For the sake of simplicity and consistency, we'll use a subset of the <a href="http://www.dacapobench.org/">DaCapo benchmark suite</a>.&nbsp; It's an open source group of real world applications that put a good strain on a system both from a processor and memory workload perspective. We are aware of customers who use DaCapo to gauge performance, and due to its availability and ease of use, enables anyone interested to run their own set of tests in fairly short order.<br /></p> 
  <p><b>The Hardware</b></p> 
  <p> It would have been grand to run all these benchmarks on one platform, most notably the beloved <a href="http://www.raspberrypi.org/">Raspberry Pi</a>, but unfortunately it has its limitations:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>There is no Java SE-E server compiler (c2) for the Raspberry Pi.&nbsp; Why?&nbsp; Because the Pi is based on an ARMv6 instruction set whereas the Java SE-E c2 compiler requires a minimum ARMv7 instruction set.</li> 
    <li>Demonstrating how rapidly advances are being made in the hardware arena, the Raspberry Pi, within the context of these tests, is quite a humble platform.&nbsp; With 512MB RAM, it runs out of memory when running some of the large DaCapo component applications.</li> 
  </ul>For these tests we'll primarily use a quad-core Cortex-A9 based system, and for one test we'll utilize a single core Marvell Armada system just to compare what effect the number of cores has on server compiler performance.&nbsp; The devices in question are: 
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  <ol> 
    <li>Boundary Devices BD-SL-i.MX6, quad core 1GHz Cortex-A9 (Freescale i.MX6), 1GB RAM, Debian Wheezy distribution, 3.0.35 kernel (for both armel and armhf configurations)<br /></li> 
    <li>GlobalScale D2Plug, single core 800MHz ARMv6/v7 processor (Marvell PXA510), 1GB RAM, Debian Wheezy distribution, 3.5.3-cubox-di+ kernel for armhf<br /></li> 
  </ol> <a name="armel-vs-armhf"></a> 
  <blockquote> </blockquote> 
  <p><b>Java SE-E <i>armel</i> vs. <i>armhf</i></b></p> 
  <p>The chart that follows compares the relative performance of the <i>armel</i> JavaSE-E 7u40 JRE with the <i>armhf</i> JavaSE-E 7u40 JRE for 8 of the DaCapo component applications.&nbsp; These tests were conducted on the Boundary Devices BD-SL-i.MX6.&nbsp; Both armel and armhf environments were based on the Debian Wheezy distribution running a 3.0.35 kernel.&nbsp; For all charts, the smaller the result, the faster the run.<br /></p> 
  <p><img src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAeMAAAF7CAIAAABuIHRBAAAgAElEQVR4nO3dL3DyXNqA8chHIpFIJJJBITHfLLISlalE1jFrysyKF2ZndqreQTGVOFA7XYesRCCQSCQynzgl5M9JTlrIOTc9128e0ScFkpzC1TQJIYgAALIFURT9/fffrwAAef7++++vUr++vjr+fQEA0FF9ptQAIBelBgDpKDUASEepAUA6Sg0A0lFqAJCOUgOAdJQaAKSj1AAgHaUGAOkoNQBIR6kBQDpKDQDSUWoAkI5SA4B0lBq4yX7eC4IgCILefF/9xkEQBOHawuI9kG+NpG8odYnry8rVayr1ws48hbPf09+qwoOW33EdFtz4xtdUainyD5OZreDX7jf68jgr5QSlLkGpS7gtdUEi42dxcUINT/Tv3bGmUqcfNv0wpjWXpXJf3P/iF45Sl6DUJVy+tK61Ssx7HepK/d3n9Y/veL9H+HqAMAzzj5N78OuPQeQr+PulJtR6lLoEpS7hsNQVWuiu1PED/HBYvsa1N9+vc6XWvlpv/91So8p9uXXYfj1KXYJSlygodX4/b2azV/NyzD0JSx9EdKl1o6LdXCx64V02qNfXRbneQj9+lSJnGNL4++E6t2iJb+kP+iUnZlYn9VjJHTeJ22n35ySXrurTpmynUTWORkk/EOkbUeoSlLqEpkmFR+PyBS6OlvFBjMfboshZqbX3/kapky/3fKkLXqzG13CFIY1v0+vleqL7VnwT3bRKR3aLAhuUjNV3nzbf2j53OEpFg2D8ewoKpS5RVGrt67Qs1ZkbmR8k0j2vqz3xza/dH98xdWdtfo2ljl/salKu1AW/RaqV2jCkuk5lGlQwKV4v3dED3YZk4VGGwoE2P230f398v9SOR6nkzydKXYJSl6i0nzoXqfy9zM9A/cEm3YvmegMXpS4akaqlvsz6crO7lbpkUbV/D2XWtXzveOLGZWmr/LtXO9DGp02+1Hdga5TKnyBfEyl1CUpdoqhL+tRpnutqkv4JaHgQ3VKkH8W4EyM/B/XoP9/7od1UTC1jaakzG9TRvUtd9eeSfxABpTY+bTLPgx8flnQySpXO9aTUJSh1ie/sp9b/PWzYGVv2IIXLUjm49y71dRFyd6xU6uwGdenQ3Gs/9eOU2vi00azlj3+ClkepbF9+wDZ1FZS6RL7UmsjpIpW6Y5XtRv3ej4zMa8L2EcWSUFcqteHVGqRPN/jeuR+VhlR6qQ1PG83amp4xYkapWoMpdQlKXSKXjdIqF6Ql9+aO6g9SsDSZTVRLpS7c86FbttS075S6eg20sy8dUvGlLn3aFN20fFewlFGq9Jyj1CUodYmSd8vlNrJN+7J1B9eLHyR3kF6zRWu11InMakOQ63jBicUFd9MGvvgQbfHSlf1cHqDUpqdNybkUpp+QlFHSPK0596MCSp2X27+rO9MqL/P6KHmPgvlBirc/NSc5GRfFtH7mO5bt+cjeomyZixal4JdSxUepuE6PUOrip03BqOiOoBQ+vsNRKnuGUOoKKHVe+vmce9qnn3Hh+vL/7A1LtyrND5J/Ymce5CfHJX94x+uqVPrj9fJIlwnfLLVmGau8dI1D+hClLnvaZH9wFc6UFDNK+jUo+BVNqfMoNQBIR6kBQDpKDQDSUWoAkI5SA4B0lBoApKPUACAdpQYA6Sg1AEhHqQFAOkoNANJRagCQjlIDgHSUGgCko9QAIB2lBgDpKDUASEepAUA6Sg0A0lFqAJCOUgOAdJQaAKSj1AAgHaUGAOkoNQBIR6kBQDpKDQDSUWoAkE5EqffzXqD05vt46joM8lPrmAgAskko9ToM11EUqWJ/FVT7ZR0TAUA8CaWOrcNkSb/yfZ1ex0QAkE9EqeO9H3FI9/Neep/FV2rvPlFruVy+XvzrX/96BeCT5XJ5x77dxauEUl/s572vWrstdZKAYQHgO1GlvvYzt6ciXNcz0UjEsADwm /></p> 
  <p>In all 8 tests, the armhf binary is faster, some only slightly, and in one case (eclipse) a few percentage points faster.&nbsp; The big performance gain associated with the armhf standard deals with floating point operations, and in particular, the passing of arguments directly into floating point registers.&nbsp; The performance gains realized by the newer armhf 
standard will be seen more in the native application realm than 
for Java SE-Embedded primarily because&nbsp; the Java SE-E armel VM already uses FP registers for Java floating point methods.&nbsp; There are still however certain floating point workloads that may show a modest performance increase (in the single digit percent range) with JavaSE-E armhf over Java SE-E armel.</p> <a name="c1-vs-c2"></a> 
  <p><b>Java SE-E Client Compiler (c1) vs. Server Compiler (c2)</b></p> 
  <p>In this section, we'll show tests results for two different platforms, 
the first a single core system, followed by the same tests on a 
quad-core system.&nbsp; To further demonstrate how workload changes 
performance, we'll take advantage of the ability to run the DaCapo component applications
 in three different modes: small, default (medium) and large.&nbsp; The first
 chart displays the aggregate time required to run the tests for the 
three modes, utilizing both the 7u40 client (c1) compiler and the server
 (c2) compiler.&nbsp; As expected, c1 outperforms c2 by a wide margin for the tests that run only briefly.&nbsp; As 
the total time to run the tests increases from small to large, the c2 
compiler gets a chance to &quot;warm up&quot; and close the gap in performance.&nbsp; 
But it never does catch up.&nbsp;&nbsp; <br /></p>

/></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>Contrast the first chart with the one that follows where small, medium and large versions of the tests were run on a quad core system.&nbsp; The c2 compiler is better able to utilize the additional compute resources supplied by this platform, the result being that initial gap in performance between c1 and c2 for the small version of the test is only 19%.&nbsp; By the time we reach the large version, c2 outperforms c1 by 7%.&nbsp; The moral of the story here is, given enough resources, the server compiler might be the better of the VMs for your workload if it is a long-lived process.<br /></p>

/> </p> <a name="javase-e-vs-openjdk"></a> 
  <p><b>Java SE-E 7u40 <i>armhf</i> vs. Open JDK <i>armhf </i></b></p> 
  <p>For this final section, we'll break out performance on an application-by-application basis for the following JRE/VMs:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li> Java SE Embedded 7u40 armhf Client Compiler (c1)</li> 
    <li>Java SE Embedded 7u40 armhf Server Compiler (c2)</li> 
    <li>OpenJDK 7 IcedTea 2.3.10 7u25-2.3.10-1~deb7u1 OpenJDK Zero VM (build 22.0-b10, mixed mode)<br /></li> 
    <li>OpenJDK 7 IcedTea 2.3.10 7u25-2.3.10-1~deb7u1 JamVM (build 1.6.0-devel, inline-threaded interpreter with stack-caching)<br /></li> 
    <li>OpenJDK 6 IcedTea6 1.12.6 6b27-1.12.6-1~deb7u1 OpenJDK Zero VM (build 20.0-b12, mixed mode)</li> 
    <li>OpenJDK 6 IcedTea6 1.12.6 6b27-1.12.6-1~deb7u1 JamVM (build 1.6.0-devel, inline-threaded interpreter with stack-caching)</li> 
    <li>OpenJDK IcedTea6 1.12.6 6b27-1.12.6-1~deb7u1 CACAO (build 1.6.0+r68fe50ac34ec, compiled mode)</li> 
  </ul> 
  <p>The OpenJDK packages were pulled from the Debian Wheezy distribution.</p> 
  <p>It appears the bulk of performance work to OpenJDK/Arm still revolves around the OpenJDK 6 platform even though Java 7 was released over two years ago (and Java 8 is coming soon).&nbsp; Regardless, Java SE still outperforms most OpenJDK tests by a wide margin, and perhaps more importantly appears to be the much more reliable platform considering the number of tests that failed with the OpenJDK variants.&nbsp; As <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/part_deux_comparing_jvms_on">demonstrated in previous benchmark results</a>, the older armel OpenJDK VMs appear to be more stable than the armhf versions tested here.&nbsp; Considering the stated direction by the major linux distributions is to migrate towards the armhf binary standard, this is a bit eye opening.<br /> </p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Compact Profiles Demonstrated
DATE: 08/12/2013 12:22:30
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p> Following up on an <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/a_first_look_at_compact">article introducing compact profiles</a>, the video that follows demonstrates how this new feature in the upcoming Java 8 release can be utilized.&nbsp; The video:</p> 
  <ul> </ul> 
  <ol> </ol> 
  <ul> 
    <li>Describes the compact profile feature and the rationale for its creation.<br /></li> 
    <li>Shows how to use the new <tt>jrecreate</tt> utility to generate compact profiles that can be readily deployed.<br /></li> 
    <li>Demonstrates that even the smallest of profiles (less than 11MB) is robust enough to support very popular and important software frameworks like OSGi.</li> 
  </ul> 
  <ol> </ol> 
  <ul> </ul><br /> 
  <iframe height="315" frameborder="0" width="420" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/TCaBno_Euqk"></iframe> 
  <p> The software demonstrated is in early access.&nbsp; For those interested in trying it out before the formal release of Java 8, there are two options:</p> 
  <ol> </ol> 
  <ul> </ul> 
  <ol> 
    <li>Members of the Oracle Partner Network (OPN) with a gold membership or higher can download the early access Java 8 binaries of Java SE-Embedded shown here.&nbsp; For those not at this level, it may still be possible to get early access software, but it will require a qualification process beforehand.</li> 
    <li>It's not as intimidating as it sounds, you can pull down the source code for OpenJDK 8, and <a href="http://hg.openjdk.java.net/jdk8/jdk8/raw-file/tip/README-builds.html">build it yourself</a>.&nbsp; By default, compact profiles are not built, but <a href="http://mail.openjdk.java.net/pipermail/jdk8-dev/2013-February/002062.html">this forum post shows you how</a>.&nbsp; The reference platform for this software is linux/x86.&nbsp; Functionally, the generated compact profiles will contain the pared down modules for each compact profile, but you'll find the footprint for each to be much larger than the ones demonstrated in this video, as none of the Java SE-Embedded space optimizations are performed by default.</li> 
  </ol> 
  <ul> </ul> 
  <ol> </ol> 
  <p>Not having any premium privileges on YouTube, the maximum allowed length of a video is 15 minutes.&nbsp; There's actually lots more to talk about with compact profiles, including enhancements to java tools and utilities (<tt>javac</tt>, <tt>jar</tt>, <tt>jdeps</tt>, and the <tt>java</tt> command itself) that have incorporated intelligence for dealing with profiles.</p> 
  <p>Hmm.&nbsp; Maybe there's an opportunity for a <i>Compact Profiles Demonstrated Part II</i>?<br /></p> 
  <p><br /></p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: guest
EMAIL: james.connors@oracle.com
URL: null
DATE: 08/14/2013 13:14:15

<p>Hi Alexis,</p>


<p>To your questions:</p>


<p>1. In terms of documentation, the best place to look currently is the JEP (Java Enhancement Proposal for Compact Profiles <a href="http://openjdk.java.net/jeps/161)." rel="nofollow">http://openjdk.java.net/jeps/161).</a>  If you have access (OPN Gold) to the early access builds, there is some additional documentation in the bundle.</p>


<p>2. For Java 8 we are providing 3 defined subsets of the JRE and providing a tool called jrecreate that will enable you to do some customizations on the defined profiles themselves.  For example, you can choose the type of VM (minimal, client, server) the should be associated with the created profile.  The ability to create your own custom runtime based on the profile of your application is a feature we will be providing in Java 9.</p>


<p>3. Compact Profiles bring with it a series of enhancements to Java development tools.  In particular, &quot;javac -profile&quot; will check to see if your source code references an api not in the specified profile.  If that is the case, javac will not compile the source.   </p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: An Introduction to Java 8 Compact Profiles
DATE: 07/31/2013 13:00:37
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p> Java SE is a very impressive platform indeed, but with all that functionality comes a large and ever increasing footprint.&nbsp; It stands to reason then that one of the more frequent requests from the community has been the desire to deploy only those components required for a particular application instead of the entire Java SE runtime environment.&nbsp; Referred to as subsetting, the benefits of such a concept would seem to be many:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>A smaller Java environment would require less compute resources, thus opening up a new domain of devices previously thought to be too humble for Java.</li> 
    <li>A smaller runtime environment could be better optimized for performance and start up time.</li> 
    <li>Elimination of unused code is always a good idea from a security perspective.</li> 
    <li>If the environment could be pared down significantly, there may be tremendous benefit to bundling runtimes with each individual Java application.</li> 
    <li>These bundled applications could be downloaded more quickly.<br /></li> 
  </ul> 
  <p>Despite these perceived advantages, the platform stewards (Sun, then Oracle) have been steadfast in their resistance to subsetting.&nbsp; The rationale for such a stance is quite simple: there was sincere concern that the Java SE platform would fragment.&nbsp; Agree or disagree, the Java SE standard has remained remarkably in tact over time.&nbsp; If you need any further evidence of this assertion, compare the state of Java SE to that of Java ME, particularly in the mobile telephony arena.&nbsp; Better still, look how quickly Android has spawned countless variants in its brief lifespan.</p> 
  <p>Nonetheless, a formal effort has been underway having the stated goal of providing a much more modular Java platform.&nbsp; Called <a href="http://openjdk.java.net/projects/jigsaw/">Project Jigsaw</a>, when complete, Java SE will be composed of a set of finer-grained modules and will include tools to enable developers to identify and isolate only those modules needed for their application.&nbsp; However, implementing this massive internal change and yet maintaining compatibility has proven to be a considerable challenge.&nbsp; Consequently <a href="http://mreinhold.org/blog/late-for-the-train">full implementation of the modular Java platform has been delayed until Java 9</a>.</p> 
  <p>Understanding that Java 9 is quite a ways off, an interim solution will be available for Java 8, called <b><a href="http://openjdk.java.net/jeps/161">Compact Profiles</a></b>.&nbsp; Rather than specifying a complete module system, Java 8 will define subset profiles of the Java SE platform specification that developers can use to deploy.&nbsp; At the current time three compact profiles have been defined, and have been assigned the creative names <i>compact1</i>, <i>compact2</i>, and <i>compact3</i>. The table that follows lists the packages that comprise each of the profiles.&nbsp; Each successive profile is a superset of its predecessor.&nbsp; That is to say, the compact2 profile contains all of the packages in compact1 plus those listed under the compact2 column below.&nbsp; Likewise, compact3 contains all of compact2 packages plus the ones listed in the compact3 column.<br /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <pre><code>compact1                     compact2                    compact3
--------------------------   -----------------------     --------------------------
java.io                      java.rmi                    java.lang.instrument
java.lang                    java.rmi.activation         java.lang.management
java.lang.annotation         java.rmi.registry           java.security.acl
java.lang.invoke             java.rmi.server             java.util.prefs
java.lang.ref                java.sql                    javax.annotation.processing
java.lang.reflect            javax.rmi.ssl               javax.lang.model
java.math                    javax.sql                   javax.lang.model.element
java.net                     javax.transaction           javax.lang.model.type
java.nio                     javax.transaction.xa        javax.lang.model.util
java.nio.channels            javax.xml                   javax.management
java.nio.channels.spi        javax.xml.datatype          javax.management.loading
java.nio.charset             javax.xml.namespace         javax.management.modelbean
java.nio.charset.spi         javax.xml.parsers           javax.management.monitor
java.nio.file                javax.xml.stream            javax.management.openmbean
java.nio.file.attribute      javax.xml.stream.events     javax.management.relation
java.nio.file.spi            javax.xml.stream.util       javax.management.remote
java.security                javax.xml.transform         javax.management.remote.rmi
java.security.cert           javax.xml.transform.dom     javax.management.timer
java.security.interfaces     javax.xml.transform.sax     javax.naming
java.security.spec           javax.xml.transform.stax    javax.naming.directory
java.text                    javax.xml.transform.stream  javax.naming.event
java.text.spi                javax.xml.validation        javax.naming.ldap
java.util                    javax.xml.xpath             javax.naming.spi
java.util.concurrent         org.w3c.dom                 javax.script
java.util.concurrent.atomic  org.w3c.dom.bootstrap       javax.security.auth.kerberos
java.util.concurrent.locks   org.w3c.dom.events          javax.security.sasl
java.util.jar                org.w3c.dom.ls              javax.sql.rowset
java.util.logging            org.xml.sax                 javax.sql.rowset.serial
java.util.regex              org.xml.sax.ext             javax.sql.rowset.spi
java.util.spi                org.xml.sax.helpers         javax.tools
java.util.zip                                            javax.xml.crypto
javax.crypto                                             javax.xml.crypto.dom
javax.crypto.interfaces                                  javax.xml.crypto.dsig
javax.crypto.spec                                        javax.xml.crypto.dsig.dom
javax.net                                                javax.xml.crypto.dsig.keyinfo
javax.net.ssl                                            javax.xml.crypto.dsig.spec
javax.security.auth                                      org.ieft.jgss
javax.security.auth.callback
javax.security.auth.login
javax.security.auth.spi
javax.security.auth.x500
javax.security.cert</code></pre> 
  <p>You may ask what savings can be realized by using compact profiles?&nbsp; As Java 8 is in pre-release stage, numbers will change over time, but let's take a look at a snapshot early access build of Java SE-Embedded 8 for ARMv5/Linux.&nbsp; <i>A reasonably configured compact1 profile comes in at less than 14MB</i>.&nbsp; Compact2 is about 18MB and compact3 is in the neighborhood of 21MB.&nbsp; For reference, the latest Java 7u21 SE Embedded ARMv5/Linux environment requires 45MB.</p> 
  <p>So at less than one-third the original size of the already space-optimized Java SE-Embedded release, you have a very capable runtime environment.&nbsp; If you need the additional functionality provided by the compact2 and compact3 profiles or even the full VM, you have the option of deploying your application with them instead.</p> 
  <p>In the next installment, we'll look at Compact Profiles in a bit more detail.<br /></p> 
  <p><br /></p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: guest
EMAIL: james.connors@oracle.com
URL: null
DATE: 09/18/2013 12:34:41

<p>The custom capability you ask for will be part of Java 9.  Compact Profiles are an interim solution which subset the JRE into a small number of options, each with well known functionality.  As Java 9 won't be out for quite a bit, compact profiles aren't going away any time soon.  In fact we've modified many Java SE tools to compact profile aware, including javac, jar and the java command.</p>


<p>If you want to see a video demonstrating compact profiles, check out the blog entry that follows this one:</p>


<p><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/compact_profiles_demonstrated" rel="nofollow">https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/compact_profiles_demonstrated</a></p>

Functionally, the generated compact profiles will contain the pared down modules for each compact profile, but you'll find the footprint for each to be much larger than the ones demonstrated in this video, as none of the Java SE-Embedded space optimizations are performed by default.&quot;</p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jim Connors
EMAIL: james.connors@oracle.com
URL: null
DATE: 08/15/2014 19:42:28

<p>In general, our experience is that customers have had good success moving applications from CDC to Java SE Embedded with Compact Profiles.  With compact1, migration from CDC/FP to Java 8 SE was one of the main factors determining which APIs were and were not not included.</p>


<p>That being said we cannot guarantee all CDC/FP applicaitions will run on Java SE Embedded compact1 implementations.  There have been numerous bug fixes and improvements (most notably security fixes) to the APIs as compared to the very dated CDC/FP APIs that might impact application compatibility.</p>

--------
AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Is it armhf or armel?
DATE: 03/17/2013 01:53:58
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
Arm processors come in all makes and sizes, a certain percentage of 
which address a market where cost, footprint and power requirements are 
at a premium.&nbsp; In this space, the inclusion of even a floating point 
unit would be considered an unnecessary luxury.&nbsp; To perform floating 
point operations with these processors, software emulation is required.</p> 
  <p>Higher-end Arm processors come bundled with additional capability 
that enables hardware execution of floating point operations.&nbsp; The 
difference between these two architectures gave rise to two separate Embedded Application Binary Interfaces or EABIs for ARM: <i>soft float</i> and <i>VFP (Vector Floating Point)</i>.&nbsp; Although there is forward compatibility between soft and hard 
float, there is no backward compatibility.&nbsp; And in fact, when it comes 
to providing binaries for <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/downloads/javase/index.html">Java SE Embedded for Arm</a>,
 Oracle provides two separate options: a soft float binary and a VFP binary.&nbsp; In the Linux community, 
releases built upon both these EABIs are refereed to as <i>armel</i> based distributions.<br /></p> 
  <p> 
  Enter <i>armhf</i>.&nbsp; Although a big step up in performance, the VFP EABI utilizes 
less-than-optimal argument passing when a floating point operations take
 place.&nbsp; In this scenario, floating point arguments must first be 
passed through integer registers prior to executing in the floating 
point unit.&nbsp; A new EABI, referred to as <i>armhf</i> optimizes the calling convention for floating point operations by passing arguments directly into floating point registers.&nbsp; It furthermore includes a more efficient system call convention.&nbsp; The end result is applications compiled with the <i>armhf</i> standard should demonstrate modest performance improvement in some cases, and significant improvement for floating point intensive applications.</p> 
  <p>Alas, <i>armhf</i> represents yet another binary incompatible standard, but one that has already gained considerable traction in the community. Although still relatively early, the transition from <i>armel</i> to <i>armhf</i> is underway.&nbsp; In fact, <a href="https://lists.ubuntu.com/archives/ubuntu-devel/2012-November/036106.html">Ubuntu has already announced that future releases will only be built to the <i>armhf</i> standard</a>, effectively obsoleting <i>armel</i>. As mentioned in <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/henrik/entry/oracle_releases_jdk_for_linux">Henrik's Stahl's Blog</a>, an <i>armhf</i> version of Java SE Embedded is in the works, and we have already made available a <a href="http://jdk8.java.net/fxarmpreview/javafx-arm-developer-preview.html"><i>armhf</i>-based developer Preview of JDK 8 with JavaFX</a>.</p> 
  <p>In the interim, we will have to deal with the incompatibilities between <i>armel</i> and <i>armhf</i>.&nbsp; Most recently we've seen a rash of failed attempts to run the ArmV7 VFP Java SE Embedded binary on top of an <i>armhf</i>-based Linux distro.&nbsp; During diagnosis, the question becomes, how can I determine whether my Linux distribution is based on <i>armel</i> or <i>armhf</i>?&nbsp; Turns out this is not as straightforward as one might think.&nbsp; Aside from experience and anecdotal evidence, one possible way to ascertain whether you're running on <i>armel</i> or <i>armhf</i> is to run the following obscure command: 
  </p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>$ readelf -A /proc/self/exe | grep Tag_ABI_VFP_args</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>If the <tt>Tag_ABI_VFP_args</tt> tag is found, then you're running on an <i>armhf</i> system.&nbsp; If
      nothing is returned, then it's <i>armel</i>.&nbsp; To show you an example, here's what happens on a Raspberry Pi running the <a href="http://www.raspbian.org/">Raspbian</a> distribution:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>pi@raspberrypi:~$ readelf -A /proc/self/exe | grep
        Tag_ABI_VFP_args<br />
        &nbsp; Tag_ABI_VFP_args: VFP registers</tt></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>This indicates an <i>armhf</i> distro, which in fact is what Raspbian is.&nbsp; On the original, <a href="http://www.raspberrypi.org/downloads">soft-float Debian Wheezy</a> distribution, here's what happens:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><tt>pi@raspberrypi:~$ readelf -A /proc/self/exe | grep
        Tag_ABI_VFP_args</tt> <br /></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Nothing returned indicates that this is indeed <i>armel</i>.</p> 
  <p>Many thanks to the folks participating in <a href="http://www.raspberrypi.org/phpBB3/viewtopic.php?f=33&amp;t=20873">this Raspberry Pi forum</a> topic for providing this suggestion.<br /></p> 
  <p><br /></p> 
  <p><br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Source Code for JavaFX Scoreboard Now Available
DATE: 02/07/2013 04:23:17
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>For the last few years, many of the JavaFX related blog posts found here have made reference to all or parts of a Scoreboard application written in JavaFX.&nbsp; For example, <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/a_raspberry_pi_javafx_electronic">the entry prior to this</a> contains a video demonstrating how this Scoreboard application can be run on an embedded device such as a <a href="http://www.raspberrypi.org/">Raspberry Pi</a> and displayed on an ordinary flat screen TV.</p> 
  <p>Alongside those posts, some have asked for access to the source code to this application.&nbsp; I've always felt uncomfortable releasing the code, not under the delusion that it has any commercial value, but rather because, let us say, it was not developed with the strictest software engineering standards in mind.&nbsp; Having gotten over those insecurities (not really), I've decided to place it in a <a href="http://home.java.net/">java.net</a> project called <a href="http://java.net/projects/javafx-scoreboard">javafx-scoreboard</a>.</p> 
  <p>If you are interested in gaining access to this code you'll need to do the following:</p> 
  <ol> 
    <li>Register on <a href="https://java.net/projects">http://home.java.net/projects</a>&nbsp; to get a java.net account.&nbsp; It's free and painless.&nbsp; If you already have an account, terrific! You can skip this step.</li> 
    <li>Send an email to me at <a href="mailto:james.connors@oracle.com">james.connors@oracle.com</a>&nbsp; asking to be added to the <a href="http://java.net/projects/javafx-scoreboard">javafx-scoreboard</a> project.&nbsp; Include your java.net username in the email.</li> 
    <li>Once you're added to the project, you'll have download access to the contents of the project.</li> 
  </ol> 
  <p>For grins, here's document that gives an <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/javafx-scoreboard/README-v1.1.html">overview of the Scoreboard application</a>.</p> 
  <p> Cheers.<br /><br /> </p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: A Raspberry Pi / JavaFX Electronic Scoreboard Application
DATE: 12/23/2012 04:41:01
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
As evidenced at the recently completed <a href="http://www.oracle.com/javaone/index.html">JavaOne</a> 2012 conference, community excitement towards the <a href="http://www.raspberrypi.org/">Raspberry Pi</a> and its potential as a Java development and deployment platform was readily palpable.&nbsp; Fast forward three months, Oracle has announced the availability of a <a href="http://jdk8.java.net/fxarmpreview/javafx-arm-developer-preview.html">JDK 8 (with JavaFX) for Arm Early Access Developer Preview</a> where the reference platform for this release is none other than the Raspberry Pi.</p> 
  <p>What makes this especially interesting to me is the addition of JavaFX to the Java SE-Embedded 8 platform.&nbsp; It turns out that at $35US, the (not so) humble Raspberry Pi has a very capable graphics processor, opening up a Pandora's box of graphics applications that could be applied to this beloved device.&nbsp; As a first step in becoming familiar with just how this works, I decided to dust off a two year old JavaFX scoreboard application, originally written for a Windows laptop, and see how it would run on the Pi.&nbsp; Low and behold, the application runs unmodified (without even a recompile).</p> 
  <p>The video that follows shows how an ordinary flat screen TV can be converted into a full screen electronic scoreboard driven by a Raspberry Pi.&nbsp; The requirements for such a solution are incredibly straightforward: (1) the TV needs access to a power receptacle and (2) it must be within range of a WiFi network in order to receive scoreboard update packets.&nbsp; The device is so compact and miserly from a power perspective, that we velcro the Pi to the back of the TV and get our power from the TV's USB port. If you can spare a few moments, it just might be worth your while to take a look.<br /></p> 
  <p><br /> </p> 
  <iframe height="315" frameborder="0" width="420" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/GZAhOx-YYL0"></iframe> 
  <p><br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Sprinkle Some Magik on that Java Virtual Machine
DATE: 11/05/2012 18:17:50
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
GE Energy, through its Smallworld subsidiary, has been providing geospatial software solutions to the utility and telco markets for over 20 years.&nbsp; One of the fundamental building blocks of their technology is a dynamically-typed object oriented programming language called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magik_%28programming_language%29">Magik</a>.&nbsp; Like Java, Magik source code is compiled down to bytecodes that run on a virtual machine -- in this case the Magik Virtual Machine.</p> 
  <p>Throughout the years, GE has invested considerable engineering talent in
 the support and maintenance of this virtual machine.&nbsp; At the same time 
vast energy and resources have been invested in the Java Virtual 
Machine. The question for GE has been whether to continue to make that 
investment on its own or to leverage massive effort provided by the Java 
community? Utilizing the Java Virtual Machine instead of maintaining its
 own virtual machine would give GE more opportunity to focus on 
application solutions.&nbsp;&nbsp; <br /></p> 
  <p>At last count, there are dozens, perhaps hundreds of examples of programming languages that have been hosted atop the Java Virtual Machine.&nbsp; Prior to the release of Java 7, that effort, although certainly possible, was generally less than optimal for languages like Magik because of its dynamic nature.&nbsp; Java, as a statically typed language had little use for this capability.&nbsp; In the quest to be a more universal virtual machine, Java 7, via <a href="http://www.jcp.org/en/jsr/detail?id=292">JSR-292</a>, introduced a new bytecode called <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">invokedynamic</font>.&nbsp; In short, <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">invokedynamic</font> affords a more flexible method call mechanism needed by dynamic languages like Magik.</p> 
  <p>With this new capability GE Energy has succeeded in hosting their Magik environment on top of the Java Virtual Machine.&nbsp; So you may ask, why would GE wish to do such a thing?&nbsp; The benefits are many:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>Competitors to GE Energy claimed that the Magik environment was proprietary.&nbsp; By utilizing the Java Virtual Machine, that argument gets put to bed.&nbsp; JVM development is done in <a href="http://openjdk.java.net/">open source</a>, where contributions are made world-wide by all types of organizations and individuals.<br /></li> 
    <li>The unprecedented wealth of class libraries and applications written for
 the Java platform are now opened up to Magik/JVM platform as first
 class citizens.</li> 
    <li>In addition, the Magik/JVM solution vastly increases the developer pool to include the 9 million Java developers -- the largest developer community on the planet.<br /></li> 
    <li>Applications running on the JVM showed substantial performance gains, in some cases as much as a 5x speed up over the original Magik platform.<br /></li> 
    <li>Legacy Magik applications can still run on the original platform.&nbsp; They can be seamlessly migrated to run on the JVM by simply recompiling the source code.<br /></li> 
    <li>GE can now leverage the huge Java community.&nbsp; Undeniably the best virtual machine ever created, hundreds if not thousands of world class developers continually improve, poke, prod and scrutinize all aspects of the Java platform.&nbsp; As enhancements are made, GE automatically gains access to these.<br /></li> 
    <li>As Magik has little in the way of support for multi-threading, GE will benefit from current and future Java offerings (e.g. <a href="http://openjdk.java.net/projects/lambda/">lambda expressions</a>) that aim to further facilitate multi-core/multi-threaded application development.<br /></li> 
    <li>As the JVM is available for many more platforms, it broadens the reach of Magik, including the potential to run on a class devices never envisioned just a few short years ago.&nbsp; For example, <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/downloads/javase/index.html">Java SE compatible runtime environments are available for popular embedded ARM/Intel/PowerPC configurations</a> that could theoretically host this software too.<br /></li> 
  </ul>As compared to other JVM language projects, the Magik integration differs in that it represents a serious commercial entity betting a sizable part of its business on the success of this effort.&nbsp; Expect to see announcements not only from General Electric, but other organizations as they realize the benefits of utilizing the Java Virtual Machine.<br />
-----

<p>Hi,</p>


<p>The compiler does not convert Magik to Java. It compiles Magik to bytecode. The performance benefits you suggest are available though. The biggest challenge in achieving high performance form a dynamic dispatch language like Magik on the JVM is to ensure that call sites do not become mega morphic and then when they do the dispatch code is as fast as possible.</p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: guest
EMAIL: null
URL: null
DATE: 01/21/2013 17:40:14

<p>I've been using Magik for over 20 years, never had to complain about it. Great language. Shame it wasnt made public earlier, as it would probably have caught like Java, Python or Ruby did.<br/>
It is indeed closer to Python and Ruby than it is to Java. Now it is strange to me hearing that Magik is interpreted. Magik is  compiled into pseudo-code, just like Java is, and also works on a (Magik) virtual machine.<br/>
The interactive Magik terminal compiles (just in time) and executes code. Code then resides in memory as VM code, not as original Magik language. Hence the distinction with an interpreted language. <br/>
Now yes, the port to JVM will allow simpler integration of existing Java capabilities into existing Magik apps. <br/>
But Magik will definitely stay an interesting language for those who know it.<br/>
The downside is for old Magik programmers who will no more have an edge with this special rare skill. But I suppose that's the way it goes :)</p>

AUTHOR: guest

<p>I too have been a Magik developer for over 20 years and I am delighted by this development as it will open the way for Smallworld to be deployed on as many platforms as supported by the JVM. But I am always taken aback by references to Magik programmers having an edge with special rare skills. A programming language is just a set of instructions and once you know one well, learning another is relatively straight forward. The actual rare and special skill is the knowledge of Smallworld libraries and specific domain knowledge be that Telco, Utility, Landbase, etc. So don't expect a sudden rush of great Smallworld programers coming from the Java community. They will come but they will have to do their internship like the rest of us.</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Raspberry Pi and Java SE:  A Platform for the Masses 
DATE: 10/09/2012 13:36:58
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Personal
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
One of the more exciting developments in the embedded systems world has been the announcement and availability of the <a href="http://www.raspberrypi.org/">Raspberry Pi</a>, a very capable computer that is no bigger than a credit card.&nbsp; At $35 US, initial demand for the device was so significant, that very long back orders quickly ensued. After months of patiently waiting, mine finally arrived.&nbsp; </p> 
  <p><img width="604" height="403" src="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/raspberry-pi/raspberry-pi.jpg" /></p> 
  <p>Those initial growing pains appear to have been fixed, so availability now should be much more reasonable. At a very high level, here are some of the important specs:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>Broadcom BCM2835 System on a chip (SoC)</li> 
    <li>ARM1176JZFS, with 
floating point, running at 700MHz</li> 
    <li>Videocore 4 GPU capable of BluRay quality playback</li> 
    <li>256Mb RAM</li> 
    <li>2 USB ports and Ethernet</li> 
    <li>Boots from SD card</li> 
    <li>Linux distributions (e.g. Debian) available</li> 
  </ul> 
  <p> So what's taking place taking place with respect to the Java platform and Raspberry Pi?</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>A Java SE Embedded binary suitable for the Raspberry Pi is available for download (Arm v6/7) <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/downloads/javase/index.html">here</a>.&nbsp; Note, this is based on the <i>armel</i> architecture, a variety of Arm designed to support floating point through a compatibility library that operates on more platforms, but can hamper performance.&nbsp; In order to use this Java SE binary, select the available <a href="http://downloads.raspberrypi.org/images/debian/7/2012-08-08-wheezy-armel/2012-08-08-wheezy-armel.zip">Debian distribution for your Raspberry Pi</a>.</li> 
    <li>The more recent <a href="http://www.raspbian.org/">Raspbian distribution</a> is based on the <i>armhf</i> (hard float) architecture, which provides for more efficient hardware-based floating point operations.&nbsp; However armhf is not binary compatible with armel.&nbsp; As of the writing of this blog, Java SE Embedded binaries are not yet publicly available for the armhf-based Raspbian distro, but as mentioned in <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/henrik/entry/oracle_releases_jdk_for_linux">Henrik Stahl's blog</a>, an armhf release is in the works.</li> 
    <li>As demonstrated at the just-completed <a href="http://www.oracle.com/javaone/index.html">JavaOne 2012 San Francisco</a> event, the graphics processing unit inside the Raspberry Pi is very capable indeed, and makes for an excellent candidate for JavaFX.&nbsp; As such, plans also call for a Pi-optimized version of JavaFX in a future release too.</li> 
  </ul> 
  <ol> </ol>A thriving community around the Raspberry Pi has developed at light speed, and as evidenced by the packed attendance at Pi-specific sessions at Java One 2012, the interest in Java for this platform is following suit. So stay tuned for more developments...<br /> 
  <p><br /> </p> 
  <p> </p>

<p>From a Java perspective, my (not so) educated guess is that performance might improve slightly for a majority of cases.  Perhaps we'll see some performance gain from the fact that (1) the JVM will be compiled with a newer and better optimizing compiler and (2) more platform specific (less generic) compiler options might be utilized to build the JVM.</p>


<p>One would expect floating point intensive Java applications to improve markedly.  Additionally function/method calling conventions have improved under armhf.  So if you have an application that does a large amount of method calling proportionally, performance might improve nicely there too.</p>


<p>As soon as an armhf JVM become publicly available, I intend on comparing armel and armhf versions and publishing to this blog.  My guess is the results that will again draw the most attention will be the comparison between Java SE-E and OpenJDK </p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jonathan S. Fisher
EMAIL: exabrial@gmail.com
URL: http://thecodemechanic.wordpress.com/
DATE: 11/24/2012 20:14:13

<p>Oracle's embedded Java license is a major hinderance. It states that a device used as a general computation machine doesn't require a license.</p>


<p>RaspberryPi can be used as a general computation device, but it's physical size leads it to embedded applications, which requires a license from Oracle. Oracle's license is designed so you have to negotiate with Oracle, and likely, they're going to want a per device fee. Honestly, this whole thing scares me (dealing with lawyers and salesmen) so I'd probably choose to just develop in Ruby or Python, which is already widely accepted on the device.</p>


<p>If Oracle wants a foothold with Java on this device, they need to rethink their 1990's microsoft-esk strategy.</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Take Two: Comparing JVMs on ARM/Linux
DATE: 03/19/2012 18:52:12
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>Although the intent of the previous article, entitled <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/comparing_jvms_on_arm_linux">Comparing JVMs on ARM/Linux</a>, was to introduce and highlight the availability of the HotSpot server compiler (referred to as <i>c2)</i> for Java SE-Embedded ARM v7,&nbsp; it seems, based on feedback, that everyone was more interested in the OpenJDK comparisons to Java SE-E.&nbsp; But there were two main concerns:<br /></p> 
  <ol> </ol> 
  <ul> 
    <li>The fact that the previous article compared Java SE-E 7 against OpenJDK 6 might be construed as an unlevel playing field because version 7 is newer and therefore potentially more optimized.</li> 
    <li>That the generic compiler settings chosen to build the OpenJDK implementations did not put those versions in a particularly favorable light.</li> 
  </ul> 
  <ol> </ol> 
  <p>With those considerations in mind, we'll institute the following changes to this version of the benchmarking:<br /></p> 
  <ol> 
    <li>In order to help alleviate an additional concern that there is some sort of benchmark bias, we'll use a different suite, called DaCapo.&nbsp; Funded and supported by many prestigious organizations, DaCapo's aim is to benchmark real world applications.&nbsp; Further information about DaCapo can be found at <a href="http://dacapobench.org">http://dacapobench.org</a>.</li> 
    <li>At the suggestion of Xerxes Ranby, who has been a great help through this entire exercise, a newer Linux distribution will be used to assure that the OpenJDK implementations were built with more optimal compiler settings.&nbsp; The Linux distribution in this instance is Ubuntu 11.10 Oneiric Ocelot.</li> 
    <li>Having experienced difficulties getting Ubuntu 11.10 to run on the original D2Plug ARMv7 platform, for these benchmarks, we'll switch to an embedded system that has a supported Ubuntu 11.10 release.&nbsp; That platform is the <a href="http://www.freescale.com/">Freescale i.MX53 Quick Start Board</a>.&nbsp; It has an ARMv7 Coretex-A8 processor running at 1GHz with 1GB RAM.</li> 
    <li>We'll limit comparisons to 4 JVM implementations:</li> 
    <ul> 
      <li>Java SE-E 7 Update 2 c1 compiler (default)<br /></li> 
      <li>Java SE-E 6 Update 30 (c1 compiler is the only option)<br /></li> 
      <li>OpenJDK 6 IcedTea6 1.11pre 6b23~pre11-0ubuntu1.11.10.2 CACAO build 1.1.0pre2</li> 
      <li>OpenJDK 6 IcedTea6 1.11pre 6b23~pre11-0ubuntu1.11.10.2 JamVM build-1.6.0-devel</li> 
    </ul> 
  </ol> 
  <p>
  Certain OpenJDK implementations were eliminated from this round of testing for the simple reason that their performance was not competitive.&nbsp; The Java SE 7u2 c2 compiler was also removed because although quite respectable, it did not perform as well as the c1 compilers.&nbsp; Recall that c2 works optimally in long-lived situations.&nbsp; Many of these benchmarks completed in a relatively short period of time.&nbsp; To get a feel for where c2 shines, <a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/comparing_jvms_on_arm_linux">take a look at the first chart in this blog</a>.<br /></p> 
  <p>The first chart that follows includes performance of all benchmark runs on all platforms.&nbsp; Later on we'll look more at individual tests.&nbsp; In all runs, smaller means faster.&nbsp; The DaCapo aficionado may notice that only 10 of the 14 DaCapo tests for this version were executed.&nbsp; The reason for this is that these 10 tests represent the only ones successfully completed by all 4 JVMs.&nbsp; Only the Java SE-E 6u30 could successfully run all of the tests.&nbsp; Both OpenJDK instances not only failed to complete certain tests, but also experienced VM aborts too.<br /></p> 
  <ol> </ol>

  <p>One of the first observations that can be made between Java SE-E 6 and 7 is that, for all intents and purposes, they are on par with regards to performance.&nbsp; While it is a fact that successive Java SE releases add additional optimizations, it is also true that Java SE 7 introduces additional complexity to the Java platform thus balancing out any potential performance gains at this point.&nbsp; We are still early into Java SE 7.&nbsp; We would expect further performance enhancements for Java SE-E 7 in future updates.</p> 
  <p>In comparing Java SE-E to OpenJDK performance, among both OpenJDK VMs, Cacao results are respectable in 4 of the 10 tests.&nbsp; The charts that follow show the individual results of those four tests.&nbsp; Both Java SE-E versions do win every test and outperform Cacao in the range of 9% to 55%.<br /></p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Xerxes R&aring;nby
EMAIL: xerxes@zafena.se
URL: http://https://twitter.com/#!/xranby
DATE: 03/29/2012 05:03:23

<p>Of course its worth noting that all three working ARM variants of OpenJDK + Zero are left out in the above test:</p>


<p>1. The optimized interpreter + Thumb2 JIT Zero *mixed mode* jvm that performed the best of the openjdk variants in the last study. ( icedtea6-1.11.1 )<br/>
<a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/comparing_jvms_on_arm_linux" rel="nofollow">https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/comparing_jvms_on_arm_linux</a><br/>
2. The assembler-optimized Zero *interpreted mode* ( also found in icedtea6-1.11.1 -Xint ) this one was also included in the last study.<br/>
<a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/comparing_jvms_on_arm_linux" rel="nofollow">https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/entry/comparing_jvms_on_arm_linux</a><br/>
3. The pure c++ Zero interpreter from OpenJDK upstream &lt;- the zero variant shipped in oneiric, i can understand that you left this one out of this study above.</p>


<p>Nice to see the some big speedup especially for CACAO when switching from Debian armel armv4+ to Ubuntu armel armv7 thumb2 tuning.</p>


<p>Please note that in the next Ubuntu 12.04 long term support release, targeting servers, the userspace EABI have switched from the armel &quot;softfp&quot; to armhf &quot;hard&quot; for extra performance. All OpenJDK JVM variants, JamVM, CACAO and Zero have been updated to support this armhf EABI hard in order to be ready to best serve the ARM server market.</p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Trevor Robinson
EMAIL: trevor@scurrilous.com
URL: null
DATE: 07/17/2012 19:50:57

<p>J.U., Ubuntu 12.04 uses the new hard-float ABI (aka armhf or gnueabihf), whereas 7u4 only supports the soft-float ABI (aka armel or gnueabi). You need to remove the symlink you created and install the soft-float libc and gcc support libraries:</p>


<p>sudo apt-get install -y libc6-armel libsfgcc1</p>

--------
AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Comparing JVMs on ARM/Linux
DATE: 02/15/2012 00:04:00
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
For quite some time, Java Standard Edition releases have included both client and server bytecode compilers (referred to as <i>c1</i> and <i>c2</i> respectively), whereas Java SE-Embedded binaries only contained the client c1 compiler.&nbsp; The rationale for excluding c2 stems from the fact that (1) eliminating optional components saves space, where in the embedded world, space is at a premium, and (2) embedded platforms were not given serious consideration for handling server-like workloads.&nbsp; But all that is about to change.&nbsp; In anticipation of the ARM processor's legitimate entrance into the server market (<a href="http://www.calxeda.com/">see Calxeda</a>), Oracle has, with the latest update of Java SE-Embedded (7u2), <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/downloads/javase/index.html">made the c2 compiler available for ARMv7/Linux platforms</a>, further enhancing performance for a large class of traditional server applications.&nbsp; <br /></p> 
  <p>These two compilers go about their business in different ways.&nbsp; Of the two, c1 is a lighter optimizing compiler, but has faster start up.&nbsp; It delivers excellent performance and as the default bytecode compiler, works extremely well in almost all situations.&nbsp; Compared to c1, c2 is the more aggressive optimizer and is suited for long-lived java processes.&nbsp; Although slower at start up, it can be shown to achieve better performance over time.&nbsp; As a case in point, take a look at the graph that follows.</p> 
  <p><img style="width: 612px; height: 478px;" /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>One of the most popular Java-based applications, Apache Tomcat, was installed on an ARMv7/Linux device.&nbsp;&nbsp; The chart shows the relative performance, as defined by mean HTTP request time, of the Tomcat server run with the c1 client compiler (red line) and the c2 server compiler (blue line).&nbsp; The HTTP request load was generated by an external system on a dedicated network utilizing the <i>ab</i> (Apache Bench) program.&nbsp; The closer the response time is to zero the better, you can see that for the initial run of 25,000 HTTP requests, the c1 compiler produces faster average response times than c2.&nbsp; It takes time for the c2 compiler to &quot;warm up&quot;, but once the threshold of 50,000 or so requests is met, the c2 compiler performance is superior to c1.&nbsp; At 250,000 HTTP requests, mean response time for the c2-based Tomcat server instance is 14% faster than its c1 counterpart.<br /></p> 
  <p>It is important to realize that c2 assumes, and indeed requires more resources (i.e. memory).&nbsp; Our sample device with 1GB RAM, was more than adequate for these rounds of tests.&nbsp; Of course your mileage may vary, but if you have the right hardware and the right workload, give c2 a further look.</p> 
  <p>While discussing these results with a few of my compadres, it was suggested that OpenJDK and some of its variants be included in on this comparison.&nbsp; The following chart shows mean http request times for 6 different configurations:</p> 
  <ol> 
    <li>Java SE Embedded 7u2 c1 Client Compiler</li> 
    <li>Java SE Embedded 7u2 c2 Server Compiler</li> 
    <li>OpenJDK Zero VM (build 20.0-b12, mixed mode) OpenJDK 1.6.0_24-b24 (IcedTea6 1.12pre) </li> 
    <li>JamVM (build 1.6.0-devel, inline-threaded interpreter with stack-caching) OpenJDK 1.6.0_24-b24 (IcedTea6 1.12pre)</li> 
    <li>CACAO (build 1.1.0pre2, compiled mode) OpenJDK 1.6.0_24-b24 (IcedTea6 1.12pre)</li> 
    <li>Interpreter only: OpenJDK Zero VM (build 20.0-b12, interpreted mode) OpenJDK 1.6.0_24-b24 (IcedTea6 1.12pre)</li> 
  </ol>

style="width: 614px; height: 465px;" /></p> 
  <p>Results remain pretty much unchanged, so only the first 4 runs (25K-100K requests) are shown.&nbsp; As can be seen, The Java SE-E VMs are on the order of 3-5x faster than their OpenJDK counterparts irrespective of the bytecode compiler chosen.&nbsp; One additional promising VM called <i>shark</i> was not included in these tests because, although it built from source successfully, it failed to run Apache Tomcat.&nbsp; In defense of shark, the ARM version may still be in development (i.e. non-stable) mode.<br /></p> 
  <p>Creating a really fast virtual machine is hard work and takes a lot of time to perfect.&nbsp; Considering the resources expended by Oracle (and formerly Sun), it is no surprise that the commercial Java SE VMs are excellent performers.&nbsp; But the extent to which they outperform their OpenJDK counterparts is surprising.&nbsp; It would be no shock if someone in the know could demonstrate better OpenJDK results.&nbsp; But herein lies one considerable problem:&nbsp; it is an exercise in patience and perseverance just to locate and build a proper OpenJDK platform suitable for a particular CPU/Linux configuration.&nbsp; No offense would be taken if corrections were presented, and a straightforward mechanism to support these OpenJDK builds were provided.<br /></p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: guest
EMAIL: xerxes@zafena.se
URL: http://labb.zafena.se
DATE: 02/15/2012 20:59:46

<p>Thank you an introduction to the ARM OpenJDK JVM porting work.</p>


<p>The OpenJDK Zero *mixed-mode* JVM used in this compare includes the now re-maintained ARM Thumb2 JIT and assembler interpreter port that got re-introduced in the IcedTea6-1.11 release. <br/>
Many of the OpenJDK JVM like CACAO and JamVM are by design tuned for embedded and client use and thus show strength in both low memory overhead and fast startup time.</p>


<p>When testing JVM performance on ARM its important to remember that the default optimization settings used by the compilers to build the JVM do matter.</p>


<p>The Debian 6.0.4 squeeze &quot;armel&quot; distribution use ARMv4t optimization by default. This low optimization level enable the Debian built packages run on as many kind of different ARM broads and CPU's as possible. The trade-off are that you basically disable all VFP, floating point, optimizations and make synchronization code slower by forcing the JVM to call the Linux kernel helper instead of using faster ARMv7 atomic instructions directly.</p>


<p>To give OpenJDK JVM a better match i would suggest re-running the benchmark using OpenJDK built on top of Debian wheezy &quot;armhf&quot; that by default optimize for the ARMv7 thumb2 instruction-set and make use of the VFP unit inside the CPU, also the &quot;armhf&quot; ABI allows better argument passing between library functions inside the VFP registers of the CPU. Two OpenJDK JVM that are already updated to support this new &quot;armhf&quot; ABI are JamVM and Zero.</p>


<p>You could also run a benchmark using OpenJDK JVMs built using the Ubuntu Precise &quot;armel&quot; tool-chains that still use the legacy soft-float ABI while still adding ARMv7 Thumb2 and VFP optimizations. All OpenJDK JVM tested in this compare would run better by simply using a higher optimization level during the build.</p>


-----

<p>I got a bit confused about HW requirments of c2 (server) optimized apps.<br/>
I built a jre with jrecreate tool using the default arguments. the generated log shows that the target platforme is ARMv6. On the other hand c2 require ARMv7.</p>


<p>Our application is a long running server so will benifit from c2 optimizations when running on our target IMX6 (ARMv7). Now the question is, will the app built with c2 work using the jre built for ARMv6 on top of the ARMv7 hardware?</p>


<p>thnx.</p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jim Connors
EMAIL: james.connors@oracle.com
URL: null
DATE: 06/02/2015 00:00:34

<p>Hi Malek,</p>


<p>The c2-generated JRE should work on your iMX6 system. To demonstrate, this is what I did.</p>


<p>First: generate c2-based JRE from 1.8.0_33 eJDK:<br/>
$ jrecreate.sh --dest jre1.8.0_33-c2 --vm server</p>


<p>tar up the generated JRE<br/>
$ tar cvfz jre1.8.0_33-c2.tar.gz jre1.8.0_33-c2</p>


<p>Copy c2 JRE over to an ARMv6 system.  In this instance, we'll use a Raspberry Pi.</p>


<p>pi@pi0 ~ $ ./jre1.8.0_33-c2/bin/java -version<br/>
Error occurred during initialization of VM<br/>
Server VM is only supported on ARMv7+ VFP</p>


<p>Copy c2 JRE over to iMX6 based system (Boundary Devices BD-SL-i.MX6)</p>


<p>jimc@imx6-armhf:~$ ./jre1.8.0_33-c2/bin/java -version<br/>
java version &quot;1.8.0_33&quot;<br/>
Java(TM) SE Embedded Runtime Environment (build 1.8.0_33-b05)<br/>
Java HotSpot(TM) Embedded Server VM (build 25.33-b05, mixed mode)</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Tomcat Micro Cluster
DATE: 01/03/2012 15:33:41
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p> The term <a href="http://www.webopedia.com/TERM/M/micro-server.html">Micro Server</a> has been bandied about recently as a means to provide a certain class of server functionality. As embedded systems continue their inexorable drive towards better performance, and standard hardware/software architectures become ubiquitous, the notion of using low-cost, low-power, small-footprint devices as servers becomes quite realistic.&nbsp; Just as data center managers have utilized multitudes of affordable rack mount servers to provide scalability, why not duplicate that effort with these off-the-shelf devices?</p> 
  <p>The video that follows takes the Micro Server to its next logical evolution: <i>The Micro Cluster</i>.&nbsp; Built from commodity hardware (and by commodity I mean The Home Depot), the cluster board has a rack mount form factor that can house 12 <a href="http://www.plugcomputer.org/">Plug Computers</a>.&nbsp; As the <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/downloads/javase/index.html">Java SE HotSpot Virtual Machine</a><a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/downloads/javase/index.html"> is available for the Plug Computer (ArmV5/Linux)</a>, we'll utilize Apache Tomcat to demonstrate a <i>Tomcat Micro Cluster</i>.&nbsp; Over time, as the individual compute nodes increase in performance and capacity, this should become even more compelling.<br /></p> 
  <iframe height="315" frameborder="0" width="420" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/LXDH1MhSXw4"></iframe>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Serial Port Communication for Java SE Embedded
DATE: 08/19/2011 10:06:51
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>The need to communicate with devices connected to serial ports is a common application requirement.&nbsp; Falling outside the purview of the Java SE platform, serial and parallel port communication has been addressed with a project called <a href="http://rxtx.qbang.org/wiki/index.php/Main_Page">RXTX</a>.&nbsp; (In the past, you may have known this as javacomm).&nbsp; With RXTX,&nbsp; Java developers access serial ports through the RXTXcomm.jar file.&nbsp; Alongside this jar file, an underlying native layer must be provided to interface with the operating system's UART ports.&nbsp; For the usual suspects (Windows, Linux/x86, MacOS, Solaris/Sparc), pre-compiled binaries are readily available.&nbsp; To host this on an alternative platform, some (hopefully minimal) amount of work is required.</p> 
  <p>Here's hoping the following notes/observations might aid in helping you to build RXTX for an embedded device utilizing one of our <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/overview/javase/index.html">Java SE Embedded</a> binaries.&nbsp; The device used for this particular implementation is my current favorite: the <a href="http://www.plugcomputer.org/">Plug Computer</a>.</p> 
  <p>Notes on Getting RX/TX 2.1-7-r2 Working on a Plug Computer<br /><br />1. At this early juncture with Java 7, be wary of mixing Java 7 with code from older versions of Java. The class files generated by the JDK7 javac compiler contain an updated version byte with a value that results in older (Java 6 and before) JVMs refusing to load these classes.<br /><br />2. The RXTX download location <a href="http://rxtx.qbang.org/wiki/index.php/Download">http://rxtx.qbang.org/wiki/index.php/Download</a> has binaries for many platforms including Arm variants, but none that worked for the Plug Computer, so one had to be built from source.<br /><br />3. Using the native GCC for the Plug Computer and the RXTX source, binaries (native shared objects) were compiled for the armv5tel-unknown-linux-gnu platform.<br /><br />4. The RXTX &quot;stable&quot; source code found at the aforementioned site is based on version rxtx 2.1-7r2.&nbsp; This code appears to be pretty long in the tooth, in that it has no knowledge of Java 6.&nbsp; Some changes need to be made to accommodate a JDK 6 environment.&nbsp; Without these modifications, RXTX will not build with JDK6<br /><br />SUGGESTED FIX, most elegant, not recommended:<br />Edit the configure.in file in the source directory and look for the following:<br /></p> 
  <pre>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; case $JAVA_VERSION in
    1.2*|1.3*|1.4*|1.5*)
</pre> 
  <p>and change the second line to:<br /></p> 
  <pre>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 1.2*|1.3*|1.4*|1.5*|1.6*)
</pre> 
  <p>Upon modification, the autogen.sh script found in the rxtx source directory must be re-run to recreate the ./configure script.&nbsp; Unfortunately, this requires loading the autoconf, automake and libtool packages (plus dependencies) and ended up resulting in libtool incompatibilies when running the resultant ./configure script.<br /><br />RECOMMENDED FIX:<br />Instead, edit ./configure and search for the occurrences (there are more than one) of<br /></p> 
  <pre>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; case $JAVA_VERSION in
    1.2*|1.3*|1.4*|1.5*)
</pre> 
  <p>and change the second line to:<br /></p> 
  <pre>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 1.2*|1.3*|1.4*|1.5*|1.6*)
</pre> 
  <p>Run './configure', then 'make' to generate the RXTXcomm.jar and platform specific .so shared object libraries.<br /><br />5. You may also notice in the output of the make, that there were compilation errors for source files which failed to find the meaning of &quot;UTS_RELEASE&quot;.&nbsp; This results in some of the shared object files not being created.&nbsp; These pertain to the non-serial aspects of RXTX.&nbsp; As we were only interested in librxtxSerial.so, this was no problem for us.<br /><br />6. Once built, move the following files into the following directories:<br /></p> 
  <pre>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; # cd rxtx-2.1-7-r2/
    # cp RXTXcomm.jar $JAVA_HOME/lib/ext
    # cd armv5tel-unknown-linux-gnu/.libs/
    # cp librxtxSerial-2.1-7.so $JAVA_HOME/lib/arm
    # cd $JAVA_HOME/lib/arm
    # ln -s librxtxSerial-2.1-7.so librxtxSerial.so
</pre> 
  <p>Now Java applications which utilize RXTX should run without any java command-line additions.</p> 
  <p> The RXTXcomm.jar file can be downloaded <a href="http://rxtx.qbang.org/ToyBox/2.1-7-build1/RXTXcomm.jar">here</a>.&nbsp; To spare you the effort, a few pre-built versions of&nbsp;  <span style="font-family: monospace;"></span>librxtxSerial-2.1-7.so are provided at this location:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/rxtx-armv5tel-unknown-linux-gnu/librxtxSerial-2.1-7.so">librxtxSerial-2.1-7.so</a> for ARMv5 based Plug Computers</li> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/rxtx-armv6l-unknown-linux-gnu-ARMEL/librxtxSerial-2.1-7.so">librxtxSerial-2.1-7.so</a> for ARMv6l <i>armel</i> based systems (e.g. original Raspberry Pi Debian distro)</li> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/rxtx-armv6l-unknown-linux-gnu-ARMHF/librxtxSerial-2.1-7.so">librxtxSerial-2.1.7.so</a> for ARMv6l <i>armhf</i> (hard float) systems (e.g. Raspberry Pi Raspbian distro)<br /></li> 
    <li><a href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/rxtx-arm7l/librxtxSerial-2.1-7.so">librxtxSerial-2.1-7.so</a> for ARMv7l architecture - Many thanks to <i>Daniel Ryan</i><br /></li> 
  </ul> 
  <p>If you've gone through this exercise on any additional architectures, send them my way and I'll post them here.<br /></p> 
  <p> </p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Observations in Migrating from JavaFX Script to JavaFX 2.0
DATE: 06/21/2011 05:34:49
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p class="MsoNormal"><b><span style="font-size: 16pt; line-height: 115%;">Observations in Migrating from JavaFX
Script to JavaFX 2.0</span></b><b><span style="font-size: 16pt; line-height: 115%;"><i><br /></i></span></b></p> 
  <p class="MsoNormal"><b>Introduction</b></p> 
  <p class="MsoNormal">Having been available for a few years now, there is a decent
body of work written for JavaFX using the JavaFX Script language.<span> </span>With the general availability announcement of JavaFX 2.0 Beta, the natural question arises about
converting the legacy code over to the new JavaFX 2.0 platform.<span> </span>This article reflects
on some of the observations encountered while porting source code over from
JavaFX Script to the new JavaFX API paradigm.<span></span></p> 
  <p class="MsoNormal"><b>The Application</b></p> 
  <p class="MsoNormal">The program chosen for migration is an implementation of the
Sudoku game and serves as a reference application for the book <i>JavaFX – Developing Rich Internet
Applications</i>. <span> </span>The design of the
program can be divided into two major components: (1) A user interface (ideally
suited for JavaFX design) and (2) the puzzle generator.<span> </span>For the context of this article, our primary
interest lies in the user interface.<span> </span>The
puzzle generator code was lifted from a sourceforge.net project and is written entirely
in Java.<span> </span>Regardless which version of the
UI we choose (JavaFX Script vs. JavaFX 2.0), no code changes were required for the puzzle generator code.</p> 
  <p class="MsoNormal">The original user interface for the JavaFX Sudoku application
was written exclusively in JavaFX Script, and as such is a suitable candidate to
convert over to the new JavaFX 2.0 <span style="font-style: italic;"></span>
model.<span> </span><span> </span>However, a few notable points are worth
mentioning about this program.<span> </span>First
off, it was written in the JavaFX 1.1 timeframe, where certain capabilities of
the JavaFX framework were as of yet unavailable.<span> </span>Citing two examples, this program creates
many of its own UI controls from scratch because the built-in controls were yet
to be introduced.<span> </span>In addition, layout of
graphical nodes is done in a very manual manner, again because much of the
automatic layout capabilities were in flux at the time.<span> </span>It is worth considering that this
program was written at a time when most of us were just coming up to speed on
this technology.<span> </span>One would think that
having the opportunity to recreate this application anew, it would look a lot
different from the current version.</p> 
  <p class="MsoNormal"><b>Comparing the Size of
the Source Code</b></p> 
  <p class="MsoNormal">An attempt was made to convert each of the original UI JavaFX
Script source files (suffixed with .fx) over to a Java counterpart.<span> </span>Due to language feature differences, there
are a small number of source files which only exist in one version or the
other. <span> </span>The table below summarizes the
size of each of the source files. </p> 
  <table cellspacing="0" cellpadding="0" border="1" style="border-collapse: collapse; border: medium none;" class="MsoTableGrid"> 
    <tbody> 
      <tr> 
        <td width="164" valign="top" style="width: 122.9pt; border: 1pt solid black; padding: 0in 5.4pt;"> 
          <p align="center" style="margin-bottom: 0.0001pt; text-align: center; line-height: normal;" class="MsoNormal"><b><span style="color: #c00000;">JavaFX Script source file</span></b></p> 
        </td> 
        <td width="66" valign="top" style="width: 49.25pt; border-width: 1pt 1pt 1pt medium; border-style: solid solid solid none; border-color: black black black -moz-use-text-color; padding: 0in 5.4pt;"> 
          <p align="center" style="margin-bottom: 0.0001pt; text-align: center; line-height: normal;" class="MsoNormal"><b><span style="color: #c00000;">Number of Lines</span></b></p> 
        </td> 
        <td width="82" valign="top" style="width: 61.45pt; border-width: 1pt 1pt 1pt medium; border-style: solid solid solid none; border-color: black black black -moz-use-text-color; padding: 0in 5.4pt;"> 
          <p align="center" style="margin-bottom: 0.0001pt; text-align: center; line-height: normal;" class="MsoNormal"><b><span style="color: #c00000;">Number of Character</span></b></p> 
        </td> 
        <td width="240" valign="top" style="width: 2.5in; border-width: 1pt 1pt 1pt medium; border-style: solid solid solid none; border-color: black black black -moz-use-text-color; padding: 0in 5.4pt;"> 
          <p align="center" style="margin-bottom: 0.0001pt; text-align: center; line-height: normal;" class="MsoNormal"><b><i><span style="color: #c00000;"></span></i><span style="color: #c00000;"> JavaFX 2.0 Java source file</span></b></p> 
        </td> 
        <td width="87" valign="top" style="width: 65.35pt; border-width: 1pt 1pt 1pt medium; border-style: solid solid solid none; border-color: black black black -moz-use-text-color; padding: 0in 5.4pt;"> 
          <p align="center" style="margin-bottom: 0.0001pt; text-align: center; line-height: normal;" class="MsoNormal"><b><span style="color: #c00000;">Number of Lines</span></b></p> 
        </td> 
        <td width="81" valign="top" style="width: 61pt; border-width: 1pt 1pt 1pt medium; border-style: solid solid solid none; border-color: black black black -moz-use-text-color; padding: 0in 5.4pt;"> 
          <p align="center" style="margin-bottom: 0.0001pt; text-align: center; line-height: normal;" class="MsoNormal"><b><span style="color: #c00000;">Number of Characters</span></b></p> 
        </td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td width="164" valign="top" style="width: 122.9pt; border-width: medium 1pt 1pt; border-style: none solid solid; border-color: -moz-use-text-color black black; padding: 0in 5.4pt;">

          <p align="center" style="margin-bottom: 0.0001pt; text-align: center; line-height: normal;" class="MsoNormal"><b><span style="color: #c00000;">87,800<br /></span></b></p>
