  </table> 
  <p class="MsoNormal"> </p> 
  <p class="MsoNormal">A few notes about this table are in order:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li><span style="font-family: Symbol;"><span><span style="font: 7pt &quot;Times New Roman&quot;;"></span></span></span>The number of lines in each file was determined
by running the Unix ‘wc –l’ command over each file.<span></span></li> 
    <li><span style="font-family: Symbol;"><span><span style="font: 7pt &quot;Times New Roman&quot;;"></span></span></span>The number of characters in each file was
determined by running the Unix ‘ls –l’ command over each file.</li> 
    <li><span style="font-family: Symbol;"><span><span style="font: 7pt &quot;Times New Roman&quot;;"></span></span></span>The examination of the code could certainly be
much more rigorous. No standard formatting was performed on these files.&nbsp; All comments however were deleted.</li> 
  </ul> 
  <p class="MsoNormal">There was a certain expectation that the new Java version
would require more lines of code than the original JavaFX script version.<span> </span>As evidenced by a count of the total number
of lines, the Java version has about 22% more lines than its FX Script
counterpart.<span></span></p> 
  <p class="MsoNormal"><span> </span><span> </span>Furthermore, there was an additional expectation
that the Java version would be more verbose in terms of the total number of
characters.&nbsp; In fact the preceding data shows that on average the Java source files contain fewer characters per line than the FX files.&nbsp; But that's not the whole story.&nbsp; Upon further examination, the FX Script source files had a disproportionate number of blank characters.&nbsp; Why?&nbsp; Because of the nature of how one develops JavaFX Script code.&nbsp; The object literal dominates FX Script code.&nbsp; Its not uncommon to see object literals indented halfway across the page, consuming lots of meaningless space characters.</p> 
  <p class="MsoNormal"><b>RAM consumption</b></p> 
  <p class="MsoNormal">Not the most scientific analysis, memory usage for
the application was examined on a Windows Vista system by running the Windows
Task Manager and viewing how much memory was being consumed by the Sudoku
version in question.<span> </span>Roughly speaking, the
FX script version, after startup, had a RAM footprint of about 90MB and remained
pretty much the same size.<span> </span>The Java
version started out at about 55MB and maintained that size throughout its execution.</p> 
  <p class="MsoNormal"><b>What About Binding?</b></p> 
  <p class="MsoNormal">Arguably, the most striking observation about the conversion
from JavaFX Script to JavaFX 2.0 concerned the need for data synchronization, or lack thereof.<span> </span>In JavaFX Script, the primary means to synchronize
data is via the bind expression (using the “bind” keyword), and perhaps to a
lesser extent it’s “on replace” cousin.<span> </span>The bind keyword does not exist in Java, so for JavaFX 2.0 a Data Binding API has been introduced as a replacement.</p> 
  <p class="MsoNormal">To give a feel for the difference between the two versions
of the Sudoku program, the table that follows indicates how many binds were
required for each source file.<span> </span>For
JavaFX Script files, this was ascertained by simply counting the number of
occurrences of the <i>bind</i> keyword.<span> </span>As can be seen, binding had been used
frequently in the JavaFX Script version (and does not take into consideration
an additional half dozen or so “<i>on
replace”</i> triggers). The JavaFX 2.0 program achieves the same functionality as the original JavaFX Script version,
yet the equivalent of binding was only needed twice throughout the Java version of the source code. <span></span></p>

  <p class="MsoNormal"><b> </b></p> 
  <p style="margin-bottom: 0.0001pt; line-height: normal;" class="MsoNormal"><span style="font-family: &quot;Courier New&quot;;"> </span></p> 
  <p style="margin-bottom: 0.0001pt; line-height: normal;" class="MsoNormal"><b><span>Conclusions</span></b></p> 
  <p style="margin-bottom: 0.0001pt; line-height: normal;" class="MsoNormal"><span> </span></p> 
  <p style="margin-bottom: 0.0001pt; line-height: normal;" class="MsoNormal"><span>As the JavaFX 2.0 technology is so new, and experience
with the platform is the same, it is possible and indeed probable that some of
the observations noted in the preceding article may not apply across other
attempts at migrating applications.<span> </span>That
being said, this first experience indicates that the migrated Java code will
likely be larger, though not extensively so, than the original Java FX Script
source.<span> </span>Furthermore, although very
important, it appears that the requirements for data synchronization via
binding, may be significantly less with the new platform.</span></p> 
  <p style="margin-bottom: 0.0001pt; line-height: normal;" class="MsoNormal"><span> </span></p> <span></span><span></span>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: guest
EMAIL: james.connors@oracle.com
URL: null
DATE: 06/27/2011 13:29:06

<p>As per a previous blog entitled, Why I like the New JavaFX, here's some more rationale:</p>


<p>The new JavaFX results in, for lack of a better term, more predictability. One of the primary JavaFX Script mechanisms used to define and place graphical assets (Nodes) into the scenegraph is the object literal notation. Being static in nature, the notion of a sequential flow of events inside an object literal may not make much sense. To compensate, JavaFX Script introduces the powerful concept of binding. Without question, binding is very elegant, but it comes with a price. It is not uncommon to see liberal and arguably unnecessary use of binding throughout JavaFX script code.</p>


<p>One can argue that bringing JavaFX to Java should lessen, but certainly not eliminate, the need for binding. Rather than defining objects inside a static initializer, JavaFX API objects are created and defined sequentially by constructor and method calls respectively. By defining objects in an order that makes sense, there is potential to eliminate a certain class of dependencies that were previously resolved with binding. </p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jim Connors
EMAIL: james.connors@oracle.com
URL: null
DATE: 08/31/2011 13:58:42

<p>Here's the current situation:  Even though JavaFX 2.0 is in public Beta, the API is still in flux, so much so that every time I pull down a newer internal build, something breaks.  The code used for this test is hopelessly obsolete with regards to the latest API.  The good news (I think) is that we are hopefully coming to and end to all these changes, where we'll draw a line in the sand and say the API is frozen.  But that hasn't happened yet.  Once it does, then, given some time, I'll migrate over to the latest stable API and post.</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Java SE Embedded Development Made Easy
DATE: 06/01/2011 06:12:20
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>Slowly but surely this old dog (who can learn new tricks, but at a snail's pace) came to the realization that although still quite relevant, a whole generation of people prefer not to read lengthy writings, but would rather digest information in small pieces using new media formats.&nbsp; Thus the rationale for the following blog...</p> 
  <p>Certainly no thespian when it comes to public speaking, I will say this:&nbsp; based upon my experience demonstrating Java SE on embedded devices, people have definitely expressed genuine interest.&nbsp; Maybe it was the cool device (i.e. Plug Computer) which was used, or maybe this combination of hardware and software inspired the audience to think of the possibilities presented by this new platform.&nbsp; Either way, I thought it might make sense to capture a shortened presentation/demonstration session.&nbsp; Following is a 30 minute session broken down into two 15 minute videos (because YouTube only allows videos of 15 minutes or less for mere mortals). They aim to demonstrate how 
developers already familiar with the Java SE development paradigm can 
leverage that knowledge to seamlessly develop on very capable embedded 
processors.&nbsp; Happy viewing!</p>

<p>This HOL far exceeded my expectations. It all worked well, with a decent footprint and performance, and real debuggers! On a $99 PlugComputer module. When I can start actually using this? So I have been puzzled by the lack of followup by the team. Is JSE Embedded real? Where can we download what we need to try using it in a new product? And dare we do so if that product has to ship for, say, 5-10 years (which is actually on the low end of many embedded system life cycles)? What support is there if we are not a billion dollar customer and are &quot;only&quot; planning to ship perhaps 500-5000 systems per year, to start? Is embedded Java a viable option for us? Inquiring minds want to know...</p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jim Connors
EMAIL: james.connors@oracle.com
URL: null
DATE: 03/07/2012 18:29:19

<p>Him Michela,</p>


<p>The answer to your question is, it depends.  I have demonstrated Bluetooth/Java functionality on the Plug Computer with BlueCove, an implementation of JSR 82, the Bluetooth Java specification.  However, the specification only supports a handful of the 25 or so Bluetooth profiles.  In addition to the lack of an API for these higher end profiles, linux kernel and user software modules are also required which typically are not included (yet) in the standard embedded linux distributions.</p>

<p>&gt;&gt;Is it possible to run Xlet and Lwuit based java application on it?</p>


<p>Not sure about Xlets, but check out the LWUIMB project at <a href="http://lwuimb.thenesis.org/" rel="nofollow">http://lwuimb.thenesis.org/</a> for a description of a port of LWUIT that can run on top of Java SE.</p>

There is no executable to install, rather the software is gzip'ed.  Just extract to install.</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: The Shackles are Off
DATE: 05/26/2011 20:51:58
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Personal
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>Today marks an important milestone for JavaFX 2.0.&nbsp; Prior to this date, only a select few partners had early access privilege to the technology, and for good reason.&nbsp; During that early access period, the API was in a very fluid state, and from personal experience I can tell you that changes to the JavaFX 2.0 API were done almost on a build-by-build basis.&nbsp; The announcement of a public beta not only makes the software available worldwide, but also signals that the JavaFX 2.0 API is on much more solid footing.</p> 
  <p>So check out the <a href="http://javafx.com">javafx.com</a> website for the latest JavaFX 2.0 information.&nbsp;&nbsp; Three software downloads are now available:</p> 
  <ol> 
    <li><b>JavaFX 2.0 Beta SDK</b> - the JavaFX functionality needed to develop directly via the command line or with other tools</li> 
    <li><b>JavaFX 2.0 Beta Runtime</b> - the runtime environment required for running JavaFX 2.0 applications&nbsp;</li> 
    <li><b>JavaFX 2.0 Beta Plugin for NetBeans IDE 7.0</b> -&nbsp; for building, previewing, and debugging JavaFX applications in NetBeans 7.<br /></li> 
  </ol> 
  <p> </p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: The Unofficial Java SE Embedded SDK
DATE: 03/15/2011 07:34:43
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
Developing applications for embedded platforms gets simpler all the time, thanks in part to the tremendous advances in microprocessor design and software tools.&nbsp; And in particular, with the availability of Java SE compatible Virtual Machines for the popular embedded platforms, development has never been more straightforward.<br /></p> 
  <p>The real beauty behind Java SE Embedded development lies in the fact that you can use your favorite IDE (Eclipse, NetBeans, JDeveloper ...) to create, test and debug code in the <b><i>identical</i></b> fashion in which you'd develop a standard desktop or server application.&nbsp; When the time comes to try it out on a Java SE Embedded capable device, it's just a matter of shipping the bytecodes over to the device and letting it run.&nbsp; There is no need for complicated emulators, toolchains and cross-compilers.&nbsp; The exact same bytecodes that ran on your PC, run <i><b>unmodified</b></i> on the embedded device.</p> 
  <p>In fact, because all versions of Java SE (embedded or not) share a considerable amount of common code, we have plenty of anecdotal evidence which supports the notion that behavior -- correct or incorrect -- manifests itself identically across platforms.&nbsp; We refer specifically here to bugs.&nbsp; Now no one wants bugs, but believe it or not, our customers like the fact that behavior is consistent across platforms whether it's right or not. &quot;Bug for bug compatibility&quot; has actually become a strong selling point!</p> 
  <p>Having espoused the virtues of transparently developing off device, many still wish to test and debug on-device regularly as part of their development cycle.&nbsp; If you're the touchy/feely type, there are ample examples of affordable and supported off-the-shelf devices that could fit the bill for an <i>Unofficial Java SE Embedded SDK</i>.&nbsp; One such platform is the <a href="http://plugcomputer.org/">Plug Computer</a>.</p> 
  <p><img src="/jtc/resource/java_se-e/plug.jpg" /><img src="/jtc/resource/java_se-e/plug2.jpg" /> The reference platform for the Plug Computer is supplied by <a href="http://www.marvell.com/platforms/plug_computer/">Marvell Technology Group</a>. Manufacturers then license the technology from Marvell to create their own specific implementations.&nbsp; Two such vendors are <a href="http://www.globalscaletechnologies.com/">GlobalScale</a> and <a href="http://www.ionicsplug.com/products.html">Ionics</a>.&nbsp; These are incredibly capable devices that include Arm processors in the 1.2GHz to 2.0GHz range, and sport 512MB of RAM and flash.&nbsp; There are a host of external port and interface options including USB, µUSB, SATA, GBE, SD, WiFi, ZigBee, Z-Wave and soon HDMI.&nbsp; Additionally, several Linux distros are available for these systems too.&nbsp; The typical cost for a base model is $99, and perhaps the most disruptive aspect of these systems,<i> they consume on average about 5 watts of power</i>.</p> 
  <p>Alongside developing in the traditional manner, the ability to step through and examine state on these devices via remote debugging comes as a standard feature with the Java SE-E VM.&nbsp; Furthermore, you can use the JConsole application from your desktop to remotely monitor performance and resource consumption on the device.<br /></p> 
  <p>So what would a bill of materials look like for <i>The Unofficial Java SE Embedded SDK</i>?&nbsp; Pretty simple actually:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li><a href="http://netbeans.org/downloads/index.html">Latest NetBeans Java SE IDE</a> (or any other of your favorite Java IDEs)&nbsp;</li> 
    <li><a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/javase/downloads/embedded-jsp-135769.html">Java SE Embedded ARMv5 Linux - Headless</a></li> 
    <li> <a href="http://www.ionicsplug.com/nimbus100.html">Ionics Nimbus 100</a> or <a href="http://www.globalscaletechnologies.com/p-22-sheevaplug-dev-kit-us.aspx">GlobalScale SheevaPlug Dev Kit</a></li> 
  </ul>That's about it.&nbsp; Of course, for higher level functionality, you can add additional packages.&nbsp; For example, <a href="www.apache.org">Apache</a> runs beautifully here.&nbsp; Could anyone imagine a large number of these devices acting as a parallel web server?<br /> <br />
-----
--------
AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Java SE Embedded Refreshed
DATE: 12/16/2010 10:17:15
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>As embedded processor designs continue their inexorable drive towards ever increasing capability, the natural desire to utilize more robust software platforms, previously reserved for powerful desktop computers and servers, follows suit.&nbsp; Recognizing this trend, a version of the Java Standard Edition platform, called <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/overview/index.html">Java SE Embedded</a>, has been developed to address this growing market.&nbsp; In addition to bringing all of the benefits of the ubiquitous Java Standard Edition to the most popular embedded processors, static footprint and memory optimizations have been realized.&nbsp; To get a feel for some of the space savings, check out this <a href="http://java.dzone.com/articles/reduced-footprint-java-se-brin">article</a>.</p> 
  <p>Partly due to the turmoil surrounding the Oracle acquisition of Sun, a refresh of the Java SE Embedded binaries took longer than anticipated.&nbsp; The new versions are now available for <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/downloads/index.html">download</a> with these <a href="http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/embedded/overview/sys-reqs-embedded-159989.html">supported configurations</a>.&nbsp; During that time frame, Java SE-E engineers were able take care of some internal housekeeping which should help for better synchronization of future releases of Java SE with Java SE-E.&nbsp; In the past, Java SE-E engineers would take a snapshot of the Java Standard Edition code and incorporate their modifications to create a new release, forking from the standard edition source,.&nbsp; Now, the Java SE-E code is part of the overall Java SE project, such that future Java SE enhancements and bug fixes should now be automatically incorporated into the Java SE-E code base.</p> 
  <p>The refreshed Java SE-E binaries are based on the Java SE 6 Update 21, and represent substantial security, performance and quality enhancements.&nbsp; Benchmark tests show that, for example, simply replacing the previous versions of the Java SE-E virtual machines with the latest binaries produces on average about 20% performance gain for the same hardware/OS combination.&nbsp; Having recently introduced a Just-In-Time (or JIT) compiler to the Android Dalvik Virtual Machine, we thought it would be an interesting exercise to compare performance of Java SE-E to Android on identical hardware.&nbsp; For a full explanation of the results and methodology, check out <a href="http://blogs.oracle.com/javaseembedded/2010/11/how_does_android_22s_performance_stack_up_against_java_se_embedded.html">Bob Vandette's blog</a> on this topic.&nbsp; To cut to the chase, for the selected benchmarks, Java SE-E outperforms Android by a factor of two.&nbsp; </p> 
  <p>Improving virtual machine performance is a tedious process that takes time.&nbsp; The bottom line is this:&nbsp; Java SE Virtual Machine engineers have been at this for a very long time, and have had the benefit of fifteen years of scrutiny from the computer science community.&nbsp; It will take considerable time and effort for Android to come close to this capability.&nbsp; In the interim, the Java Virtual Machine performance and quality improvement marches on.<br /></p> 
  <p><img src="/jtc/resource/java_se-e/plug.jpg" /><img src="/jtc/resource/java_se-e/plug2.jpg" />&nbsp;&nbsp; Next up: Take a look at these pictures.&nbsp; Based upon Marvell's Plug Computer design, these amazing devices pack a 1+GHz Arm processor with 512MB RAM and consume a minuscule 5 watts of power. They run Java SE-E beautifully.&nbsp; Combined with an IDE like <a href="http://netbeans.org">NetBeans</a>, this makes for an ideal, although unofficial, Java SE Embedded Development Kit.<br /></p>

            <p class="MsoNormal">I have a vested interest in seeing the original JavaFX
Script based platform prosper. <span> </span>As an early
adopter of this technology, a good portion of my life these past few years has
been spent developing, blogging and even co-authoring a book on the
subject.<span> </span>So when the inevitable demise
of JavaFX Script was formally announced, those of us intimately involved did
not take it lightly.</p> 
            <p class="MsoNormal"><span></span>Perhaps not unlike yourselves, I’ve viewed the plans to morph JavaFX into a Java API with a bit of
skepticism. <span> </span>The new resulting coding paradigm
is unquestionably less stylish than its predecessor and can be downright
verbose. <span> </span>But the new way grows on you. <span> </span>Having the privilege to experiment with early
versions, I’ve come to like the new platform.<span> </span>In fact I like it a lot.<span> </span>Here’s
why I think the new JavaFX platform is more attractive:</p> 
            <p class="MsoNormal"><b>The community has
spoken and it doesn’t feel the need for yet another scripting language.</b><span> </span>The attempt to lure graphics designers into
the Java camp by offering a simplified Rich Internet Application environment never
really panned out.<span> </span>Why? <span> </span>First, there already exists a wealth of mature,
established RIA scripting alternatives.<span> </span>These address much of what designers need and JavaFX Script is not sufficiently
different enough.<span> </span>Second, the clamor to
provide RIA capabilities in Java comes from the Java community proper, not the
graphics artists. <span> </span><span> </span>These developers have a lot invested in Java
and are not interested in learning a new language.<span> </span>What they want is a Java API with RIA capabilities.<span> </span>By making JavaFX a first class citizen of the
Java platform, it goes a long way towards meeting these desires.</p> 
            <p class="MsoNormal"><b>The JavaFX API is a
more universal solution.</b><span> </span>By building
an API in Java, the opportunity for developers of other dynamic languages (like
JRuby and JavaScript) to access JavaFX has been made much easier.<span> </span>Moreover, as the trend to host other
languages atop the Java Virtual Machine accelerates, these too will profit from
this move.</p> 
            <p class="MsoNormal"><b>No more mapping between
JavaFX Script and Java</b>.<span> </span>A derivative
of Java, one of the touted advantages of JavaFX Script is its ability to
seamlessly integrate and leverage the wealth of Java code written already.<span> </span>Indeed a very important benefit, Java/JavaFX
Script integration is mostly straightforward; however there are subtle
differences between both languages that the developer must take into
consideration.<span> </span>Mapping primitive Java
data types to JavaFX basic types can be an issue.<span> </span>The original JavaFX classes can only extend
Java classes that contain a default (no arguments) constructor.<span> </span>Features familiar to Java programmers, like
multidimensional arrays, generics, annotations, and multi-threading have no
real equivalent in JavaFX Script.<span> </span>Bringing the JavaFX class libraries directly onto the Java platform
eliminates all of these concerns. <span> </span>If you
want to use some external Java code, just use it.</p> 
            <p class="MsoNormal"><b>Superior Development
Environment</b>.<span> </span>Attempting to debug JavaFX
Script within an Integrated Development Environment is at best a tricky
endeavor and at worst a waste of time. <span> </span>Additionally
only NetBeans, and to a lesser extent Eclipse, are the only viable JavaFX
Script capable IDEs.<span> </span>As the new JavaFX platform
is entirely based on Java, not only is debugging support first rate, the option
of choosing other Java IDEs opens up considerably.</p> 
            <p class="MsoNormal"><b>The new JavaFX results in, for lack of a better term, more
predictability</b>. <span> </span>One of the primary JavaFX
Script mechanisms used to define and place graphical assets (Nodes) into the
scenegraph is the object literal notation.<span> </span>Being static in nature, the notion of a sequential flow of events inside
an object literal may not make much sense.<span> </span>To compensate, JavaFX Script introduces the powerful concept of binding.<span> </span>Without question, binding is very elegant, but
it comes with a price.<span> </span>It is not
uncommon to see liberal and arguably unnecessary use of binding throughout JavaFX
script code.<span> </span></p> 
            <p class="MsoNormal">One can argue that bringing JavaFX to Java should lessen,
but certainly not eliminate, the need for binding.<span> </span>Rather than defining objects inside a static initializer,
JavaFX API objects are created and defined sequentially by constructor and method
calls respectively.<span> </span>By defining objects
in an order that makes sense, there is potential to eliminate a certain class
of dependencies that were previously resolved with binding. </p> 
            <p class="MsoNormal"><b>Improved performance.</b><span> </span>Although by default the JavaFX compiler generates
Java bytes codes from JavaFX Script source, there is a command-line option
which can be invoked to save the intermediate Java code that is produced as
part of the process.<span> </span>A brief perusal of
this source shows that even the most humble of JavaFX Script constructs churns
out a lot of complicated Java code.<span> </span>Eliminating
this overhead is bound to improve performance in many instances.<span> </span>Furthermore, significant optimizations to
memory and static footprint as well as startup time are underway.<span> </span>Finally a new lightweight, fast graphics
subsystem, dubbed project prism, will obviate the need to utilize older Java
graphics windowing systems. </p> 
            <p class="MsoNormal"><b>It’s not how you
feel, it’s how you look. </b><span> </span>A
superficial difference, but nonetheless one that should not be underestimated, lies
in what your code looks like.<span> </span>In JavaFX
Script, graphical Nodes are typically placed in the scenegraph via the
definition of object literals.<span> </span><span> </span>Even the least sophisticated of object literal
scenegraphs can be grouped and nested several levels deep, each nesting moving
the source code further to the right.<span> </span>Some JavaFX Script code is so indented it leaves little room to write
anything of consequence without having to split a line of code into two or more
lines.</p> 
            <p class="MsoNormal">It didn’t take very long to come up with these talking
points.<span> </span>No doubt, as development progresses
and more folks jump on board, additional benefits will become apparent. </p></m:defjc></m:rmargin></m:lmargin></m:dispdef></m:smallfrac>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: NetBeans with Subversion, SSH and Windows
DATE: 02/19/2010 19:59:31
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>Having spent too much time wrestling with the various components required to get NetBeans to access a subversion repository via ssh, I thought it might make sense to jot down a few notes in an effort to save others from such hardships.<br /></p> 
  <p>NetBeans does have built-in support for CVS, Mercurial and Subversion, but that doesn't mean that these source code revision systems work in a turnkey fashion.&nbsp; In particular, subversion, especially with Windows, does require some work.</p> 
  <p> 
  1. To start:<br />Get the <a href="http://java.sun.com/javase/downloads/index.jsp">latest JDK</a>.&nbsp; At the time of this writing it was JDK 6 update 18<br />Get the <a href="http://netbeans.org/downloads/index.html">latest version of NetBeans</a>,&nbsp; in this case, NetBeans 6.8.&nbsp; Install it referencing the latest JDK and furthermore, once inside NetBeans, utilize the update manager to make sure all of your modules are the latest and greatest.<br /></p> 
  <p>2. Upon starting NetBeans, the menu running along the top of the window has a &quot;Team&quot; entry.&nbsp; Click on Team and follow Subversion-&gt;Checkout (No, you won't be able to check anything out yet). This will bring up an error window which requires you to install a subversion client.<br /></p> 
  <p>Selecting the default action, which installs a subversion plugin for NetBeans, didn't result in much success. Instead choose the second option: &quot;Install Subversion Commandline Client&quot;. &nbsp;The recommended download for the client points to CollabNet, which is an excellent choice.&nbsp; Unfortunately the version referred to (v 1.5.5 for Windows) may ultimately prove problematic if you're communicating with a subversion repository that's based on a later version. For this exercise,  <a href="http://www.open.collab.net/downloads/subversion/">CollabNet Subversion Command-Line Client v1.6.9 (for Windows)</a> was downloaded and installed.<br /></p> 
  <p>3. Once Subversion is installed, clicking on Team-&gt;Subversion-&gt;Checkout again will hopefully bring up a second window which requires you fill in two textfields: (1) A repository URL and (2) a tunnel command.&nbsp; Let's focus on the tunnel command first.&nbsp; For windows this requires the equivalent of the Unix/Linux ssh command.&nbsp; To get this functionality <a href="http://www.chiark.greenend.org.uk/%7Esgtatham/putty/download.html">download and install a version of the PuTTY software package</a>.</p> 
  <p>4. Unlike the Collabnet Subversion Client, PuTTY installation does not put the executables of this package into your PATH.&nbsp; Make sure to add the PuTTY path (typically C:\\Program Files\\PuTTY) to your PATH.</p> 
  <p>5. You'll need to edit a subversion configuration file to specify the tunnel command as follows:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <pre>C:\\&gt;cd %APPDATA%\\Subversion
C:\\&gt;edit config 
</pre> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Look for the comment that starts like this:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <pre>#ssh = $SVN_SSH ssh</pre> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Uncomment it and make it look like the statement below.&nbsp; The <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">plink.exe</font> executable is part of the PuTTY software bundle:<br /></p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <pre>ssh = $SVN_SSH plink.exe -l <b><i>user</i></b> -pw <b><i>password</i></b> 
</pre> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Where <i>user</i> is your remote user name and <i>password</i> is your user's password. You can also use private key authentication if you're uncomfortable with putting your password in the clear, which might look something like:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <pre>ssh = $SVN_SSH plink.exe -l <i>user</i> -i C:\\my_private_key.ppk</pre> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>6. Prior to trying out Netbeans, first connect to your subversion repository via ssh as follows:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <pre>C:\\&gt; plink.exe <b><i>user</i></b>@<b><i>host</i></b></pre> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Prior to running this command, the NetBeans attempt to access the subversion repository hangs, apparently looking for a host fingerprint cache entry.&nbsp; The <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">plink.exe</font> command above accomplishes its creation, once the correct password is entered.&nbsp; In addition, it assures that SSH is correctly set up on your Subversion repository server.<br /></p> 
  <p>7. Returning to the Team-&gt;Subversion-&gt;Checkout selection from the main NetBeans window, now it's time to fill in the two textfields.&nbsp; The first entry should look something like this:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <pre>svn+ssh://<i><b>host</b></i>/<b><i>subversion_repository_path</i></b></pre> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>for example:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <pre>svn+ssh://127.0.0.1/home/svn/myProject</pre> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Next should come the tunnel command which should be similar to the entry placed in the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">%APPDATA%\\Subversion\\config</font> file, namely</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <pre>plink -l <i><b>user</b></i> -pw <i><b>password</b></i></pre> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p> Where, again, <i>user i</i>s your remote user name and <i>password</i> is this user's password.</p> 
  <p>8. With information correctly filled in, and assuming your subversion server is correctly configured, you should be able to begin utilizing subversion through ssh with NetBeans.&nbsp; For further information, check out:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li><a href="http://netbeans.org/kb/docs/ide/subversion.html">Guided Tour of Subversion</a> with NetBeans</li> 
    <li><a href="http://wiki.netbeans.org/FaqSubversionSSH">NetBeans Subversion FAQ with SSH</a><br /></li> 
  </ul> 
  <p>Good Luck!<br /></p> 
  <p><br /> </p> 
  <p><br /></p>
-----

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: JavaFX, Sockets and Threading: Lessons Learned
DATE: 02/10/2010 14:47:20
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
When contemplating how machine-dependent applications might communicate with Java/JavaFX, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Java_Native_Interface">JNI or the Java Native Interface</a>, having been created for just such a task, would likely be the first mechanism that comes to mind.&nbsp; Although JNI works just fine thank you, a group of us ultimately decided against using it for a small project because, among others:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>Errors in your JNI implementation can corrupt the Java Virtual Machine in very strange ways, leading to difficult diagnosis.<br /></li> 
    <li>JNI can be time consuming and tedious, especially if there's a varied amount of interchange between the Native and Java platforms.</li> 
    <li>For each OS/Platform supported, a separate JNI implementation would need to be created and maintained.</li> 
  </ul> 
  <p>Instead we opted for something a bit more mundane, namely sockets.&nbsp; The socket programming paradigm has been around a long time, is well understood and spans a multitude of hardware/software platforms.&nbsp;&nbsp; Rather than spending time defining JNI interfaces, just open up a socket between applications and send messages back and forth, defining your own message protocol.&nbsp; Following are some reflections on using sockets with JavaFX and Java.&nbsp; For the sake of simplicity, we'll skip the native stuff and focus on how sockets can be incorporated into a JavaFX application in a thread safe manner.<br /></p> 
  <h4>Sockets and Threading<br /></h4> 
  <p>Socket programming, especially in Java, lends itself to utilizing threads.&nbsp; Because a socket <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">read()</font> will block waiting for input, a common practice is to place the read loop in a background thread enabling you to continue processing while waiting for input at the same time.&nbsp; And if you're doing this work entirely in Java, you'll find that both ends of the socket connection -- the &quot;server&quot; side and the &quot;client&quot; side -- share a great deal of common code.&nbsp; Recognizing this, an abstract class called <a href="/jtc/resource/socket/GenericSocket.java">GenericSocket.java</a> was created which is responsible for housing the common functionality shared by &quot;server&quot; and &quot;client&quot; sockets including the setup of a reader thread to handle socket reads asynchronously.</p> 
  <p>For this simple example, two implementations of the abstract <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">GenericSocket</font> class, one called <a href="/jtc/resource/socket/SocketServer.java">SocketServer.java</a>, the other called <a href="/jtc/resource/socket/SocketClient.java">SocketClient.java</a> have been supplied.&nbsp; The primary difference between these two classes lies in the type of socket they use.&nbsp; SocketServer.java uses <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">java.net.ServerSocket</font><a href="http://java.net.ServerSocket">,</a>&nbsp; while SocketClient.java uses <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">java.net.Socket<span style="text-decoration: underline;"></span></font>.&nbsp; The respective implementations contain the details required to set up and tear down these slightly different socket types.</p> 
  <h4>Dissecting the Java Socket Framework<br /></h4> 
  <p>If you want to utilize the provided Java socket framework with JavaFX, you need to understand this very important fact:&nbsp; <b><i>JavaFX is not thread safe and all JavaFX manipulation should be run on the JavaFX processing thread</i></b>.<sup>1</sup> If you allow a JavaFX application to interact with a thread other than
the main processing thread, unpredictable errors will
occur.&nbsp; Recall that the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">GenericSocket</font> class created a reader thread to handle socket reads.&nbsp; In order to avoid non-main-thread-processing and its pitfalls with our socket classes, a few modifications must take place.</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><font size="1">[1] Stolen from <a href="http://www.amazon.com/JavaFX-Developing-Rich-Internet-Applications/dp/013701287X/ref=sr_1_2?ie=UTF8&amp;s=books&amp;qid=1264908598&amp;sr=8-2">JavaFX: Developing Rich Internet Applications</a> - Thanks <a href="/clarkeman">Jim Clarke</a></font></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <h5>Step 1: Identify Resources Off the Main Thread<br /></h5> 
  <blockquote> </blockquote> 
  <p>The first step to operating in a thread safe manner is to identify those resources in your Java code, residing off the main thread, that might need to be accessed by JavaFX.&nbsp; For our example, we define two abstract methods, the first, <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">onMessage()</font>, is called whenever a line of text is read from the socket.&nbsp; The <a href="/jtc/resource/socket/GenericSocket.java">GenericSocket.java</a> code will make a call to this method upon encountering socket input. Let's take a look at the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SocketReaderThread</font> code inside <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">GenericSocket</font>, to get a feel for what's going on.</p> 
  <pre>    class SocketReaderThread extends Thread {

        @Override
        public void run() {
            String line;
            waitForReady();
            /\*
             \* Read from from input stream one line at a time
             \*/
            try {
                if (input != null) {
                    while ((line = input.readLine()) != null) {
                        if (debugFlagIsSet(DEBUG_IO)) {
                            System.out.println("recv&gt; " + line);
                        }
                        /\*
                         \* The onMessage() method has to be implemented by
                         \* a sublclass.  If used in conjunction with JavaFX,
                         \* use Entry.deferAction() to force this method to run
                         \* on the main thread.
                         \*/
                        <b>onMessage(line);</b>
                    }
                }
            } catch (Exception e) {
                if (debugFlagIsSet(DEBUG_EXCEPTIONS)) {
                    e.printStackTrace();
                }
            } finally {
                notifyTerminate();
            }
        }
</pre> 
  <p> Because <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">onMessage()</font> is called off the main thread and inside <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SocketReaderThread</font>, the comment states that some additional work, which we'll explain soon, must take place to assure main thread processing.<br /></p> 
  <p>Our second method, <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">onClosedStatus()</font>, is called whenever the status of the socket changes (either opened or closed for whatever reason).&nbsp; This abstract routine is called in different places within <a href="/jtc/resource/socket/GenericSocket.java">GenericSocket.java</a> -- sometimes on the main thread, sometimes not.&nbsp; To assure thread safety, we'll employ the same technique as with <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">onMessage()</font>.</p> 
  <h5>Step 2: Create a Java Interface with your Identified Methods<br /></h5> 
  <p> Once identified, these method signatures have to be declared inside a Java interface.&nbsp; For example, our socket framework includes a <a href="/jtc/resource/socket/SocketListener.java">SocketListener.java</a> interface file which looks like this:</p> 
  <pre>   package genericsocket;

   public interface SocketListener {
       public void onMessage(String line);
       public void onClosedStatus(Boolean isClosed);
   }
</pre> 
  <h5>Step 3: Create Your Java Class, Implementing Your Defined Interface<br /></h5> 
  <p>With our <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SocketListener</font> interface defined, let's take a step-by-step look at how the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SocketServer</font> class is implemented inside <a href="/jtc/resource/socket/SocketServer.java">SocketServer</a><a href="/jtc/resource/socket/SocketServer.java">.java</a>.&nbsp; One of the first requirements is to import a special Java class which will allow us to do main thread processing, achieved as follows:
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  </p> 
  <pre>   import com.sun.javafx.runtime.Entry;
</pre> 
  <p>Next, comes the declaration of <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SocketServer</font>.&nbsp; Notice that in addition to extending the abstract <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">GenericSocket</font> class it also must implement our <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SocketListener</font> interface too:<br /></p> 
  <pre>   public class SocketServer extends GenericSocket implements SocketListener {
</pre> 
  <p>Inside the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SocketServer</font> definition, a variable called <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">fxListener</font> of type <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SocketListener</font> is declared:</p> 
  <pre>       private SocketListener fxListener;
</pre> 
  <p>The constructor for <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SocketServer</font> must include a reference to <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">fxListener</font>.&nbsp; The other arguments are used to specify a port number and some debug flags.<br /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <pre>       public SocketServer(SocketListener <b>fxListener</b>,
                int port, int debugFlags) {
            super(port, debugFlags);
            <b>this.fxListener = fxListener;</b>
       }
</pre> 
  <p>Next, let's examine the implementation of the two methods which are declared in the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SocketListener</font> interface.&nbsp; The first, <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">onMessage()</font>, looks like this:</p> 
  <pre>       /\*\*
        \* Called whenever a message is read from the socket.  In
        \* JavaFX, this method must be run on the main thread and
        \* is accomplished by the Entry.deferAction() call.  Failure to do so
        \* \*will\* result in strange errors and exceptions.
        \* @param line Line of text read from the socket.
        \*/
       @Override
       public void onMessage(<b>final</b> String line) {
           <b>Entry.deferAction</b>(new Runnable() {

               @Override
               public void run() {
                   fxListener.onMessage(line);
               }
           });
       }
</pre> 
  <p>As the comment points out, the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">Entry.deferAction()</font> call enables <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">fxListener.onMessage()</font> to be executed on the main thread.&nbsp; It takes as an argument an instance of the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">Runnable</font> class and, within its<font face="courier new,courier,monospace"> run()</font> method, makes a call to <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">fxListener.onMessage()</font>.&nbsp; Another important point to notice is that <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">onMessage()</font>'s <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">String</font> argument&nbsp; must be declared as <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">final</font>.</p> 
  <p>Along the same line, the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">onClosedStatus()</font> method is implemented as follows:</p> 
  <pre>       /\*\*
        \* Called whenever the open/closed status of the Socket
        \* changes.  In JavaFX, this method must be run on the main thread and
        \* is accomplished by the Entry.deferAction() call.  Failure to do so
        \* will\* result in strange errors and exceptions.
        \* @param isClosed true if the socket is closed
        \*/
       @Override
       public void onClosedStatus(<b>final</b> Boolean isClosed) {
           <b>Entry.deferAction</b>(new Runnable() {

               @Override
               public void run() {
                   fxListener.onClosedStatus(isClosed);
               }
           });
       }
</pre> 
  <p>Another <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">Runnable</font> is scheduled via <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">Entry.deferAction()</font> to run <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">fxlistener.onClosedStatus()</font> on the main thread. Again, <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">onClosedStatus()</font>'s Boolean argument must also be defined as <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">final</font>.</p> 
  <h4>Accessing the Framework within JavaFX<br /></h4> 
  <p>With this work behind us, now we can integrate the framework into JavaFX.&nbsp; But before elaborating on the details, lets show screenshots of two simple JavaFX applications, <i>SocketServer</i> and <i>SocketClient</i> which, when run together, can send and receive text messages to one another over a socket.</p> 
  <p><a href="/jtc/resource/socket/SocketServer.jnlp"><img src="/jtc/resource/socket/SocketServer.jpg" style="width: 371px; height: 264px;" /></a> <a href="/jtc/resource/socket/SocketClient.jnlp"><img src="/jtc/resource/socket/SocketClient.jpg" style="width: 371px; height: 265px;" /></a></p> 
  <p>These JavaFX programs were developed in NetBeans and utilize the recently announced <a href="http://wiki.netbeans.org/JavaFXComposer">NetBeans JavaFX Composer tool</a>.&nbsp; You can click on the images to execute these programs via Java WebStart.&nbsp; Note: depending upon your platform, your system may ask for permission prior to allowing these applications to network.&nbsp; Source for the JavaFX applications and the socket framework in the form of NetBeans projects can be downloaded <a href="/jtc/resource/socket/socketfx.zip">here</a>.</p> 
  <h5>Step 4: Integrating into JavaFX</h5> 
  <p>To access the socket framework within JavaFX, you must implement the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SocketListener</font> class that was created for this project.&nbsp; To give you a feel for how this is done with our JavaFX <i>SocketServer</i> application, here are some code excerpts from the project's Main.fx file, in particular the definition of our <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">ServerSocketListener</font> class:</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <pre>public class ServerSocketListener extends SocketListener {
    public override function onMessage(line: String) {
        insert line into recvListView.items;
    }
    public override function onClosedStatus(isClosed : java.lang.Boolean) {
        socketClosed = isClosed;
        tryingToConnect = false;
        if (autoConnectCheckbox.selected) {
            connectButtonAction();
        }
    }
}
</pre> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p>Sparing all of the gory details, the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">onMessage()</font> method will place the line of text read from the socket in to a JavaFX <a href="http://java.sun.com/javafx/1.2/docs/api/javafx.scene.control/javafx.scene.control.ListView.html">ListView</a> control which is displayed in the program user interface.&nbsp; The <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">onClosedStatus()</font> method primarily updates the local <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">socketClosed</font> variable and attempts to reconnect the socket if the autoconnect option has been selected.</p> 
  <p>To demonstrate how the socket is created, we examine the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">connectButtonAction()</font> function:<br /></p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <pre>var socketServer : SocketServer;

...

public function connectButtonAction (): Void {
     if (not tryingToConnect) {
            if (socketClosed) {
                <b>socketServer = new
                    SocketServer(ServerSocketListener{},
                    java.lang.Integer.parseInt(portTextbox.text),
                    javafx.util.Bits.bitOr(GenericSocket.DEBUG_STATUS,
                                           GenericSocket.DEBUG_IO));</b>
                tryingToConnect = true;
                <b>socketServer.connect();</b>
            }
        }
    }
</pre> 
  </blockquote> 
  <blockquote> 
    <pre></pre> 
  </blockquote> 
  <pre></pre> 
  <p>Whenever the user clicks on the &quot;Connect&quot; button, the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">connectButtonAction()</font> function will be called.&nbsp; On invocation, if the socket isn't already open, it will create a new <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SocketServer</font> instance.&nbsp; Recognize also that the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SocketServer</font> constructor includes an instance of the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">ServerSocketListener</font> class which was defined above.</p> 
  <p>To round this out, when the user clicks on the &quot;Disconnect&quot; button, the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">disconnectButtonAction()</font> function is called.&nbsp; When invoked, it tears down the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SocketServer</font> instance.<br /></p> 
  <pre>   function disconnectButtonAction (): Void {
        tryingToConnect = false;
        socketServer.shutdown();
   }</pre> 
  <h4>Conclusion</h4> 
  <p>Admittedly, there's a fair amount to digest here.&nbsp; Hopefully, by carefully reviewing the steps and looking at the <a href="/jtc/resource/socket/socketfx.zip">complete code listing</a>, this can serve as a template if you wish to accomplish something similar in JavaFX.<br /></p> 
  <pre><p>
</p></pre> 
  <p><br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: GlassFish on a Handheld
DATE: 12/31/2009 14:14:14
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>Until now, the idea of running something akin to a Java EE application server on a small handeld device would have been greeted with ridicule.&nbsp; Suddenly  that notion doesn't seem so ridiculous when considering the recent technology that's been made available. In particular, the following software advances make this pipe dream more of a reality:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li> <a href="http://java.sun.com/javase/embedded/">Java Standard Edition for Embedded Devices</a>: A series of Java virtual machines are available from Sun for many of the popular embedded hardware/OS platforms. They are not only Java SE compatible, but have been space optimized from a static footprint and RAM perspective to perform in embedded environments.&nbsp; To give you an idea of some of those optimizations, read <a href="http://java.dzone.com/articles/reduced-footprint-java-se-brin">this</a>.<br /></li> 
    <li><a href="http://java.sun.com/developer/technicalArticles/JavaEE/JavaEE6Overview.html">Java Enterprise Edition 6 Platform Specification and the Web Profile</a>:&nbsp; The Java EE 6 specification allows for the creation of a subset of the component technologies, called &quot;profiles&quot;.&nbsp; The first of these has been dubbed the Web Profile and contains the common technologies required to create small to medium web applications.&nbsp; Rather than having to use a full blown Java EE application server in all its glory, you can take advantage of a significantly smaller, less complex framework.<br /></li> 
    <li><a href="https://embedded-glassfish.dev.java.net/">Embedded GlassFish</a>: This capability, which is now part of GlassFish v3, enables you to run GlassFish inside your Java application, as opposed to the other way around. Simply put, there is no need install GlassFish or create GlassFish domains in this scenario.&nbsp; Instead, you include an instance of glassfish-embedded-web.jar in your classpath, make a few GlassFish Embedded API calls from your standard Java application, and voila! you've got a web application up and running.<br /></li> 
  </ul> 
  <h4>The Hardware</h4> 
  <p>Rather than opting for one of the many embedded development platforms around (because I'm cheap), I instead decided to investigate what was available from a handheld perspective and see if that environment could be adapted to suit my needs.&nbsp; After some searching, it looked like the Nokia N810 just might fit the bill.&nbsp; Courtesy of my buddy Eric Bruno, here's a picture of the N810:<br /></p> 
  <p><img src="/jtc/resource/embedded-glassfish/N810.jpg" /> </p> 
  <p>To get a feel for this very capable device, check out <a href="http://www.ddj.com/java/208801979">Eric's Article</a>. What most interested me was that (1) it has 128MB RAM, (2)&nbsp; a 400MHz Arm v6 processor, (3) runs a common embedded version of Linux (<a href="http://maemo.org/">maemo</a>), (4) has a version of Java SE Embedded (from Sun) which runs fine on this platform and (5) can be had for a relatively affordable price on eBay.</p> 
  <h4>The Operating System</h4> 
  <p>The Nokia N810 is powered by the <a href="http://maemo.org/">maemo</a> distribution, an open source platform with a thriving community of developers.&nbsp; Knowing full well that any attempt to get a web application up and running on this device would stretch its resources to the limit, it was necessary to reclaim as much RAM as possible before starting out.&nbsp; Here's a description of some of the kludgery involved:<br /></p> 
  <ol> 
    <li>You'll need to download and install some additional applications which can be retrieved from the N810's Application Manager program.&nbsp; They include: <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">rootsh</font> to enable root access to the device and <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">openssh-client</font> and <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">openssh-server</font> to remotely access the device.<br /></li> 
    <li>A quick and dirty way to reclaim RAM is to shut down the X-server and kill all of the windowing applications that run in parallel. There are certainly more elegant ways to do this, but in addition to being cheap, I'm lazy too.&nbsp; What you quickly find out is that any attempt to manually kill off some of these processes results in a reboot of the tablet.&nbsp; Why? Because by default, the N810 includes a watchdog process that monitors the state of the system.&nbsp; If it detects any irregularities, it forces a restart.</li> 
    <li>You can get around this problem by putting the device into what is called &quot;R&amp;D&quot; mode.&nbsp; This is achieved by downloading the &quot;flasher&quot; utility from maemo.org and establishing a USB connection between the N810 and your host computer.&nbsp; Directions for this process can be found <a href="http://wiki.maemo.org/Updating_the_tablet_firmware">here</a>.</li> 
    <li>Once established, you can invoke the following flasher command:&nbsp; <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">flasher3.5 --set-rd-flags=no-lifeguard-reset. </font>If this was done successfully, you'll notice that a wrench appears on the tablet screen when it is rebooted.<br /></li> 
    <li>Once in R&amp;D mode you'll have to remotely ssh into the device via the WiFi connection. The following script called <a href="/jtc/resource/embedded-glassfish/set-headless.sh"><font face="courier new,courier,monospace">set-headless.sh</font></a> has been provided to kill off the windowing system.&nbsp; After executing this script, the N810 in effect becomes a headless device.&nbsp; The only way to communicate with it is through the network.</li> 
  </ol> 
  <h4>The Environment</h4> 
  <p>Here's what was required to get the web application up and running:</p> 
  <ol> 
    <li>Ssh into the device.<br /></li> 
    <li>Download an <a href="http://java.sun.com/javase/downloads/embedded.jsp">evaluation copy of Java SE Embedded</a> (ARMv6 Linux - Headless).&nbsp; For this example the download file was gunzip'ed and untar'ed into the N810 device's /usr directory resulting in a new /usr/ejre1.6.0_10 directory.</li> 
    <li>Download a copy of <a href="http://download.java.net/maven/glassfish/org/glassfish/extras/glassfish-embedded-web/3.0/">glassfish-embedded-web-3.0.jar</a> and place this file in the /usr/ejre1.6.0_10/lib directory.</li> 
    <li>Modify your PATH variable to include /usr/ejre1.6.0_10/bin and set your JAVA_HOME variable to /usr/ejre1.6.0_10</li> 
    <li>Create a temporary directory, for this example we'll create a /root/tmp directory.</li> 
    <li>Compile the following Java source file, <a href="/jtc/resource/embedded-glassfish/Embedded.java">Embedded.java</a>,&nbsp; on a JDK equipped system, which is a slightly hacked version of the original provided by <a href="/alexismp">Alexis Moussine-Pouchkine</a>.</li> 
    <li>Create a glassfish directory under /root/tmp/ and place the compiled Embedded.class file there</li> 
    <li>Download the sample hello web application, <a href="https://glassfish.dev.java.net/downloads/quickstart/hello.war">hello.war</a>, and place it in the /root/tmp directory.<a href="https://glassfish.dev.java.net/downloads/quickstart/hello.war"><br /></a></li> 
    <li>Reclaim as much RAM as possible by running the <a href="/jtc/resource/embedded-glassfish/set-headless.sh">set-headless.sh</a> script<br /></li> 
    <li>Run the web application from inside the /root/tmp directory via the the following command-line invocation: <br /></li> 
  </ol> 
  <pre>     # java -cp /usr/ejre1.6.0_10/lib/glassfish-embedded-web-3.0.jar:. glassfish/Embedded hello.war 600
</pre> 
  <p>As the N810 cannot match even the most modest of laptops in terms of performance, be prepared to wait around a little before the application is ready.&nbsp; Check this <a href="/jtc/resource/embedded-glassfish/log.txt">output</a> to see what is printed out to the console during invocation.</p> 
  <p>For this run the, N810 was assigned a WiFi IP address of 192.168.1.102, thus the browser is pointed to that address with port 8888.&nbsp; Here's what comes up:</p> 
  <p><img src="/jtc/resource/embedded-glassfish/N810hello1.jpg" /> </p> 
  <p>And Interacting with the web app produces this:</p> 
  <p> <img src="/jtc/resource/embedded-glassfish/N810hello2.jpg" /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>So this is obviously not ready for prime time, but it does open up a whole lot more possibilities in the near future.</p> 
  <p>Happy New Year!&nbsp;</p>
-----

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Consumer Electronics: Your Utility Company's Best Friend
DATE: 11/04/2009 13:30:39
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Personal
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
If you're reading this article, the chances are real good that your home is full of electronic gadgets.&nbsp; Moreover many are permanently plugged into wall sockets. &nbsp; Having recently purchased a <a href="http://www.p3international.com/products/p4460.html">P3 International P4460 Kill A Watt EZ</a> power meter, I've been running around getting a feel for how much energy some of these common components use. 
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  <p><img src="/jtc/resource/consumer_electronics/p4460.jpg" /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>For this first table, I wanted to see how much energy was being consumed by audio/visual components that were <i>plugged into the wall, but not powered on.</i>&nbsp; Indeed some of these numbers are eye-opening:</p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <table height="288" cellspacing="0" cellpadding="0" border="1" width="640"> 
    <tbody> 
      <tr> 
        <td align="center"><u><b>&nbsp;Device</b></u></td> 
        <td align="center"><u><b>&nbsp;Watts consumed (powered off)<br /></b></u></td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td> Visio 22&quot; LCD HDTV (circa 2007)</td> 
        <td>&nbsp;1.58</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td> Visio 32&quot; LCD HDTV (circa 2009)</td> 
        <td>&nbsp;1.18</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td> 25&quot; RCA Tube TV (circa 1990)</td> 
        <td>&nbsp;4.17</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td> Marantz AV Surround Reveiver SR7200</td> 
        <td>&nbsp;2.79</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td> Marantz CD Changer CC9100</td> 
        <td>&nbsp;1.43</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td> Marantz DVD Player DV6200</td> 
        <td>&nbsp;3.60</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td> Boston Micro90pv II Subwoofer</td> 
        <td> <span style="color: #ff0000;">14.53</span></td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td> Microsoft Xbox 360</td> 
        <td>&nbsp;3.64</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td> Nintendo Wii</td> 
        <td>&nbsp;2.70</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td> Sony PlayStation 2</td> 
        <td>&nbsp;0.67</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td> Scientific Atlanta Explorer 4250HD set top</td> 
        <td> <span style="color: #ff0000;">17.27</span></td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td> Scientific Atlanta Explorer 8300HD DVR set top</td> 
        <td> <span style="color: #ff0000;">20.00</span></td> 
      </tr> 
    </tbody> 
  </table> 
  <p>The granularity of the P4460 is hundredths of kilowatt-hours, which means that in order to get a decent reading on a low-power device, you've got to leave it attached for a while.&nbsp; No doubt some of these measurements could be more accurate, but I think you get the point.&nbsp; Of note:</p> 
  <ol> 
    <li>The 4 components that comprise the audio system consume over 22 watts all day, every day 24x7.</li> 
    <li>As set top boxes gain more functionality (e.g. DVR), they suck up even more power.&nbsp;&nbsp; At this rate, you'd think the power utility would subsidize the cable companies to get as many of these things installed as possible.</li> 
    <li>All told, the devices in the preceding table consume an amount of energy similar to a 75 watt incandescent light bulb being left on all the time.</li> 
  </ol> 
  <p>For the most part, these components are non-critical, and one could argue that some (e.g&nbsp; the stereo) should be connected to a power strip with an on/off button. This should not pose much of an inconvenience.&nbsp; However, it's a completely different story when it comes to the video components.&nbsp; When powered cycled, set top boxes take forever to reach full functionality as they reboot over the cable network.&nbsp; And televisions, if left untethered to electric power for too long, go through this whole channel search sequence when they're powered up.&nbsp; In short, when it comes to video, you're currently stuck with paying the hidden price of &quot;convenience&quot;.</p> 
  <p>For the computer nerd in us, here's a table comprising some of the computer/network-related components that are left on 24x7.</p> 
  <table height="200" cellspacing="0" cellpadding="0" border="1" width="625"> 
    <tbody> 
      <tr> 
        <td valign="top" align="center"><u><b>Device<br /></b></u></td> 
        <td align="center"><u><b>&nbsp;Watts Consumed<br /></b></u></td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td valign="top">Belkin Wireless G Router<br /></td> 
        <td>&nbsp;5.09</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td valign="top">Motorola SBV5120 SURFboard Cable Modem<br /></td> 
        <td>&nbsp;5.40</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td valign="top">Netgear ProSafe 16 port 10/100 Switch (FS116)<br /></td> 
        <td>&nbsp;8.33</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td valign="top"> 
          <table height="20" cellspacing="0" cellpadding="0" border="0" width="465" style="border-collapse: collapse;"><col width="303" style="width: 227pt;" /> 
            <tbody> 
              <tr height="17" style="height: 12.75pt;"> 
                <td height="17" width="303" style="height: 12.75pt; width: 227pt;">HP Photosmart
  c6280 printer (circa 2008) (networked)<br /></td> 
              </tr> 
            </tbody> 
          </table> 
        </td> 
        <td>&nbsp;4.78</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td valign="top">HP Laserjet 4P (circa 1994)<br /></td> 
        <td>&nbsp;5.32</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td valign="top">Canon ImageCLASS MF4270 all-in-one laser printer (networked) (circa 2009)<br /></td> 
        <td>&nbsp;3.60</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td valign="top">Fit-PC Slim with external USB hard disk (network file server)<br /></td> 
        <td>&nbsp;7.50</td> 
      </tr> 
    </tbody> 
  </table> 
  <p>A decade ago, devices like those listed above were virtually non-existent in the home.&nbsp; So what's the cost of being Internet ready anytime, anywhere?&nbsp; About 40 watts all day and all night. Some final points:</p> 
  <ol> 
    <li>In general, carefully consider keeping around older gadgets.&nbsp; The circa 1990s devices definitely use more energy than their newer, more functional counterparts.</li> 
    <li>Notice no computers are included.&nbsp; You know you have more than one, some (present company included) have way more than one.&nbsp; Let's hope you're turning these off or at least putting them to sleep at night!<br /></li> 
  </ol>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Rainer
EMAIL: rainer@ultra-secure.de
URL: http://www.ultra-secure.de
DATE: 11/19/2009 16:46:17

<p>Reading this a bit late, but thought you might want to know:<br/>
When Switzerland's Telco Swisscom started to roll out their first generation DVR/IPTV solution to customers, it drew so much power (even when not running) that critics quickly calculated that if people would actually buy and use that device in large numbers (like TVs or old-school VCRs), there would simply be not enough power in Switzerland's electrical grid to make them run all day.<br/>
It would cost maybe a dollar or two to fit every &quot;TV&quot; with a little flash storage to make the configuration persistent. Then you could power it off completely when you don't need it. It works for wireless routers, so why not in every other device?<br/>
Imagine if you would need to have your car sort-of-running all the time because it would lose some of its &quot;configuration&quot; otherwise.</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Java RTS 2.2 Early Access 2 Released
DATE: 09/08/2009 05:29:44
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
Sun recently announced the availability of a second edition of its Java Real-Time System (Java RTS) version 2.2 Early Access.&nbsp; In a <a href="/jtc/entry/early_access_version_of_java">previous blog post</a>, many of the important new features of this release were highlighted.&nbsp; But I felt compelled to jot down a few additional words, not only because there's a newer version of the software to evaluate, but because there's one interesting component that deserves a little more exposure.</p> 
  <p>I spent some time today speaking with my compadre, Eric Bruno, author of <a href="http://www.amazon.com/Real-Time-Java-Programming-RTS/dp/0137142986/ref=sr_1_2?ie=UTF8&amp;s=books&amp;qid=1251922771&amp;sr=8-2"><i><b>Real-Time Java Programming: With Java RTS</b></i></a>.&nbsp; He clued me in on the fact that our engineers have performed the daunting task of backporting the JDK 7 client compiler onto Java RTS 2.2.&nbsp; The engineering team is particularly proud of this feat, and ought to be.&nbsp; It not only enhances Java RTS performance, it also enables them to better stay in lock step with the latest client hotspot VM developments, something the latest Java SE 6 releases may not entirely do.&nbsp; For some insight into the engineering trade-offs associated creating this latest version of Java RTS, check out <a href="/roland/entry/64_bit_java_rts">Roland Westerlin's entry</a>.<br /></p> 
  <p>You can find further information about Java RTS <a href="http://java.sun.com/javase/technologies/realtime/index.jsp">here</a>.&nbsp; And you can download a version of the software for evaluation <a href="http://java.sun.com/javase/technologies/realtime/rts/">here</a>.</p> 
  <p>Cheers.</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: JavaFX Scenegraph Performance Revisited
DATE: 08/11/2009 08:14:07
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p> </p> 
  <p>Prior to the release of JavaFX 1.2, an <a href="/jtc/entry/node_count_and_javafx_performance">earlier blog post</a> explained how creating an application with a scenegraph containing a large number of nodes can have performance implications.&nbsp; That entry was subsequently picked up by <a href="http://java.dzone.com/">Java Lobby</a> and recently posted <a href="http://java.dzone.com/articles/node-count-and-javafx">here</a>.&nbsp; Partly because it was a few months old, it resulted in a rash of, shall we say, interesting comments.</p> 
  <p>As one commenter pointed out, the initial results represent JavaFX performance in the 1.0/1.1 timeframe.&nbsp; JavaFX 1.2 has since been released, and performance has improved substantially.&nbsp; As a case in point, you can click on the image below to run the clock application.&nbsp; This same application, developed and compiled with JavaFX 1.1 can be run from the previous blog post.&nbsp; Further instructions are <a href="/jtc/entry/node_count_and_javafx_performance">there</a>.&nbsp; <br /></p> 
  <p><a href="/jtc/resource/javafx-node-perf-2/Digits.jnlp"><img src="/jtc/resource/javafx-node-perf-2/BulbClockNode.jpg" /></a></p> 
  <p>This application, compiled with JavaFX 1.2 and run on identical hardware,
uses about a third of the CPU resources of the original version.&nbsp; Specifically, using OpenSolaris <a href="http://docs.sun.com/app/docs/doc/816-5166/vmstat-1m?l=ru&amp;a=view">vmstat(1M)</a> to monitor CPU usage, the following average statistics were collected for one minute when the clock display is updated every 10th of a second.&nbsp; The abbreviations have the following meanings:<br /></p> 
  <ul> 
    <li><b>us</b> = percentage usage of CPU time in <i>user</i> space<br /></li> 
    <li><b>sy</b> = percentage usage of CPU time in <i>system</i> space<br /></li> 
    <li><b>id</b> = percentage usage of CPU time idling</li> 
    <li>The sum of (us + sy + id) should approximate 100%. </li> 
  </ul> 
  <p>And here are the utilization numbers:</p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <table cellspacing="1" cellpadding="1" border="1" style="width: 100%;"> 
    <tbody> 
      <tr> 
        <td align="center"><b>&nbsp;Version</b></td> 
        <td align="center"><b>&nbsp;# Nodes per Digit<br /></b></td> 
        <td align="center"><b>&nbsp;CPU Utilization<br /></b></td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td>&nbsp;BulbClockNode JavaFX 1.1<br /></td> 
        <td>&nbsp;27 BulbNodes<br /></td> 
        <td>&nbsp;us: 22%&nbsp; sy: 2%&nbsp; id: 76%</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td>&nbsp;BulbClockNode JavaFX 1.2</td> 
        <td>&nbsp;27 BulbNodes</td> 
        <td>&nbsp;us: 7%&nbsp; sy: 2%&nbsp; id: 91%</td> 
      </tr> 
    </tbody> 
  </table><br />Yes,&nbsp; performance has improved significantly and will continue to do so.&nbsp; In fact, the JavaFX team is promising even better results with the advent of JavaFX 1.3 (code named SoMa), when considerable refining of the underlying architecture will take place.&nbsp; At this stage in it's lifecycle, it's important to &quot;subscribe&quot; to the JavaFX technology.&nbsp; Advances are coming fast and furious, and they don't promise to slow down anytime soon.<br />

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Getting the JavaFX Screen Size 
DATE: 07/24/2009 10:11:08
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>In a <a href="/jtc/entry/avoiding_unnecessary_bindstorms">previous post</a>, I showed one way in which you could size the nodes in your scenegraph relative to the screen size without having to use binding, thus eliminating the potential for a <a href="/jtc/entry/avoiding_unnecessary_bindstorms">bindstorm</a>.&nbsp; At the time, the suggestion was to get the screen coordiantes via a call to AWT (Advanced Windowing Toolkit) as follows:</p> 
  <pre>&nbsp;<span style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">var AWTtoolkit = java.awt.Toolkit.getDefaultToolkit ();</span><br style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);"><span style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);"> var screenSizeFromAWT = AWTtoolkit.getScreenSize ();</span>
</pre> 
  <p> This currently works for JavaFX Desktop deployments but is far from an optimal solution primarily because it is non-portable.&nbsp; There is no guarantee that AWT will exist-- in fact I'm pretty sure it will not exist -- in the JavaFX Mobile and JavaFX TV space.&nbsp; So attempting to utilize the code snippet above in a JavaFX Mobile or TV context will result in an error.&nbsp; Unfortunately at the time of the original post (Java FX 1.1), I didn't know of a better alternative.</p> 
  <p>With the advent of JavaFX 1.2, this problem is solved.&nbsp;&nbsp; A new class called <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">javafx.Stage.Screen</font>&nbsp; is provided which describes the characteristics of the display.&nbsp; The API definition of the class can be found <a href="http://java.sun.com/javafx/1.2/docs/api/javafx.stage/javafx.stage.Screen.html">here</a>.&nbsp; So now you can query the JavaFX runtime in a portable fashion to get the screen coordinates as follows:</p> 
  <pre>&nbsp;<code style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);"><b>import</b> javafx.stage.\*;
 //set Stage boundaries to consume entire screen
 Stage {</code><p><code style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">     fullscreen: true
     width: <a href="http://Screen.primary.bounds.minX">Screen.primary.bounds.minX</a>  as Integer
     height: <a href="http://Screen.primary.bounds.minY">Screen.primary.bounds.minY</a>  as Integer
     <span style="font-weight: bold;">...</span></code><code style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">
 }</code>

</p><p>&nbsp;Cheers. 
</p><p>&nbsp;</p></pre>
-----

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Early Access version of Java RTS 2.2 Released
DATE: 06/10/2009 11:46:33
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>Just in time for the start of JavaOne 2009, Sun released the latest version of its real-time Java platform, Java RTS 2.2, in early access form.&nbsp; New features of this release include but are not limited to:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>OS Platform support for Solaris 10 (x86 and Sparc - Update 5 &amp; 6), Red Hat MRG
1.1 and&nbsp; SUSE Linux Enterprise Real-Time 10 (SP2 Update 4)</li> 
    <li>64-bit support for the three operating systems mentioned above. In
addition, 32-bit support continues to be supported.</li> 
    <li>Faster throughput. Java RTS 2.2 utilizes a new dynamic (JIT) compiler targeted
for Java SE 7 which contains several performance improvements including a
new
register allocator.</li> 
    <li>TSV (Thread Scheduling Visualizer) enhancements.<br /></li> 
    <li>NetBeans plug-in support for Java RTS and TSV.&nbsp;</li> 
    <li>New Initialization-Time-Compilation (ITC) options including the ability to wild card a set of methods vs listing single methods and support for specifying compilation based on thread type.</li> 
    <li>Auto-tuning and startup improvements made to the Real-time Garbage Collector (RTGC).</li> 
    <li>New documentation including a <i>Java RTS Quick Start Guide</i> to
get new users started quickly with minimum programming and
configuration.</li> 
  </ul> 
  <p> A 90-day evaluation is available here:<a class="moz-txt-link-freetext" href="http://java.sun.com/javase/technologies/realtime/index.jsp"> http://java.sun.com/javase/technologies/realtime/index.jsp</a>.&nbsp; For more detailed information on OS support and new functionality, check out the
<a href="http://java.sun.com/javase/technologies/realtime/reference/doc_2.2/release/JavaRTSReleaseNotes.html">2.2
Release Notes.</a><br /><br /></p>

<p> Since the beginning, the deadlines set forth for producing our <a href="http://jfxbook.com">book</a> have been aggressive to say the least.&nbsp; Ultimately, we wanted them to be physically available for sale at the JavaOne 2009 conference.&nbsp; These things arrived literally hot off the press, having been flown in the opening day of the show.&nbsp; This effort was not in vain; the results are in.&nbsp; According to our friends at Digital Guru, JavaFX was definitely the hot topic of the week and <a href="http://www.amazon.com/JavaFX-Developing-Rich-Internet-Applications/dp/013701287X/ref=sr_1_3?ie=UTF8&amp;s=books&amp;qid=1244432995&amp;sr=1-3"><i>JavaFX - Developing Rich Internet Applications</i></a> was the best seller.&nbsp; Furthermore as of 9:52AM Eastern Time 09-June-2009, Amazon reports that its sales rank is a very respectable 21,873.&nbsp; Not bad for just a few days showing.<br /></p> 
  <p><img src="/jtc/resource/javafx-book/front-cover.jpg" style="width: 201px; height: 268px;" /></p> 
  <p>These sales were no doubt fueled by Larry Ellison's cameo appearance during the first JavaOne keynote.&nbsp; Clearly hampered by what he could and could not say due to Oracle's pending acquisition of Sun, Larry went out of his way to promote the future of JavaFX.&nbsp; He must have mentioned it a half dozen times during his 10 minute or so appearance.&nbsp; On behalf of Eric and the other Jim, thanks bud.<br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: You Are What You Eat?
DATE: 05/11/2009 08:21:31
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Personal
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>So you may ask, what effect does the food we eat have on our blood pressure?&nbsp; My experience, documented below, won't likely hold up to whole lot of scientific scrutiny, but it's good enough lesson for me.</p> 
  <p>A combination of poor eating habits and genetics lands me in that ever-increasing group of individuals who have high blood pressure.&nbsp; At first I was what you might consider borderline hypertensive.&nbsp; Being thin and active, in conjunction with my doctor, we only monitored my levels to make sure they didn't get worse.&nbsp; Unfortunately they did.</p> 
  <p>In an attempt at avoiding medication, I started seriously watching my sodium intake.&nbsp; The resulting change in blood pressure was noticeable in pretty short order.&nbsp;&nbsp; See the table below for daily readings for the last two weeks.&nbsp; In general, I try to take my reading at or around 8:00AM if possible.</p> 
  <p>Having spent my whole life eating pretty much what I please, it's not easy to make this lifestyle change.&nbsp;&nbsp; But with the help of loved ones, things have been going quite well.&nbsp; But then came Mother's day (5/10).&nbsp;&nbsp; A combination of brunch with my parents and dinner with my in-laws put the kabash on any diet plans.&nbsp; I knew this would happen, and was frankly looking forward to the feast.&nbsp; It only took one day for my pressure to skyrocket.&nbsp; Amazing.&nbsp; But man did I enjoy eating the waffles, bacon, crumbcake, bagels, Mimosas (Orange Juice and Champagne) cookies, macaroni, meatballs, turkey, chocoloate cake, Coke, Espresso, wine, Sambuca and Cognac to name a few.<br /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p>
