AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Node Count and JavaFX Performance
DATE: 04/06/2009 07:06:14
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>In a recent blog entry entitled <a href="/michaelheinrichs/entry/best_practices_for_javafx_mobile1">Best Practices for JavaFX Mobile Applications (Part 2)</a>, Michael Heinrichs espouses that keeping the scenegraph as small as possible helps JavaFX applications perform optimally. Regardless what version of JavaFX you're using, this is sage advice.&nbsp; Having spent some time trying to create components for a scoreboard-like application, I was concerned over the amount of CPU time being consumed by the clock component pictured directly below.</p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><a href="/jtc/resource/javafx-node-perf/BulbClockNode.jnlp"><img src="/jtc/resource/javafx-node-perf/BulbClockNode.jpg" /></a><br /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>You can click on the preceding image to run this mini application via Java WebStart.&nbsp;&nbsp; By placing your mouse over any of the digits and typing in, via keyboard input, a valid number, you can set the clock.&nbsp; Clicking on the &quot;START/STOP&quot; text will toggle the clock on and off.&nbsp; Like many scoreboard clocks, when the time remaining is less than one minute, 10ths of seconds are displayed.&nbsp; It is during this phase, when digits are being updated every tenth of a second, that this application can be especially taxing.&nbsp; You can imagine how troublesome this clock might be if it were to be part of say a hockey scoreboard which could have an additional 4 penalty clocks ticking simultaneously.</p> 
  <p>The major factor affecting performance appears to be the sheer number of nodes in the scenegraph that require recalculation for every clock tick.&nbsp; For this first implementation, each of the five clock digits is comprised of 27 <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">BulbNode</font>s, (my naming) which are switched on or off depending upon what value needs to be displayed.</p> 
  <p>In an effort to see how decreasing the node count might effect
performance, this second implementation of the clock component uses the
same underlying framework, except that each digit is now composed of 7
LED <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SegmentNode</font>s (my naming again) instead of 27 <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">BulbNode</font>s. &nbsp; You can run this version of the clock component by clicking on the image that follows.</p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <div style="text-align: left;" id="BulbClockNodeApplet"><a href="/jtc/resource/javafx-node-perf/LEDClockNode.jnlp"><img src="/jtc/resource/javafx-node-perf/LEDClockNode.jpg" /><br /></a></div> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>For our final example, in order to truly minimize node count, each digit is represented by a single <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">ImageView</font> node. When the value of a digit changes, a new <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">Image</font>
is displayed.&nbsp; By caching all of the possible digit values (blank, 0-9)
you can very quickly switch images.&nbsp; No doubt, prettier images can be created, but I think you get the point.&nbsp; Click on the image that follows to try this version.<br /></p> 
  <p><a href="/jtc/resource/javafx-node-perf/ImageClockNode.jnlp"><img src="/jtc/resource/javafx-node-perf/ImageClockNode.jpg" /></a><br /></p> 
  <p><font size="3"><b>The Results</b></font></p> 
  <p>The slower the compute platform, the more pronounced the differences in performance should be.&nbsp; Thinking along those lines, a very modest 1.4 GHz Pentium M laptop was chosen as the test environment to compare CPU utilization for these applications.&nbsp; OpenSolaris provides an easy-to-use well-known command-line tool called <a href="http://docs.sun.com/app/docs/doc/816-5166/vmstat-1m?l=ru&amp;a=view">vmstat(1M)</a>, which was chosen as the mechanism to analyze the individual applications. In contrast, the Performance Tab which is part of the Windows Task Manager, seemed to produce wild performance variations.<br /></p> 
  <p>For each run,&nbsp; the clocks were set to one minute, and run until the time expired.&nbsp; The data shown below represents the average CPU utilization, after startup, for each of the three implementations.&nbsp; In particular we'll look at the following fields returned by <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">vmstat</font>:<br /> </p> 
  <ul> 
    <li><b>us</b> - percentage usage of CPU time in <i>user</i> space<br /></li> 
    <li><b>sy</b> - percentage usage of CPU time in <i>system</i> space<br /></li> 
    <li><b>id</b> - percentage usage of CPU time idling</li> 
  </ul> The sum of (us + sy + id) should approximate 100%. 
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  <p> </p> 
  <table height="139" cellspacing="1" cellpadding="1" border="1" width="739"> 
    <tbody> 
      <tr> 
        <td style="width: 33%;"> <br /></td> 
        <td align="center" style="width: 33%;"> <b>Number of Nodes per Digit</b><br /></td> 
        <td align="center" style="width: 33%;"> <b>CPU Utilization </b><br /></td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td style="width: 33%;"> <b>Implementation 1: </b><font face="courier new,courier,monospace">BulbClockNode</font><br /></td> 
        <td style="width: 33%;">&nbsp;27 <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">BulbNode</font>s<br /></td> 
        <td style="width: 33%;">&nbsp;us: 22%&nbsp; sy: 2%&nbsp; id: 76%<br /></td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td style="width: 33%;"> <b>Implementation 2: </b><font face="courier new,courier,monospace">LEDClockNode</font><br /></td> 
        <td style="width: 33%;">&nbsp;7 <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SegmentNod</font>es<br /></td> 
        <td style="width: 33%;">&nbsp;us: 9%&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; sy: 2%&nbsp; id: 89%<br /></td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td style="width: 33%;"> <b>Implementation 3: </b><font face="courier new,courier,monospace">ImageClockNode</font><br /></td> 
        <td style="width: 33%;">&nbsp;1 <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">ImageNode</font><br /></td> 
        <td style="width: 33%;">&nbsp;us: 3%&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; sy: 2%&nbsp; id: 95%<br /></td> 
      </tr> 
    </tbody> 
  </table> 
  <p><br />The JavaFX engineering team is well aware of this limitation, and hopes to redesign the underlying scenegraph plumbing in the future.&nbsp; Regardless, it's still a good idea to take into consideration the size of your scenegraph.</p> 
  <p>JavaFX book status:&nbsp; Our upcoming book, <a href="http://jfxbook.com"><i>JavaFX: Developing Rich Internet Applications</i></a>, is in copy edit.&nbsp; Coming soon: Rough cuts of certain chapters will be available on <a href="http://safaribooksonline.com/Corporate/Index/">Safari</a>.<br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Hooray! Acrobat Reader for [Open]Solaris x86
DATE: 03/24/2009 14:17:56
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
Today truly marks a milestone in the history of Solaris for the x86/x64 platform.&nbsp; One of the most ubiquitous applications, Adobe Reader (notably Acrobat), is now available on [Open]Solaris for both Sparc and x86.</p> 
  <p>It's been a long time coming, one argument for the lack of Solaris x86 support till now was that [Open]Solaris didn't have the critical mass.&nbsp; That's a hard one to swallow for the following reasons:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>There's been a Solaris Sparc version for a long time.&nbsp; Solaris x86 downloads outnumber Sparc downloads by a large factor.</li> 
    <li>Versions for AIX and HP-UX, which have a significantly smaller installed base, are available.&nbsp; Maybe IBM and HP payed a lot of money for this?</li> 
    <li>Until recently there were versions even for the likes of OS/2 and UnixWare.</li> 
  </ul> 
  <p>Perhaps <a href="http://projects.gnome.org/evince/">open source alternatives</a> are becoming good enough to pose a threat?&nbsp; Whatever the reason, we welcome the arrival of Acrobat Reader and Adobe's change of heart.</p> 
  <p>You can get your copy <a href="http://get.adobe.com/reader/otherversions/">here</a>.</p> 
  <p>Happy Downloading!<br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Bindstorming
DATE: 03/19/2009 12:20:02
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>It is within our nature, even in the most infinitesimal way, to leave our mark on this world before we exit it.&nbsp; I'd like to coin the following term, heretofore unseen in the JavaFX space, and submit it as my humble contribution to the human collective:</p> 
  <p><b>bindstorm</b> \\'bïnd•storm\\ (noun): condition where a multitude of JavaFX bind recalculations severely hampers interactive performance</p> 
  <p>Yeah, I know, using the word you wish to define inside its definition is bad, but there is precedent for this: (1) Fancy-schmancy, hoity-toity college dictionaries do it all the time. (2) Mathematicians and computer scientists call this <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recursion">recursion</a>: that mysterious concept which developers use to impress others of their programming prowess.<br /></p> 
  <p>Don't get me wrong, JavaFX binding is incredibly powerful.&nbsp; Heck, we dedicated a whole chapter to it in our soon-to-be-released book <a href="jfxbook.com">JavaFX: Developing Rich Internet Applications</a>.&nbsp; But binding does come with a price, and like most anything else, over-consumption can lead to abuse.</p> 
  <p>Consider this use case: you've got a JavaFX application with dozens or maybe even hundreds of <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">Node</font>s that are part of the scenegraph.&nbsp; Each of the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">Node</font>s are ultimately sized and positioned in proportion to <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">height</font> and <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">width</font> instance variables that are passed on down.&nbsp; If you define <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">width</font> and <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">height</font> at startup and have no interest in a resizeable interface, then you stand a good chance of avoiding the use of many <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">bind</font> expressions.&nbsp; The one potential twist here is that if you're sincerely interested in a non-resizeable application, but want it to consume the entire screen, what do you do?&nbsp; As screens come in all shapes and sizes, you may not know what the resolution is at start time.&nbsp; JavaFX has an elegant solution for this which uses <i>binding</i>.</p> 
  <p>Here's a simple application which defines a <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">Rectangle</font> and <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">Circle</font> that fill the entire screen.&nbsp; You can click anywhere within the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">Circle</font> to exit the application.&nbsp; Notice the number of <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">bind</font>s required to get this to work.<br /></p> 
  <pre>import javafx.stage.\*;
import javafx.scene.\*;
import javafx.scene.shape.\*;
import javafx.scene.paint.\*;
import javafx.scene.input.\*;

function run() : Void {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; var stage: Stage = Stage {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">fullScreen: true</b>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; scene: Scene {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; content: [
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Rectangle {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; width: <b style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">bind</b> stage.width
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; height: <b style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">bind</b> stage.height
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; fill: Color.BLUE
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Circle {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; centerX: <b style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">bind</b> stage.width / 2
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; centerY: <b style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">bind</b> stage.height / 2
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; radius: <b style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">bind</b> if (stage.width &lt; stage.height) then
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; stage.width / 2 else stage.height / 2
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; fill: Color.RED
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; onMouseClicked: function(me: MouseEvent) {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; FX.exit();
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ]
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }
}
</pre> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p>Imagine what this would look like if you had lots of complex custom components with many more dependencies on <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">height</font> and <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">width</font>.&nbsp; In addition to the potential performance impact, this could be error-prone and cumbersome to code.&nbsp; To avoid the over usage of binding and the potential for a <i>bindstorm</i>, applications of this sort could be re-written as follows:</p> 
  <pre>import javafx.stage.\*;
import javafx.scene.\*;
import javafx.scene.shape.\*;
import javafx.scene.paint.\*;
import javafx.scene.input.\*;

function run() : Void {
    <span style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">var AWTtoolkit = java.awt.Toolkit.getDefaultToolkit ();</span><br style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);"><span style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">    var screenSizeFromAWT = AWTtoolkit.getScreenSize ();</span>
    Stage {
        fullScreen: true
        scene: Scene {
            content: [
                Rectangle {
                    width: <span style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">screenSizeFromAWT.width</span>
                    height: <span style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">screenSizeFromAWT.height</span>
                    fill: Color.BLUE
                }
                Circle {
                    centerX: <span style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">screenSizeFromAWT.width / 2</span>
                    centerY: <span style="color: rgb(82, 0, 255);">screenSizeFromAWT.height / 2</span>
                    radius: <span style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">if (screenSizeFromAWT.width &lt;</span><br style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);"><span style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">                        screenSizeFromAWT.height) then</span><br style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);"><span style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">                        screenSizeFromAWT.width / 2</span><br style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);"><span style="color: rgb(0, 3, 255);">                        else screenSizeFromAWT.height / 2</span>
                    fill: Color.RED
                    onMouseClicked: function(me: MouseEvent) {
                        FX.exit();
                    }
                }
            ]
        }
    }
}

</pre> 
  <p>We achieve the same effect as the first example by first making a call to a method in the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">java.awt.Toolkit</font> package.&nbsp; With this information we can statically define our scenegraph without the use of binding.</p> 
  <p>There is one caveat to this solution.&nbsp; As the AWT (Advanced Windowing Toolkit) is an integral part of Java SE, this code should be portable across all JavaFX desktops.&nbsp; However, if you wish to deploy a JavaFX Mobile solution, the AWT calls would likely change.&nbsp; Is there a mechanism that might work across both models?</p> 
  <p>As a final thought, while we're on this theme of coining terms, my compadres <a href="/clarkeman/">Jim Clarke</a> and <a href="http://www.ericbruno.com/">Eric Bruno</a>, co-authors of the <a href="http://jfxbook.com">aforementioned JavaFX book</a>, jokingly asked what word could be used to describe this scenario:</p> 
  <p>
&quot;Condition where binds lead to binds that leads back to the original bind, ending up in a Stack fault?&quot;<br /> <br />BindQuake? BindTsunami? Bindless? BindSpin? BindHole (BlackHole)? BindPit? <br /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <pre></pre>
-----

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Registering Multiple Actions (or Handlers) in JavaFX
DATE: 02/24/2009 06:38:34
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>Java developers, especially those performing any type of GUI work, will ultimately encounter Java's event-driven programming paradigm.&nbsp; In short, if programmers want to act upon some kind of event they bundle up a chunk of code into a Java method, typically referred to as a handler, and register the handler with that event.&nbsp; Whenever that event occurs, the handler code will automatically be executed.<br /></p> 
  <p>JavaFX provides a similar mechanism.&nbsp; For a straightforward example, the code below defines a simple timer in JavaFX with a resolution of 1 second.&nbsp; Each time a second expires, the function specified by the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">action</font> instance variable will be executed.&nbsp; Here's what it looks like:</p> 
  <pre>import javafx.animation.\*;

public class SimpleTimer {
    public def timeline = Timeline {
        repeatCount: 5
        interpolate: false
        keyFrames: [
            KeyFrame {
                time: 1s
                <b>action: function () : Void {
                    println("tick");
                }</b>
            }
        ]
    }
}

</pre> 
  <p>Adding a <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">run()</font> function, as follows, to the bottom of this source will enable you run an instance of this timer:</p> 
  <pre>function run() : Void {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; var s = SimpleTimer{};
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; s.timeline.playFromStart();
} 
</pre> 
  <p>The output from this run looks like this:<br /></p> 
  <pre>tick
tick
tick
tict
tick 
</pre> 
  <p>It's all well and good if you only need a single action.&nbsp; What if you wanted to perform multiple actions and/or dynamically add or subtract a number of actions?&nbsp; We can enhance our previous <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SimpleTimer</font> class to dynamically register and unregister handlers by taking advantage of two of JavaFX's features: <i>sequences</i> and <i>function pointers</i>.</p> 
  <p>Our new class provides more flexibility:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>It defines an instance variable called <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">duration</font>, which enables the user to specify the resolution of a clock tick at object instantiation.<br /></li> 
    <li>It defines two additional public functions called <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">registerHandler()</font> and <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">unRegisterHandler()</font>
which take a function pointer (a handler) as an argument.&nbsp; By registering a
handler, the function will be included in the list of handlers to be
executed each time the specified duration expires.</li> 
    <li>A handler is registered by inserting it's function pointer argument into an internal sequence of function pointers called <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">handlers[]</font>.</li> 
    <li>A handler is similarly unregistered by deleting it's function pointer argument from the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">handlers[]</font> sequence.</li> 
    <li>The action instance variable, which is part of the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">KeyFrame</font> instance, now calls an internal function called <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">runHandlers()</font>.&nbsp; <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">runHandlers()</font> sequentially executes the functions found in the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">handlers[]</font> sequence.</li> 
  </ul>Here's the new class:<br /> 
  <pre>import javafx.animation.\*;

public class Timer {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; /\*\*
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \* User-definable:&nbsp; specifies the length of time for one cycle.
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \*/
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; public var duration = 100ms;

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; public def timeline = Timeline {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; repeatCount: Timeline.INDEFINITE
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; interpolate: false
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; keyFrames: [
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; KeyFrame {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; time: duration
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>action: runHandlers</b>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ]
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; // Holds the list of handlers to run
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; protected var handlers: function() [];

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; /\*\*
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \* Add the function, represented by the handler argument, to the list
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \* of handlers.&nbsp; These will run when the elapsed time, specified
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \* by the duration instance variable, expires.
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \*/
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; public function registerHandler(handler : function()) : Void {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; for (func in handlers) {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; if (handler == func) {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; return;&nbsp; // handler already registered -- skip
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; insert handler into handlers;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; /\*\*
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \* Remove the function, represented by the handler argument, from
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \* the list of handlers.
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \*/
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; public function unRegisterHandler(handler : function()) : Void {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; delete handler from handlers;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; protected function runHandlers() : Void {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; for (handler in handlers) {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; handler();
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }
} 
</pre> 
  <p>To test this class out, we'll add a <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">run()</font> function at the script level.&nbsp; The <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">run()</font> function creates a <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">Timer</font> instance and registers two handler functions, <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">decrementTenthsRemaining()</font> and <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">processTicks()</font>.&nbsp; Here's the code:</p> 
  <pre>function run() : Void {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; var t = Timer {};
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; var tenthsRemaining = 100;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; var decrementTenthsRemaining = function() : Void {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; tenthsRemaining -= 1;
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; var processTick = function() : Void {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; if (tenthsRemaining mod 10 == 0) {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; println("seconds left: {tenthsRemaining / 10}");
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; if (tenthsRemaining == 0) {
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; t.timeline.stop();
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; }
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; };
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; t.registerHandler(decrementTenthsRemaining);
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; t.registerHandler(processTick);
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; t.timeline.play();
}</pre> 
  <p>And this is the output from the run:<br /></p> 
  <pre>seconds left: 9
seconds left: 8
seconds left: 7
seconds left: 6
seconds left: 5
seconds left: 4
seconds left: 3
seconds left: 2
seconds left: 1
seconds left: 0</pre> 
  <p> Shameless Promotion:&nbsp; Keep up to date with the latest status of our upcoming JavaFX Book entitled<a href="http://www.amazon.com/JavaFX-Developing-Rich-Internet-Applications/dp/013701287X"> <i><b>JavaFX: Developing Rich Internet Applications</b></i></a> at <a href="http://jfxbook.com">jfxbook.com</a>.<br /></p> 
  <p><br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: JavaFX Book Coming to a Theatre Near You
DATE: 02/17/2009 22:08:16
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>Despite the considerable attention JavaFX has garnered, publications (i.e. books) that discuss JavaFX in any detail are few and far between, and anything that has been published, as good as it may have been, is unfortunately hopelessly out of date.&nbsp; The reality is that up until recently, the JavaFX platform has been a moving target.&nbsp; With the advent of JavaFX 1.1 however, the platform has stabilized to the point where you should begin to see legitimate books appearing on the subject.</p> 
  <p><a href="/clarkeman/">Jim Clarke</a>, <a href="http://dobbscodetalk.com/index.php?option=com_myblog&amp;blogger=EBruno&amp;Itemid=29">Eric Bruno</a> and I have been working steadfastly on a book entitled <a href="http://www.amazon.com/JavaFX-Developing-Rich-Internet-Applications/dp/013701287X"><i><b>JavaFX: Developing Rich Internet Applications</b></i></a>, which should be among the first -- if not the first -- of these new books.&nbsp; From our standpoint the content is nearly finished.&nbsp; What remains is the editing and publication process which takes a few additional months to complete.&nbsp; Plans call for this book to be available in time for the <a href="http://java.sun.com/javaone/">JavaOne 2009 Conference</a>, if not earlier. <br /></p> 
  <p>We also plan on making rough cuts of certain chapters available on Safari.&nbsp; As soon as these are ready, we'll let you know.&nbsp; Finally, check out our website, <a href="http://jfxbook.com">jfxbook.com</a>, dedicated to the book.&nbsp; There you will find additional resources that accompany the book, including sample code and applications.&nbsp; One such application is a JavaFX version of the popular Sudoku game, pictured below.</p> 
  <p><img src="/jtc/resource/javafx-book/Sudoku.JPG" /></p> 
  <p>Visit <a href="http://jfxbook.com">jfxbook.com</a> and give it a try.<br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Overhead in Increasing the Solaris System Clock Rate 
DATE: 01/14/2009 06:48:39
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
In a previous entry entitled <a href="/jtc/entry/real_time_java_and_high">Real-Time Java and High Resolution Timers</a>, we discussed how Sun's Java Real-Time System requires access to timers with a resolution greater than the default 10ms to do anything really interesting.&nbsp;&nbsp; It was also stated that most modern processors have an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/APIC">APIC or Advanced Programmable Interrupt Controller</a> which supports much finer-grained clock tick rates.</p> 
  <p>Unfortunately there are many instances where a system does indeed contain an APIC, but it is not exposed by the BIOS.&nbsp; Furthermore, we've found that some of the embedded, low-power x86-based processors do not contain an APIC at all.&nbsp; For an example, take a look at the AMD Geode LX 800 based <a href="/jtc/entry/opensolaris_on_the_smallest_system">fit-PC Slim</a>.<br /></p> 
  <p>So if you wanted to utilize higher resolution timers for this class of system, you'd have to resort to alternative methods.&nbsp; Solaris and OpenSolaris provide two <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">/etc/system</font> parameters called <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">hires_tick </font>and <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">hires_hz</font> to facilitate increasing your default system clock tick.&nbsp; By adding the following line to <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">/etc/system</font>, you'll increase the system clock tick rate from the default of 100 per second to 1,000 per second, effectively changing the clock resolution from 10ms to 1ms.</p> 
  <pre>&nbsp;&nbsp; set hires_tick=1</pre> 
  <p>If you want to further increase the clock resolution, you can do so via the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">hires_hz</font> system tunable parameter.&nbsp; Although formally unsupported, it does work.&nbsp;&nbsp; In order to, for example, increase the clock tick rate to 10,000, add this to <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">/etc/system</font>:</p> 
  <pre>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">set hires_tick=1
    set hires_hz=10000</font></pre> 
  <p>To achieve the desired effect above, you must include both the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">hires_tick</font> assignment in addition to setting the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">hires_hz</font> parameter.</p> 
  <p>These modifications do not come without side-effects, and depending upon the hardware in question and the granularity of the desired timer resolution, they could be significant.&nbsp; In short, it takes additional CPU cycles to field all those timer interrupts.&nbsp; So I thought it'd be interesting to see what effect changing the clock tick rate had on two separate systems.&nbsp;&nbsp; Here they are:</p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <table cellspacing="1" cellpadding="1" border="1" style="width: 75%;"> 
    <tbody> 
      <tr> 
        <td style="width: 33%;"><b>&nbsp;System</b></td> 
        <td style="width: 33%;">&nbsp;fit-PC Slim<br /></td> 
        <td style="width: 33%;">&nbsp;Panasonic Toughbook CF-30 (Revision F)<br /></td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td style="width: 33%;"><b>&nbsp;CPU</b></td> 
        <td style="width: 33%;">&nbsp;AMD Geode LX 800 (500 Mhz)<br /></td> 
        <td style="width: 33%;">&nbsp;Intel Core 2 Duo L7500 1.60GHz</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td style="width: 33%;"><b>&nbsp;OpenSolaris Version<br /></b></td> 
        <td style="width: 33%;">&nbsp;snv_98</td> 
        <td style="width: 33%;">&nbsp;snv_101b</td> 
      </tr> 
    </tbody> 
  </table> 
  <p>The measuring tool used for this simple experiment is <font face="courier new,courier,monospace"><a href="http://docs.sun.com/app/docs/doc/816-5166/vmstat-1m?l=en&amp;a=view">vmstat(1m)</a></font>.&nbsp; Solaris aficionados will likely point out that there are much more accurate alternatives, but I think <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">vmstat(1m)</font> gives a decent feel for what's going on without having to expend a whole lot of extra energy.&nbsp; In particular we'll look at the following fields returned by issuing a '<font face="courier new,courier,monospace">vmstat 5</font>', and picking one of the interim samples:<br /></p> 
  <ul> 
    <li><b>in</b> - interrupts per second</li> 
    <li><b>us</b> - percentage usage of CPU time in <i>user</i> space<br /></li> 
    <li><b>sy</b> - percentage usage of CPU time in <i>system</i> space<br /></li> 
    <li><b>id</b> - percentage usage of CPU time idling</li> 
  </ul> 
  <p>The sum of (us + sy + id) should approximate 100%. &nbsp;The table below shows sample vmstat output on various clock tick settings for our two hardware platforms. <br /></p> 
  <table cellspacing="1" cellpadding="1" border="1" width="750" height="175"> 
    <tbody> 
      <tr> 
        <td><font size="2"><b>Clock tics/sec</b><br /></font></td> 
        <td><font size="2">&nbsp;100<br /></font></td> 
        <td><font size="2">&nbsp;1000</font></td> 
        <td><font size="2">&nbsp;10000</font></td> 
        <td><font size="2">&nbsp;100000</font></td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td><font size="2"><b>/etc/system settings</b><br /></font></td> 
        <td> 
          <pre><font size="2">&nbsp;none (default)
</font></pre> 
        </td> 
        <td> 
          <pre><font size="2">&nbsp;set hires_tick=1
</font></pre> 
        </td> 
        <td> 
          <pre><font size="2">set hires_tick=1</font>
<font size="2">set hires_hz=10000
</font></pre> 
        </td> 
        <td> 
          <pre><font size="2">set hires_tick=1</font>
<font size="2">set hires_hz=100000
</font></pre> 
        </td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td><font size="2"><b>vmstat(5) sample fit-PC</b><br /></font></td> 
        <td> 
          <pre> in: 201
 us: 0
 sy: 1
 id: 99</pre> 
        </td> 
        <td><font size="2"> </font> 
          <pre> in: 2001
 us: 0
 sy: 5
 id: 95</pre> 
        </td> 
        <td> 
          <pre> in: 19831
 us: 0
 sy: 43
 id: 57<font size="2"> 
</font></pre> 
        </td> 
        <td><font size="2"> </font>n/a<br /></td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td valign="top"> 
          <p> <font size="2"><b>vmstat(5) sample CF-30</b><br /></font></p> 
        </td> 
        <td valign="top"> 
          <pre> in: 471
 us: 0
 sy: 0
 id: 99</pre> 
        </td> 
        <td valign="top"> 
          <pre> in: 2278
 us: 0
 sy: 1
 id: 99</pre> 
        </td> 
        <td valign="top"> 
          <pre> in: 20299
 us: 0
 sy: 5
 id: 95</pre> 
        </td> 
        <td valign="top"> 
          <pre> in: 200307
 us: 0
 sy: 21
 id: 79</pre> 
        </td> 
      </tr> 
    </tbody> 
  </table><br /> 
  <p>Notes/Conclusions:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>The vmstat(5) was let run for about a minute.&nbsp; The output above shows one of the typical 5 second samples.&nbsp; The other 5 second samples are almost identical.<br /></li> 
    <li>The user (us) CPU time numbers give us a reasonable idea that these
systems were predominantly in an idle state when being sampled.</li> 
    <li>The number of interrupts serviced per second is directly proportional to the clock tick rate.</li> 
    <li>And of course, the larger the number of interrupts, the more system CPU time is required.</li> 
    <li>The amount of overhead taken up by increasing the clock rate is a function of system capability.&nbsp; The CF-30 not only has a much faster processor, it also has two cores to share the interrupt load.&nbsp; As such it could accommodate a much higher clock tick rate.&nbsp; For the fit-PC, performance is impacted profoundly even at modest clock tick rates.<br /></li> 
  </ul>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Why JavaFX is Relevant
DATE: 12/04/2008 11:00:58
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>This week marks the <a href="http://www.sun.com/aboutsun/media/features/2008-1204/index.jsp">formal release of JavaFX 1.0</a>.&nbsp; During the interval between the early marketing blitz and now, we've heard a lot from our friends in the press and the blogosphere, and in many instances what they had to say was not very pretty.&nbsp; Some think the <i>Rich Internet Application</i> platform battle lines are already drawn between Adobe and Microsoft, and dismiss Sun as having arrived too late to the party.&nbsp; Others opine that JavaFX's underlying Java platform is so yesterday.&nbsp; In fact Java is the primary reason why JavaFX will, much to the chagrin of many, receive serious consideration.&nbsp; Here's why:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li><b>Java is ubiquitous</b>.&nbsp; It is the proven, de-facto platform for web-based deployment.&nbsp; On the desktop, it is estimated that approximately 90% of PCs have Java installed. In fact the major PC OEMs have seen fit to install it for you out of the box.&nbsp; In the mobile world, Java is the dominant deployment platform.&nbsp; Billions (that's with a 'b') of devices run Java. <br /></li> 
  </ul> 
  <ul> 
    <li><b>The Java development community is arguably the largest on the planet</b>.&nbsp; Java gained initial widespread acclaim as a productive development environment, and continues to do so.&nbsp; As JavaFX is an evolution of Java and seamlessly integrates with it, human nature tells us that individuals will naturally want to work with and leverage that which they already know and are familiar with.</li> 
  </ul> 
  <ul> 
    <li><b>Alternatives are still no match for the Java Virtual Machine</b>.&nbsp; It has been extensively studied, vetted, scrutinized, poked, prodded, abused, cloned, and optimized more than any other virtual machine in the history of computing. And just in case you're under the impression that the Java Virtual Machine is limited only to the Java (and now JavaFX script) programming languages, think again.&nbsp; At last count there were over 200 projects integrating modern dynamic languages to the Java VM.&nbsp; That list includes the usual suspects like PHP, Ruby, JavaScript, Python, and [insert your favorite language here]. <br /></li> 
  </ul> 
  <ul> 
    <li><b>The amount of Java Standard Edition online updates is staggering</b>.&nbsp; We know.&nbsp; We supply the downloads.&nbsp; And once a desktop is upgraded, it will be able to take full advantage of the features JavaFX brings to the table, effectively trivializing the barriers to entry.</li> 
  </ul>Many of our most talented folks have been working feverishly to reach this milestone.&nbsp; That being said, there's still lot's more work to do.&nbsp; But we're off to a real nice start.&nbsp; Check out <a href="http://javafx.com">http://javafx.com</a>.&nbsp; Hmm.&nbsp; looks like the site is a little sluggish right now.&nbsp; Maybe we underestimated all the interest?<br /> 
  <p> </p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: OpenSolaris on the Smallest System Yet?
DATE: 11/05/2008 06:26:03
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>One of my compadres forwarded me this <a title="link" href="http://www.fit-pc.com/">link</a> from CompuLab, an Israeli company which has created this real small, very energy efficient PC.&nbsp; It may just be the smallest full-featured system to date.&nbsp;</p> 
  <p><img width="362" height="335" src="/jtc/resource/fitpc/500_keys.jpg" /> </p>  I'm a sucker for these types of things, and thought it would be interesting to get a reduced footprint version of OpenSolaris up and running on it.&nbsp; Haven't gotten around to playing with wi-fi, or for that matter the graphics (as the reduced footprint version of OpenSolaris has no windowing system), but every indication points to a system that doesn't require any magic incantations to get OpenSolaris up and running.&nbsp; Here are some of the specs<br /><br /> 
  <table border="0"> 
    <tbody> 
      <tr> 
        <td style="background-color: #f6f6f6;"><b>CPU:</b> AMD Geode LX800 500MHz&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; </td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td style="background-color: #e2e2e2;"><b>Chipset:</b>&nbsp;AMD CS5536</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td style="background-color: #f6f6f6;"><b>Display:</b>&nbsp;Integrated Geode LX display controller up to 1920x1440&nbsp;&nbsp; </td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td style="background-color: #e2e2e2;"><b>Memory:</b>&nbsp;512MB DDR 333MHz soldered on-board</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td style="background-color: #f6f6f6;"><b>Hard disk:</b>&nbsp;2.5” IDE 60GB</td> 
      </tr> 
      <tr> 
        <td style="background-color: #e2e2e2;"><b>Ports:</b> <br />
			&nbsp;RJ45 Ethernet port 100Mbps<br />
			&nbsp;WLAN 802.11b/g 54Mbps<br />
			&nbsp;3 x USB 2.0 HiSpeed 480Mbps <br />
			&nbsp;mini RS-232 (cable available from CompuLab)<br />
			&nbsp;VGA DB15<br />
			&nbsp;Stereo line-out audio (headphones)<br />
			&nbsp;Stereo line-in audio / Mic</td> 
      </tr> 
    </tbody> 
  </table> 
  <p><br />The system has a serial port, and upon request, CompuLab will provide the required custom serial cable.&nbsp; By instructing <a href="http://www.sun.com/bigadmin/features/articles/grub_boot_solaris.jsp">GRUB</a> to redirect the console to the serial port, you can connect the serial cable to another system and communicate directly with the console.&nbsp; To get a rough idea of how to accomplish this, check <a href="/jtc/entry/enabling_remote_mdb_debugging_of">here</a>.&nbsp; The screenshots below show that from an OpenSolaris terminal window, you can use the <a href="http://docs.sun.com/app/docs/doc/816-5165/tip-1?a=view"><font face="courier new,courier,monospace">tip(1)</font></a> command to accomplish this.<br /></p> 
  <p>So the question is, how minimal is this configuration?&nbsp; Issuing `<font face="courier new,courier,monospace">ps -ef</font>' shows that only 14 processes are currently running, of which I'm sure there's an opportunity to eliminate one or two if need be.</p> 
  <p><img src="/jtc/resource/fitpc/ps-ef.jpg" /></p> 
  <p>To give you an idea of how much RAM is currently consumed, here's what issuing the 'memstat' macro under <font face="courier new,courier,monospace"><a href="http://opensolaris.org/os/community/mdb/">mdb(1m)</a></font> yields:</p> 
  <p><img src="/jtc/resource/fitpc/memstat.jpg" /></p> 
  <p>Who says Solaris can't fit in small places?<br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Fast Booting Solaris
DATE: 07/23/2008 07:28:42
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
A veteran Java ONE keynote presenter, <a href="http://www.perronerobotics.com/">Perrone Robotics</a> has developed some real interesting technologies which take the concept of using autonomous (i.e. unmanned) vehicles to a whole new level.&nbsp; One of their key ingredients is the MAX software platform which utilizes common commercially available components to enable Perrone to very quickly and cost-effectively retrofit nearly any vehicle in short order.</p> 
  <p><img src="http://www.perronerobotics.com/files/images/JavaOneUAV.png" /> <br /></p> 
  <p>The MAX robotics platform runs on a (roughly 4&quot; x 6&quot;) low-power PC board atop Solaris and <a href="http://java.sun.com/javase/technologies/realtime/">Sun's Java Real-Time System (Java RTS)</a>.&nbsp; This combination gives Perrone the ability to leverage the huge Java development community, and assures that their critical software components behave in a predictable and deterministic fashion.</p> 
  <p>During the Java ONE 2007 conference, I was speaking with Paul Perrone about the notion of creating a <a href="/jtc/entry/framework_to_help_create_small">minimized version of Solaris</a> over which his platform might run.&nbsp; The helicopter pictured above, boots from a relatively small (4-8Gb)&nbsp; IDE flash drive, where standard Solaris takes up a substantial chunk.&nbsp; It leaves them precious little space to collect valuable information like telemetry or terrain data.&nbsp; Paul asked to revist this idea for a future project.&nbsp; That's where we left off.</p> 
  <p>Not that we've ignored them since, but it wasn't until a year later that small Solaris reared its head again.&nbsp; In addition to saving space, their main interest in this environment was in seeing <i>how much faster Solaris might boot up</i>.&nbsp; The ability to be fully functional from power-up in as short a time as possible is of critical importance.</p> 
  <p>So before investigating what advantages there might be, let's provide some background information:<br /><br /><b>Hardware<br /></b></p> 
  <p>Two separate systems were used, and for argument's sake, represent two ends of the x86 compute spectrum.&nbsp;</p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p> </p> 
    <table cellspacing="1" cellpadding="1" border="1" class="htmtableborders" style="width: 628px; height: 161px; display: table;"> 
      <tbody> 
        <tr> 
          <td> <br /></td> 
          <td align="center"> <b>Embedded Profile</b><br /></td> 
          <td align="center"><b>Modern Profile&nbsp;</b></td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
          <td> <b>System</b></td> 
          <td><a href="http://www.igologic.com/Details.asp?BID=&amp;BCat=&amp;SID=&amp;SCat=&amp;ID=14&amp;Title=i84810">iGologic i84810</a><br /></td> 
          <td><a href="http://catalog2.panasonic.com/webapp/wcs/stores/servlet/ModelDetail?displayTab=O&amp;storeId=11201&amp;catalogId=13051&amp;itemId=100537&amp;catGroupId=12871&amp;surfModel=Toughbook-30">Panasonic Toughbook CF-30 (Rev. F)<br /></a></td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
          <td> <b>CPU</b></td> 
          <td>1GHz Celeron M<br /></td> 
          <td>Core 2 Duo L7500 1.60GHz<br /></td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
          <td> <b>RAM</b></td> 
          <td>512MB</td> 
          <td>1GB</td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
          <td> <b>Disk</b></td> 
          <td><a href="http://usa.apacer.com/us/products/Embedded_Flash_Solutions_PATA_Flash_Series.htm">4GB Flash IDE Drive</a><br /></td> 
          <td>Solid State SATA Drive<br /></td> 
        </tr> 
      </tbody> 
    </table> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><b>Operating Systems</b></p> 
  <p>Minimal configurations were created for Solaris 10 08/07 (Update 4) and OpenSolaris Nevada build 93.&nbsp; These configurations boot entirely into RAM and consume less than 100MB ramdisk space.&nbsp; With a little more effort they can be may significantly smaller.&nbsp; The original blog post describing the environment is <a href="/jtc/entry/framework_to_help_create_small">here</a>.&nbsp;&nbsp; You can download the framework for these hardware/OS combinations <a href="/jtc/resource/fast_boot_solaris/min_sol_framework.tar">here</a>, and can get a feel for the build environment by taking a look at this <a href="/jtc/resource/fast_boot_solaris/README.txt">README</a>.<br /></p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><b>Definitions</b></p> 
  <p><b></b>Within the context of this discussion, here are the key terms along with their meanings.<br /> </p> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p><b><i>Total Boot Time</i></b>: This is the
time it takes from power-up till a user is prompted to log in.&nbsp;
Typically for a full Solaris installation, the windowing system must
first start up and present the user with a login screen.&nbsp; In a minimal
Solaris environment, there is no windowing system.&nbsp; Instead, the <i>total boot time</i> is defined as the time it takes from power-up till a user is prompted with the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">console login:</font> prompt.</p> 
    <p><i><b>POST Time</b></i>: POST or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power-on_self-test">Power On Self Test</a>
is the time taken by the system at pre-boot to handle things like
diagnostics,&nbsp; BIOS and device initialization.&nbsp; For this discussion,
we'll define <i>POST time</i> as the time it takes from power-up until the user is presented with a <a href="http://www.gnu.org/software/grub/">GRUB</a>
boot menu.&nbsp; We call out this segment of the total time because in many
cases we are at the mercy of the PC/BIOS manufacturer and can't overly
influence how quickly or slowly this proceeds.</p> 
    <p><i><b>Solaris Boot Time</b></i>:
The time it takes from being presented with a GRUB boot menu till a
Solaris user is prompted to log in.&nbsp; Again, depending upon whether a
windowing system is configured or not, this may represent the time it
takes to be prompted with a login screen or <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">console login:</font> prompt respectively.&nbsp; This represents the segment of time that we can influence.<br /></p> 
    <p>We can conclude from these definitions that: </p> 
    <p><i>&nbsp;&nbsp; Total Boot Time</i> = <i>POST Time</i> + <i>Solaris Boot Time</i></p> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p> </p> 
  <p><b>Results</b></p> 
  <p align="center"><b>Embedded Profile: iGoLogic i84810 system&nbsp;</b></p> 
  <blockquote> </blockquote> 
  <blockquote> 
    <p> </p> 
    <table cellspacing="1" cellpadding="1" border="1" style="width: 100%;"> 
      <tbody> 
        <tr> 
          <td> <b>OS</b><br /></td> 
          <td> <b>Post Time</b><br /></td> 
          <td> <b>Solaris Boot Time</b><br /></td> 
          <td><b>Total Boot Time </b><br /></td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
          <td>&nbsp;Solaris 10 08/07<br /></td> 
          <td>13 sec<br /></td> 
          <td>26 sec<br /></td> 
          <td>39 sec<br /></td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
          <td>&nbsp;OpenSolaris Nevada Build 93<br /></td> 
          <td>13 sec<br /></td> 
          <td>18 sec<br /></td> 
          <td>31 sec&nbsp;</td> 
        </tr> 
      </tbody> 
    </table> 
    <p align="center"> </p> 
    <p align="center"><b>Modern Profile: Panasonic Toughbook CF-30</b><br /></p> 
    <p> </p> 
    <table cellspacing="1" cellpadding="1" border="1" style="width: 100%;"> 
      <tbody> 
        <tr> 
          <td> <b>OS</b></td> 
          <td> <b>POST Time</b><br /></td> 
          <td> <b>Solaris Boot Time</b><br /></td> 
          <td><b>&nbsp;Total Boot Time<br /></b></td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
          <td>&nbsp;Solaris 10 08/07<br /></td> 
          <td>&nbsp;6 sec<br /></td> 
          <td>&nbsp;18 sec<br /></td> 
          <td>&nbsp;24 sec<br /></td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
          <td>&nbsp;OpenSolaris Nevada Build 93<br /></td> 
          <td>&nbsp;6 sec<br /></td> 
          <td>&nbsp;9 sec<br /></td> 
          <td>&nbsp;15 sec<br /></td> 
        </tr> 
      </tbody> 
    </table><br /> 
  </blockquote> 
  <p><b>Conclusions/Notes</b></p> 
  <p>1. These times were taken by hand with the stopwatch feature on my Timex.&nbsp; If anything, the times might be slightly shorter than actually recorded as there is a human delay in reacting to seeing the necessary prompts.&nbsp; I ran the tests several times, and the same numbers consistently appear.<br /></p> 
  <p>2. The version of the OS appears to matter a lot, as OpenSolaris nvx_93 boots up nearly twice as fast as Solaris 10 U4 on the same hardware.</p> 
  <p>3. The type of I/O device subsystem seems to be a big factor too.&nbsp; For example, by switching out the IDE Flash Drive with a 5400 RPM IDE hard disk, i84810 total boot time decreased by about 6 seconds for both Solaris 10 and OpenSolaris.&nbsp;</p> 
  <p>4. The minimal Solaris environment is currently only available in 32-bit mode.<br /> </p> 
  <p>5. With relative ease, Solaris can be configured to boot in less that 10 seconds on modern x86 hardware.&nbsp; My unofficial record stands at 9 seconds (or slightly less).&nbsp;&nbsp; No doubt it could boot faster on more robust hardware (eliminating POST time).&nbsp; Any takers?<br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Real-Time Java in a Zone
DATE: 06/17/2008 13:25:11
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
As is often the case, Sun's technologies and offerings are being
applied in ways which we hadn't necessarily anticipated.&nbsp; Yet another
example has reared its head in the govenrment/military space where
customers have expressed interest in using Sun's <a href="http://java.sun.com/javase/technologies/realtime/">Java Real-Time System </a>with <a href="http://www.sun.com/software/solaris/ds/trusted_extensions.jsp">Solaris Trusted Extensions</a>.&nbsp;
As it stands right now, Java RTS will neither operate nor install
within the confines of such an environment.&nbsp; Let's investigate why this
is so, and see what current opportunities there are for
working around this shortcoming.<br /> </p> 
  <p>So what is it that
causes Trusted Extensions and Java RTS not to play together nicely?&nbsp;&nbsp;
It happens to revolve around Trusted Extension's extensive usage of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solaris_Containers">Solaris zones</a>
to limit access between differing security levels.&nbsp; Solaris packages must be specifically configured to accommodate zones, which has yet to formally take place with Java RTS.&nbsp; As zones are a core
component of Solaris, we can, for the sake of simplicity, just use
standard Solaris to demonstrate how we can work around this temporary limitation.&nbsp; These modifications
should apply to Trusted Extensions just as well.&nbsp;&nbsp; To get Java RTS to work within a zone, follow these steps:<br /> </p> 
  <p>1. Install the Java RTS cyclic driver (only) in the global zone.<br /> </p> <span style="font-family: monospace;">global# <span style="font-weight: bold;">pkgadd -d . SUNWrtjc</span><br /> <br />
Processing package instance &lt;SUNWrtjc&gt; from &lt;/cyclic/src/packages_i386&gt;<br />
## Installing package &lt;SUNWrtjc&gt; in global zone<br /> <br />
Java Real-Time System cyclic driver(i386) 2.1.0,REV=2008.06.13.16.10<br />
Copyright 2008 Sun Microsystems, Inc.&nbsp; All rights reserved.<br />
Use is subject to license terms.<br />
Using &lt;/&gt; as the package base directory.<br />
## Processing package information.<br />
## Processing system information.<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp; 5 package pathnames are already properly installed.<br />
## Verifying package dependencies.<br />
## Verifying disk space requirements.<br />
## Checking for conflicts with packages already installed.<br />
## Checking for setuid/setgid programs.<br /> <br />
This package contains scripts which will be executed with super-user<br />
permission during the process of installing this package.<br /> <br />
Do you want to continue with the installation of &lt;SUNWrtjc&gt; [y,n,?] y<br /> <br />
Installing Java Real-Time System cyclic driver as &lt;SUNWrtjc&gt;<br /> </span><br /> <br />
2. Create a zone called '<font face="courier new,courier,monospace">rtjzone</font>':<br /> 
  <pre>global# <b>mkdir -p /zone</b>
bash-3.00# <b>zonecfg -z rtjzone</b>
rtjzone: No such zone configured
Use 'create' to begin configuring a new zone.
zonecfg:rtjzone&gt; <b>create</b>
zonecfg:rtjzone&gt; <b>set zonepath=/zone/rtjzone</b>
zonecfg:rtjzone&gt; <b>verify</b>
zonecfg:rtjzone&gt; <b>commit</b>
zonecfg:rtjzone&gt; <b>exit</b></pre> 
  <pre>global# <b>zoneadm list -vc</b>
&nbsp; ID NAME&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; STATUS&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; PATH&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; BRAND&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; IP
   0 global&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; running&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; /&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; native&nbsp;&nbsp; shared
   - rtjzone&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; configured /zone/rtjzone&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; native&nbsp;&nbsp; shared</pre> 
  <pre>global# <b>zoneadm -z rtjzone install</b>
Preparing to install zone &lt;rtjzone&gt;.
Creating list of files to copy from the global zone.
Copying &lt;6984&gt; files to the zone.
Initializing zone product registry.
Determining zone package initialization order.
Preparing to initialize &lt;1074&gt; packages on the zone.
Initialized &lt;1074&gt; packages on zone.
Zone &lt;rtjzone&gt; is initialized.
Installation of &lt;1&gt; packages was skipped.
The file &lt;/zone/rtjzone/root/var/sadm/system/logs/install_log&gt; contains a log of the zone installation.</pre> 
  <p><br />
3.&nbsp; Modify the zone to allow access to the cyclic device, and to allow additional privileges</p> 
  <pre>global# <span style="font-weight: bold;">zonecfg -z rtjzone</span>
zonecfg:rtjzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set limitpriv=default,proc_priocntl,proc_lock_memory,proc_clock_highres</span>
zonecfg:rtjzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">add device</span>
zonecfg:rtjzone:device&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set match=/dev/cyclic</span>
zonecfg:rtjzone:device&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">end</span>
zonecfg:rtjzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">verify</span>
zonecfg:rtjzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">commit</span>
zonecfg:rtjzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">exit

</span>global#<span style="font-weight: bold;"> zoneadm -z rtjzone reboot
</span></pre>
Note:&nbsp; One privilege that is useful with Java RTS is <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">sys_res_config</font>.&nbsp;
This is typically used to assign a real-time process to a <a href="http://developers.sun.com/solaris/articles/solaris_processor.html">processor set</a>.&nbsp;
Unfortunately zones cannot currently be given this privilege.&nbsp; You can
however, from the global zone, assign a processor set to a zone, which
might be a reasonable workaround.<br /> <br /> 
  <p>4.&nbsp; Get a copy of the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SUNWrtjv</font> package and modify it so
that it will install in a zone.&nbsp; The <a href="/jtc/resource/rts-zone/postinstall">postinstall</a> script and <a href="/jtc/resource/rts-zone/postremove">postremove</a>
script must replaced with those provided by these hyperlinks just
mentioned.<br /> </p> 
  <pre>rtjzone# <span style="font-weight: bold;">cd /scratch</span>
rtjzone# <span style="font-weight: bold;">ls</span>
SUNWrtjv	postinstall	postremove
rtjzone# <span style="font-weight: bold;">cp postinstall SUNWrtjv/install/</span>
rtjzone# <span style="font-weight: bold;">cp postremove SUNWrtjv/install/

</span></pre>
5. Modify the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SUNWrtjv pkgmap</font> file with the appropriate sizes,
checksums and last access dates.&nbsp; The source code for a sample C
program, called <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">pkgmap_info</font>, which prints out the necessary information, can be found <a href="/jtc/resource/rts-zone/pkgmap_info.c">here</a>.<br /> <br /> 
  <pre>rtjzone# <span style="font-weight: bold;">cd SUNWrtjv</span><br style="font-weight: bold;">rtjzone# <span style="font-weight: bold;">grep post pkgmap</span>
1 i postinstall 5402 42894 1194344457
1 i postremove 2966 34854 1194344457

rtjzone# <span style="font-weight: bold;">cp pkgmap_info.c /tmp</span>
rtjzone# <span style="font-weight: bold;">cc -o /tmp/pkgmap_info /tmp/pkgmap_info.c
</span>rtjzone# <span style="font-weight: bold;">cd /scratch/SUNWrtjv/install/
</span>rtjzone#<span style="font-weight: bold;"> /tmp/pkgmap_info postinstall
</span>postinstall 5820 9488 1213727841
rtjzone# <span style="font-weight: bold;">/tmp/pkgmap_info postremove
</span>postremove 3092 45039 1213727538<span style="font-weight: bold;">
</span>
</pre>
Replace the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">postinstall</font> and <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">postremove</font> entries in the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">pkgmap</font> file with
those produced by the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">pkgmap_info</font> program.&nbsp; You cannot simply use the
example data above because the last access times will not match.&nbsp; Doing so will cause the install to fail.<br /> <br /> <br />
6. Install the Java RTS <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SUNWrtjv</font> package inside the zone.<br /> <br /> <span style="font-family: monospace;">rtjzone# <span style="font-weight: bold;">cd /scratch</span><br />
rtjzone# <span style="font-weight: bold;">pkgadd -d . SUNWrtjv</span><br /> <br />
Processing package instance &lt;SUNWrtjv&gt; from &lt;/scratch&gt;<br /> <br />
Java Real-Time System runtime environment(i386) 1.5.0_13_Java-RTS-2.0_01-b08_RTSJ-1.0.2,REV=2007.11.06.12.47<br />
Copyright 2005 Sun Microsystems, Inc.&nbsp; All rights reserved.<br />
Use is subject to license terms.<br /> <br />
Where should this package be installed? (/opt): <span style="font-weight: bold;">/opt</span><br /> <br />
To achieve predictable temporal behavior, the Java Real-Time System<br />
must be granted access to a number of privileged Solaris resources.<br />
By default, access to these privileged resources is only granted to<br />
the superuser (root). They can also be granted to additional users<br />
by creating a rights profile, that is, a collection of authorizations,<br />
that can later be assigned to an administrative role or directly<br />
to a user.<br /> <br />
As part of this package installation, a local 'Java Real-Time System User'<br />
rights profile can be created on this machine.<br />
This rights profile should NOT be created if such an action conflicts<br />
with your computer security management policies. If unsure, contact<br />
your system administrator or your computer security manager.<br />
Also refer to the product's release notes for further details regarding<br />
the privileges required by the Java Real-Time System.<br /> <br />
Should a local 'Java Real-Time System User' rights profile be created? [y,n] (no): <span style="font-weight: bold;">y<br /> </span>Using &lt;/opt&gt; as the package base directory.<br />
## Processing package information.<br />
## Processing system information.<br />
## Verifying package dependencies.<br />
## Verifying disk space requirements.<br />
## Checking for conflicts with packages already installed.<br />
## Checking for setuid/setgid programs.<br /> <br />

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Are Solaris RAM Disks Swappable?
DATE: 05/29/2008 06:09:53
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>As memory access is typically orders of magnitude faster than disk access, the idea of using a part of RAM as an in-memory storage device has been one of the earliest performance optimizations realized by the computer science community.&nbsp; Even though today this type of optimization takes place transparently inside modern operating systems (via mechanisms like the disk buffer cache),&nbsp; there are still circumstances where manually creating a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RAM_disk">RAM disk</a> might still be quite useful.<br /><br />A Solaris customer using the <a href="http://docs.sun.com/app/docs/doc/816-5166/ramdiskadm-1m?l=ko&amp;a=view">ramdiskadm(1m)</a> utility to create a Ram disk device for a real-time application posed the following question: &quot;Are RAM disks swappable?&quot;. From a real-time perspective, a RAM disk not only yields significantly better seek performance but also provides for more deterministic behavior compared to the traditional rotating disk platter.&nbsp; The customer's concern here is that, under dire circumstances, is it possible for operating system swap out the RAM disk memory?&nbsp; Needless to say, if a swap out occurred it would put a big crimp in the real-time application's predictability.<br /></p><p>To get an idea of what's going on when a RAM disk is created, let's use the Solaris <a href="http://docs.sun.com/app/docs/doc/816-5166/kstat-1m?a=view">kstat(1m)</a> kernel statitics utility to see how memory is being allocated.&nbsp; First let's see what memory looks like before creating a RAM disk:</p><pre># <b>pagesize</b><br />4096<br /># <b>kstat -n system_pages | grep pagesfree</b><br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; pagesfree&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 96334<br /># <b>kstat -n system_pages | grep pageslocke</b>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;   pageslocked&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 26170
</pre><p>So, on this particular system, where a page is 4096 bytes,&nbsp; there are currently 96334 pages free, and 26170 pages that are locked.&nbsp; Now let's create a 50MB RAM disk:</p><pre># <b>ramdiskadm -a rd 50m</b><br />/dev/ramdisk/rd<br /># <b>ramdiskadm</b><br />Block Device&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Size&nbsp; <br />Removable<br />/dev/ramdisk/rd&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 52428800&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Yes&nbsp;<br /># <b>kstat -n system_pages | grep pagesfree</b><br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; pagesfree&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 83507<br /># <b>kstat -n system_pages | grep pageslocked</b><br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; pageslocked&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; 38988<br /></pre><p>Let's subtract the original number of <i>pageslocked</i> from the latest 
value and multiply by the pagesize:
</p><pre># <b>pagesize</b><br />4096<br /># <b>bc</b><br /><b>(38988-26170)\*4096</b><br />52502528<br /><b>\^D</b><br /></pre><p>
The increase in locked pages can be attributed to the 
creation of the RAM disk (50m + a small amount of overhead).&nbsp; So yes, these pages are locked into memory.&nbsp; But it would be nice to get a definitive statement on what <i>pageslocked</i> actually means.&nbsp; According to McDougal, Mauro and Gregg's <a href="http://www.amazon.com/Solaris-Performance-Tools-Techniques-OpenSolaris/dp/0131568191/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&amp;s=books&amp;qid=1212008613&amp;sr=8-1"><i>Performance and Tools: DTrace and 
MDB Techniques for Solaris 10 and OpenSolaris</i></a>, p<i>ageslocked</i> is &quot;Total number of pages locked into memory by the kernel and user processes&quot;.&nbsp; Furthermore, the man page for <a href="http://docs.sun.com/app/docs/doc/816-5168/plock-3c?a=view">plock(3C)</a>, a related library routine which enables programmers to lock memory segments, states that &quot;Locked segments are immune to all routine swapping&quot;.&nbsp; What's routine swapping?</p><p>  Hmm.&nbsp; Anybody care to shed some light on this?<br /></p>
-----

<p>Looking at the opensolaris source, the physical pages allocated by a ramdisk are locked.  They cannot be swapped out.  I would assume this is the same for Solaris 10 and prior.</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Working Around a Simple Directory Server Install Issue with Windows
DATE: 05/19/2008 07:03:39
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>While working with a customer evaluating Sun's Directory Server
Enterprise Edition 6.3 for Windows, we came across a problem during
very basic install and setup.&nbsp; For posterity's sake, I thought it might
make sense to document the issue and offer a few potential workarounds.&nbsp; <br />
</p><p><b>How to replicate<br /></b></p><p>1. Install the directory server.&nbsp; In this case the downloadable image has been unzipped into the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">c:\\tmp\\dsee63\\DSEE_ZIP_DISTRIBUTION</font> directory<br /></p><pre>  C:\\tmp\\dsee63\\DSEE_ZIP_Distribution&gt; <b>dsee_deploy --no-inter -i /tmp/ds63</b>                                                                              <br />  Unzipping sun-ldap-shared-l10n.zip ...                                      <br />  Unzipping sun-ldap-directory.zip ...                                    <br />  Unzipping sun-ldap-console-gui-help-l10n.zip ...                                                <br />  Configuring Cacao at /tmp/ds63/dsee6/cacao_2                                            <br />  ...<br />  You can now start your Directory Server Instances<br />  You can now start your Directory Proxy Server Instances<br /></pre><p>2. Create a new Directory Server instance and start the instance<br /></p><pre>  C:\\tmp\\dsee63\\DSEE_ZIP_Distribution&gt;<b>cd \\tmp\\ds63</b><br />  C:\\tmp\\ds63&gt; <b>set PATH=c:\\tmp\\ds63\\ds6\\bin;c:\\tmp\\ds63\\dsrk6\\bin;%PATH%</b><br />  C:\\tmp\\ds63&gt;<b>dsadm create /tmp/instance</b><br />  Choose the Directory Manager password:<br />  Confirm the Directory Manager password:<br />  Use 'dsadm start '/tmp/instance'' to start the instance<br />  C:\\tmp\\ds63&gt;<b>dsadm start /tmp/instance</b><br />  Waiting for Directory Server instance 'C:/tmp/instance' to start...<br />  Directory Server instance 'C:/tmp/instance' started: pid=2144<br /></pre><p>3. Create a suffix.&nbsp; This is where the installation fails:<br /></p><pre>C:\\tmp\\ds63&gt;<b>dsconf create-suffix -h localhost -p 1389 dc=example,dc=com</b><br />Enter &quot;cn=Directory Manager&quot; password:<br />Unable to bind securely on &quot;localhost:1389&quot;.<br />The &quot;create-suffix&quot; operation failed on &quot;localhost:1389&quot;.<br /></pre><p>The problem here is described in <a href="http://docs.sun.com/app/docs/doc/820-2759/ds?a=view">The Sun Java System Directory Server Enterprise Edition 6.3 Release Notes</a>:</p><dl><dt><i>On Windows systems, Directory Server does not allow
Start TLS by default.</i></dt><dd>
<p><i>This issue affects server instances on Windows systems only.
This issue is due to performance on Windows systems when Start TLS is used.</i></p>

<p><i>To work around this issue, consider using the <kbd><b>-P</b></kbd> option
with the <kbd>dsconf</kbd> command to connect using the SSL port directly.
Alternatively, if your network connection is already secured, consider using
the <kbd><b>-e</b></kbd> option with the <kbd>dsconf</kbd> command.
The option lets you connect to the standard port without requesting a secure
connection.</i></p>

</dd></dl><p>&nbsp;</p><p><b>Two Potential Workarounds</b><br /><br />Workaround 1: Issue the '<font face="courier new,courier,monospace">dsconf create-suffix</font>' command by directly connecting to the SSL port (<font face="courier new,courier,monospace">-P 1636</font>)<br /></p><pre>C:\\tmp\\ds63&gt;<b>dsconf create-suffix -h localhost -P 1636 dc=example,dc=com</b><br />Certificate &quot;CN=TECRA-A1, CN=1636, CN=Directory Server, O=Sun Microsystems&quot; pres<br />ented by the server is not trusted.<br />Type &quot;Y&quot; to accept, &quot;y&quot; to accept just once, &quot;n&quot; to refuse, &quot;d&quot; for more details<br />: <b>Y</b><br />Enter &quot;cn=Directory Manager&quot; password:<br /></pre><p>Workaround 2: Modify the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">ds-start-tls-enabled</font> attribute that is stored in the directory server configuration.</p><p>a. Create a file, say <font face="arial,helvetica,sans-serif">c:\\tmp\\modify.ldif</font> which looks like:</p><p><font face="courier new,courier,monospace">&nbsp; dn: cn=config<br />&nbsp; changetype: modify<br />&nbsp; replace: ds-start-tls-enabled<br />&nbsp; ds-start-tls-enabled: on</font></p><p>b. Issue an <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">ldapmodify</font> command something like this:</p><pre>  C:\\tmp&gt;<b>ldapmodify -h localhost -p 1389 -D &quot;cn=Directory Manager&quot; -w </b><i>password</i><b> &lt; c:\\tmp\\modify.ldif</b><br />  modifying entry cn=config</pre><p>c. Confirm modification via <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">ldapsearch</font> command:<br /></p><pre>  C:\\tmp&gt;<b>ldapsearch -b &quot;cn=config&quot; -h localhost -p 1389 -D &quot;cn=Directory Manager&quot; -w </b><i>password</i><b> &quot;cn=config&quot; ds-start-tls-enabled</b><br />  version: 1<br />  dn: cn=config<br />  ds-start-tls-enabled: on<br /><br />  dn: cn=config,cn=chaining database,cn=plugins,cn=config<br /><br />  dn: cn=config,cn=ldbm database,cn=plugins,cn=config</pre><p>c. Stop and restart the directory server instance</p><pre>  C:\\tmp\\ds63&gt;<b>dsadm stop /tmp/instance</b><br />  Directory Server instance 'C:/tmp/instance' stopped<br /><p>  C:\\tmp\\ds63&gt;<b>dsadm start /tmp/instance</b><br />  Directory Server instance 'C:/tmp/instance' started: pid=3560 <br /></p></pre><p>d. Try creating a suffix with the standard port (1389):</p><pre>  C:\\tmp\\ds63&gt;<b>dsconf create-suffix -h localhost -p 1389 dc=example1,dc=com</b><br />  Enter &quot;cn=Directory Manager&quot; password:<br /><br /></pre><p>Note:&nbsp; Directory Server Enterprise Edition 6.3 is supported on Windows Server 2003, but not for Windows XP.&nbsp; Although not formally supported, it is possible to experiment with XP.<br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Using Solaris to Burn the OpenSolaris 2008.05 CD Image
DATE: 05/06/2008 21:20:53
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
If you're using Solaris or OpenSolaris as a platform to download and burn the recently announced OpenSolaris 2008.05 image, the instructions found <a href="http://opensolaris.org/os/project/indiana/resources/getit/">here</a> may need a slight modification.&nbsp; After downloading the <a href="http://dlc.sun.com/osol/opensolaris/latest.iso">Live CD</a>, you are instructed to use the cdrw(1) command as follows to burn the image to CD:</p><blockquote><pre id="line721">cdrw -i /path/to/iso/image/os200805.iso<br /></pre></blockquote><p>Here's what might happen on your system when you attempt to use the '<font face="courier new,courier,monospace">cdrw -i</font>' command:</p><p><img vspace="0" hspace="0" border="0" align="bottom" src="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/resource/opensolaris/term1.jpg" /><br /></p><p>The cdrw(1) command fails because it thinks the ISO image is larger than the default capacity of the CD.&nbsp; The solution to this problem is explained in these excerpts from the <a href="http://docs.sun.com/app/docs/doc/819-2239/cdrw-1?a=view">cdrw(1)</a> man page:</p><blockquote><p style="direction: ltr;"><i>&quot;Most commercially available drives allow writing beyond 74 minutes as
long as the media has the capacity (such as 80–minute media). However,
the ability to write beyond 74 minutes might not be supported by the
drive in use. If the drive supports this feature, then use the -C o<kbd></kbd>ption
to indicate that the tool should rely on the capacity indicated by the media.</i></p></blockquote>
<blockquote><i><kbd></kbd></i><i>&quot;-C [option] Uses stated media capacity. Without this option, <kbd>cdrw</kbd>
uses a default value for writable CD media, which is 74 minutes for an
audio CD, 681984000 bytes for a data CD, or 4.7 Gbytes for a DVD.&quot;</i>&nbsp;<br /></blockquote>This time let's invoke 'cdrw' with the -C option.<br /><p><img vspace="0" hspace="0" border="0" align="bottom" src="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/resource/opensolaris/term2.jpg" /><br /> </p><p>Voila.<br /></p><p><br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Modifying and Respinning a Bootable Solaris ISO Image
DATE: 04/21/2008 06:28:13
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
As an adjunct to the <a href="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/entry/enabling_remote_mdb_debugging_of">previous blog post</a>, a slightly customized boot environment capable of enabling serial console kernel debugging was required to diagnose Solaris install problems.&nbsp; The post itself mentioned that a nice way to accomplish this was to set up PXE network boot via <a href="http://www.sun.com/bigadmin/features/articles/jumpstart_x86_x64.jsp">Solaris jumpstart</a>.&nbsp; It is indeed flexible and enables one to experiment with modifications and quickly test whether they perform as expected.&nbsp; The one downside to this environment is that an additional DHCP/TFTP boot server has to be configured.&nbsp; To eliminate that service, you could, once the customizations are well understood, simply create a new version of the Solaris install DVD with your customizations.&nbsp; Let's run through the process for a simple example.</p><p>1. Get a Solaris DVD.&nbsp; For this example, we'll use an interim build of the upcoming Solaris 10 Update 5.</p><p>2. Extract the the entire contents of the DVD.</p><pre># <b>lofiadm -a /export/iso/s10x_u5b10_dvd.iso</b><br />/dev/lofi/1<br /># <b>mkdir -p /iso</b><br /># <b>mount -r -F hsfs /dev/lofi/1 /iso</b><br /># <b>cd /iso</b><br /># <b>mkdir /export/modified-s10x_u5b10</b><br /># <b>find . -depth -print | cpio -pudm /export/modified-s10x_u5b10</b><br />4516208 blocks<br /></pre><p>3. Modify the content contained in <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">/export/modified-s10x_u5b10</font>.&nbsp; In this case, we'll change the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">boot/grub/menu.lst</font> file found in this directory to look like:</p><pre>#<br />#pragma ident   &quot;@(#)install_menu       1.1     05/09/01 SMI&quot;<br />#<br />default=0<br />timeout=30<br />serial --unit=0 --speed=9600<br />terminal serial<br />title Solaris Serial Console ttya<br />        kernel /boot/multiboot kernel/unix -B install_media=cdrom,console=ttya<br />        module /boot/x86.miniroot <br /></pre><p>4. Issue the following magic incantation to create a bootable ISO image based on the contents of the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">/export/modified-s10x_u5b10 </font>directory.</p><pre># <b>mkisofs -R -b boot/grub/stage2_eltorito -no-emul-boot -boot-load-size 4 -R -L -r -D -U \\<br />-joliet-long -max-iso9660-filenames -boot-info-table -o \\<br />/export/iso/modified-s10x_u5b10.iso /export/modified-s10x_u5b10</b><br /><p>...</p><p>Size of boot image is 4 sectors -&gt; No emulation<br />  1.80% done, estimate finish Fri Apr 18 15:55:13 2008<br />  2.25% done, estimate finish Fri Apr 18 15:59:07 2008<br />  2.70% done, estimate finish Fri Apr 18 15:58:37 2008<br />  3.15% done, estimate finish Fri Apr 18 15:58:48 2008<br />...</p><p> 98.43% done, estimate finish Fri Apr 18 15:59:37 2008<br /> 98.88% done, estimate finish Fri Apr 18 15:59:37 2008<br /> 99.33% done, estimate finish Fri Apr 18 15:59:36 2008<br /> 99.78% done, estimate finish Fri Apr 18 15:59:36 2008<br />Total translation table size: 2048<br />Total rockridge attributes bytes: 3075577<br />Total directory bytes: 18921472<br />Path table size(bytes): 148014<br />Max brk space used 1b24000<br />1112471 extents written (2172 MB)&nbsp;</p></pre><p>Voila!&nbsp; Acknowledgements to Tom Haynes and this <a href="http://blogs.sun.com/tdh/entry/getting_a_solaris_bootable_dvd">blog post</a> which served as an excellent guide.<br /></p>
