AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Enabling Remote Console Debugging of Solaris x86 Boot/Install
DATE: 04/14/2008 13:28:44
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>Our partners do a fair amount of business supplying ruggedized Solaris-powered <a href="http://www.panasonic.com/business/toughbook/products.asp">Panasonic Toughbook</a> computers to their US government/military customers.&nbsp; As a regular part of the product cycle, Sun usually works with both the integrators and Panasonic to assure that as new models become available, Solaris runs on these systems properly.&nbsp; Furthermore, when we can get our grubby little hands on the systems, we'll run them through our certification suite of tests and formally place them on the Solaris <a href="http://www.sun.com/bigadmin/hcl/">Hardware Compatibility List</a>.&nbsp; As an example, here's the certification report for one of the versions of the <a href="http://www.sun.com/bigadmin/hcl/data/systems/details/1432.html">Panasonic Toughbook CF-29</a>.</p><p>Panasonic recently introduced a new version of the <a href="http://catalog2.panasonic.com/webapp/wcs/stores/servlet/ModelDetail?displayTab=O&amp;storeId=11201&amp;catalogId=13051&amp;itemId=100537&amp;catGroupId=12871&amp;surfModel=Toughbook-30">Toughbook CF-30</a> (referred to as revision F) which tweaks some of the computer subsystems resulting in an all-too-familiar scenario: namely, these differences cause the current version of Solaris to fail to install.&nbsp; Note: Solaris is not alone here, all Operating Systems must continually play this cat and mouse game to support the latest hardware/firmware advances.</p><p>Our initial hypothesis lead us to believe that the problem was related to the introduction of the Intel ICH8 SATA chipset.&nbsp; So we called on some of our Solaris I/O experts, based out of Sun's Beijing office, to take a peek at what was going on.&nbsp; As the laptop is currently in New York, we needed a way for folks half way around the world to have access to this system.&nbsp; There are lots of mechanisms available to remotely diagnose systems, what's somewhat unique here is the following: (1) the diagnosis takes place very early in the boot stage, way before any windowing or networking is set up and (2) The system in question is a laptop, not a server, where things like Lights Out Management (LOM) service processors are non-existent.</p><p>The solution here was to utilize decades old RS-232 technology combined with some of features of the <a href="http://www.sun.com/bigadmin/features/articles/grub_boot_solaris.jsp">GRUB bootloader</a>.&nbsp; Here are two requirements needed:<br /></p><ul><li>A serial connection must be established between the system to be diagnosed, which will be referred to henceforth as the <i>target</i>, and the system which accesses the target which we'll refer to as the <i>remote host</i>. &nbsp; Unfortunately most laptop manufacturers have seen fit to eliminate serial port connectors in lieu of using USB to serial converters as a replacement technology.&nbsp; At the early stages of boot, USB/serial capability is not yet available, so these systems are not good candidates for this type of diagnosis.&nbsp; Thankfully the target in question here, the Panasonic CF-30 Toughbook, still comes with a serial port.</li></ul><ul><li>A Jumpstart environment capable of installing Solaris on x86/x64 systems is strongly recommended.&nbsp; As part of the process described below, we'll be modifying the target's GRUB environment&nbsp; (<font face="courier new,courier,monospace">menu.lst</font>).&nbsp; If you chose to use a DVD boot/install environment instead, you'd need to modify and burn a new DVD for each change made to the target's boot environment.&nbsp; It took a bit of time to find the right incantations in the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">menu.lst</font> file to get what was needed here; continually re-burning DVDs would have been excruciating.&nbsp; This exercise is left to the reader, <a href="http://www.sun.com/bigadmin/features/articles/jumpstart_x86_x64.jsp">here's a good start</a> to understanding the jumpstart setup process.</li></ul><p>Here's how to set up the remote console environment:</p><p>1. A null modem cable must be physically connected between the <i>remote host </i>and <i>target</i>.&nbsp; The most common cable required will be a DB-9 female-to-female connector.&nbsp; Your configuration may vary.</p><p>2. Check the BIOS of the <i>remote host</i> and <i>target</i> and make sure serial ports are enabled.</p><p>3. Running Solaris on the <i>remote host</i>, we'll be using the <a href="http://docs.sun.com/app/docs/doc/816-0210/6m6nb7mn9?a=view"><font face="courier new,courier,monospace">tip(1)</font></a> command to access the <i>target</i> via serial port.&nbsp; Edit the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">/etc/remote</font> file and look for the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">hardwire:</font> entry.&nbsp; Modify it to look like this:</p><pre>hardwire:\\<br />        :dv=/dev/term/a:br#9600:el=\^C\^S\^Q\^U\^D:ie=%$:oe=\^D:<br /><br /></pre><p>4. As part of setting up a jumpstart install for the <i>target</i>, a series of files are created in the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">/tftpboot</font> directory of the jumpstart server.&nbsp; Under <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">/tftpboot</font>, there should be a custom <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">menu.lst</font> file for each managed install, suffixed by the unique network MAC address of the system in question.&nbsp; For example, the network MAC address for the CF-30 in question is 0:b:97:db:c0:97.&nbsp; The related /tftpboot file for the CF-30 turns out to be <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">/tftpboot/menu.lst.01000B97DBC097.</font> As your target will have a different MAC address, it's <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">menu.lst</font>&nbsp; file will have a different suffix in the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">/tftpboot</font> directory.&nbsp; Edit that custom menu.lst file (for example, <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">/tftpboot/menu.lst.01000B97DBC097)</font> to look as follows:</p><pre>default=0<br />timeout=30<br />serial --unit=0 --speed=9600<br />terminal serial<br />title Solaris_10 s10x_u5b10<br />        kernel /I86PC.Solaris_10-1/multiboot kernel/unix -B console=ttya,install_media=192.168.1.5:/export/s10x_u5b10 -vkd<br />        module /I86PC.Solaris_10-1/x86.miniroot<br /><br /></pre><p>The key modifications here involve (1) inclusion of the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">serial --unit=0 --speed=9600</font> and <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">terminal serial</font> lines plus (2) additional arguments added to the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">kernel</font> directive.&nbsp; Grub is very fussy about the order and placement of arguments; playing around with these will likely change grub's behavior.&nbsp;</p><p>5.&nbsp; From the <i>remote host</i>, access the serial console of the <i>target</i> by issuing:</p><pre>$ tip hardwire</pre><p>6. Inside a terminal window, here's what the serial console looks like, after the system has been power cycled and runs through the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power-on_self-test">POST</a> sequence:<br /></p><p><img vspace="0" hspace="0" border="0" align="bottom" src="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/resource/remote-console/grub_console.jpg" /><br /></p><p>After the miniroot is loaded, you'll be presented with an mdb prompt and a screen which looks like this:</p><p><img vspace="0" hspace="0" border="0" align="bottom" src="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/resource/remote-console/mdb_prompt.jpg" /></p><p>You can now issue mdb commands to diagnose.&nbsp; In this scenario you should also be able to reboot the&nbsp; system without any other manual intervention, like this:</p><p><img vspace="0" hspace="0" border="0" align="bottom" src="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/resource/remote-console/mdb_reboot.jpg" />&nbsp;</p><p>Here's what issuing the mdb commands '<font face="courier new,courier,monospace">:c</font>' and '<font face="courier new,courier,monospace">$c</font>' look like in this environment.&nbsp;&nbsp; From this simple&nbsp; trace we can ascertain that the SATA drivers were never even loaded.&nbsp; Turns out this is likely a VM problem.&nbsp; Here's the filed <a href="http://bugs.opensolaris.org/view_bug.do?bug_id=6687394">bug report</a>.</p><p><img vspace="0" hspace="0" border="0" align="bottom" src="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/resource/remote-console/simple_mdb.jpg" /><br /></p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: guest
EMAIL: null
URL: null
DATE: 08/14/2008 10:27:51

<p>Rainer,</p>


<p>For the sake of this discussion, the Solaris boot process takes place in two phases.  You have modified the menu.lst file which associates the console with the GRUB phase.  My guess is that the console at the Solaris level is not configured the way you want.   Take a look at the bootenv.rc file and specifically the 'setprop console ...' line to see what it says.</p>


<p>Ultimately, once this file is modified it needs to be placed in the boot_archive which is referenced by GRUB at boot time.  By default, at shutdown time, the boot_archive service runs to update the boot_archive if any of its component files are changed.</p>


<p>Hope this helps.  There are lots of other resources on the net which explain this far better and in far more detail.</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Reduced Footprint Java SE: Bringing Java Standard Edition Down to Size
DATE: 03/26/2008 11:50:16
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
A <a href="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/entry/reducing_your_java_se_runtime">previous blog post</a> demonstrated how you can, with minimal effort, lessen the disk footprint of a typical Java SE 5.0 runtime environment by about a third without violating the <a href="http://java.sun.com/j2se/1.5.0/jdk-1_5_0_14-license.txt">Java Standard Edition Licensing agreement</a>. That post focused primarily on removing optional files and compressing class library jar files.&nbsp;  It turns out that with a little more engineering,&nbsp; there is significant opportunity for further space optimization.</p><p>These additional savings involve more intimate knowledge of the inner workings of Java SE.&nbsp; Sun performs this engineering work and provides a series of <a href="http://java.sun.com/javase/embedded/#functionality">reduced footprint versions of Java SE</a>, in binary form, for some of the most common embedded platforms.&nbsp; They include some of these enhancements:<sup>1</sup></p><ul><li><b>Headless configuration</b> - The inclusion of graphics subsystems like AWT and Swing comprise a large chunk of the Java SE footprint.&nbsp; If your device has no graphics capability (i.e. is headless) why would you need to include this functionality?&nbsp; Headless configurations:</li></ul><blockquote><ol><li>Do not support keyboard or mouse input</li><li>Cannot create windows or display graphics</li><li>Throw a <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">HeadlessException</font> when a graphics API is called</li><li>Still support a functional Java 2D API for printing and off-screen rendering</li><li>Are still 100% Java SE compatible<br /></li></ol></blockquote><ul><li><b>Eliminate client or server compiler</b> - Sun's Java SE implementations include two HotSpot compilers, tuned and designed for specific environments.&nbsp; The client compiler focuses on things like fast user interactivity and quick startup, while the server compiler's priority is on optimizing large, long-lived server applications.&nbsp; The Java SE VM can only use one of these compilers at a time, so removing the unused one can save considerable space.</li></ul><ul><li><b>Minimizes memory consumption</b> - Standard Java SE allocates RAM to house things like the JIT code cache and the object heap.&nbsp; By default, each one of these areas (and others) can be assigned 64 MB.&nbsp; In an embedded platform where the total amount of RAM might only be 64MB, Java SE will simply not have enough memory to run.&nbsp; Java SE Embedded on the other hand, will automatically adapt memory usage to the amount of available RAM.&nbsp; Consequently, 64MB is a reasonable amount of RAM for a large set of embedded Java applications.</li></ul><ul><li><b>Space vs. speed tradeoffs</b> - (1) Java SE implements a thread lookup table, which in layman's terms, helps save a few instructions when switching between Java threads.&nbsp; By eliminating this table, a couple of MBs of RAM can be spared from your application's working set. (2) Java SE also sets aside an area (mmap'ed) for loading jar files into random access memory, which as was explained to me, may help performance, but may also result in duplicate copies of jar files in memory.&nbsp; Removal of this area further reduces the Resident Set Size.</li></ul><p><b>An Example</b></p><p>Following through with the exmaple in the last post., let's start with an unmodified version of <a href="http://java.sun.com/products/archive/j2se/5.0_13/index.html">Java SE 5.0 Update 13 for Linux/x86</a>.&nbsp; By default, the static footprint is approximately 88MB.<br /></p><pre><font face="courier new,courier,monospace">   jimc@jalopy:/tmp&gt; <b>du -sk ./jre1.5.0_13/</b><br />   88185&nbsp;&nbsp; ./jre1.5.0_13/</font><br /></pre>After following the directions in the <a href="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/entry/reducing_your_java_se_runtime">previous post</a>, we can pare it down to roughly 60MB.<br /><pre>   jimc@jalopy:/tmp&gt; <b>du -sk /tmp/jre1.5.0_13/</b><br />   59358   /tmp/jre1.5.0_13/ <br /></pre><p>Downloading <a href="http://java.sun.com/javase/downloads/embedded.jsp">Sun's reduced footprint version of Java SE for x86/Linux</a> yields:<!--<--><br /></p><pre>&nbsp;&nbsp; jimc@jalopy:/tmp&gt; <b>du -sk /tmp/jre1.5.0_10/</b><br /><b></b>   31003   /tmp/jre1.5.0_10/<br /></pre><pre></pre><p>This version of the JRE is about one-third it's original size, and furthermore has been modified to use significantly less memory than the standard Java SE offerings.&nbsp; Note: we are comparing slightly different updates of Java SE 1.5 (update 13 vs. update 10).&nbsp; They are indeed not identical but their differences should be considered, for the sake of argument, negligible.&nbsp;</p><p><font size="1">[1] Many thanks to Bob Vandette, who though presentation and conversation, supplied this information.&nbsp; One of many sources comes from Bob's Java ONE 2007 session called, <a title="http://developers.sun.com/learning/javaoneonline/2007/pdf/TS-2602.pdf"><i>Deploying Java Platform Standard Edition (Java SE Platform) in Today's Embedded Devices</i> (TS-2602)</a></font> <br /></p><p><br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Reducing Your Java SE Runtime Environment Footprint (Legally)
DATE: 02/14/2008 08:29:41
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>Because the resource requirements for <a href="http://java.sun.com/javase/index.jsp">Java Standard Edition</a> are by no means insignificant, developers interested in utilizing Java on smaller platforms have traditionally opted for one of the <a href="http://java.sun.com/javame/index.jsp">Java Micro Edition</a> configurations.&nbsp;&nbsp; It should therefore come as no surprise that some of the Standard Edition functionality has to be sacrificed in these constrained Java ME environments.&nbsp; However, as the trend towards more capable devices continues, it becomes reasonable to once again consider the advantages of utilizing Java SE.&nbsp; Unfortunately, with a static footprint that could easily exceed 100MB, Java SE may still be too large to swallow for a lot of applications.<br /><br />It is duly noted that the criticism leveled at Sun for exacting too much control
over the Java platform has been widespread.&nbsp; Like it or not though, one benefit of Sun's initial stewardship has been that Java SE
has remained a standard platform, and threats to splinter it have thus
far been reasonably thwarted.&nbsp; Accordingly, in order to avoid incompatibilities, there are restrictions spelled out in the <a href="http://java.sun.com/j2se/1.5.0/jdk-1_5_0_14-license.txt">Java SE Licensing Agreement</a> which prohibit modification of the Java SE binary distribution.</p><p>That&nbsp; being said, there are a list of optional files, specified by the Java Runtime Environment's <a href="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/resource/java_se/JRE_SAMPLE_README.txt"><font face="courier new,courier,monospace">README</font></a> file which can be removed, without ramification, to lessen the footprint.&nbsp; They include:<br /></p><ul><li>Deployment tools (e.g. Java Web Start, Java plugin)</li><li>IDL and RMI tools (e.g. rmid, tnameserv)<br /> </li><li>Security tools (e.g. policytool, keytool)</li><li>orbd</li><li>Localization (charsets.jar)</li></ul><p>In addition, further space optimization can be achieved by compressing the class library files contained in the <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">rt.jar</font> file.&nbsp; By default, Java SE ships this jar file uncompressed.&nbsp; The tradeoff here is space vs. performance, i.e. the classloader must expend cycles to uncompress the Java classes as they are being loaded.</p><p><b>An Example</b></p><p>So let's download a sample JRE from <a href="http://java.sun.com">java.sun.com</a> and see how it's footprint can be minimized.&nbsp;&nbsp; For this example, we'll use <a href="http://java.sun.com/products/archive/j2se/5.0_13/index.html">Java SE 1.5.0 Update 13 for Linux x86</a>.</p><p>After installation, the JRE is approximately 88MB</p><pre><font face="courier new,courier,monospace">   jimc@jalopy:/tmp&gt; <b>du -sk ./jre1.5.0_13/</b><br />   88185&nbsp;&nbsp; ./jre1.5.0_13/</font><br /></pre><p>Here's what it looks like after removal of the optional files<br /></p><pre>   jimc@jalopy:/tmp&gt; <b>cd jre1.5.0_13/</b><br /><b></b><br />   jimc@jalopy:/tmp/jre1.5.0_13&gt; <b>/bin/rm -rf lib/charsets.jar lib/ext/sunjce_provider.jar \\</b><br /><b>   lib/ext/localedata.jar lib/ext/ldapsec.jar </b><b>lib/ext/dnsns.jar bin/rmid \\</b><br /><b>   bin/rmiregistry bin/tnameserv bin/keytool bin/kinit bin/klist bin/ktab \\</b><br /><b>   bin/policytool bin/orbd bin/servertool bin/javaws, lib/javaws/ and lib/javaws.jar</b><br /><b></b><br />   jimc@jalopy:/tmp/jre1.5.0_13&gt; <b>cd ..</b><b></b><br />   jimc@jalopy:/tmp&gt; <b>du -sk ./jre1.5.0_13/</b><br />   77227   ./jre1.5.0_13/<br /></pre><p>And after rt.jar has been compressed</p><pre>   jimc@jalopy:/tmp&gt; <b>mkdir rtjar</b><br />   jimc@jalopy:/tmp&gt; <b>cd rtjar/</b><br /><b></b><br />   jimc@jalopy:/tmp/rtjar&gt; <b>jar -xf /tmp/jre1.5.0_13/lib/rt.jar</b><br /><b></b>   jimc@jalopy:/tmp/rtjar&gt; <b>zip -q -r /tmp/rt .</b><br />   jimc@jalopy:/tmp/rtjar&gt; <b>mv /tmp/rt.zip /tmp/jre1.5.0_13/lib/rt.jar</b><br /><b></b><br />   jimc@jalopy:/tmp/rtjar&gt; <b>du -sk /tmp/jre1.5.0_13/</b><br />   59358   /tmp/jre1.5.0_13/  </pre><p><b>Conclusion</b></p><p>In many cases, you can lop off about a third of the Java Runtime Environment footprint with no ill effects.&nbsp; In a future post, we'll discuss how Sun has further reduced Java SE significantly, not only from the point of view of static footprint, but also from a RAM usage perspective too.&nbsp; For a preview you can check out Sun's <a href="http://java.sun.com/javase/embedded/">Java SE Embedded</a> technology.<br /><br /></p>
-----
COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jos Hirth
EMAIL: blogs.sun@kaioa.com
URL: http://kaioa.com
DATE: 03/27/2008 08:43:08

<p>Compressing rt.jar like this doesn't make much sense. Deflate isn't all that great after all. Most installers utilize LZMA is is far superior to Deflate.</p>


<p>The best strategy is using Pack200 (without GZip compression) and an installer which uses LZMA on top. The installer also has to take care of unpack200-ing the jars after the initial decompression. This is fairly easy with NSIS for example.</p>


<p>Of course this strategy is meant to reduce the size of the download. The used disk space after installation won't be affected. Well, it would be possible to recompress the jars (after un-lzma-ing and unpack200-ing). The price is a slightly longer install and slightly longer loading times. It's not that bad though - Java's deflate routines are highly optimized.</p>


<p>Well, 6uN (that Kernel thing) will solve most of this issue.</p>


-----
COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jim Connors
EMAIL: james.connors@sun.com
URL: null
DATE: 03/27/2008 10:12:33

<p>Your points are quite valid with regards to installation.  The theme of this post was to demonstrate how to optimize the disk footprint of an \*already installed\* JRE without significant re-engineering.    Although a generic solution, I think these suggestions apply more to the embedded systems folks than they do to general purpose desktop/server solutions.  In many instances in the embedded world, installation is done with entire images, rather than using traditional package installation.</p>


<p>Using zip to compress jar files is simple and universal, does shrink the the jar files by about a half, and is understood by the jar classloader.  It would be cool to use even better compression, but that would require a  modified classloader.  If there are alternatives that the stock classloaders understand, I'd sincerely like to hear about them.    If we could further reduce the on-disk footprint, that would be great.</p>


-----
COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jos Hirth
EMAIL: blogs.sun@kaioa.com
URL: http://kaioa.com
DATE: 03/28/2008 00:11:56

<p>Zip/Deflate compression can be improved by a few percent with tools like DeflOpt (formerly BJWFlate), KZip, and ZipMix. The first two just try very hard to compress the files as good as possible and ZipMix takes two zips and puts the best compressed individual files into a new zip file.</p>


<p><a href="http://www.advsys.net/ken/utils.htm" rel="nofollow">http://www.advsys.net/ken/utils.htm</a><br/>
<a href="http://www.walbeehm.com/download/" rel="nofollow">http://www.walbeehm.com/download/</a></p>


<p>Obfuscators can also help quite a bit. Using multiple obfuscators can even help more. However, generally it's a &quot;bit&quot; on the crazy side. It's something we all did over at JGO (<a href="http://javagaming.org" rel="nofollow">http://javagaming.org</a> ) for the 4k competitions. It's only worth it if every byte counts. (But even then it's crazy.)</p>


<p>Well, the only kind of archives you can directly load are stored jars and deflated jars. Pack200 can be quickly added with a custom classloader. However, the format isn't suited for loading specific classes and the format itself isn't really well compressed (it's highly compressable though). While Pack200 helps compression immensely, it's only good for reducing the size of a download (applet, webstart, installer etc).</p>


<p>Here is some diagram of the effect of Deflate, LZMA, GZip, and Pack200 (on a pure code jar):<br/>
<a href="http://kaioa.com/svg/compression_ratio.svgz" rel="nofollow">http://kaioa.com/svg/compression_ratio.svgz</a> (looks better with Opera)</p>


<p>Some mix of LZMA and Pack200 with directly accessible files would be great, but I don't really think that's something we'll see any time soon. It's just too specific and it would be sort of slow.</p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jim Connors
EMAIL: james.connors@sun.com
URL: null
DATE: 05/18/2008 21:22:07

<p>Compressing rt.jar will have some adverse effect on startup.  It appears to be proportional to the clock speed of the embedded CPU.  For clocks in excess of 1Ghz, this may be negligible.  For slower clock cycles the effect can be modest.  You mileage will vary, but we have some unofficial anecdotal evidence of 10% or more degradation in some cases.  Of course you could always uncompress rt.jar and forego this space optimization altogether.</p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Zeugme
EMAIL: zeugme@nowhere.com
URL: null

<p>I may have a slot for a performance test un few months. I was wondering the inpact of compression knowing that disk IO are slower than memory IO. Compressing should minimize disk IO (using more CPU) and finally be quicker ...<br/>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Good Things Come To Those Who Wait
DATE: 01/31/2008 06:52:51
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>Java RTS 2.1 EA (Early Access) marks the arrival of a commitment made some time back, namely that Sun would provide a version of the Java Real-Time System for Linux.&nbsp; Perhaps to some, it was long time in the making, but in fact there are at least 2 good reasons why a Linux version wasn't available till now:</p><ol><li>Until recently, there was no standard Linux release/kernel which had true real-time support.&nbsp; Typically the versions available were non-standard and did not constitute any considerable volume.&nbsp; Mainstream Linux distributions are only now incorporating the necessary real-time underpinnings.</li><li>Porting the Java Real-Time System VM to additional platforms is non-trivial.</li></ol><p>Support and testing for Java RTS 2.1 EA at this time is limited to the currently shipping <a title="Red Hat Enterprise MRG 1.0" href="http://www.novell.com/products/realtime/">SUSE Linux Enterprise Real Time10</a> platform and the upcoming <a title="Red Hat Enterprise MRG 1.0" href="http://www.redhat.com/mrg">Red Hat Enterprise MRG 1.0</a> release.&nbsp; It is however possible that other versions of Linux could run Java RTS 2.1 EA as it utilizes the real-time POSIX programming interface.&nbsp; At minimum they would require a 2.6.21 kernel or greater and a glibc of 2.5 or greater.&nbsp; In addition, the latest RT patch would also be needed.</p><p>This announcement pertains only to Linux, but of course a 2.1 EA version for both Solaris x86/x64 and Sparc will be shortly forthcoming. In the interim, a version of Java RTS 2.0 update 1 is readily available.&nbsp; Documentation for both Java RTS 2.0 and 2.1 EA can be found <a href="http://java.sun.com/javase/technologies/realtime/reference/rts_productdoc.html" title="here">here</a>.<br /> </p><p>Regardless of platform, an evaluation version of the software is available for download at the <a title="Java RTS download page" href="http://java.sun.com/javase/technologies/realtime/rts/">Java RTS download page</a>.<br /><br />&nbsp;</p>
-----

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: James Connors
EMAIL: James.Connors@Sun.COM
URL: null
DATE: 09/11/2008 09:21:23

<p>Currently, the cyclic driver that comes with Java RTS is specific to a particular Solaris 10 update release.  During installation, if your version of the OS doesn't match what Java RTS requires, it won't install.</p>


<p>You'd need the source code for the cyclic driver and recompile on your platform.  Unfortunately this isn't public.  In the future we hope to better decouple the cyclic driver dependency from Solaris, but for now, if you're interested in using Java RTS on Solaris, you'll have to choose one of the supported platforms.</p>


<p>FYI, for a little more info on the cyclic driver, check out this blog:<br/>
<a href="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/entry/real_time_java_and_high" rel="nofollow">http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/entry/real_time_java_and_high</a></p>


<p>-- Jim C</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Java RTS 2.0 Update 1 Released
DATE: 12/19/2007 12:37:07
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>After blogging about the <a href="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/entry/real_time_java_and_high">relationship between high resolution timers and Java RTS</a>, a <a href="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/entry/real_time_java_and_high#comment-1190962811000">comment was posted</a> by a reader stating that they couldn't use Java RTS 2.0 with the latest Solaris 10 update.&nbsp; Unfortunately that reader was correct.</p><p>At the time of the post, Sun had released Solaris 10 Update 4, while Java RTS 2.0 would only support Solaris 10 update 3.&nbsp; To further exacerbate the situation, once Sun releases a new version of Solaris, they do their best to encourage users to use the latest version by making it difficult (if not impossible) for them to locate previous update versions.</p><p>There is a dependency between the aforementioned high resolution timer interface and Solaris which is update specific.&nbsp; We're working to rectify this situation, but as the process behind Solaris is deliberately bureaucratic, this might take a while.&nbsp; In the interim we'll need to keep in better lock step with Solaris releases.</p><p>On November 27, Java RTS 2.0 update 1 was released:&nbsp;</p><ul><li>It supports the latest Solaris 10 update (called 08/07 or update 4)</li><li>Support for Java SE has been updated to Java SE 5 update 13<br /></li><li>A new Java <a href="http://java.sun.com/javase/technologies/realtime/reference/doc/release/JavaRTSDTraceProvider.html">RTS DTrace Provider</a> is available</li><li>Other enhancements are listed <a href="http://java.sun.com/javase/technologies/realtime/reference/doc/release/JavaRTSReleaseNotes.html#enhancements">here</a><br /></li></ul><p>&nbsp;</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: General Purpose Always Wins ... Why Not for the Real-Time Market Too?
DATE: 10/15/2007 06:19:02
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>
The brief but brilliant era of computing has seen companies come and go, many relegated to the ash heap of history because they failed to heed this simple rule:&nbsp;&nbsp;
<br />&nbsp;<br />&nbsp; <i>In the long run general purpose solutions will always win out over specialized proprietary ones.&nbsp;</i>
<br />&nbsp;<br />As a long time employee of Sun Microsystems, I've witnessed firsthand the effects, both positive and negative, that this law has had on my company.&nbsp; Sun can attribute it's initial meteoric rise to the fact that it offered a viable alternative to the popular minicomputer platform of the day.&nbsp; The first Sun workstations were built from commercial off-the-shelf components, and although nearly equal in performance to the minicomputer, they were so much more affordable that they became good enough.&nbsp; And of course over time, as Moore's law might dictate, those initial deficiencies quickly dissipated, and in fact surpassed traditional minicomputer capabilities.<br />&nbsp;<br />Somewhere along the line Sun lost sight of this ideal and began incorporating more proprietary technology into their products.&nbsp; At first the strategy appeared to be successful as Sun was well positioned to reap huge benefits during the Internet bubble.&nbsp; Meanwhile, low-cost general purpose servers were continuously improving.&nbsp; When the bubble burst they in turn became good enough alternatives to the powerful but costly Sun servers.&nbsp; The resulting decline of Sun was rapid, and it's taken the better part of a decade for us to recover.&nbsp;&nbsp; This story has been told -- and will continue to be again and again -- for those refusing to learn this lesson.&nbsp; A professor of mine once told me, &quot;If there's anything we've learned from history, it's that we haven't learned anything from history&quot;.</p><p>When markets mature, even those where technology is paramount, economic considerations dominate. General purpose systems scale on every dimension (unit, management, training and integration costs) whereas proprietary systems do not.&nbsp; A switch to more standard components should in no way be construed as stifling innovation.&nbsp; Rather, general purpose systems help create new innovation by building from common elements economically appealing in their own right, and presumably even more economically beneficial once combined.<sup>1</sup></p><blockquote><font size="1">[1] The above paragraph was taken in bits and pieces from an email exchange with Dave Hofert, Java Embedded and Real-Time Marketing Manager.&nbsp; His points were so compelling I couldn't help myself.</font><br /></blockquote><p>Real-time industrial controllers could be the next market ripe for disruption.&nbsp; Admittedly this is an entrenched and conservative lot.&nbsp; But the economics of general purpose computing cannot be denied.&nbsp; As organizations strive to further eliminate cost out of their system, revisiting usage and deployment of industrial controllers, typically via custom proprietary <a title="PLCs" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Programmable_logic_controller">PLCs</a>, is falling under review.&nbsp;
Consequently, at the behest of one of the world's largest industrial corporations, Sun has partnered with <a href="http://www.igologic.com/" title="iGoLogic">iGoLogic</a>, a systems integrator, and <a href="http://www.axiomtek.com/" title="Axiomtek">Axiomtek</a>, an industrial PC board manufacturer, to create the real-time controller platform pictured below.<br />&nbsp;<br />Among others, here are some of the compelling benefits of this platform:&nbsp;
<br /></p><ul><li><b>It's based on standard x86 hardware.</b>&nbsp; The motherboards aren't much larger than a postcard, are energy efficient, and yet have PC class processing power.&nbsp; The number of competing manufacturers in this space eliminates vendor lock-in and insures price sensitivity and further rapid innovation.
</li></ul><ul><li><b>It runs on standard <a title="Solaris" href="http://www.sun.com/software/solaris/">Solaris</a></b><b>.</b>&nbsp; No custom proprietary real-time operating system is required.&nbsp; <a title="One of the best kept secrets of Solaris" href="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/entry/solaris_was_real_time_before">One of the best kept secrets of Solaris</a> is its real-time capabilities.
</li></ul><ul><li><b>Real-time applications are developed using <a title="Sun's Java Real-Time System" href="http://java.sun.com/javase/technologies/realtime/index.jsp">Sun's Java Real-Time System</a></b>, enabling you to leverage the largest development community on the planet.&nbsp; Obscure development languages and highly specialized environments are longer needed. <br /></li></ul><ul><li>Industrial Networking Protocols (e.g <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Profibus" title="Profibus">PROFIBUS</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethercat" title="Ethercat">EtherCAT</a>) are easily migrated to this platform, partly because of the wealth of development tools available.</li></ul><ul><li>The system utilizes an IDE flash drive from <a href="http://usa.apacer.com/us/products/Embedded_Flash_Solutions_PATA_Flash_Series.htm" title="Apacer">Apacer</a>.&nbsp; In addition to eliminating the moving parts associated with a traditional disk drive, it consumes less power and makes the system more resistant to shock and vibration.&nbsp; Overcoming the longevity limitations of flash memory, Apacer has done some interesting work on wear leveling algorithms effectively extending the lifetime of the flash device well past the expected lifetime of the industrial controller.<br /></li></ul><p><img vspace="0" hspace="0" border="0" align="bottom" src="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/resource/rt-industrial/fig3.jpg" /></p><p><img vspace="0" hspace="0" border="0" align="bottom" src="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/resource/rt-industrial/fig2.jpg" /></p><p>&nbsp;<br />Let this be our first salvo fired over the proprietary industrial encampment.&nbsp; We believe the opportunity is immense, but also understand that to achieve any measure of success, partnering with organizations who are truly experts in this arena is critical.&nbsp; If you think you can add further value, we'd love to talk.<br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Real-Time Java and High Resolution Timers
DATE: 06/19/2007 06:57:47
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>Any modern x86/x64 processor worth its salt comes equipped with an <a title="Advanced Programmable Interrupt Controller" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/APIC">Advanced Programmable Interrupt Controller</a>, or APIC.&nbsp; Among the features that an APIC provides is access to high resolution timers.&nbsp; Without such capability, the default interrupt source for timer and cyclic operations has a precision on the order of 10 milliseconds -- hardly fine-grained enough for any serious real-time work.</p><p>The <a title="cyclic subsystem" href="http://blogs.sun.com/eschrock/date/20041127">cyclic subsystem</a>, introduced in Solaris 8, gives Solaris the capability to support high resolution timers.&nbsp; The <a title="Sun Java Real-Time System" href="http://java.sun.com/javase/technologies/realtime/index.jsp">Sun Java Real-Time System</a> version 2.0, available for Solaris on both x86 and Sparc platforms, includes an additional package called <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">SUNWrtjc</font>:&nbsp; <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">Java Real-Time System cyclic driver</font>.&nbsp; This package exposes an interface to the cyclic subsystem, normally only available to the kernel.&nbsp; For those already familiar with Sun's Java RTS, you've no doubt noticed that you either need to run as superuser or assign a set of fine-grained privileges to an ordinary user account. (<font face="courier new,courier,monospace">sys_res_config, proc_priocntl, proc_lock_memory</font><i> </i>and<i> </i><font face="courier new,courier,monospace">proc_clock_highres</font><i>).&nbsp; </i>The<i> </i><font face="courier new,courier,monospace">proc_clock_highres</font> privilege gives access to those timers.<i><br /></i></p><p>Originally developed on an AMD Athlon-based PC, I recently moved a Real-Time Java project over to my Toshiba Tecra A1 laptop running the same version of Solaris and Java RTS.&nbsp; With the goal of getting in a little development time during a long flight, that migration suddenly casued timings to be all wrong.&nbsp; Why, might you ask, would moving an application from one seemingly identical Solaris environment to another cause this unusual behavior?&nbsp; Turns out that even though the laptop, a Pentium 4M based system, has an APIC, it was never exposed by the laptop BIOS.&nbsp; In this scenario, regardless what you do from a Solaris perspective, you'll never get access to the high-res timers.&nbsp; </p><p>This phenomenon appears to be most prevelant in laptops.&nbsp; As they account for about 50% (or more?) of PCs sold today, developers have a realistic chance of running into this situation.&nbsp; You can issue this magic Solaris incantation to determine if your system has high-res timer support:</p><p>&nbsp;&nbsp; <font face="courier new,courier,monospace"># echo &quot;psm_timer_reprogram::print&quot;&nbsp; | mdb -k</font><br />
<br />If anything but '<font face="courier new,courier,monospace">apic_timer_reprogram</font>' is then displayed, your machine
has no exposed APIC, and is probably unsuitable for Java RTS.&nbsp; In some cases the BIOS may be configurable with regards to APIC support; in many others it is simply not available.<br /></p><p>In the absence of an APIC, there is the potential to improve the high-resolution timing by setting the following tunable in <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">/etc/system</font>:
<br />
<br />&nbsp;&nbsp; <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">set hires_tick=1
</font><br />
<br />Following a reboot, this would change the clock tick frequency from 100 to 1000 ticks per second. This frequency can then be further tuned by setting the hires_hz tunable to the desired value:
<br />
<br />&nbsp;&nbsp; <font face="courier new,courier,monospace">set hires_hz=10000</font>
<br />
<br />The default value is 1000 ticks per second; higher values are not
officially supported.
<br />
<br />Note that tuning your machine in this way does not come without cost.&nbsp; It is likely to degrade overall
performance, as the system will need to spend a larger proportion of time handling the larger frequency of clock interrupts.<sup>1</sup></p><p>&nbsp;&nbsp; <font size="1">[1] Thank you&nbsp; Christophe Lizzi for your explanation of the problem and potential workaround.</font><br />&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;</p>
-----

<p>I don't understand how setting hires_tick=1 changes the clock tick frequency from 100 to 1000 ticks per second. COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: James Connors
EMAIL: James.Connors@Sun.COM
URL: null
DATE: 01/12/2009 18:09:20

<p>Trevor,</p>


<p>I just did a little experimenting here.  In order to specify a higher clock frequency, you must first set hires_tick=1 regardless what you do with hires_hz.  Placing an entry in /etc/system which looks like this:</p>


<p>   set hires_tick=1</p>


<p>is identical to:</p>


<p>   set hires_tick=1<br/>
   set hires_hz=1000</p>


<p>To set an even higher clock tick frequency (at your own peril), say 10000, edit /etc/system to look like this:</p>


<p>   set hires_tick=1<br/>
   set hires_hz=10000</p>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: James Connors
EMAIL: James.Connors@Sun.COM
URL: null
DATE: 01/16/2009 07:51:31

<p>Re: Can Java RTS be installed on OpenSolaris?</p>


<p>1) First off, is it not supported.</p>


<p>2) The cyclic driver mentioned above is compiled specifically for a Solaris release.  In order for it to work on the version of OpenSolaris you're using, it's source would have to be re-compiled on a system running the same version.  We hope to decouple this dependency in the future.</p>


<p>3) Unfortunately the cyclic driver source code is not publicly available.</p>


<p>3) Without the cyclic driver, as mentioned above, you won't get high resolution timers.  You can increase the clock rate in Solaris as the blog entry suggests to get a finer resolution, and try running JavaRTS.  For the overhead ramifications take a look at this follow-on entry:</p>


<p><a href="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/entry/overhead_in_increasing_the_solaris" rel="nofollow">http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/entry/overhead_in_increasing_the_solaris</a></p>


<p>Cheers</p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Real-time Java Meets Wall Street
DATE: 03/30/2007 07:46:43
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>Jim Clarke, Eric Bruno and I have been selected to present this year at <a title="Java ONE" href="http://java.sun.com/javaone/sf/">Java ONE</a>.&nbsp; The title of our session is called &quot;TS-1205 The Sun Java Real-Time System Meets Wall Street&quot;.&nbsp; We're scheduled to present on Wednsday, May 9 at 10:55AM - 11:55AM Pacific in the Moscone Convention Center in San Francisco.&nbsp; If you plan on attending, please stop by and see us.&nbsp; For those that can't make the event, we'll be sure to post the slides once we're permitted.<br /></p><p>To get an idea of what we'll discuss, take a look at the abstract below;&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;ABSTRACT</p><p><i>Today's financial institutions use program trading systems to automatically execute split-second trades based on sophisticated algorithms that assess current market conditions. Trade decisions and execution must occur in a timely fashion to capitalize on the market. Missing a trade opportunity, even by a few seconds, can lead to significant losses.<br /><br />With improvements in Java technology performance, firms are starting to use Java technology to implement these algorithms and thereby realizing productivity gains over more-traditional C/C++ development. However, in these time-critical systems, there is still no guarantee that at any instant, the process will not be interrupted by the Java platform garbage collector. Although trade execution occurs most of the time within an acceptable response time, the trade execution is delayed every once in a while, due to garbage collection or some other system event.<br /><br />Sun's implementation of the Real-Time Specification for Java (JSR 001), the Sun Java Real-Time System, enables real-time processing through techniques that protect important threads from garbage collection and other system interrupts. This means that trading systems can confidently monitor the market and take action consistently within a calculated window of opportunity.<br /><br />To demonstrate the impact of these techniques, Sun's OEM Software Systems Engineering technical team has written a demonstration of a trading system that uses real trade data. The demonstration compares a regular Java virtual machine against the Sun Java Real-Time System. For each run, a graph shows the difference in the actual trade price and the price when the trade should have executed. Running this trading system with the Sun Java Real-Time System shows that no money is lost due to garbage collection latencies. The results will be contrasted with the same application run with the standard (non-real-time) Java virtual machine.<br /><br />This presentation demonstrates a working system and proves real-time Java technology's ability to satisfy hard real-time trading requirements.</i><br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Crosstool Environment in a Solaris Zone
DATE: 03/10/2017 23:25:02
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p><span style="font-weight: bold;">Background</span><br />
The task of building Java ME CDC-HI binaries and their associated cross
development environments tends to be very linux-centric.&nbsp; Utilities like <a href="http://www.kegel/crosstool">Crosstool</a>,
which make
this process much more tolerable, also make various linux and GNU
assumptions that differ from
standard Solaris.&nbsp; By introducing new paths and GNU versions of
applications, it is possible to mimic this environment in
Solaris.&nbsp;&nbsp; Furthermore, by creating a new zone with this environment, we
can isolate these changes without affecting other Solaris system
settings.<br />
<br />
1. Create a Solaris zone called 'toolzone'</p><p><i>Note: This step is system specific.&nbsp; For example, the hostname and IP address for 'toolzone' was predefined.&nbsp; In addition, the network interface (i.e. 'set physical=iprb0') is likely to be different also.</i><br />
<br />
Here's the command-line session for creating the zone:<br />
</p><pre>phoenix://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">mkdir /zone</span><br />phoenix://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">zonecfg -z toolzone</span><br />toolzone: No such zone configured<br />Use 'create' to begin configuring a new zone.<br />zonecfg:toolzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">create</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set zonepath=/zone/toolzone</span><br /><span style="font-weight: bold;"></span><span style="font-weight: bold;"></span>zonecfg:toolzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set autoboot=true</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">add net</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone:net&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set address=toolzone</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone:net&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set physical=iprb0</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone:net&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">end</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">info</span><br />zonepath: /zone/toolzone<br />autoboot: true<br />pool:<br />inherit-pkg-dir:<br />        dir: /lib<br />inherit-pkg-dir:<br />        dir: /platform<br />inherit-pkg-dir:<br />        dir: /sbin<br />inherit-pkg-dir:<br />        dir: /usr<br />net:<br />        address: toolzone<br />        physical: iprb0<br />zonecfg:toolzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">verify</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">commit</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">\^D<br /><br /></span>phoenix://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">zoneadm list -vc</span><br />  ID NAME             STATUS         PATH<br />   0 global           running        /<br />   - toolzone      configured        /zone/toolzone<br /><br />phoenix://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">zoneadm -z toolzone</span><span style="font-weight: bold;"> install</span><br />Preparing to install zone &lt;toolzone&gt;.<br />Creating list of files to copy from the global zone.<br />Copying &lt;6666&gt; files to the zone.<br />Initializing zone product registry.<br />Determining zone package initialization order.<br />Preparing to initialize &lt;945&gt; packages on the zone.<br />Initialized &lt;945&gt; packages on zone.<br />Zone &lt;toolzone&gt; is initialized.<br />Installation of &lt;2&gt; packages was skipped.<br />Installation of these packages generated warnings: &lt;SUNWcsu SUNWsogm&gt;<br />The file &lt;/zone/toolzone/root/var/sadm/system/logs/install_log&gt; contains a log of the zone installation.<br /></pre>2.&nbsp; Configure the zone.<br />
<br />
2a. Create a zone-specific /usr/bin directory and copy the contents of
the
global zone's /usr/bin to this new directory.<br />
<pre style="font-weight: bold;"><span style="font-weight: normal;">phoenix://#</span> mkdir -p /zone/toolzone/usr/bin<br /><span style="font-weight: normal;">phoenix://#</span> cd /usr/bin<br /><span style="font-weight: normal;">phoenix://#</span> tar cf - . | (cd /zone/toolzone/usr/bin; tar xfp -)<br /></pre>
2b. Create a loopback mount for toolzone to this new version of
/usr/bin:<br />
<pre><span style="font-weight: normal;">phoenix://</span># <span style="font-weight: bold;">zonecfg -z toolzone</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">addfs</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone:fs&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set dir=/usr/bin</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone:fs&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set special=/zone/toolzone</span><span style="font-weight: bold;">/usr/bin</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone:fs&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">set type=lofs</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone:fs&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">end</span><br />zonecfg:toolzone&gt; <span style="font-weight: bold;">\^D</span><br /></pre>
2c. Boot and configure the newly created zone:<br />
<pre>phoenix://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">zoneadm -z toolzone</span><span style="font-weight: bold;"> boot</span><br />phoenix://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">zlogin -C toolzone</span><br /></pre>
2d. Change the path of the default shell to /usr/bin/bash.&nbsp; This
is required for crosstool to operate correctly.<br />
<pre>toolzone://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">cd /usr/bin</span><br />toolzone:bin/# <span style="font-weight: bold;">mv sh sh.ORIG</span><br />toolzone:bin/# <span style="font-weight: bold;">ln -s /usr/bin/bash sh</span><br /></pre>
2e. Create a new user in toolzone.&nbsp; For this example, we'll
use 'cdc'.&nbsp; This exercise is left to the user.<br />
<pre>toolzone://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">grep cdc /etc/passwd</span><br />cdc:x:600:10:CDC-HI build user:/export/home/cdc:/usr/bin/bash<br /><span style="font-weight: bold;"></span></pre>
2f. Create a directoy called /opt/gnulinks and make sure the 'cdc' user
owns it.&nbsp; This directory will house versions and links&nbsp; to
GNU utilities which differ from their Solaris counterparts.<br />
<pre>toolzone://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">mkdir /opt/gnulinks</span><br />toolzone://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">chown cdc:staff /opt/gnulinks</span><br />toolzone://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">ls -ld /opt/gnulinks</span><br />drwxr-xr-x   2 cdc      staff        512 Jun 19 13:14 /opt/gnulinks<br /></pre>
<br />
3. As the newly created 'cdc' user, configure the zone to build both
the crosstool environment and CDC-HI<br />
<br />
3a. Login to the zone as the 'cdc' user.<br />
<pre>phoenix://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">zlogin -l cdc toolzone</span><br />[Connected to zone 'toolzone' pts/6]<br />Last login: Mon Jun 19 12:57:05 on pts/6<br />Sun Microsystems Inc.   SunOS 5.10      Generic January 2005<br />toolzone:~/$<br /></pre>
3b. To get a zone-specific prompt, you might want to have an entry
in ~cdc/.bash_profile like:<br />
<pre>export PS1='\\h:\\W/\\$ '<br /></pre>
3c.&nbsp; Create links in /opt/gnulinks/bin which point to GNU versions
of unix utilities.&nbsp; This can be accomplised by executing the <a href="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/resource/crosstool/gnulinks.sh" title="gnulinks.sh">gnulinks.sh</a> script which looks like:<br />
<pre><span style="font-weight: bold;"></span>#!/bin/sh<br />GNULINKS_DIR=/opt/gnulinks/bin<br />GNU_PROGS=&quot;ar as egrep grep m4 make nm objcopy objdump strings strip tar thumb&quot;<br /><br />mkdir -p ${GNULINKS_DIR}<br />cd ${GNULINKS_DIR}<br />for PROG in ${GNU_PROGS}<br />do<br />    ln -s /usr/sfw/bin/g${PROG} ${PROG}<br />done<br /><br />toolzone:~/$ <b>sh gnulinks.sh</b><br /></pre>
3d. Modify PATH of 'cdc' user by putting the following line in
~cdc/.bash_profile:<br />
<pre>export PATH=/opt/gnulinks/bin:/usr/sfw/bin:$PATH<br /><br />toolzone:~/$ <span style="font-weight: bold;">which tar</span><br />/opt/gnulinks/bin/tar<br /></pre><p>3e.&nbsp; Build and install GNU specific utilities required for
crosstool which differ from those provided by Solaris.&nbsp; They include
binutils, fileutils, gawk(1), patch(1) and sed(1).&nbsp; A <a href="http://blogs.sun.com/jtc/resource/crosstool/build-gnu-bins.sh" title="build-gnu-bin.sh">build-gnu-bin.sh</a> is furnished to automate this, and looks like:<br /></p><pre><span style="font-weight: bold;"></span><span style="font-weight: bold;"></span><span style="font-weight: bold;"></span><span style="font-weight: bold;"></span>#!/bin/sh<br />GNULINKS_DIR=/opt/gnulinks<br /><br />mkdir -p ${HOME}/GNU<br /><br />for PROG in $\*<br />do<br />    cd ${HOME}/GNU<br />    PREFIX=`echo ${PROG} | sed 's/-.\*//'`<br />    wget ftp://ftp.gnu.org/gnu/${PREFIX}/${PROG}.tar.gz<br />    tar xzf ${PROG}.tar.gz<br />    cd ${PROG}<br />    ./configure --prefix=${GNULINKS_DIR}<br />    make<br />    make install<br />done<br /><br />toolzone:~/$ <b>sh build-gnu-bin.sh fileutils-4.1 gawk-3.1.5 patch-2.5.4 sed-4.1.4<br /></b></pre><p>3f. Create a native version of gcc(1).&nbsp; The Solaris 10 version of gcc found in /usr/sfw/bin uses the stock Solaris linker, ld(1), found in /usr/ccs/bin.&nbsp; Later versions of glibc will only accept the GNU version of ld(1).</p><pre>toolzone:~/$ <b>cd ~/GNU</b><br />toolzone:GNU/$ <b>wget ftp://ftp.gnu.org/gnu/gcc/gcc-3.4.3/gcc-3.4.3.tar.bz2</b><br /><b></b>toolzone:GNU/$ <b>bunzip2 gcc-3.4.3.tar.bz2</b><br /><b></b><b></b>toolzone:GNU/$ <b>tar -xf gcc-3.4.3.tar</b><br />toolzone:GNU/$ <b>cd gcc-3.4.3</b><br />toolzone:gcc-3.4.3/$ <b>./configure --prefix=/opt/gnulinks</b><br />toolzone:gcc-3.4.3/$ <b>make</b> <br />toolzone:gcc-3.4.3/$ <b>make</b> <b>install</b><br /></pre><p>3g. Build and install a GNU version of binutils using the aforementioned build-gnu-bin.sh script.</p><p>&nbsp;</p><pre>toolzone:~/$ <b>sh build-gnu-bin.sh binutils-2.16</b></pre><p>&nbsp;4a.&nbsp; As the 'cdc' user, build a cross development environment using
crosstool.&nbsp; These instructions are taken from <a href="http://www.kegel.com/crosstool/crosstool-0.43/doc/crosstool-howto.html#quick" title="http://www.kegel.com/crosstool/crosstool-0.43/doc/crosstool-howto.html#quick">http://www.kegel.com/crosstool/crosstool-0.43/doc/crosstool-howto.html#quick</a><br />
</p><pre>toolzone:~/$ <span style="font-weight: bold;">cd</span><br />toolzone:~/$ <span style="font-weight: bold;">wget http://kegel.com/crosstool/crosstool-0.43.tar.gz</span><br />toolzone:~/$ <span style="font-weight: bold;">tar -xzvf crosstool-0.43.tar.gz</span><span style="font-weight: bold;"><br /></span>toolzone:~/$ <span style="font-weight: bold;">su -</span><br />toolzone://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">mkdir /opt/crosstool</span><br />toolzone://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">chown cdc:staff /opt/crosstool</span><br />toolzone://# <span style="font-weight: bold;">exit<br /></span>toolzone:~/$<br /></pre>
4b. As an example, use these configuration files to build a crosstool
for a Sharp Zaurus SL-5000D running OpenZaurus 3.5.1:<br />
<pre>toolzone:~/$ <span style="font-weight: bold;">cd ~/crosstool-0.43</span><br />toolzone:crosstool-0.43/$ <span style="font-weight: bold;">cat oz-3.5.1.sh</span><br />#!/bin/sh<br />set -ex<br />TARBALLS_DIR=$HOME/downloads<br />RESULT_TOP=/opt/crosstool<br />export TARBALLS_DIR RESULT_TOP<br />GCC_LANGUAGES=&quot;c,c++&quot;<br />export GCC_LANGUAGES<br /><br /># Really, you should do the mkdir before running this,<br /># and chown /opt/crosstool to yourself so you don't need to run as root.<br />mkdir -p $RESULT_TOP<br /><br /># Build the toolchain.  Takes a couple hours and a couple gigabytes.<br /><br />eval `cat arm-softfloat.dat oz-3.5.1.dat` sh all.sh --notest<br /><br />echo Done.<br /><br />toolzone:crosstool-0.43/$ <span style="font-weight: bold;">cat oz-3.5.1.dat<br /></span>BINUTILS_DIR=binutils-2.15<br />GCC_DIR=gcc-3.4.2<br />GLIBC_DIR=glibc-2.3.2<br />LINUX_DIR=linux-2.4.18<br />GLIBCTHREADS_FILENAME=glibc-linuxthreads-2.3.2<br />GDB_DIR=gdb-6.4<span style="font-weight: bold;"><br /></span></pre>
4c. Build the toolchain<br />
<pre>toolzone:crosstool-0.43/$ <span style="font-weight: bold;">sh oz-3.5.1.sh &gt; OUT.oz-3.5.1 2&gt;&amp;1 &amp;</span><span style="font-weight: bold;"><br /></span></pre>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Solaris Was Real-time Before Real-time Was Cool
DATE: 02/23/2007 07:37:48
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>In the financial services market, there is a general trend to move key systems close to, or even right inside the exchanges themselves -- the idea being that the nearer you are to the source, the less network infrastructure and latency you'll experience.&nbsp; With this advantage firms can potentially take on additional transaction loads at higher transaction rates.&nbsp; These systems typically use the latest Intel or AMD processors and run a commercial distribution of Linux.<sup>1</sup></p><blockquote><font size="1">[1] Thank you <a title="Eric Bruno" href="http://blogs.sun.com/ericbruno">Eric Bruno</a> for your brief description, and for unknowingly letting me (slightly) plagiarize your comments.</font><br /></blockquote><p>Indeed these co-located systems perform as expected <i>almost</i> all the time.&nbsp; But there are periodic intervals where the latency increases by several orders of magnitude, the ramifications of which could be financially disastrous.&nbsp; After eliminating other components, the street seems to be focusing its wrath on commercial Linux distributions and their lack of real-time capabilities.<br /><br />The linux community is actively working to include underpinnings to support real-time, but as of yet these capabilities are not part of a standard major (i.e. Red Hat, SuSE) distribution.&nbsp; Instead, an alternate version of linux with real-time extensions is offered.&nbsp; These extensions are in effect separate non-standard OS releases, and have not had the soak time required by many institutions.</p><p>Back in the early 90's, I volunteered to move over to Sun's newly formed SunSoft business unit.&nbsp; One of it's main charters was to push the concept of running Solaris on alternate, i.e. Intel, platforms.&nbsp; (<i>Don't get me started here, can you imagine where Solaris would be right now if Sun had actually taken this initiative seriously back then?</i>)&nbsp; As part of that transition, I had the opportunity to take a Solaris Internals course, and couldn't help but notice the effort put in architecturally to address short latencies.&nbsp; I still have the course notebook; it is dated September 1993.</p><p>The point is Solaris already has the real-time capabilities claimed by these add-on linux extensions.&nbsp; It is built into the operating system, has been for quite some time, is rock solid and doesn't require any additional components.&nbsp; A partial list of features include:</p><ul><li><b>Real-time Scheduling Class</b> - In order to allow equal opportunity to system resources, traditional Unix schedulers transparently change process priorities to give competing processes a fair chance.&nbsp; Although well suited for timesharing systems, this is unacceptable real-time behavior.&nbsp; Since its outset, Solaris through its SVR4 roots, introduced the concept of alternate scheduling classes.&nbsp; It includes a real-time scheduling class, which furnishes fixed-priority process scheduling at the highest priority levels in the system.<br /></li></ul><ul><li><b>Fine-Grained Processor Control / Processor Sets</b> - Solaris allows threads and applications to be bound to specific individual processors. In addition, processors within a system can be grouped together as a processor set and dedicated to real-time tasks.<sup>2</sup>&nbsp; Here's a nice <a title="article describing processor sets" href="http://developers.sun.com/solaris/articles/solaris_processor.html">article describing processor sets</a>. Dated June 2001,&nbsp; processor sets have been a part of Solaris since release 2.6.</li></ul><ul><li><b>Interrupt Sheltering</b> - Available since Solaris 7, this feature enables CPUs to be sheltered from unbound interrupts.&nbsp; It can be used in conjunction with processor sets to shelter all CPUs in a processor set.&nbsp; Note: At least one processor in the system must be kept unsheltered. <br /></li></ul><ul><li><b>Priority Inheritance</b> - Priority inversion occurs when a high-priority thread blocks on a resource that is held by a lower-priority thread. A runnable thread with a priority between the high and low-priority threads creates a priority inversion because it can receive processor resources ahead of the high-priority thread.&nbsp;</li></ul><blockquote>To avoid priority inversions with kernel synchronization primitives, Solaris employs a transient priority inheritance protocol. The protocol enables the low-priority thread holding the resource to “inherit” the higher priority of the blocked high-priority thread. This approach gives the blocking low-priority thread the CPU resources it needs to complete its task as soon as possible so that it can release the synchronization primitive. Upon completion, all threads are returned to their respective priorities by the kernel.<sup>3</sup><br /></blockquote><ul><li><b>High Resolution Timers</b> - 
Solaris 8 introduces the cyclic subsystem; this allows for timers
of much better granularity -- in the microsecond and nanosecond range -- without burdening the system with a high
interrupt rate.</li></ul><ul><li><b>Memory Locking</b> - The paging in and out of data from disk to memory may be considered normal behavior for virtual memory systems, but it is unacceptable for real-time applications.&nbsp; Solaris addresses this problem by allowing the
locking down of a process' pages into memory, using <a href="http://docs.sun.com/app/docs/doc/816-5168/6mbb3hrhq?a=view" title="mlock(3C)">mlock(3C)</a> or <a href="http://docs.sun.com/app/docs/doc/816-5168/6mbb3hrhr?a=view" title="mlockall(3C)">mlockall(3C)</a> system calls.<br /></li></ul><ul><li><b>Early Binding</b> - By default, linking of dynamic libraries in the Solaris
is done on an as-needed basis. The runtime binding for a dynamically linked
function isn't determined until its first invocation. Though flexible,
this behavior can induce indeterminism and unpredictable jitter in the
timing of a real-time application. To avoid jitter, the Solaris provides for early binding of dynamic libraries. By setting
the LD_BIND_NOW environment variable to &quot;1&quot;, libraries are bound at application
startup time. Using early binding together with memory locking is a very
effective approach to avoiding jitter.<sup>4</sup></li></ul><blockquote><p><font size="1">[2,3,4] Shameful plagiarism from</font><font size="1"> <a href="http://www.sun.com/software/whitepapers/wp-realtime/wp-realtime.pdf" title="Scalable Real-Time Computing in the Solaris ™ Operating Environment. A Technical White Paper">Scalable Real-Time Computing in the Solaris ™ Operating Environment. A Technical White Paper</a>. To further prove the maturity of Solaris' real-time features, this document was written in the Solaris 8 time frame.&nbsp; It was copyrighted in 2000.</font></p></blockquote><p>So why not give Solaris more consideration?&nbsp; It's way more mature.&nbsp; And in the unlikely event (chcukle, chuckle) that a lower-latency OS might not solve all your performance problems, I'd put my money on Solaris and <a href="http://www.sun.com/bigadmin/content/dtrace/" title="DTrace">DTrace</a> over anything Linux could offer in finding the real problem.<br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: The Verbal Regret Coefficient
DATE: 02/16/2007 08:40:01
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Personal
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>I am convinced that scientists will some day find an explanation for
my continual verbal ineptitude.&nbsp; It will probably be identified as a
sequence in our genome and they'll call it something like <i>The Verbal Regret Coefficient</i>.&nbsp;
We are all born with it, and we can't escape how it influences us
everyday.&nbsp; In advance of its discovery, I propose this definition: The
Verbal Regret Coefficient is that innate ensemble of dispositions which
guarantees that after uttering a number of words, you will regret
having said a certain percentage of them.&nbsp; <br /></p>
<p>My first choice for a name was<i> Regret Coefficient</i>,
but incredibly a quick search seems to indicate that the insurance
industry already uses that term.&nbsp;&nbsp; If my theory catches on -- and you'll know that when someone dedicates a wikipedia entry -- I
don't want to have to deal with copyright and trademark infringement.&nbsp;
So Verbal Regret Coefficient it is.</p>
<p>The Verbal Regret Coefficient
or VRC will ultimately be quantified, and like a cholesterol count,
we'll all be assigned a VRC value.&nbsp; Most will fall into an average
range, but there will be outliers.&nbsp; A high VRC manifests itself
behaviorally in many ways: some high VRC'ers are purposely
controversial, outspoken, brash or arrogant.&nbsp; While others simply fail
to think before they speak.&nbsp; In case you're wondering, I am one of
those outliers with a dangerously high VRC.&nbsp; Furthermore, I'm quite
sure I don't fit into the arrogant and controversial group.&nbsp; But it's
not all good news for those with a low VRC. &nbsp; Although low VRC'ers tend
not to say anything regrettable, they tend also not to say anything of
substance either.&nbsp; We call these people politicians.<br /></p>
<p>If the current social trend continues, we'll get away with blaming any verbal <i>faux pas</i>
to our VRC.&nbsp; They'll be VRC support groups and celebrity VRC rehabilitation clinics.&nbsp;&nbsp;
Or maybe we could avoid all this silliness and just heed the advice of
Mark Twain who once said:</p>
<blockquote><i><span class="body">It is better to keep your mouth closed and let people think you are a fool than to open it and remove all doubt.</span></i><br /></blockquote>
<p>But that is sooooo hard.&nbsp;</p>
<p>
</p>
<p><br /></p>

AUTHOR: Jim Connors-Oracle
TITLE: Framework to Help Create Small Footprint RAM Resident Solaris Configurations
DATE: 02/07/2007 13:33:44
PRIMARY CATEGORY: Sun
STATUS: publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
-----
BODY: 
<p>As Sun continues to avail more of its intellectual property to the
community, the advantages Sun employees have regarding access to
internal resources almost disappear.&nbsp; In fact now when attempting
to post questions to internal Sun mail aliases, I am often times
redirected to the community.&nbsp; The ramifications of this change hit me
square in the gut this summer.</p> 
  <p>Having stumbled upon an internal project investigating how Solaris might be minimized for
embedded use, I thought an interesting offshoot of this effort might be
to create a ZFS appliance.&nbsp; This device would boot from flash entirely
into RAM, and all state would be maintained by the ZFS volumes.&nbsp; Turns
out this may be a little more tricky than anticipated, and future ZFS
enhancements to Solaris (ZFS boot) may make this idea moot.</p> 
  <p>Based on an <a title="OpenSolaris ZFS discussion" href="http://www.opensolaris.org/jive/thread.jspa;jsessionid=59EB539300ABA51B51E57225AF3F5245?messageID=50448&amp;#50448">OpenSolaris ZFS discussion</a>
I initiated, observers went off and wrote about this topic elsewhere, some
predicting that Sun would be releasing embedded ZFS appliances.&nbsp; Whoa,
hold on there, not so fast.&nbsp; We have no plans (at least that I know of) to do any such thing.&nbsp; This was nothing more than a pet project of mine.&nbsp; Serves me right for announcing that I was a Sun employee.</p> 
  <p> But there was some good that came out of this dialog.&nbsp; In addition to learning
the valuable lesson of being careful what you write, interest in the notion of using Solaris as
an &quot;embedded&quot; OS was quite apparent.&nbsp;&nbsp; As a consequence,&nbsp; I thought it might make sense to
publish the basic framework used to create a custom Solaris
miniroot.&nbsp; Included below is the introduction section of the <a title="README" href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/embedded_solaris/README.txt">README</a> file:&nbsp;<br /></p> 
  <blockquote><i>1.0 Introduction</i><br /><br /><i>With
the advent of Solaris 10 Update 1 and its migration to the grub(5)
bootloader, it becomes quite feasible and straightforward to consider
creating small footprint &quot;embedded&quot; versions of Solaris which boot
directly into RAM. This project is based upon work done by Shudong Zhou
to create a minimized Solaris for embedded use.&nbsp; The doc/ directory
contains some of the original documentation and scripts used to build
such an environment.</i><br /><br /><i>It is expected that entities may
want to provide further functionality and customizations to this
environment. In order to assist in this endeavor, the original work has
been enhanced to utilize the Java-based ant(1) build tool. For a
further description on how a miniroot image is created, see the section
on &quot;Understanding the ant(1) build process&quot;.</i></blockquote> 
  <p>You can download the framework <a title="here" href="https://blogs.oracle.com/jtc/resource/embedded_solaris/eos_framework.zip">here</a></p> 
  <p>Miscellaneous notes:</p> 
  <ul> 
    <li>This
framework assumes availability of a standard Solaris distribution.&nbsp;
Although not confirmed, I suspect it may not be real hard to
augment for OpenSolaris.</li> 
    <li>The current framework produces a RAM resident version of Solaris that is about 60MB in size.&nbsp; Note: there is no windowing included.<br /></li> 
    <li>These
configs are system specific.&nbsp; The reference implementation for the
included framework is an iGoLogic i84810 motherboard.&nbsp; Some key specs
are:</li> 
    <ul> 
      <li>1 GHz Celeron Processor</li> 
      <li>512 MB RAM on board</li> 
      <li>Compact Flash slot on bottom of motherboard</li> 
      <li>146mm x 104mm<br /></li> 
      <li>Runs Solaris 10 U3 out of the box<br /></li> 
      <li>For more info contact iGoLogic an <a title="http://www.igologic.com" href="http://www.igologic.com/">http://www.igologic.com</a></li> 
    </ul> 
    <li>Once an image is created, you'll need to set up grub(5) on your media.&nbsp; Here's a pointer to a URL explaining <a title="Grub on a stick" href="http://uadmin.blogspot.com/2005/11/grub-on-stick.html">Grub on a stick</a>.&nbsp; A copy of this URL is also included in the framework under the doc/ directory.<br /></li> 
    <li>Here's a picture of the motherboard with ruler included to give you a feel for its size.&nbsp; <br /></li>

COMMENT: 
AUTHOR: Jim Connors
EMAIL: james.connors@sun.com
URL: null
DATE: 02/20/2007 06:29:21
This solution can boot from both USB and flash.  I honestly don't know how the flash socket interfaces with the motherboard, only that the BIOS gives options to boot from flash or USB.  The trick is to get grub on either your USB and/or flash device.

As to whether you'll be able to boot right out of the box depends upon your system.  This boot image is definitely machine-specific.  Your system may require special device drivers, among others, which aren't included in this miniroot.

With regards to size, the image is approximately 60MB expanded.  Grub actually stores it as a compressed image, consequently it takes up only about 30MB on flash.  The image, plus the minimal Grub overhead is all that's needed.  In effect the flash or USB device, is read-only, much like the way a CD-ROM is used to install Solaris.  Once the image is booted, Solaris runs entirely from RAM.
